The Infinity of God's Knowledge
Concludes discussion on God's knowledge being infinite and encompassing all.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of God's knowledge being infinite and how it encompasses infinite objects like numbers and shapes.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is boundless and never fails to relate to anything.

This lesson examines a concluding passage from a section on divine attributes, specifically focusing on the infinity of God's knowledge. The author clarifies that 'not finite' means His knowledge never ends nor becomes incapable of relating to objects, and it even encompasses infinite realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic carefully, noting the key terms like 'غير متناه' and 'محيط'.
2
Understand the definition
The passage defines what 'not finite' means: it does not cease and does not fail to pertain to the known. Consider why this clarification is important.
3
Consider the scope
The passage adds that God's knowledge encompasses infinite things like numbers and shapes. Reflect on how this attribute relates to God's omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وختم هذا المبحث بأن علم الله تعالى غير متناه بمعنى أنه لا ينقطع ولا يصير بحيث لا يتعلق بالمعلوم، ومحيط بما هو غير متناه كالأعداد والأشكال.
And he concluded this section with [the statement] that the knowledge of God Most High is not finite, meaning that it does not cease and does not become such that it does not pertain to the known, and it encompasses that which is infinite, like numbers and shapes.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage concludes a preceding discussion on the nature of divine knowledge. It defines God's knowledge as 'not finite' (غير متناه), clarifying the meaning: it does not stop or become incapable of relating to objects of knowledge. The author also affirms that it encompasses infinite things such as numbers and geometric shapes, demonstrating its boundless scope.

Key Terms
علم الله
غير متناه
محيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'infinite knowledge' means God knows an infinite number of things sequentially, one after another.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'not finite' means it never ceases and does not fail to relate to the known, implying that His knowledge is always present and comprehensive.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in what sense is God's knowledge 'not finite'?
A) It has no beginning.
B) It never ceases and does not fail to relate to the known.
C) It is limited to finite objects.
D) It is only about necessary truths.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is infinite because it never ceases and covers everything, including infinite objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's knowledge never ceases and encompasses infinite numbers and shapes. But does your awareness of His all-encompassing knowledge ever cease when you are alone or when you sin? How can you train yourself to live with the constant awareness that nothing is hidden from Him?
?
This passage tells us that God's knowledge extends to all things, infinite in scope. Yet, do you truly believe that every thought, every glance, every intention is known to Him? If so, why do you still allow yourself to be heedless? What concrete step will you take today to remind yourself of His infinite knowledge?
?
The author uses numbers and shapes as examples of infinite things God knows. But what about your own life—your past, present, future, and even your hidden doubts? If God knows all of that infinitely, how does that change the way you approach repentance or your reliance on Him? Will you commit to a specific act of turning back to Him today?

The Ash'ari Doctrine of Divine Will
States consensus on God's will and defines Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the universal consensus that God is described as 'willing' and the specific Ash'ari teaching that everything that occurs is willed by God, including things He prohibits.

Core Idea
God's will encompasses all that happens, even prohibited acts.

This passage opens the fourth section on God's will (إرادة). The author first establishes that all rational theologians and philosophers agree that God is called 'willing' (مريد), based on revelation and the nature of His action. He then concludes with the core Ash'ari position: everything God wills occurs, and everything that occurs is willed by Him, whether good or evil, commanded or prohibited. This view aligns with the Salaf and contrasts with the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: first the consensus, then the doctrinal conclusion.
The passage is dense with technical terms; take your time.
2
Identify the basis for consensus
The author says the consensus is based on scriptural usage and the rational proof that God acts by choice. Consider why choice implies will.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari definition
Focus on the sentence beginning 'مذهب أهل الحق'. Break down what it means for everything willed to exist and everything existing to be willed.
Note the key phrase 'وإن لم يكن مرضياً ولا مأموراً به بل منهياً عنه' — this is the distinctive claim.
4
Contrast with the Mu'tazila
The passage ends by noting Mu'tazila opposition in 'the two principles'. Reflect on what two issues they likely denied.
The two principles are likely: (1) that God wills all events, (2) that even sinful acts are willed by God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع في أنه تعالى مريد وهو اتفاق بين المتكلمين والحكماء وجميع الفرق على إطلاق القول بأنه مريد، وشاع ذلك في كلام الله تعالى وكلام الأنبياء عليهم السلام ودل عليه ما ثبت من كونه تعالى فاعلاً بالاختيار. وختم هذا المبحث بقوله : مذهب أهل الحق أن كل ما أراد الله تعالى فهو كائن وأن كل كائن فهو مراد له وإن لم يكن مرضياً ولا مأموراً به بل منهياً عنه وهذا ما السلف أن ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن وخالفت المعتزلة في الأصلين.
The fourth section: on that He Most High is willing, and it is an agreement among the theologians and philosophers and all sects to absolutely say that He is willing, and that became widespread in the speech of God Most High and the speech of the prophets, peace be upon them, and it is indicated by what has been established of His being an agent by choice. And he concluded this section with his statement: The doctrine of the people of truth is that everything that God Most High wills exists, and everything that exists is willed by Him, even if it is not approved nor commanded but rather prohibited. And this is what the Salaf [hold]: that what God wills occurs, and what He does not will does not occur. And the Mu'tazila opposed in the two principles.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal presentation of the fourth section on God's will (إرادة). The author first notes the universal consensus (اتفاق) among theologians, philosophers, and all sects in affirming that God is described as 'willing' (مريد). This is supported by scriptural expressions and the rational proof that God acts by choice (فاعلاً بالاختيار). The passage then gives the conclusion of the section: the position of Ahl al-Haqq (أهل الحق) is that whatever God wills necessarily happens, and everything that happens is willed by God, even if it is not morally approved, commanded, or even prohibited. This is the Salaf's view, summarized as 'what God wills is, and what He does not will is not'. The Mu'tazila are noted as opposing in two matters (الأصلين), likely referring to their denial that all events are willed by God.

Key Terms
مريد
إرادة
مذهب أهل الحق
السلف
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will is limited to what He commands or approves, and that He does not will evil or prohibited acts.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that everything God wills occurs, and everything that occurs is willed by Him, even if it is not approved nor commanded but rather prohibited. Thus, God's will covers all events, including evil and sin.

Checkpoint
What is the position of Ahl al-Haqq regarding God's will?
A) Only good acts are willed by God.
B) Everything that God wills occurs, and everything that occurs is willed by Him.
C) God does not will evil.
D) God's will is only about essential matters.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine affirms that God's will is comprehensive: everything that happens is willed by God, including what He prohibits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin in private, do you truly believe that sin occurred by God's will? How does that belief affect the sincerity of your repentance and your hope in His mercy?
?
Can you sincerely thank God for a trial that came from His will, even if it involved someone's injustice? Where do you feel resistance in your heart to accepting that even evil is under His will?
?
If God's will is absolutely comprehensive, what comfort does that give you when you face uncertainty or suffering? What step can you take this week to deepen your trust in His decree?

God is Hearing, Seeing, Living: Attributes of Perfection
Affirms divine hearing, seeing, and life as necessary and universally agreed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attributes of hearing, seeing, and life are essential divine attributes known by religious necessity, scripture, and consensus.

Core Idea
God's hearing, seeing, and life are known by religious necessity, not just reason.

This lesson presents the fifth section of the text, which affirms three essential divine attributes: hearing, seeing, and life. The author asserts that these are not merely intellectual deductions but are known by necessity of religion (ḍarūrat al-dīn) and are firmly established in the primary sources. Moreover, there is universal consensus (ijmāʿ) among religious people and rational thinkers. Understanding this helps us appreciate the foundational role of revelation and consensus in affirming divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noticing the key terms: سميع, بصير, حي, ضرورة الدين, إجماع.
The text is compact; read it twice.
2
Identify the sources of knowledge
The passage mentions three sources: religious necessity, scripture (Book and Sunnah), and consensus. Consider how each contributes to establishing these attributes.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by citing Imam al-Haramayn. Think about why the author invokes a scholar at this point to reinforce the doctrine.
Imam al-Haramayn is al-Juwayni, a major Ash'ari theologian.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس: في أنه تعالى سميع بصير حي. وقال : قد علم بالضرورة من الدين، وثبت في الكتاب والسنة بحيث لا يمكن إنكاره ولا تأويله أن الباري تعالى حي سميع بصير وانعقد إجماع أهل الأديان بل جميع العقلاء على ذلك. وختم هذا المبحث بما قاله إمام الحرمين في هذا الشأن.
The fifth section: on that He Most High is hearing, seeing, living. And he said: It is known by necessity of religion, and established in the Book and the Sunnah in a way that cannot be denied nor interpreted allegorically, that the Creator Most High is living, hearing, seeing, and the consensus of the people of religions — rather all rational people — has been concluded upon that. And he concluded this section with what Imam al-Haramayn said in this regard.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth section, which deals with God being hearing (سميع), seeing (بصير), and living (حي). The author asserts that these attributes are known by necessity of religion (بالضرورة من الدين) and are firmly established in the Quran and Sunnah, such that they cannot be denied or allegorically interpreted. Additionally, there is consensus (إجماع) among the people of religions — indeed all rational people — on these attributes. The section concludes by citing Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) on the matter.

Key Terms
سميع
بصير
حي
ضرورة الدين
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these attributes are metaphorical, not real.
Correction: The passage asserts they are known by religious necessity and cannot be allegorized.

Checkpoint
How does the passage describe the knowledge that God is living, hearing, and seeing?
A) It is a rational proof.
B) It is known by necessity of religion and established by text and consensus.
C) It is only confirmed by prophets.
D) It is a philosophical deduction.

Key Takeaway
God's hearing, seeing, and life are essential attributes known by religious necessity, revelation, and consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's hearing and seeing are known by religious necessity, why do you still act at times as if He does not hear your whispers or see your secret deeds? What specific situation this week can you change to live more consciously of His presence?
?
This passage says all rational people agree that God is living, hearing, and seeing. Do you truly believe that, or are there moments when your behavior suggests otherwise? What would it cost you to align your daily actions with this universal truth?
?
The passage asserts that these attributes cannot be interpreted allegorically. Is there any part of your understanding of God's attributes that you have softened or explained away to make them more comfortable? What step will you take today to affirm His perfect attributes without reservation?

God is Speaking and the Eternal Speech
Proves God as speaker and gives two arguments for the pre-eternity of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theology establishes that God is speaking and that His speech is pre-eternal, based on prophetic testimony and rational arguments.

Core Idea
God’s speech is eternal; He is the speaker in whom speech inheres, not a creator of sounds.

This lesson introduces the sixth section on God's attribute of speech. It first establishes that God is indeed 'speaking' through the testimony of prophets, whose truthfulness is independently verified by miracles. Then it presents two rational arguments for the pre-eternity of God's speech, distinguishing it from created sounds and mental forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once then the English translation. Notice the concise style and the logical flow from proving God is speaking to proving His speech is eternal.
The passage contains two main units: the proof of God as speaker, and two arguments for pre-eternity.
2
Understand the proof for God being speaking
The proof relies on recurrent reports from prophets (تواتر) and their miracles, avoiding circularity. Also a rational analogy to hearing and sight is mentioned.
This part assures that God is described as 'speaking' (متكلم) without needing a separate divine speech to confirm the prophets.
3
Learn the first argument for pre-eternity
The first argument defines the speaker as the one in whom speech inheres (قام به الكلام), not the one who produces speech elsewhere.
This distinguishes God's speech from created voices.
4
Learn the second argument for pre-eternity
The second argument explains that true speech consists of ordered audible letters, not mental or written forms.
God's speech is not the imagined or written form but the eternal attribute.
5
Reflect on the distinctions
Consider why these arguments are necessary: to affirm that God's speech is eternal and not a created accident.
This connects to the broader Islamic theology of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس : في أنه متكلم - وقد تواتر القول بذلك عن الأنبياء وقد ثبت صدقهم بدلالة المعجزات من غير توقف على إخبار الله تعالى عن صدقهم بطريق التكلم ليلزم الدور وقد يستدل على ذلك بدليل عقلي على قياس ما مر في السمع والبصر واستدل على قدم الكلام بوجهين . أحدهما : أن المتكلم من قام به الكلام لا من أوجد الكلام ولو في محل آخر. وأما الثاني : فلأن الكلام في المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة لا في الصورة المرسومة في الخيال أو المخزونة في الحافظة أو المنقوشة بأشكال الكتابة.
The sixth section: on that He is speaking — and the statement to that effect has been recurrently reported from the prophets, and their truthfulness has been established by the evidence of miracles, without depending on God's informing about their truthfulness through the attribute of speech, lest there be circularity. And a rational proof can also be adduced for that, analogous to what was mentioned regarding hearing and sight. And he argued for the pre-eternity of speech with two aspects. The first: that the speaker is he in whom speech inheres, not he who produces speech even in another locus. The second: because speech consists of ordered audible letters, not in the form drawn in the imagination, or stored in memory, or inscribed in shapes of writing.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is from the sixth section on God's attribute of speech (كلام). It begins by establishing that God is described as 'speaking' (متكلم). The proof is twofold: first, reports from prophets are recurrent (متواتر) and their truthfulness is proven by miracles, without needing God's speech to confirm them — otherwise circularity would arise. Second, a rational proof (دليل عقلي) similar to that for hearing and sight is mentioned. The author then gives two arguments for the pre-eternity (قدم) of God's speech. The first defines the speaker as the one in whom speech subsists (قام به الكلام), not merely the one who creates speech in another object. The second argument explains that true speech consists of ordered audible letters (المنتظم من الحروف المسموعة), not the pictorial form in the imagination, the stored form in memory, or the inscribed shapes in writing. This distinguishes God's eternal speech from created speech.

Key Terms
متكلم
كلام
قدم الكلام
حروف مسموعة
تواتر
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some equate speech with producing sound in air or with mental concepts.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the speaker is one in whom speech inheres, and that true speech is ordered audible letters, not mental or written forms.

Checkpoint
What is the second argument for the pre-eternity of divine speech?
A) Speech is an accident that cannot subsist in God.
B) True speech consists of ordered audible letters, not mental or written forms.
C) The prophets spoke about it.
D) It is necessary for God to be called speaking.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is an eternal attribute inhering in Him, not a created sound or mental concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech is an eternal attribute inhering in Him, and not merely created sounds, how does that change the reverence with which you recite the Qur'an? Will you recite it as if you are connecting directly to an eternal reality, or as just words?
?
The passage warns against reducing speech to mental concepts or written inscriptions. Is your understanding of God's word merely intellectual, or does it engage your heart and soul in a way that transforms your daily life?
?
The prophets' truthfulness is established without needing God's speech to confirm it, to avoid circularity. Do you personally rely on external proofs to maintain your faith, or have you cultivated a direct certainty that God's revelation is true, independent of constant reassurance?

Attributes of the Noble Quran
Enumerates three attributes of the Quran with proof texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three specific attributes of the Quran mentioned by the author (dhikr, Arabic, and revelation by Jibril) and the conclusion that the Quran is one.

Core Idea
The Quran is a reminder, Arabic, and revealed by Jibril, yet it is one.

In this passage, the author begins by enumerating key attributes of the Quran derived from scriptural verses. He then asserts that the Quran is one, setting the stage for the subsequent theological discussion about the nature of God's eternal speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the three attributes with proofs
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify each attribute and the verse that supports it.
2
Understand the significance of 'one'
The author ends the list with 'one' (واحد). Reflect on why unity is emphasized despite multiple attributes.
This unity is the basis for the debate that follows.
3
Notice the transition
Observe how the passage prepares for the discussion on eternal speech by establishing the Quran's unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تناول صفات القرآن الكريم فقال بأنه ذكر. قال تعالى : وإِنَّهُ ذِكْرٌ لكَ وَلِقَوْمِكَ . . وعربي لقوله تعالى : ( إنا جعلناه قرآناً عربياً ) و نزل به جبريل لقوله تعالى نزل به الروح الأمين على قلبك لتكون من المرسلين . واحد . ثم ذكر أدلة علماء الكلام في هذه القضية، وذكر الخاتمة بأن كلامه الأزلي واحد . قال عبد الله بن سعيد: إنه في الأزل ليس شيئاً من الأقسام، وإنما يصير أحدهما فيما لا يزال. وقد ضعف هذا الرأي، ثم ذكر رأي الإمام الرازي (وهو في الأزل خبر ومرجع البواقي إليه . وقد ضعف صاحب المقاصد هذا الرأي أيضاً.
Then he took up the attributes of the Noble Quran and said that it is a reminder (dhikr). God Most High said: 'And it is a reminder for you and your people.' And Arabic, according to His saying: 'We have made it an Arabic Quran.' And Jibril brought it down, according to His saying: 'The Trustworthy Spirit brought it down upon your heart that you may be among the warners.' One. Then he mentioned the arguments of the theologians on this matter, and mentioned the conclusion that His eternal speech is one. 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id said: It is in pre-eternity not any of the divisions, but rather it becomes one of them in the future. And this opinion was deemed weak. Then he mentioned the opinion of Imam al-Razi—that it is in pre-eternity news (khabar) and the reference of the rest to it. And the author of al-Maqasid also deemed this opinion weak.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage continues the section on divine speech by discussing the attributes of the Noble Quran. It lists three attributes: it is a 'reminder' (ذكر) as per the verse, it is Arabic as per another verse, and it was brought down by Jibril. The author then states the Quran is 'one' (واحد). Next, he turns to theological arguments about the nature of God's eternal speech, concluding that it is one. He then presents two minority views: that of 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id (likely Ibn Kullab) who said that in pre-eternity the divine speech is not any of the categories (command, prohibition, etc.) but becomes one in the future, and that of Imam al-Razi who held that it is in pre-eternity 'news' (خبر) and the other categories refer back to it. Both views are deemed weak by the author of al-Maqasid (i.e., al-Taftazani).

Key Terms
ذكر
عربي
نزل به جبريل
واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Quran having multiple attributes means it is multiple in pre-eternity.
Correction: The author emphasizes that the Quran is one, despite its attributes.

Checkpoint
Which attribute of the Quran is supported by the verse 'We have made it an Arabic Quran'?
A) It is a reminder
B) It is Arabic
C) It was revealed by Jibril
D) It is one

Key Takeaway
The Quran is defined by three scriptural attributes (dhikr, Arabic, revelation by Jibril) yet remains a single entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what ways do you treat the Quran as a 'reminder' (dhikr) in your daily life? Do you allow it to remind you of Allah, or do you read it merely out of habit?
?
If the Quran is Arabic, and you are not a native Arabic speaker, how do you approach the fact that the very words of Allah are in a language you may not fully understand? Does this challenge you to learn or to seek deeper meaning?
?
The Quran is one. Yet we often focus on parts (verses, surahs) separately. How can you better approach the Quran as a single, unified message from Allah to you personally?

Debate on Eternal Speech: Ibn Sa'id and al-Razi
Presents and critiques Ibn Sa'id's and al-Razi's views on eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two minority views of Ibn Sa'id and al-Razi on the nature of God's eternal speech and why the author considers them weak.

Core Idea
Ibn Sa'id and al-Razi proposed different categorizations of eternal speech, both rejected.

After establishing the Quran's attributes and unity, the author turns to the theological debate about God's eternal speech. He presents two alternative views by Ibn Sa'id and al-Razi, but dismisses both as weak, affirming the mainstream position that eternal speech is a single undivided attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sa'id's view
Read the part about Ibn Sa'id: he says the eternal speech is not any division in pre-eternity but becomes one in the future.
This view tries to avoid multiplicity.
2
Read al-Razi's view
Read the part about al-Razi: he says it is essentially 'news' (khabar) in pre-eternity and other categories refer back to it.
3
Understand why both are weak
The author explicitly says both views are weak. Reflect on why the mainstream might reject these distinctions.
The mainstream holds that eternal speech is one attribute encompassing all categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تناول صفات القرآن الكريم فقال بأنه ذكر. قال تعالى : وإِنَّهُ ذِكْرٌ لكَ وَلِقَوْمِكَ . . وعربي لقوله تعالى : ( إنا جعلناه قرآناً عربياً ) و نزل به جبريل لقوله تعالى نزل به الروح الأمين على قلبك لتكون من المرسلين . واحد . ثم ذكر أدلة علماء الكلام في هذه القضية، وذكر الخاتمة بأن كلامه الأزلي واحد . قال عبد الله بن سعيد: إنه في الأزل ليس شيئاً من الأقسام، وإنما يصير أحدهما فيما لا يزال. وقد ضعف هذا الرأي، ثم ذكر رأي الإمام الرازي (وهو في الأزل خبر ومرجع البواقي إليه . وقد ضعف صاحب المقاصد هذا الرأي أيضاً.
Then he took up the attributes of the Noble Quran and said that it is a reminder (dhikr). God Most High said: 'And it is a reminder for you and your people.' And Arabic, according to His saying: 'We have made it an Arabic Quran.' And Jibril brought it down, according to His saying: 'The Trustworthy Spirit brought it down upon your heart that you may be among the warners.' One. Then he mentioned the arguments of the theologians on this matter, and mentioned the conclusion that His eternal speech is one. 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id said: It is in pre-eternity not any of the divisions, but rather it becomes one of them in the future. And this opinion was deemed weak. Then he mentioned the opinion of Imam al-Razi—that it is in pre-eternity news (khabar) and the reference of the rest to it. And the author of al-Maqasid also deemed this opinion weak.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage continues the section on divine speech by discussing the attributes of the Noble Quran. It lists three attributes: it is a 'reminder' (ذكر) as per the verse, it is Arabic as per another verse, and it was brought down by Jibril. The author then states the Quran is 'one' (واحد). Next, he turns to theological arguments about the nature of God's eternal speech, concluding that it is one. He then presents two minority views: that of 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id (likely Ibn Kullab) who said that in pre-eternity the divine speech is not any of the categories (command, prohibition, etc.) but becomes one in the future, and that of Imam al-Razi who held that it is in pre-eternity 'news' (خبر) and the other categories refer back to it. Both views are deemed weak by the author of al-Maqasid (i.e., al-Taftazani).

Key Terms
كلام أزلي
عبد الله بن سعيد
الرازي
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that eternal speech must be a specific category (e.g., command or news) in pre-eternity.
Correction: The mainstream holds that eternal speech is one undivided attribute; attempts to specify a category are considered weak.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sa'id, what is the state of divine speech in pre-eternity?
A) It is news (khabar).
B) It is not any of the divisions.
C) It is both command and prohibition.
D) It is one of the divisions.

Key Takeaway
Both Ibn Sa'id and al-Razi's attempts to categorize eternal speech are rejected; the mainstream view is that eternal speech is one undivided attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that even respected scholars like Ibn Sa'id and al-Razi had views on divine speech that were ultimately deemed weak. When you hold a belief, is it because you've inherited it from a respected teacher, or have you critically examined it against the evidence? How willing are you to let go of a cherished opinion when it conflicts with established theological consensus?
?
The mainstream position affirms that God's speech is one undivided eternal attribute. When you read the Qur'an, do you consciously remind yourself that it is not merely a temporal revelation but a reflection of God's eternal speech? How might recognizing its eternity deepen your reverence and awe when reciting or listening to it?
?
Ibn Sa'id speculated that in pre-eternity divine speech had no category, and al-Razi insisted it was always 'news.' Both overstepped into unguided speculation. Where in your own faith do you find yourself wanting to define God beyond what He has revealed? What step can you take today to humbly accept the boundaries of knowledge set by revelation?

Disputed Divine Attributes: An Overview
Section header enumerating disputed attributes and groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope of the seventh section: which attributes are considered disputed among theological schools, and which groups are reviewed.

Core Idea
Not all divine attributes are universally agreed; even among Ahl al-Sunna there is debate.

This lesson introduces the seventh section of the chapter, which deals with attributes that are debated among theologians. The author clarifies that he is focusing on the views of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth) who affirm pre-eternal attributes, and he will review the positions of the Zahiris and Ash'aris. Understanding this context prepares you for the detailed arguments to follow.

Guided Path

Both are within Ahl al-Sunna, yet they differ on certain attributes.
3
List the specific attributes
The passage mentions pre-eternity (القدم), the Istawa (الاستواء), the Hand (اليد), and the Face (الوجه). These are examples of disputed attributes.

The passage ends by stating that this section is the conclusion of the third chapter. It serves as a summary of the discussion on attributes.

The Possibility of Knowing God's Essence
Sanusi's conclusion on knowing God's essence through three means.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowledge of God's essence is possible through multiple divinely granted means, as argued by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Knowing God's essence is possible through inspiration, divine knowledge, or direct vision.

This lesson examines a pivotal debate in Islamic theology: can we truly know God's essence? Sanusi presents both sides of the argument before delivering his conclusion. As you read, notice how he broadens the epistemic framework beyond mere rational proof, introducing alternative pathways that challenge the skeptical view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's position.
Notice the contrast between the opening debate and the final conclusion.
2
Identify the three means of knowledge
The passage concludes with three specific ways knowledge of God's essence can be obtained. Find each one and understand what it refers to.
These means are: inspiration (إلهام), necessary knowledge (علم ضروري), and direct vision after death.
3
Understand the broader argument
Sanusi rejects the idea that ways of conceptualization are limited. Consider how this conclusion relates to the preceding debate between those who permit and those who oppose such knowledge.
This lesson focuses on the conclusion; the debate itself is referenced but not detailed.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this question matters for Islamic theology. If God's essence can be known, what are the implications for human knowledge and spiritual experience?
This connects to broader discussions in kalām about the limits of reason and the role of divine grace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني : في العلم بحقيقته تعالى. وذكر أدلة المجوزين والمخالفين في ذلك . وختم هذا المبحث بقوله : (أنا) لا نسلم انحصار طرق التصور في ذلك. بل قد يحصل بالإلهام أو بخلق الله تعالى العلم الضروري بالكسبيات أو بصيرورة الأشياء مشاهدة للنفس عند مفارقتها البدن كسائر المجردات .
The second section: on knowledge of His essence Most High. And he mentioned the proofs of those who permit and those who oppose in that. And he concluded this section with his statement: 'We do not concede the exclusivity of the ways of conceptualization in that. Rather, it may occur by inspiration or by God Most High creating necessary knowledge of acquired matters or by the things becoming observable to the soul when it separates from the body, like other abstract entities.'
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is the second section of chapter four, on the possibility of knowing God's essence (العلم بحقيقته). The author presents the arguments of both those who permit such knowledge (المجوزين) and those who deny it (المخالفين). He then concludes by rejecting the claim that the ways of conceptualization (طرق التصور) are limited to certain methods. Instead, he proposes several alternative ways: inspiration (إلهام), God creating necessary knowledge of acquired things (العلم الضروري بالكسبيات), or direct observation of things by the soul after separation from the body (مفارقتها البدن), as with other abstract entities (سائر المجردات). This suggests a broader epistemology for knowing God's essence.

Key Terms
العلم بالحقيقة
حقيقته
الإلهام
العلم الضروري
الكسبيات
المجردات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of God's essence is impossible (a view held by some theologians).
Correction: Sanusi argues that it is possible through inspiration, divinely given necessary knowledge, or direct vision after death.

Checkpoint
According to the conclusion of this section, how can knowledge of God's essence be obtained?
A) Only through rational proof.
B) Only through scripture.
C) Through inspiration, necessary knowledge, or direct vision.
D) It cannot be obtained at all.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi argues that knowledge of God's essence is possible through inspiration, divinely created necessary knowledge, or direct vision after death, thereby expanding the ways of conceptualization beyond ordinary means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever experienced a moment where you felt God's reality without any rational argument—perhaps through a sudden insight or deep inner certainty? If not, are you open to seeking that kind of direct inspiration, or do you dismiss it as unreliable? What would it take for you to trust a divinely-given insight?
?
If you truly believed that after death you will see God's essence face to face, would you live differently today? Are you preparing your soul for that vision, or are you so attached to this world that the thought of leaving it frightens you? What needs to change in your daily priorities to prepare for that encounter?
?
Sanusi says God can create necessary knowledge in you—knowledge that bypasses doubt and effort. Do you secretly prefer the comfort of rational control over the vulnerability of receiving knowledge directly from God? Are you willing to ask God to grant you that certainty, even if it upsets your current intellectual framework?

5 min
Refutation of the Four States View
Refutation of a mistaken theological view about divine essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that Allah's essence is distinguished only by four (or five) accidental states is fundamentally mistaken.

Core Idea
Confusing accidental attributes with the essence itself leads to theological error.

The author now addresses a specific theological position that attempts to explain how the Necessary Being is distinct from contingent beings. Some theologians proposed that the essence of the Necessary is essentially the same as others, and only differentiated by four states. This lesson unpacks why this view is categorically rejected as a confusion of accident with substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two main parts: the statement of the wrong view and the author's dismissal.
Pay attention to the terms 'ahwal arba‘a' (four states).
2
Identify the wrong view and its variants
The passage mentions four states (necessary existence, life, knowledge, power) and optionally a fifth (divinity). These are said to distinguish the essence which is otherwise similar to others.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author declares this an error of confusing the accidental (al-‘arid) with the subject (al-ma‘rud). The states are accidents, not constitutive of the essence.
This is a key point in kalam: attributes do not change the essence but are additional.
4
Reflect on why this matters
This refutation preserves the uniqueness of Allah's essence. If the essence were similar, then existence and attributes would be accidental, undermining tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فما ذهب إليه بعض المتكلمين أن ذات الواجب تماثل سائر الذوات وإنما تمتاز بأحوال أربعة هي الوجود الواجبي الذي قد يعبر عنه بالوجوب، والحياة والعلم التام والقدرة الكاملة، أو بحالة خامسة تسمى بالإلهية هي الموجبة لهذه الأربع تمسكاً بالوجوه المذكورة. غلط من باب اشتباه العارض بالمعروض .
فإن قيل : فكيف لم يلزم المتكلمين القائلين بتمام وجود الواجب والممكن تركب الواجب . . ؟
قلنا : لأن المتصف بالوجوب، والمقتضي للوجوب هو المخالفة الماهية لسائر الماهيات، والوجود زائد عليها.
So what some theologians have held, that the essence of the Necessary Being is similar to all other essences and is only distinguished by four states: the necessary existence which may be expressed as necessity, life, perfect knowledge, and complete power, or by a fifth state called divinity which necessitates these four, adhering to the aforementioned arguments — is an error of the type of confusing the accident with the subject. For if it is said: How did it not entail for the theologians who say that the existence of the Necessary and the contingent is complete that the Necessary is composite? We say: Because the attribute qualified by necessity, and the necessitator of necessity, is the essential difference from all other essences, and existence is additional to it.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific group of theologians (ba'd al-mutakallimin) who claim that the essence of the Necessary Being (dhat al-wajib) is similar (tumathil) to all other essences and is distinguished only by four states (ahwal): necessary existence (al-wujud al-wajibi, also called wujub), life (hayat), perfect knowledge (al-ilm al-tamm), and complete power (al-qudra al-kamila). Alternatively, they propose a fifth state called divinity (ilahiyya) that necessitates the four. The author dismisses this as an error of confusing the accident (arid) with the subject (ma'rud), meaning these states are accidental, not constitutive. The passage then presents an objection (fa-in qila): How did it not entail composition (tarkib) for those who say that existence (wujud) is fully present in both the Necessary and the contingent? The answer (qulna): Because that which is qualified by necessity (al-muttasif bi al-wujub) and that which necessitates necessity (al-muqaddi li al-wujub) is the essential difference (al-mukhalafa al-mahiyya) from all other essences, and existence is additional (za'id) to it. In other words, composition is avoided because the essence itself is different; the existence added does not cause composition as it is not part of the essence.

Key Terms
ذات الواجب
أحوال أربعة
الوجود الواجبي
الحياة
العلم التام
القدرة الكاملة
الإلهية
اشتباه العارض بالمعروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The essence of Allah is similar to other essences, and only distinguished by states like existence, life, knowledge, and power.
Correction: Allah's essence is unique and cannot be similar to any other; these attributes are not states but are real and eternal, but they do not constitute or compose the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the error in the view that the Necessary Being's essence is distinguished by four states?
A) The states are not real but imaginary.
B) It confuses the accident with the subject.
C) It denies the attributes of Allah.
D) It leads to denial of divine unity.

Key Takeaway
The essence of the Necessary Being is inherently unique and not similar to others; distinguishing attributes are not accidental states but real without causing composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever subconsciously thought of God's attributes as 'add-ons' to an ordinary essence, rather than realizing His essence is utterly unique and beyond comparison? How will you actively reshape your mental image of Allah starting today?
?
The error described is confusing what is accidental with what is essential. In your own life, do you confuse worldly, passing matters (like status, wealth, or opinions of others) with the eternal and essential (your relationship with Allah)? Write down one concrete step you will take tonight to realign your priorities.
?
If you truly believed that Allah's knowledge and power are not mere 'states' but are real and inseparable from His essence, how would your trust in Him change? Commit to one specific du'a today that reflects this deepened reliance.

Answering the Composition Objection
Dialectical objection and answer about composition of the Necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why adding existence to an essence does not necessarily lead to composition in the case of the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Existence added to a unique essence does not create composition because the essence itself necessitates existence.

After rejecting the four-states view, a potential objection arises: if existence is added to the essence, does that not make the Necessary Being composite? The author answers by distinguishing between what necessitates necessity and the existence that is added. This lesson unpacks that critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: 'fa-in qila... qulna' (if it is said... we say).
This is a standard kalam dialectical format.
2
Understand the objection
The objector asks: if both the Necessary and contingent have complete existence (tamam wujud), why doesn't the Necessary become composite (tarkib) due to adding existence?
The objection assumes existence is a separate component.
3
Grasp the answer's key distinction
The answer: what is qualified by necessity (al-muttasif bi al-wujub) and what necessitates necessity (al-muqaddi li al-wujub) is the essential difference (mukhalafa mahiyya) from all other essences. Existence (wujud) is additional (za'id) to that essence.
Essence is already unique; added existence does not cause composition because it is not a part of the essence.
4
Apply to the broader argument
This shows that composition is avoided because the essence is inherently different, not because existence is absent. The addition is accidental, not essential.
Contrast with contingent beings where essence and existence are distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فما ذهب إليه بعض المتكلمين أن ذات الواجب تماثل سائر الذوات وإنما تمتاز بأحوال أربعة هي الوجود الواجبي الذي قد يعبر عنه بالوجوب، والحياة والعلم التام والقدرة الكاملة، أو بحالة خامسة تسمى بالإلهية هي الموجبة لهذه الأربع تمسكاً بالوجوه المذكورة. غلط من باب اشتباه العارض بالمعروض .
فإن قيل : فكيف لم يلزم المتكلمين القائلين بتمام وجود الواجب والممكن تركب الواجب . . ؟
قلنا : لأن المتصف بالوجوب، والمقتضي للوجوب هو المخالفة الماهية لسائر الماهيات، والوجود زائد عليها.
So what some theologians have held, that the essence of the Necessary Being is similar to all other essences and is only distinguished by four states: the necessary existence which may be expressed as necessity, life, perfect knowledge, and complete power, or by a fifth state called divinity which necessitates these four, adhering to the aforementioned arguments — is an error of the type of confusing the accident with the subject. For if it is said: How did it not entail for the theologians who say that the existence of the Necessary and the contingent is complete that the Necessary is composite? We say: Because the attribute qualified by necessity, and the necessitator of necessity, is the essential difference from all other essences, and existence is additional to it.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific group of theologians (ba'd al-mutakallimin) who claim that the essence of the Necessary Being (dhat al-wajib) is similar (tumathil) to all other essences and is distinguished only by four states (ahwal): necessary existence (al-wujud al-wajibi, also called wujub), life (hayat), perfect knowledge (al-ilm al-tamm), and complete power (al-qudra al-kamila). Alternatively, they propose a fifth state called divinity (ilahiyya) that necessitates the four. The author dismisses this as an error of confusing the accident (arid) with the subject (ma'rud), meaning these states are accidental, not constitutive. The passage then presents an objection (fa-in qila): How did it not entail composition (tarkib) for those who say that existence (wujud) is fully present in both the Necessary and the contingent? In other words, composition is avoided because the essence itself is different; the existence added does not cause composition as it is not part of the essence.

Key Terms

المخالفة الماهية
الوجود الزائد
المتصف بالوجوب
المقتضي للوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what prevents composition in the Necessary Being when existence is added?
A) Existence is not really added.
B) The essence is simple and existence is not separate.
C) What necessitates necessity is the essential difference, not existence.
D) The four states are not true attributes.

Key Takeaway
Adding existence to a unique essence does not cause composition because the essence itself is the ground of necessity, and existence is an additional attribute not constitutive of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objection assumes that adding existence to an essence always causes composition. Have you ever subconsciously thought that Allah's attributes work like human attributes, leading to hidden comparisons? How does this answer (qulna) force you to purify your conception of Allah's oneness?
?
Since it is the unique essence itself that necessitates necessity, not existence, what does this tell you about your own reliance on Allah versus your reliance on your own existence? Are you more aware of your contingent, composite nature and your absolute need for the Necessary Being?
?
This passage shows that existence is additional to Allah's unique essence. Does this make you feel distant from Allah (as if He is beyond comprehension) or draw you closer in awe? How can you turn this theological insight into a deeper reverence and trust in His attributes?

The Reality of Tawheed and Its Boundaries
Defines tawheed and lists acceptable vs unacceptable views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what constitutes the core of tawheed and which views are considered violations or not.

Core Idea
Tawheed means no partner in divinity; some errors are minor, others are shirk.

This lesson explores the opening of the conclusion, where the author defines tawheed as the absence of a partner. He then distinguishes between views that do not break tawheed, such as affirming pre-eternal attributes, and those that do, like the pre-eternity of intellects. Finally, he lists groups that fell into shirk.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall structure.
2
Identify the two types of views
Notice that the author first lists views that do not invalidate tawheed, then those that are grave errors.
3
Examine the list of polytheistic sects
The author names Dualists, Magians, idol worshippers, and those who claim a son for God.
4
Reflect on the gravity of pre-eternal intellects
Consider why the author calls belief in pre-eternal intellects a 'tremendous matter'.
This error is contrasted with the earlier 'permissible' views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة حقيقة التوحيد عدم الشريك لم يخل بالتوحيد القول بقدم الصفات وإيجاد الحيوان لأفعاله وإن قبح لفظ الخلق وإن صح منه تفويض أمر الشرور والقبائح إلى الشيطان وأما القول بقدم العقول وإيجادها للنفوس والأجسام وقدم الأفلاك وتدبيرها لعالم العناصر فخطب هائل والمشركون وافقهم الثانوية القائلون بمبدأين نور وظلمة والمجوس القائلون بتفويض الشرور حتى الأجسام الخبيثة إلى أهرمن وإن جعل متولداً من يزدان وعبدة الأجسام لتأويلات توهموها والقائلون بالولد سبحان الله عما يشركون
قال خاتمة حقيقة التوحيد اعتقاد عدم الشريك في الألوهية وخواصها ولا نزاع لأهل الإسلام في أن تدبير العالم وخلق الأجسام واستحقاق العبادة وقدم ما يقوم بنفسه كلها من الخواص ونعني بالقدم بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالعدم وأما بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالغير فهو نفس الألوهية وبوجوب الوجود فنحن إنما نقول بالصفات القديمة دون الذوات ومع ذلك لا نجعل الصفة غير الذات والمعتزلة إنما يقولون بخلق العباد لأفعالهم دون غيرها من الأعراض والأجسام نعم تفويضهم تدبير شطر من حوادث العالم وهو الشرور والقبائح إلى الشيطان على خلاف مشيئة الله تعالى وإن كان بإقداره وتمكينه خطب صعب وأصعب منه قول الفلاسفة بقدم العقول وإيجادها للنفوس وبعض الأجسام
Conclusion: The reality of tawheed is the absence of a partner. The statement of the pre-eternity of attributes and the animal's creating its own actions, even if the word 'creation' is ugly, and if it is valid from him delegating the matter of evils and abominations to Satan – these do not corrupt tawheed. But the statement of the pre-eternity of intellects and their bringing into existence of souls and bodies, and the pre-eternity of the spheres, and their management of the world of elements – that is a tremendous matter. The polytheists, agreed with them by the Dualists who say there are two principles: light and darkness. And the Magians who say the delegation of evils, even the vile bodies, to Ahriman, even if they make him generated from Yazdan. And the worshippers of bodies due to interpretations they imagined, and those who say (God has) a son. Glory be to God above what they associate. He said: Conclusion: The reality of tawheed is the belief in the absence of a partner in divinity and its properties. There is no dispute among the people of Islam that the management of the world, the creation of bodies, the deservingness of worship, and the pre-eternity of what subsists by itself – all of these are among the properties. And we mean by pre-eternity the meaning of not being preceded by non-existence. As for the meaning of not being preceded by another, that is divinity itself, and by necessity of existence. So we only affirm the pre-eternal attributes, not the entities (essences), and yet we do not make the attribute other than the essence. The Mu'tazila only say that servants create their own actions, not other accidents and bodies. Yes, their delegation of the management of a portion of the world's events, namely evils and abominations, to Satan, contrary to the will of God, even if it is by His empowering and enabling – that is a difficult matter. And more difficult than it is the statement of the philosophers of the pre-eternity of intellects and their bringing into existence of souls and some bodies.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This concluding passage defines the reality of tawheed as the negation of a partner in divinity and its properties. The author first notes that certain claims do not invalidate tawheed: affirming the pre-eternity of attributes, the idea that animals create their own acts (even if 'creation' is not used), and delegating evils to Satan. However, the pre-eternity of intellects and celestial spheres is a grave error. The author then lists groups that fall into shirk: polytheists, Dualists (light/darkness), Magians (delegation to Ahriman), worshippers of bodies, and those who claim a son for God. The formal conclusion then clarifies: tawheed is belief in no partner in divinity and its specific properties (management of the world, creation, worship, self-subsistence). 'Pre-eternity' means not preceded by non-existence; when it means 'not preceded by another', that is divinity itself. The orthodox position affirms pre-eternal attributes but does not make them separate entities from the essence. The Mu'tazila only affirm human creation of actions, not of bodies or other accidents. The delegation of some evils to Satan is problematic, but the philosophers' claim of pre-eternal intellects creating souls and bodies is even worse.

Key Takeaway
Tawheed is fundamentally about rejecting partners with God, but some erroneous views are more severe than others.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Formal Meaning of Tawheed and Refutation of Mu'tazila and Philosophers
Formal definition of tawheed and refutation of sectarian errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of tawheed, the two meanings of pre-eternity, and the orthodox position on attributes and acts, along with refutations of Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Core Idea
Tawheed is belief in no partner in divinity and its properties; pre-eternal attributes are affirmed but not separate from essence.

This lesson presents the formal conclusion of the author's discussion of tawheed. He defines tawheed as belief in the absence of a partner in divinity and its specific properties. He then clarifies the meaning of pre-eternity, distinguishing between not being preceded by non-existence and not being preceded by another. After affirming pre-eternal attributes without separating them from the essence, he critiques the Mu'tazila for limiting creation to human actions and for delegating evils to Satan, and condemns the philosophers for their belief in pre-eternal intellects.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of tawheed
The author states: tawheed is belief in no partner in divinity and its properties.
2
Understand the properties of divinity
Properties include management of the world, creation of bodies, deservingness of worship, and self-subsistence.
3
Grasp the two meanings of pre-eternity
One meaning is not preceded by non-existence; the other (not preceded by another) is divinity itself.
4
See the orthodox position on attributes
Affirm pre-eternal attributes but not as separate entities from essence.
5
Examine the refutation of Mu'tazila
They only affirm human creation of actions, and their delegation to Satan is problematic.

Definition of Accident and Exclusion of Divine Attributes
Definition of accident and exclusion of divine attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of an accident ('araḍ) in the kalam context and why divine attributes are not considered accidents.

Core Idea
An accident is transient; divine attributes are eternal, so they are not accidents.

In this lesson, we examine a concise but critical passage from the matn. The author defines the term 'araḍ (accident) with a vivid illustration and then draws a key theological boundary: the eternal attributes of Allah do not fall under this category. This ensures that God's attributes are understood as permanent and essential, not accidental.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style: a definition followed by a conclusion.
2
Understand the definition of accident
The author defines accident as something that has no stability and does not last. The example of a passing cloud illustrates its temporary nature.
This definition is key to understanding why divine attributes are excluded.
3
Grasp why divine attributes are excluded
Because divine attributes are eternal and subsist permanently in God's essence, they do not fit the definition of an accident. This preserves God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي معنى لا قرار له ولا يدوم ومثله العارض للسحاب، ومن هاهنا لا يجعلون الصفات القديمة القائمة بذات الله تعالى أعراضاً.
Meaning something that has no stability and does not last, like the passing state of a cloud. And from this, they do not consider the eternal attributes subsisting in the essence of God, exalted, as accidents.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'arad (accident) as something that does not have stability and does not persist, illustrating it with the temporary state of a cloud (al-'arid li-s-sahab). The author then draws a theological conclusion: because the eternal attributes (sifat qadima) of God are permanent and subsist in His essence, they are not classified as accidents (a'rad). This prevents applying the category of accidental qualities to the Divine, preserving God's transcendence.

Key Terms
‘araḍ (accident)
ṣifāt qadīma (eternal attributes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any attribute of God is an accident in Aristotelian terms.
Correction: This passage clarifies that the transitory nature of accidents is inapplicable to eternal attributes.

Checkpoint

A) Something that has stability and lasts
B) Something that has no stability and does not last
C) Something eternal and unchanging
D) Something that is a passing cloud

Key Takeaway
The eternal attributes of Allah are not accidents because they are permanent and stable, unlike the transient nature of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The lesson contrasts the fleeting nature of a cloud with the permanence of Allah's attributes. In what areas of your life do you treat your own temporary states as if they were eternal, and how does this affect your view of Allah?
?
If you truly believed that every attribute of Allah is eternal and unchanging, how would that change the way you approach Him in times of doubt or difficulty? What one step can you take today to internalize this truth?

Negation of Quantities and Sensible Qualities from God
Author lists qualities negated from God and explains the basis for negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational basis for negating quantities and sensible qualities from God, and why they are impossible for Him.

Core Idea
God is beyond all bodily attributes — no color, shape, or emotion belongs to Him.

This passage from al-Maqasid establishes that the Necessary Being (God) is free from all quantitative and qualitative attributes that characterize bodies. The author lists specific examples and refutes a philosophical claim about intellectual pleasure, then grounds the negation in the fact that God is not a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and find the two notes
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the author introduces two 'tanbih' (notes) that develop the main statement.
The first note lists many attributes; the second provides the reasoning.
2
Identify the negated attributes in the first note
From the first note, list the quantities (length, width) and qualities (color, taste, etc.) that are negated. Understand that these belong to bodies.
3
Examine the refutation of intellectual pleasure
The author refutes the philosophers' claim that God experiences intellectual pleasure. Note that the refutation hinges on whether perception of the agreeable in the unseen is identical to pleasure.
This shows that even a subtle attribute like pleasure is negated unless proven.
4
Understand the second note's reasoning
The second note argues: since God is not a body, He cannot have sensible qualities perceived by senses, because such qualities are only conceivable as properties of bodies. Also, some qualities involve change, which is impossible for God.
This is a foundational argument for God's transcendence.
5
Reflect on the overall principle
Summarize: God is free from all attributes that imply corporeality, change, or passion. This is a core teaching of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواجب لا يتصف بالكميات ولا الكيفيات ( قال : تنبيه فلا يتصف بشيء من الكميات والكيفيات من الطول والعرض والصورة واللون والطعم والرائحة والفرح والغم والغضب واللذة والألم . وقول الحكماء : باللذة العقلية لما أنه يدرك كمالاتها فيبتهج بها إنما يتم لو ثبت أن إدراك الملائم في الغائب لذة أو ملزوم لها كما في الشاهد . تنبيه : لما ثبت أن الواجب ليس بجسم ظهر أنه لا يتصف بشيء من الكيفيات المحسوسة بالحواس الظاهرة أو الباطنة مثل الصورة واللون، والطعم، والرائحة، واللذة ، والألم ، والفرح والغم، والغضب، ونحو ذلك ، إذ لا يعقل منها إلا ما يخص الأجسام، وإن كان البعض منها مختصاً بذوات الأنفس. ولأن للبعض منها تغيرات وانفعالات وهي على الله تعالى محال
The Necessary Being is not described with quantities nor qualities. (He said: Note: So He is not described with any of the quantities and qualities such as length, width, form, color, taste, smell, joy, sadness, anger, pleasure, and pain. And the statement of the philosophers about intellectual pleasure, because He perceives His perfections and delights in them, is only valid if it is established that the perception of the agreeable in the absent is a pleasure or something entailing pleasure, as in the empirical world. Note: Since it is established that the Necessary Being is not a body, it appears that He is not described with any of the sensible qualities perceived by the external or internal senses such as form, color, taste, smell, pleasure, pain, joy, sadness, anger, and the like, for none of them are conceivable except as pertaining to bodies, although some of them are specific to souls. And because some of them involve changes and passions, and they are impossible for God, exalted.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage begins with a statement that the Necessary Being (al-wajib) does not possess quantities (kamiyyat) or qualities (kayfiyyat). The matn (marked by 'qala') then gives a note (tanbih) listing specific attributes negated: length, width, form, color, taste, smell, joy, sadness, anger, pleasure, pain. The commentators then mentions the philosophers' claim about intellectual pleasure (al-ladhdha al-‘aqliyya), stating that it depends on the premise that perception of the agreeable is either identical to pleasure or necessarily accompanies it, as in humans. The second tanbih explains that since the Necessary Being is not a body, He cannot have any sensible qualities perceived by external or internal senses, because such qualities are only conceivable as properties of bodies, though some like joy and sadness belong to souls. The passage concludes that some of these qualities involve changes and passions (taghayyurat wa infi‘alat), which are impossible for God.

واجب
كميات
كيفيات
جسم
كيفيات محسوسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can have sensible qualities like color, form, or emotions because they are attributed to Him in some scriptures metaphorically.
Correction: The passage clarifies that such qualities belong to bodies or involve change; God is not a body and is free from change, so these cannot be taken literally.

Key Takeaway
God's transcendence requires negating all bodily attributes; He is free from quantity, quality, and change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you consciously strive to replace those images with a deeper, transcendent understanding? What one change will you make in your dua or dhikr to reflect this?

Objection to the Philosophers' Claim of Intellectual Pleasure in God
Objection to philosophers' claim of intellectual pleasure in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the philosophers' claim that God experiences intellectual pleasure, and why the analogy from human perception is insufficient.

Core Idea
Intellectual pleasure in God is not proven by analogy with human perception.

The philosophers argued that God delights in perceiving His own perfections. But does this follow? This passage presents a sharp objection: the link between perception and pleasure may be unique to humans. Understanding this objection is key to avoiding anthropomorphism in divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow. Note that the Arabic contains an unclear phrase ('لأن كما لاته') which may be a textual corruption.
Focus on the logical structure: claim then objection.
2
Identify the two parts of the passage
Notice that the passage first presents the philosophers' claim, then gives an objection with two conditional statements.
The objection uses 'in' (if) twice.
3
Analyze the objection's logic
Understand that the objection challenges the definition and the necessity of pleasure accompanying perception, arguing that it may only apply to humans.
The final sentence emphasizes the difference between human and divine perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأثبت الحكماء اللذة العقلية لأن كما لاته أمور ملائمة وهو مدرك لها، فيبتهج بها. واعترض بأنه إن أريد أن الحاجة التي تسميها اللذة هي نفس إدراك الملائم فغير معلوم. وإن أريد أنها حاصلة البتة عند إدراك الملائم، فربما يختص ذلك بإدراكنا دون إدراكه ، فإنهما مختلفان قطعاً.
And the philosophers affirmed intellectual pleasure because He has perfections [؟] that are agreeable and He perceives them, so He delights in them. And it is objected: if what is meant is that the need which you call pleasure is itself the perception of the agreeable, it is unknown; and if what is meant is that it definitely occurs upon perception of the agreeable, then that might be specific to our perception, not His, for the two are absolutely different.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage discusses the philosophers' claim that God experiences intellectual pleasure (al-ladhdha al-‘aqliyya) because He perceives His own perfections (kamaalat) and rejoices. The author then presents an objection (i‘tirad): if the claim is that the pleasure is identical to the perception of the agreeable (idrak al-mula'im), that is not known (ghayr ma‘lum); if the claim is that pleasure necessarily accompanies such perception, then that may be specific to human perception and not to God's perception, since the two are fundamentally different. Thus the attribution of intellectual pleasure to God is not proven.

Key Terms
لذة عقلية
إدراك الملائم
غيب
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that intellectual pleasure is a necessary attribute of God that follows from His self-knowledge.
Correction: The objection shows that the claimed connection between perception of the agreeable and pleasure is either unknown or only established for human perception, not divine.

Checkpoint
The objection to the philosophers' claim about intellectual pleasure is that it may be specific to whose perception?
A) The angels'
B) God's perception alone
C) Our perception as opposed to God's
D) The prophets'

Key Takeaway
The attribution of intellectual pleasure to God is not rationally necessary and may rely on an invalid analogy from human experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have felt comfort in thinking that God experiences pleasure in His perfections. But the objection shows that such a claim is uncertain. How does the uncertainty of attributing intellectual pleasure to God affect your own confidence in rational theology? Will you adjust your reliance on human analogy when speaking about God?
?
The objection points out that what holds for our perception may not hold for God's. When you make claims about God's inner life, do you stop to check whether you are projecting human experience onto Him? What is one claim you have believed about God that you now need to reexamine in light of this lesson?
?
If intellectual pleasure in God cannot be rationally established, do you find yourself tempted to rely on scriptural texts or mystical experiences instead? How does this lesson challenge you to accept uncertainty in matters where reason is silent?

Answering 'What is God?' – Beyond Essence and Genus
Al-Maturidi explains how to answer 'What is God?' by distinguishing name, attribute, act, and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'māhiyya' is not applied to God and how al-Maturidi distinguishes four meanings of the question 'What is God?' to provide a proper answer.

Core Idea
God's essence is beyond genus and example – the question 'What is God?' requires careful distinction.

Al-Maturidi addresses a fundamental dilemma: how to answer the question 'What is God?' without falling into anthropomorphism or reducing God to a genus. He first warns against equating God's attributes with creatures and criticizes those who refuse even to call God 'existent'. He then explains why many theologians avoid the term 'māhiyya' (essence) as it implies genus, and clarifies a problematic narration attributed to Abu Hanifa. Finally, he provides a clear method: distinguish whether the questioner means name, attribute, act, or essence, and answer accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the shift from refutation to positive teaching in the last paragraph.
2
Identify the two refuted positions
The passage criticizes those who refuse to call God 'existent' and those who refuse to apply 'māhiyya'. What is the reason given for the latter?
The term māhiyya asks about genus.
3
Examine the Abu Hanifa narration
The text denies the authenticity of the reported statement and offers two interpretations if it were true. What are they?
One interpretation involves direct witness (mushāhada), the other a hidden name.
4
Learn al-Maturidi's fourfold distinction
When asked 'What is God?', al-Maturidi says we must first clarify the intention. Find the four possibilities and their corresponding answers.
The four are: name, attribute, act, and essence.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
Why is this distinction important? How does it protect against misrepresenting God?
It prevents reducing God to a genus or example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جميع السواء، ولا تساوي بين مشيئته ومشيئة (1) غيره ، ولا بين علمه وعلم غيره، وكذا الصفات. وأشنع من ذلك امتناع الملاحدة عن إطلاق اسم الموجود عليها (۲) وأما الامتناع عن إطلاق الماهية ، فمذهب كثير من المتكلمين. لأن معناها ، يقال ما هذا الشيء من أي جنس هو؟ . المجانسة (۳) قالوا : وما روي أن أبا حنيفة (٤) رحمه الله كان يقول: «إن الله تعالى مشيئة (٥) لا يعلمها إلا هو ليس بصحيح، إذ لم يوجد في كتبه، ولم ينقل عن أصحابه العارفين بمذهبه . ولو ثبت فمعناه أنه يعلم نفسه بالمشاهدة لا بدليل أو خبر أو أن له اسماً لا يعلمه غيره، فإن لفظه (٦) ما قد يقع سؤالاً عن الاسم . 1 قال الشيخ أبو منصور (٧) رحمه الله : إن سألنا سائل عن الله تعالى (۸) ما هو؟ . قلنا : إن أردت ما اسمه؟ فالله الرحمن الرحيم وإن أردت ما صفته؟ فسميع بصير، وإن أردت ما فعله؟ فخلق المخلوقات ووضع كل شيء موضعه، وإن أردت ما ماهيته (۹) ؟ فهو متعال عن المثال والجنس
All are equal, and there is no equating between His will and the will of another, nor between His knowledge and the knowledge of another, and likewise the attributes. And more grievous than that is the refusal of the atheists to apply the name 'existent' to Him. As for the refusal to apply the term 'essence' (māhiyya), it is the doctrine of many theologians. Because its meaning is: one says, 'What is this thing? From which genus is it?' — genus. They said: And what was narrated that Abū Ḥanīfa (may God have mercy on him) used to say: 'Indeed God is a will that no one knows but Him' is not authentic, since it is not found in his books and was not transmitted from his companions who knew his school. And if it were established, its meaning would be that He knows Himself by direct witness (mushāhada) without proof or report, or that He has a name that no one else knows, for his utterance may occur as a question about the name. Shaykh Abū Manṣūr (may God have mercy on him) said: If an asker asks us about God (exalted is He): 'What is He?' We say: If you mean what is His name? — 'Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.' And if you mean what is His attribute? — 'Hearing, Seeing.' And if you mean what is His act? — 'He created the creatures and placed everything in its place.' And if you mean what is His essence (māhiyya)? — Then He is exalted above example and genus.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a combined refutation and positive teaching. It begins by affirming that none of God's attributes can be equated with those of creatures. It then notes two errors: atheists refuse to call God 'existent', and many theologians refuse to apply the term 'māhiyya' (essence) to God because that term asks about genus (jins). The author then addresses a problematic narration attributed to Abu Hanifa: 'God is a will known only to Him.' The text denies its authenticity and, if it were true, interprets it as referring to God's self-knowledge through direct witness (mushāhada) without proof, or to a hidden name. Finally, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi presents a method to answer the question 'What is God?' by distinguishing four possible meanings: name (ism), attribute (ṣifa), act (fiʿl), and essence (māhiyya). For each, a proper answer is given, concluding that God's essence is beyond example and genus.

مجانسة
مشاهدة
اسم
صفة
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some consider the narration that Abu Hanifa said 'God is a will known only to Him' as authentic and take it literally, implying God is a simple attribute.
Correction: The text shows this narration is not authentic (not in his books, not from his companions) and, if it were, it can be interpreted as referring to God's self-knowledge through direct witness (mushāhada) or a hidden name.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, what should you do when someone asks 'What is God?'?
A) Give a single fixed answer.
B) First clarify what the questioner means (name, attribute, act, or essence) and answer accordingly.
C) Refuse to answer because God is beyond comprehension.
D) Say 'He is the Creator of everything.'

Key Takeaway
To answer 'What is God?' properly, one must distinguish whether the questioner is asking about His name, attribute, act, or essence, and respond accordingly, affirming that His essence is beyond any genus or example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask 'What is God?' in your mind, which of the four categories do you usually intend? Are you, perhaps unknowingly, demanding an answer about His essence—a category He has transcended? How does this lesson challenge the way you think about God?
?
Al-Maturidi says that equating God's attributes with those of creatures is a serious error. Examine your own supplications: do you ever describe God in ways that mirror human limitations? How can you more carefully speak of His attributes?
?
The text calls the atheist's refusal to call God 'existent' even more grievous. Have you ever hesitated to affirm God's existence as a reality, or felt the need to define Him by worldly standards? What does it mean for you to truly affirm that God is 'existent' beyond our categories?
