Impossibility of Contingent Events: Introduction and First Two Proofs
Author clarifies the dispute and presents the first two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real contingent attributes and relational ones, and the first two proofs (consensus and change) against Allah being described by contingent events.

Core Idea
A perfect attribute cannot be absent from the Necessary Being; change is impossible for Him.

The Karramiyya sect claimed that Allah can be described by contingent events—things that come into existence after non-existence. The author first clarifies that only real attributes, not relational or negative ones, are in dispute. He then presents four proofs against this claim; here we examine the first two, which rely on consensus and the impossibility of change in the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction
Read the opening lines where the author separates genuine contingent attributes from relational/negative ones. Understand why only the former are contested.
The Karramiyya might try to use relational attributes as evidence, but the author rules that out.
2
Study the first proof (consensus)
The first proof is based on consensus (ijma'): if an attribute is a perfection, the one qualified for it must possess it; otherwise, they do not truly have it. For Allah, if contingent events were perfections, He would always have them, which is impossible.
This shows that contingent events cannot be perfections for Allah.
3
Study the second proof (change)
The second proof: being described by a contingent event implies change (taghayyur), because the attribute appears after not being there. Change is impossible for the Necessary Being, so contingent attributes are impossible.
Change would imply potential imperfection, which Allah is free from.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع امتناع اتصاف الواجب بالحوادث ( في امتناع اتصافه بالحادث بمعنى الموجود بعد العدم خلافاً للكرامية، وأما الاتصاف بما له تعلقات حادثة أو بما يتجدد من السلوب والإضافات والأحوال فليس من المتنازع فلا يصلح تمسكاً لهم. والاستدلال بأن المصحح للاتصاف هو مطلق الصفة إذ لا عبرة بالقدم لكونه عدمياً فاسد لجواز أن يكون المصحح حقيقة الصفة القديمة أو يكون القدم شرطاً أو الحدوث مانعاً. لنا وجوه : الأول : الإجماع على أن ما يصح عليه إن كان صفة كمال لم يخل عنه وإلا لم يتصف به الثاني : أن الاتصاف بالحادث تغير، وهو عليه محال . . الثالث : أنه لو جاز لجاز في الأزل لاستحالة الانقلاب وهو يستلزم جواز وجود الحادث في الأزل لامتناع الاتصاف بالشيء بدونه . الرابع : أنه لو جاز لزم عدم خلوه عن الحادث لاتصافه قبل ذلك الحادث بضده الحادث لزواله وبقابليته الحادث لما مر . واستضعف الأول بأن يجوز أن تكون الحوادث كمالات متلاحقة مشروطاً ابتداء الكل بانقضاء الآخر وفيه نظر. والثاني : بأن التغيير بمعنى تبدل في الصفات من غير تأثر (1) عن الغير نفس المتنازع، والثالث بأن اللازم أزلية الجواز والمحال جواز الأزلية، والرابع : منع الملازمة ) . مقدمات (1) في (ج) تبر بدلاً من (تأثر).
Fourth Section: On the Impossibility of the Necessary Being Being Described by Contingent Events (Regarding the impossibility of being described by the contingent in the sense of that which exists after non-existence, in opposition to the Karramiyya. As for being described by that which has contingent relations or by renewed negations, additions, and states, that is not a matter of dispute, so it is not a valid argument for them. And the argument that the valid cause for being described is absolute attribute, since precedence (al-qidam) is not considered because it is non-existent, is corrupt, because it is possible that the valid cause is the reality of the eternal attribute, or that eternity is a condition, or that contingency is a hindrance. We have proofs: First: Consensus that whatever is valid for [an attribute], if it is an attribute of perfection, it is not devoid of it; otherwise it is not described by it. Second: Being described by the contingent is change (taghayyur), and change is impossible for Him. Third: If it were possible, it would be possible in eternity because of the impossibility of reversal, and that would entail the possibility of the existence of the contingent in eternity, since being described by a thing is impossible without it. Fourth: If it were possible, it would necessitate that He is never free of the contingent, because before that contingent He would have been described by its opposite contingent due to its passing away, and by its receptivity to the contingent as previously mentioned. And the first was weakened by [the objection] that it is possible that contingents are successive perfections, the beginning of all conditioned by the passing of the other, and there is consideration in that. And the second: that change in the sense of alteration of attributes without being affected (1) by another is the very matter under dispute. And the third: that the necessary implication is the eternity of possibility, and impossible is the possibility of eternity. And the fourth: denial of implication. ) Premises. (1) In (j) 'perish' instead of 'being affected'.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a structured kalam argument refuting the Karramiyya claim that Allah can be described by contingent events (ḥawādith) in the sense of things that come into existence after non-existence. The author first distinguishes between real attributes and relational/negative ones, which are not in dispute. He then refutes the opponents' argument that the justification for ascribing an attribute is the attribute itself regardless of pre-eternity, because pre-eternity could be a condition or contingency an obstacle. He then presents four proofs: (1) Consensus: if an attribute is one of perfection, it must be present in the Necessary Being; otherwise it is not truly possessed. (2) Contingent attributes entail change, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. (3) If it were possible, it would have been possible in pre-eternity, but that would entail a contingent existing in pre-eternity, which is impossible. (4) If possible, the Necessary Being would never be free of contingent attributes, as argued previously. The author then notes that each proof was subjected to a criticism (istid'āf): the first is weakened by the possibility that contingents are successive perfections; the second is that the change in question is exactly what is disputed; the third is that the necessary implication is eternity of possibility, not actuality; and the fourth is denial of implication. The passage ends with the word 'Premises' (muqaddamāt) and a footnote variant.

Key Terms
الحوادث
الواجب
الكرامية
التغير
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karramiyya claim that contingent events can be attributed to Allah, possibly using relational attributes as evidence.
Correction: Only real contingent events (coming into being after non-existence) are in dispute, not relational or negative attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof (al-awwal) given by the author against Allah being described by contingent events?
A) Change is impossible for Allah.
B) Consensus that a perfect attribute is never absent from the one qualified for it.
C) It would necessitate the existence of a contingent in pre-eternity.
D) It implies dualism.

Key Takeaway
The first two proofs establish that contingent events cannot be attributed to Allah because they would either contradict His perfection or imply change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a sudden shift in your circumstances – loss, pain, or unexpected blessing – do you ever catch yourself thinking that Allah's attitude toward you has changed? How does this lesson's proof that change is impossible for Him confront that assumption? What will you do the next time that feeling arises?
?
If you truly believed that every 'perfection' attribute must be present in Allah without any lack, how would that reshape your prayers and your reliance on Him? Is there an area of your life where you act as if Allah's attributes are incomplete?
?
The Karramiyya attempted to attribute contingent events to Allah to explain His actions. In your own life, have you ever reduced Allah to fit your human logic, making Him seem like a cause among other causes? How does the transcendence taught in these two proofs call you to repent of that reduction and submit to His absolute perfection?

Third and Fourth Proofs and Their Criticisms
Author presents the third and fourth proofs and then the critiques of all four proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third and fourth proofs against contingent events for the Necessary Being, and the common critiques raised against them.

Core Idea
Allowing contingent events leads to absurdities like a contingent in pre-eternity or an endless chain.

The author continues his refutation of the Karramiyya with two more proofs that demonstrate the logical impossibilities that would follow from allowing contingent events in Allah. He then addresses objections that were raised against each of the four proofs, showing the sophistication of the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
The third proof argues: if contingent events were possible for Allah, then they would have been possible in pre-eternity (since no reversal is possible for the Necessary Being). That would entail the existence of a contingent event in pre-eternity, which is contradictory.
This proof relies on the impossibility of change in divine capacity.
2
Read the fourth proof
The fourth proof: if Allah could have a contingent attribute, He would never be free from some contingent attribute, because before each contingent event He would have its opposite (which is also contingent), leading to an infinite regress or a state of pure potentiality.
This shows the impossibility of a first contingent event.
3
Review the criticisms
The author records criticisms made against each proof. For instance, the third proof is criticized by saying that the necessary implication is the eternity of possibility, not the possibility of eternity. Understand how these critiques aim to undermine the proofs.
The author does not necessarily endorse these critiques; he presents them for completeness.

?

The Mainstream Ruling on Divine Transcendence
Al-Taftazani states the mainstream view on God not being described with temporal events and what exceptions are allowed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mainstream position on God's relation to temporal events and the specific exceptions that are considered permissible without implying change in the divine essence.

Core Idea
God's essence does not change, but new relations and knowledge of new events are permissible.

Al-Taftazani begins by affirming the majority view that the Necessary Being cannot be characterized by temporal events (things that come into being after non-existence). However, he immediately clarifies that certain descriptions—negations, relations, and real attributes with changing objects—are permissible because they do not imply change in the divine essence. This passage delineates the boundaries of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Read the first sentence: the majority hold that the Necessary Being cannot be described with temporal events (al-hadith). Note that this is contrary to the Karramiyya sect.
Temporal events are defined as 'what exists after non-existence'.
2
Recognize the first exception: negations and relations
Find the part that permits describing God with negations (sulub) and relations (idafat) that arise after not having existed. Example: God not being a provider for a dead person, then being a provider for a newborn.
These descriptions refer to relationships with changing creatures, not to God’s essence.
3
Understand the second exception: real attributes with changing objects
See that real attributes (sifat haqiyya) like knowledge and power are permissible when their objects change, such as God knowing a new temporal event.
The attribute itself is eternal; only its relation to objects is temporal.
4
Note the mention of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's view
The passage references states (ahwal) according to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, where states like 'being knowing' renew with renewed known things. This will be verified later.
This is a Mu'tazili position mentioned for completeness.
5
Reflect on why these exceptions do not contradict immutability
Consider that negations, relations, and changing objects do not entail change in God's essence; they only reflect temporal aspects of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع : الجمهور على أن الواجب يمتنع أن يتصف بالحادث أي الموجود بعد العدم خلافاً للكرامية. وأما اتصافه بالسلوب والإضافات الحاصلة بعد ما لم تكن ككونه غير رازق لزيد الميت، رازقاً لعمر و المولود وبالصفات الحقيقية المتغيرة المتعلقات ككونه عالماً بهذا الحادث قادراً عليه فجائز، وكذا بالأحوال المتحققة بعد ما لم تكن كالعالميات المتجددة بتجدد المعلومات عند أبي الحسين البصري على ما سيجيء تحقيق ذلك.
The Fourth Investigation: The majority hold that the Necessary Being is impossible to be described with the temporal event, i.e., that which exists after non-existence, contrary to the Karramiyya. As for His being described with negations and relations that come about after not having been, such as His not being a provider for Zayd who is dead, and being a provider for 'Amr who is born, and with real attributes whose objects change, such as His being knowing of this temporal event and being powerful over it, it is permissible. Similarly, with states that become actual after not having been, such as states of knowledge that renew with the renewal of known things according to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, as will come later in the verification of that.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage defines the mainstream position regarding God's relation to temporal events. It begins by stating that the majority of scholars hold that the Necessary Being (al-wajib) cannot be described with temporal events (al-hadith), i.e., things that come into existence after non-existence. This is in opposition to the Karramiyya sect, who allegedly allowed such temporal attributes in God. Then the author clarifies exceptions: it is permissible for God to be described with negations (al-sulub) such as 'not being a provider for someone who is dead' and relations (al-idafat) that arise after not existing, like becoming a provider for a newborn. Also permissible are real attributes (sifat haqiqiyya) whose objects change, such as God's knowledge of a particular temporal event and His power over it. These are temporally indexed aspects of eternal attributes. Finally, the author mentions the view of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (a Mu'tazili) that states (ahwal) like 'being knowing' renew as known things renew; this is mentioned as something to be discussed later. The passage thus sets the scope of what is and is not allowed regarding change in God's descriptions.

Correction: The passage clarifies that negations and relations are permissible because they do not imply change in God's essence; they only reflect changes in creatures.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being cannot be described with temporal events, but negations, relations, and real attributes with changing objects are permissible because they do not entail change in the divine essence.

Refuting Al-Razi's Objection on Temporal Events
Al-Taftazani refutes al-Razi's objection by examining three sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes al-Razi's objection that all Islamic sects are forced to accept temporal events in God, by examining the positions of the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and philosophers.

Core Idea
Changing relations to God do not imply change in His essence.

In this passage, al-Taftazani responds to a powerful objection from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. Al-Razi argued that every Islamic theological school, despite their denials, ends up admitting that God is a locus for temporal events (hawadith). Al-Taftazani dismantles this claim by showing that for each school, the apparent changes are only relations, states, or applications of eternal attributes—not temporal events inhering in God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument structure come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the three groups mentioned: Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and philosophers.
2
Identify al-Razi's objection
Understand the core claim: that all sects are forced to say God is a locus for temporal events. Al-Taftazani states this is repelled by previous distinctions.
3
Examine each sect's case
For each sect (Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, philosophers), notice what specific example al-Taftazani gives and why it does not entail temporal events in God's essence.
Look for the key words: for Ash'aris, the attributes are eternal; for Mu'tazila, attributes are temporal states not inhering; for philosophers, relations are not existent entities.
4
Synthesize the refutation
Summarize how the passage refutes al-Razi: the changes are in relations or states, not in God's essence.
Consider why this distinction matters for the broader theological debate.
5
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding with the multiple-choice question.
The question directly tests the main point of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يندفع ما ذكره الإمام الرازي من أن القول بكون الواجب محلاً للحوادث لازم على جميع الفرق الإسلامية وإن كانوا يتبرعون عنه. أما الأشاعرة : فلأن زيداً إذا وجد ، كان الواجب غير قادر على خلقه بعدما كان وفاعلاً له ؛ عالماً بأنه موجود مبصراً لصورته سامعاً لصوته، أمراً له بالصلاة بعد ما لم يكن كذلك. وأما المعتزلة : فلقولهم بحدوث المريدية والكارهية لما يراد وجوده أو عدمه، والسامعية، والمبصرية، لما يحدث من الأصوات والألوان، وكذا بتجدد العالميات عند تجدد المعلومات عند أبي الحسين البصري. وأما الفلاسفة : فلقولهم بأن الله تعالى إضافة إلى ما حدث، ثم الفناء بالقبلية ثم المعية ثم البعدية ؛ وهم لا يقولون بوجود كل إضافة، حتى يلزم اتصافه بموجودات حادثة على ما هو المتنازع.
And by this is repelled what Imam al-Razi mentioned, that the statement that the Necessary Being is a locus for temporal events is necessary upon all Islamic sects, though they recoil from it. As for the Ash'aris: because when Zayd exists, the Necessary Being was not powerful to create him after He had been powerful and had done so? He knows that Zayd exists, sees his form, hears his voice, commands him to pray after He had not been like that. As for the Mu'tazila: because of their statement of the temporality of willing and disliking for what is intended to exist or not, and hearing and seeing for what occurs of sounds and colors, and likewise the renewal of states of knowledge at the renewal of known things according to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. As for the philosophers: because of their statement that God has a relation to what happened, then transience with priority, then simultaneity, then posteriority; and they do not say that every relation exists, so that His being described with temporal existents would be necessary, as is the point of dispute.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's claim that all Islamic theological sects are forced to admit that God is a locus for temporal events (mahall li al-hawadith). Al-Taftazani argues that by the previous distinctions (negations, relations, real attributes with changing objects), al-Razi's objection is repelled. He then examines three sects: The Ash'aris: When a new creature like Zayd exists, God's power, knowledge, seeing, hearing, and command are applied to Zayd. But these are eternal attributes with new relations, not temporal attributes inhering in God. The Mu'tazila: They assert that attributes like will, dislike, hearing, seeing are temporal because they relate to temporal objects. Also, according to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, states of knowledge renew. But for them, these are states (ahwal) not inhering in God. The Philosophers: They say God has relations to temporal things like priority, simultaneity, posteriority, but they do not affirm that every relation has an existence that would entail God being described with temporal existents. Thus, al-Razi's charge does not apply because what is at issue is whether God is described with temporal events in a way that implies change in essence, not mere relations or applications.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's objection fails because the changes attributed to God in various schools are only relations, states, or applications of eternal attributes, not temporal events in His essence.

The Basis for Divine Attributes: A Doubt and Its Answer
Al-Taftazani presents and answers a doubt about the basis for attributing a quality to the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rational basis for attributing an attribute to the Necessary Being is defended against a doubt that attempts to make it apply uniformly to temporal attributes.

Core Idea
The essence of an eternal attribute differs from a temporal one, so the ground is not uniform.

Al-Taftazani presents a doubt that challenges the coherence of distinguishing divine attributes from created ones. The objection claims that the basis for an attribute's subsistence would either apply universally or involve a negative condition. The answer refutes this by distinguishing the essences themselves and by allowing conditions or preventatives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a doubt followed by its answer.
2
Identify the doubt's two horns
The doubt claims the grounds (musahhih) for an attribute's subsistence is either simply being an attribute (which includes both eternal and temporal) or being an attribute plus the condition of eternity (which is a negative condition).
The second horn is problematic because a negative condition cannot be part of a real cause.
3
Analyze the first part of the answer
The first response denies the restriction (hasr) by proposing that the ground is the essence (mahiyya) of the eternal attribute, which is distinct from the temporal attribute's essence, even though they share the concept of attribution.
4
Analyze the second part of the answer
If one concedes the restriction, the answer allows that eternity could be a condition (shart) or temporality a preventative (mani'). This does not require the ground to be a positive cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يتمسك بأن المصحح لقيام الصفة بالواجب إما كونها صفة، فيعم القديم والحادث، وإما مع قيد القدم، أعني كونه غير مسبوق بالعدم، وهو عدمي لا يصلح جزءاً للمؤثر. وجوابه : منع الحصر لجواز أن يكون المصحح ماهية الصفة القديمة المخالفة لماهية الصفة الحادثة، على أن يكونا أمرين متخالفين متشاركين في مفهوم الوصفية، ولو سلم : يجوز أن يكون القدم شرطاً أو الحدوث مانعا.
And one may adhere to [the argument] that the grounds for the subsistence of an attribute in the Necessary Being is either its being an attribute, which then includes both the eternal and the temporal; or with the addition of the condition of eternity, i.e., its not being preceded by non-existence, which is a negative [condition] and is not suitable as part of the effective cause. And its answer: The restriction is denied because it is possible that the grounds is the essence/mahiyya of the eternal attribute, which is different from the essence of the temporal attribute, given that they are two differing things sharing the concept of attribution. And if it is conceded: it is possible that eternity be a condition or temporality a preventative.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents a doubt (shubha) and its answer concerning the basis (musahhih) for an attribute subsisting in the Necessary Being. The doubt claims: The grounds for an attribute's subsistence is either (1) simply being an attribute, which would then apply to both eternal and temporal attributes equally (thus allowing temporal attributes in God), or (2) being an attribute plus the condition of eternity (i.e., not preceded by non-existence), but this condition is a negative thing ('adami) and cannot be part of a real cause (mu'aththir). The answer responds by denying the restriction (hasr): it is possible that the grounds is the essence (mahiyya) of the eternal attribute, which is distinct from the essence of the temporal attribute; they share only the concept of attribution, but that does not imply the same mode of subsistence. If one grants the restriction, then it is possible that eternity is a condition (shart) for the applicability of the attribute, or that temporality is a preventative (mani'), not a positive cause. Thus, the objection fails.

مانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an attribute is defined as such, it must apply uniformly to all bearers.
Correction: The essence of an eternal attribute is distinct from a temporal one, so the same grounds do not apply; they share only the concept of attribution, not the mode of subsistence.

Checkpoint

B) The grounds is not simply being an attribute, but the specific essence of the eternal attribute, which differs from temporal ones.
C) The grounds is the negative condition of non-priority by non-existence.
D) Temporal attributes can subsist because they share the concept of attribution.

Key Takeaway
The basis for an attribute in the Necessary Being is its specific essence, not the general concept of attribution.

Are there areas in your own faith where you have unconsciously accepted false restrictions—limiting Allah's attributes or actions based on human analogies? What would it take to break free from that?
?
If the essence of the eternal attribute is different from the temporal, then God's attributes are not just amplified human qualities. Does your heart truly worship a God whose attributes are unique and beyond comparison, or do you reduce Him to a human-like image? What needs to change in your worship to align with this truth?

First Argument: Temporal Events Imply Deficiency
Al-Taftazani presents the first proof: temporal events imply deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against temporal events in God, which is based on the impossibility of deficiency in the divine essence.

Core Idea
God cannot acquire new perfections because prior lack would be a deficiency.

The 'preventers' (al-mani'un) who deny that temporal events can occur in God present several proofs. The first proof argues that allowing such events would necessarily imply deficiency in God, which is impossible by consensus. This argument uses a logical dichotomy: any temporal event either is a perfection or not. Both cases lead to an impossibility, thus temporal events cannot be attributed to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a premise, then a dichotomy, then the conclusion.
The passage is concise but dense. Pay attention to the phrase 'وجه اللزوم' which introduces the reasoning.
2
Understand the two cases
Identify the two possibilities for the temporal event: it is either an attribute of perfection or not. See how each case leads to a contradiction.
If it is a perfection, then God's prior lack of it is a deficiency. If it is not a perfection, then it cannot be attributed to God at all.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since both cases are impossible, the original assumption (that temporal events are permissible for God) must be false. This is the core of the argument.
The argument relies on the consensus (ijma') that God possesses all perfections and no deficiencies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المانعون بوجوه : الأول : أنه لو جاز اتصافه بالحوادث لازمة النقصان وهو باطل بالإجماع، وجه اللزوم، أن ذلك الحادث، إن كان من صفات الكمال كان الخلوه عنه، مع جواز الاتصاف به نقصاً بالاتفاق، وقد خلا عنه قبل حدوثه ، وإن لم يكن من صفات الكمال امتنع اتصاف الواجب به للاتفاق على أن كل ما يتصف هو به يلزم أن يكون صفة الكمال.
The preventers argued with proofs: The first: that if it were permissible for Him to be described with temporal events, deficiency would follow necessarily, and that is false by consensus. The aspect of the necessity: that temporal event, if it is among the attributes of perfection, then being empty of it, while it is possible to be described by it, is a deficiency by consensus, and He was empty of it before its occurrence. And if it is not among the attributes of perfection, then it is impossible for the Necessary Being to be described with it, because of the consensus that everything with which He is described necessarily must be an attribute of perfection.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (wajh) from those who forbid (al-mani'un) the description of God with temporal events. The argument is: If it were allowed, it would necessitate deficiency in God, which is impossible by consensus. The reasoning: Consider the temporal event (al-hadith). If it is an attribute of perfection (sifat al-kamal), then God's being devoid of it before it occurs, while it is possible for Him to have it, would be a deficiency (naqs), because lacking a possible perfection is a deficiency. And He was devoid of it before its occurrence. If, on the other hand, the temporal event is not an attribute of perfection, then it is impossible for God to be described with it, because there is consensus that everything God is described with must be an attribute of perfection. Therefore, in either case, temporal events cannot be attributed to God. This argument relies on the consensus that God possesses all perfections and no imperfections.

Key Terms
المانعون
النقصان
صفات الكمال
الخلوة
الاجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God could acquire a new perfection without prior deficiency.
Correction: The argument shows that acquiring a perfection after lacking it implies prior deficiency, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument of the preventers, if a temporal event is an attribute of perfection, why would its occurrence in God imply deficiency?
A) Because God cannot have any temporal attributes.
B) Because God would have been devoid of it before, and that emptiness is a deficiency.
C) Because it would mean God is not eternal.
D) Because it would imply change in God's essence.

Key Takeaway
The preventers argue that temporal events are impossible for God because they would entail deficiency, either through prior lack of a perfection or through attribution of an imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The argument says God cannot acquire perfection because prior lack is deficiency. Is there any attribute you have assigned to God that you now realize would imply a prior lack? What is it, and how will you adjust your belief?

First Proof of the Preventers: Deficiency Argument
Presents first proof, objection, and answer on temporal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deficiency argument against temporal attributes of God, the objection based on successive perfections, and the Ash'ari response.

Core Idea
Temporal attributes imply deficiency in God, even if replaced by other perfections.

The debate over whether God can be described with temporal events (al-hawadith) hinges on the concept of perfection. The preventers argue that any temporal attribute would imply a prior lack, which is deficiency in God. An objection based on the philosophers' view of celestial motions attempts to circumvent this, but the Ash'ari answer reaffirms the original position. This lesson walks through each step of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Understand the preventers' main argument: if a temporal event is a perfection, prior lack is deficiency; if not a perfection, it cannot apply to God. Notice how the proof hinges on the consensus that all God's attributes are perfections.
The term 'deficiency' (nuqsan) is key here.
2
Examine the objection
The objector, likely influenced by philosophers, argues that temporary lack is not deficiency if it is a condition for a subsequent perfection. The example of celestial motions, where each motion replaces the previous, illustrates this idea of successive perfections.
This objection challenges the universality of the 'prior lack equals deficiency' premise.
3
Analyze the answer
The Ash'ari response refutes the objection on three grounds: (1) if each individual is originated, the type is originated; (2) if God is never free from originated individuals, He would be originated; (3) in pre-eternity, God is devoid of all individuals, which is deficiency.
Focus on the logical implications of 'originated type' and 'pre-eternity'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المانعون بوجوه : الأول : أنه لو جاز اتصافه بالحوادث لزم النقصان عليه وهو باطل بالإجماع، وجه اللزوم، أن ذلك الحادث، إن كان من صفات الكمال كان الخلوه عنه، مع جواز الاتصاف به نقصاً بالاتفاق، وقد خلا عنه قبل حدوثه ، وإن لم يكن من صفات الكمال امتنع اتصاف الواجب به للاتفاق على أن كل ما يتصف هو به يلزم أن يكون صفة الكمال. واعترض : بأنا لا نسلم أن الخلو عن صفة الكمال نقص، وإنما يكون لو لم يكن حال الخلو متصفاً بكمال يكون زواله شرطاً لحدوث هذا الكمال. وذلك بأن يتصف دائماً بنوع كمال يتعاقب أفراده من غير بداية ونهاية! ويكون حصول كل لاحق مشروطاً بزوال السابق على ما ذكره الحكماء في حركات الأفلاك فالخلو عن كل فرد يكون شرطاً لحصول كمال آخر بل لاستمرار كمالات غير متناهية، فلا يكون نقصاً. وأجيب : بأن المقدمة إجماعية بل ضرورية، والسند مدفوع بأنه إذا كان كل فرد حادث، كان النوع حادثاً ضرورة، لأنه لا يوجد إلا في ضمن فرد. وبأن الواجب على ما ذكرتم لا يخلو عن الحادث، فيكون حادثاً ضرورة وبأنه في الأزل يكون خالياً عن كل فرد ضرورة امتناع الحادث في الأزل فيكون ناقصاً.
The preventers argued with proofs: The first: that if it were permissible for Him to be described with temporal events, deficiency would follow, which is false by consensus. The aspect of implication: that temporal event, if it is among attributes of perfection, then being devoid of it, despite the possibility of being described with it, is deficiency by agreement, and He was devoid of it before its occurrence. And if it is not among attributes of perfection, then description of the Necessary Being with it is impossible because of the agreement that everything with which He is described must be an attribute of perfection. Objection: We do not concede that being devoid of an attribute of perfection is deficiency; rather it would be only if at the time of being devoid He were not described with a perfection whose removal is a condition for the occurrence of this perfection. That is, that He is always described with a type of perfection whose individuals succeed one another without beginning or end, and the attainment of each subsequent is conditioned by the removal of the previous, as the philosophers mentioned regarding the movements of the spheres. So being devoid of each individual is a condition for the attainment of another perfection, indeed for the continuation of infinite perfections, so it is not deficiency. Answer: The premise is agreed upon, indeed necessary. And the argument is refuted because if every individual is originated, the type is necessarily originated, since it does not exist except within an individual. And because the Necessary Being, according to what you mentioned, is not free from the originated, so He is necessarily originated. And because in pre-eternity He is devoid of every individual necessarily due to the impossibility of the originated in pre-eternity, so He would be deficient.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of those who deny temporal attributes, along with an objection and the answer. The proof argues that if God could be described with temporal events, deficiency would result: if the event is a perfection, then He was previously devoid of it, which is deficiency; if it is not a perfection, then God cannot be described with it because all His attributes are perfections. The objection, based on philosophers' view of sequential perfections (like celestial motions), says that being devoid of a particular perfection is not deficiency if it is replaced by another perfection; indeed, it enables continuous perfections. The answer refutes this by pointing out that if each individual is originated, the entire type is originated, and God would then be originated since He is never free from originated individuals. Moreover, in pre-eternity He is devoid of any individual, which would still be deficiency.

Key Terms
النقصان
صفات الكمال
الخلو
التعاقب
حركات الأفلاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being temporarily devoid of a perfection is not deficiency if replaced by another perfection.
Correction: Even if replaced, the state of lack at any moment is deficiency for a being who must always possess all perfections; moreover, the accumulated originated individuals would make the entire type originated, implying God's origination.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection to the first proof?
A) That being devoid of a perfection is always a deficiency.
B) That God can have successive perfections without beginning or end, so temporary lack is not deficiency.
C) That temporal events cannot be perfections.
D) That the philosophers reject the proof.

Key Takeaway
The deficiency argument remains potent despite the objection of successive perfections, because originated individuals imply an originated type and pre-eternal lack is still deficiency.

Think about a time you imagined God 'responding' to your actions as if He gained new knowledge or attributes. How does this passage challenge that assumption? What specific change in your understanding of God's perfection does it demand?
?
The answer refutes the objection by stating that pre-eternal lack is deficiency even if perfections succeed each other. Do you truly believe that God's mercy, for example, was always complete and never began at a point? How does this affect your hope in seeking forgiveness? Does your prayer reflect confidence in an eternally perfect mercy, or do you act as if God's mercy is conditional on your repentance?
?
The proof shows that any lack of perfection is deficiency. When you consider your own shortcomings, do you ever subconsciously think of God as needing your worship or obedience to be 'complete'? This lesson affirms God's absolute independence and perfection. How does this truth confront your ego? Write a concrete resolution to check your thoughts about God's attributes against this standard.

Mu'tazila and Philosophers on Temporal Attributes
Presents Mu'tazila and philosophers' views and refutes a doubt about temporal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positions of the Mu'tazila and philosophers on temporal attributes and the refutation of a key doubt raised by those who permit them.

Core Idea
Two schools, one doubt: can temporal attributes subsist in the Eternal?

This lesson examines how the Mu'tazila and philosophers approached the issue of originated attributes relating to temporal objects. It then unpacks a sophisticated doubt about the basis for attributes subsisting in God, and the kalam response that denies the exclusivity of the proposed alternatives. Understanding this debate is essential for grasping the broader polemics on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's flow come through.
Notice the structure: first the Mu'tazila, then the philosophers, then the doubt and refutation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila view
Find what attributes the Mu'tazila consider originated and why. Note the example of universal knowledge according to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri.
The Mu'tazila affirm attributes but consider them temporal due to their relation to contingent objects.
3
Identify the philosophers' view
The philosophers speak of relations like priority, accompaniment, posteriority. Understand they do not affirm every relation exists.
This subtlety is crucial to avoid misrepresenting their position.
4
Analyze the doubt and its refutation
The doubt claims the basis for attribute subsistence is either attribute-ness or the condition of eternity. The refutation denies the exclusive enumeration, proposing a difference in quiddity or conditional factors.
This is a classic kalam argument: denying the exclusive disjunction.
5
Summarize the argument structure
Review how the refutation works: it does not accept the two options as exhaustive, and even if it did, it adds conditions and hindrances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة : فلقولهم بحدوث المريدية والكارهية لما يراد وجوده أو عدمه، والسامعية، والمبصرية، لما يحدث من الأصوات والألوان، وكذا بتجدد العالميات عند تجدد المعلومات عند أبي الحسين البصري. وأما الفلاسفة : فلقولهم بأن الله تعالى إضافة إلى ما حدث، ثم فنى بالقبلية ثم المعية ثم البعدية ؛ وهم لا يقولون بوجود كل إضافة، حتى يلزم اتصافه بموجودات حادثة على ما هو المتنازع ، وهذه الشبهة هي العمدة في تمسك المجوزين فلا تكون واردة في محل النزاع، وقد يتمسك بأن المصحح لقيام الصفة بالواجب إما كونها صفة، فيعم القديم والحادث، وإما مع قيد القدم، أعني كونه غير مسبوق بالعدم، وهو عدمي لا يصلح جزءاً للمؤثر. وجوابه : منع الحصر لجواز أن يكون المصحح ماهية الصفة القديمة المخالفة لماهية الصفة الحادثة، على أن يكونا أمرين متخالفين متشاركين في مفهوم الوصفية، ولوسلم : يجوز أن يكون القدم شرطاً أو الحدوث مانعاً.
As for the Mu'tazila: they hold the origination of the attributes of willing and desiring for what is intended to exist or not exist, and hearing and seeing for what occurs of sounds and colors, and likewise the renewal of universal knowledge upon the renewal of known things according to Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī. As for the philosophers: they hold that God—exalted is He—has a relation to what occurs, and then He is described by priority, then accompaniment, then posteriority; and they do not affirm the existence of every relation such that He would be necessarily described with originated existents as is the point of dispute. This doubt is the mainstay of those who permit [temporal attributes] but it does not apply to the locus of dispute. And one may argue that what makes it possible for the attribute to subsist in the Necessary Being is either [the fact that] it is an attribute, in which case it applies to both eternal and originated, or with the condition of eternity, i.e., being not preceded by non-existence, which is a non-existent and is not suitable as a part of the effect. Its answer: The enumeration is denied because it is possible that what makes it possible is the quiddity of the eternal attribute, which is different from the quiddity of the originated attribute, such that they are two different things sharing the concept of attributedness. And if it were granted, it could be that eternity is a condition or that origination is a hindrance.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the views of the Mu'tazila and philosophers regarding temporal attributes and then refutes a doubt raised by those who permit such attributes. It first states the Mu'tazila position: they consider attributes like willing, hearing, and seeing as originated because they relate to temporal objects. Then it states the philosophers' view: they allow certain relations to temporal events but do not affirm all relations. The doubt is that the basis for an attribute's subsistence in God is either the attribute itself (applying to both eternal and temporal) or the condition of eternity (a negative concept). The answer refutes this by denying the exclusive enumeration: the quiddity of an eternal attribute may differ from that of a temporal one, and even if they share the concept of attributedness, eternity could be a condition or origination a hindrance.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الفلاسفة
الصفة الحادثة
القدم
الحدوث
الوصفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Mu'tazila deny all divine attributes.
Correction: The Mu'tazila affirm attributes but consider them originated (temporal) due to their relation to contingent objects.

Checkpoint
What is the main doubt presented by those who permit temporal attributes?
A) That eternal attributes cannot be conceived.
B) That the basis for an attribute's subsistence in God is either the attribute itself or the condition of eternity.
C) That philosophers deny all relations.
D) That the Mu'tazila agree with the philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of the doubt hinges on denying that the basis for attribute subsistence is only two options; the quiddity of eternal attributes may differ fundamentally, and conditions or hindrances can be invoked.

?
The refutation denies that only two options exist for attribute subsistence. Are there aspects of your faith where you have limited God by assuming only certain possibilities? Reflect on one area (e.g., prayer, destiny, mercy) and ask yourself: 'Am I open to God being greater than my categories?' What will you do today to expand your heart's capacity for that truth?

First Objection and Answer: Freedom from Perfection as Deficiency
Taftazani presents an objection and its answer regarding freedom from perfection as deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector argues that freedom from perfection is not necessarily a deficiency and how the answer refutes this.

Core Idea
Freedom from one perfection can be a condition for another perfection.

In this passage, Taftazani presents a philosophical objection to the claim that any lack of perfection is a deficiency. The objector uses the example of celestial motions to argue that freedom from a particular perfection can be a condition for the next perfection. The answer then refutes this by showing that such a view would entail temporal attributes in the Necessary Being, which is impossible. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's claim that freedom from perfection is not always deficiency. Notice the example of celestial motions where each perfection is conditioned by the removal of the previous one.
The objector uses a philosophical example from the motion of the spheres.
2
Study the answer's three refutations
The answer first states that the premise is a matter of consensus. Then it argues that if each individual perfection is temporal, the whole type is temporal, making the Necessary Being temporal. Finally, it points out that in eternity the Necessary Being would lack all such perfections, which is a deficiency.
Each refutation targets a different aspect of the objection.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection and answer matter for the doctrine of divine attributes. The answer reinforces that the Necessary Being must be free from all temporal attributes and must possess all perfections eternally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض : بأنا لا نسلم أن الخلو عن صفة الكمال نقص، وإنما يكون لو لم يكن حال الخلو متصفاً بكمال يكون زواله شرطاً لحدوث هذا الكمال. وذلك بأن يتصف دائماً بنوع كمال يتعاقب أفراده من غير بداية ونهاية! ويكون حصول كل لاحق مشروطاً بزوال السابق على ما ذكره الحكماء في حركات الأفلاك فالخلو عن كل فرد يكون شرطاً لحصول كمال آخر بل لاستمرار كمالات غير متناهية، فلا يكون نقصاً.
وأجيب : بأن المقدمة إجماعية بل ضرورية، والسند مدفوع بأنه إذا كان كل فرد حادث، كان النوع حادثاً ضرورة، لأنه لا يوجد إلا في ضمن فرد. وبأن الواجب على ما ذكرتم لا يخلو عن الحادث، فيكون حادثاً ضرورة وبأنه في الأزل يكون خالياً عن كل فرد ضرورة امتناع الحادث في الأزل فيكون ناقصاً.
He objected: We do not accept that being free from an attribute of perfection is a deficiency. It would only be a deficiency if, in the state of being free, the subject is not characterized by a perfection whose removal is a condition for the occurrence of this perfection. That is, if it is always characterized by a type of perfection whose individuals succeed one another without beginning or end, and the occurrence of each subsequent one is conditioned by the removal of the previous one, as the philosophers mention regarding the motions of the spheres. Thus, being free from each individual is a condition for the attainment of another perfection, indeed for the continuation of infinite perfections, so it is not a deficiency.
And the answer: The premise is a matter of consensus, indeed necessary. And the support is refuted because if each individual is temporal (ḥādith), the type is temporal necessarily, since it does not exist except within an individual. And because the Necessary Being (al-wājib), according to what you mentioned, is not free from the temporal, so it would be temporal necessarily. And because in eternity (al-azal) it is free from every individual necessarily due to the impossibility of the temporal in eternity, so it would be deficient.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that being free from an attribute of perfection is not necessarily a deficiency. He gives the example of perpetual perfections like celestial motions, where each perfection is replaced by another, so freedom from one is a condition for the next. The answer refutes this by saying the premise is necessary and obvious. It argues that if each individual perfection is temporal, the whole type is temporal, and the Necessary Being would then be subject to temporal events, making it temporal. Moreover, in eternity it would lack all such perfections, which is a deficiency.

Key Terms

صفة الكمال
حادث
واجب
أزل
نقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any freedom from perfection is automatically a deficiency.
Correction: The objector shows that freedom from one perfection can be a condition for a greater perfection, though the answer refutes this for the divine case.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objector's argument?
A) The Necessary Being must have all perfections.
B) Being free from a particular perfection can be a condition for a greater perfection.
C) The Necessary Being cannot be described by temporal attributes.
D) The philosophers' view on celestial motions is false.

Key Takeaway
The objection that freedom from perfection can be a condition for another perfection is refuted by showing it would lead to temporal attributes in the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The answer shows that accumulating temporal perfections cannot lead to eternal perfection. Is there something in your life that you treat as a chain of temporary achievements, hoping it will make you complete? What would it mean to seek the One who is already perfect without addition?

Second Objection and Answer: Temporal Attributes Imply Change
Taftazani presents the philosophers' main objection that temporal attributes imply change, and answers by distinguishing possibility from occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that temporal attributes imply change in God, and the Ash'ari response that distinguishes between the eternal possibility of temporal events and their eternal occurrence.

Core Idea
The impossibility of change in God does not preclude the eternal possibility of temporal things.

This lesson tackles a central objection from the philosophers: that if God acquires new attributes, He must change. The answer hinges on a subtle distinction between possibility and actuality. Let's see how Taftazani defends the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the philosophers' claim that being described by temporal attributes implies change, which is impossible for God.
2
Identify the key distinction in the answer
The answer distinguishes between the possibility of being described in eternity (with 'in eternity' as a condition for the possibility) and the possibility of the description itself being eternal.
Note the two senses of 'possibility in eternity'.
3
Grasp the analogy
God's capacity to create the world is eternal, but His capacity to create the world in eternity (as an eternal act) is not. This illustrates the distinction.
4
Reflect on the condition of temporality
The temporal is considered with the condition of temporality; otherwise, its existence in eternity would be possible. Understand how this preserves divine immutability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : وهو العمدة عند الحكماء أن الاتصاف بالحادث تغير وهو على الله تعالى محال .
وجوابه : أن اللازم من استحالة الانقلاب جواز الاتصاف في الأزل على أن يكون في الأزل قيداً للجواز، وهو لا يستلزم إلا أزلية جواز الحادث لا جواز الاتصاف في الأزل على أن يكون قيداً للاتصاف ليلزم جواز أزلية الحادث، ولا خفاء في أن المحال جواز أزلية الحادث، بمعنى إمكان أن يوجد في الأزل ، لا أزلية جوازه، بمعنى أن يمكن في الأزل وجوده في الجملة. وهذا كما يقال : إن قابلية الإله لايجاد العالم متحققة في الأزل بخلاف قابليته لايجاد العالم في الأزل أي يمكن في الأزل أن يوجده ، ولا يمكن أن يوجده في الأزل، ومبنى الكلام ، على أن يعتبر الحادث بشرط الحدوث، وإلا فلا خفاء في إمكان وجوده في الأزل.
The second: And it is the main one according to the philosophers: that being described by the temporal (al-ḥādith) is change, and that is impossible for God, exalted.
And its answer: That which follows from the impossibility of reversal is the possibility of being described in eternity (al-azal), provided that 'in eternity' is a condition for the possibility (al-jawāz), not for the description. And it necessitates only the eternity of the possibility of the temporal (azaliyyat jawāz al-ḥādith), not the possibility of being described in eternity such that 'in eternity' is a condition for the description, which would necessitate the possibility of the eternity of the temporal. And it is clear that what is impossible is the possibility of the eternity of the temporal, meaning that it can exist in eternity, not the eternity of its possibility, meaning that its existence in general can be possible in eternity. This is like saying: God's capacity (qābiliyya) to create the world is realized in eternity, unlike His capacity to create the world in eternity; that is, He can create it in eternity [as a possibility], but He cannot create it in eternity [as an actual event]. And the basis of the discourse is that the temporal is considered with the condition of temporality; otherwise, there is no concealment of the possibility of its existence in eternity.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection, which the philosophers consider the main one: being described by a temporal accident constitutes change, which is impossible for God. The answer makes a crucial distinction. It states that what follows from the impossibility of change is that God can be described in eternity only if 'in eternity' qualifies the possibility, not the actual description. This only implies the eternal possibility of temporal events, not the possibility of the temporal being eternal. The answer illustrates with an analogy: God's capacity to create the world is eternal, but His capacity to create it in eternity (as an eternal act) is not. The key is to consider the temporal event under the condition of temporality.

Key Terms
الحادث
التغير
جواز
أزلية
قابلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may conflate the eternal possibility of temporal things with the possibility of eternal temporal things.
Correction: The answer distinguishes between azaliyyat jawāz al-ḥādith (eternity of the possibility of the temporal) and jawāz azaliyyat al-ḥādith (possibility of the eternity of the temporal). Only the former is affirmed; the latter is impossible.

Checkpoint
What distinction does the answer to the second objection make?
A) Between eternal possibility and eternal occurrence.
B) Between temporal and eternal.
C) Between change and non-change.
D) Between philosophers and theologians.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position preserves God's immutability by affirming only the eternal possibility of temporal events, not their eternal actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does His unchanging nature give you security or unease? In what area of your life do you most need to trust in God's unchangeable mercy?
?
The philosophers saw a contradiction between a temporal creation and an unchanging God. How does your own mind struggle to reconcile the idea of a timeless God with the sequence of your daily prayers, your sins, and your hopes? Do you live as though God’s will is already fully determined, or as though your choices genuinely create new events? The Fourth Objection and Its Two Proofs
Taftazani presents the fourth objection and its two supporting proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against God being free from temporal accidents and the two proofs used to support it.

Core Idea
The objection claims God would be temporal if He could have temporal accidents, using two proofs from opposites and capacity.

Taftazani presents the fourth objection from the opponents, arguing that if God could be described by temporal accidents, He would never be free from them, leading to His temporality. Two proofs are given, one based on the opposite of the temporal and the other on the capacity for temporal accidents. Let's examine the objection and these proofs closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the Arabic passage that states the objection: if it were permissible for God to be described by the temporal, then He would not be free from it and thus be temporal.
This is the core claim the opponents make.
2
Understand the entailment
The passage says the entailment has two aspects. Look for the words 'one of them' and 'the second' to identify the two proofs.
The two proofs support why not being free from the temporal implies temporality.
3
Examine the first proof: opposites
The first proof argues that the subject described by the temporal is not free from it nor from its opposite. The opposite of the temporal is also temporal, so the subject must be temporal.
Note the assumption that opposite of temporal is temporal.
4
Examine the second proof: capacity
The second proof argues that the subject is not free from the temporal nor from its capacity, and the capacity is temporal, leading to the possibility of an eternal temporal, which is impossible.
This proof relies on the idea that the capacity for temporal accidents is itself temporal.
5
Note Taftazani's initial judgement
Taftazani states that both proofs are weak ('wa kilā al-wajhayni ḍaʿīf'), without providing the refutation in this passage.
This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه لو جاز اتصافه بالحادث، لزم عدم حلوله عن الحادث فيكون حادثاً لما سبق في حدوث العالم ولمساعدة الخصم على ذلك.
وأما الملازمة فلوجهين :
أحدهما: أن المتصف بالحادث لا يخلو عنه، وعن ضده. وضد الحادث حادث، لأنه منقطع إلى الحادث، ولا شيء من القديم كذلك، لما تقرر أن ما يثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه.
وثانيهما : أنه لا يخلو عنه وعن قابليته وهي حادثة لما مر من أن أزلية القابلية تستلزم جواز أزلية المقبول فيلزم جواز أزلية الحادث وهو محال ، وكلا الوجهين ضعيف .
أما الأول : فلأنه إن أريد بالضد ما هو المتعارف فلا نسلم أن لكل صفة ضد أو أن الموصوف لا يخلو عن الضدين، وإن أريد أن مجرد ما ينافيه وجودياً كان أو عدمياً حتى إن عدم كل شيء ضد له ويستحيل الخلو عنهما ، فلا نسلم أن ضد الحادث حادث فإن القدم والحدوث إن جعلا من صفات الموجود خاصة، فعدم الحادث قبل وجوده ليس بقديم ولا حادث وإن أطلقا على المعدوم أيضاً باعتبار كونه غير مسبوق بالوجود أو مسبوقاً به فهو قديم ، وامتناع زوال القديم إنما هو في الموجود لظهور زوال العدم الأزلي لكل حادث .
وأما الثاني : فلأن القابلية اعتبار عقلي معناه إمكان الاتصاف ولو سلم فأزليتهما إنما تقتضي أزلية جواز المقبول أي إمكانه لا جواز أزليته ليلزم المحال. وقد عرف الفرق .
The fourth: If it were permissible for Him to be described by the temporal, then He would not be free from the temporal, and thus He would be temporal, based on what preceded concerning the temporality of the world and the opponent's agreement to that.
As for the entailment, it has two aspects:
One of them: The subject described by the temporal is not free from it nor from its opposite. And the opposite of the temporal is temporal, because it pertains to the temporal, and nothing of the eternal (al-qadīm) is like that, because it is established that whatever is proven eternal, its non-existence is impossible.
The second: He is not free from it nor from its capacity (qābiliyya), which is temporal, because it has passed that the eternity of the capacity entails the possibility of the eternity of the capable, and thus the possibility of the eternity of the temporal is necessitated, which is impossible. And both aspects are weak.
As for the first: For if by 'opposite' is meant the conventional opposite, then we do not accept that every attribute has an opposite or that the described is not free from the two opposites. And if it is meant merely what contradicts it, whether existent or non-existent, such that the non-existence of everything is an opposite to it and it is impossible to be free from them, then we do not accept that the opposite of the temporal is temporal. For pre-eternity (qidam) and temporality (ḥudūth), if they are made attributes of the existent specifically, then the non-existence of the temporal before its existence is neither eternal nor temporal. And if they are also applied to the non-existent by virtue of its being not preceded by existence or preceded by it, then it is eternal, but the impossibility of the removal of the eternal is only in the existent, due to the evident removal of eternal non-existence for every temporal.
As for the second: For the capacity is an intellectual consideration, meaning the possibility of being described. Even if it is granted, their eternity only entails the eternity of the possibility of the capable, i.e., its possibility, not the possibility of its eternity, which would entail the impossible. And the difference has been recognized.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and its refutation. The objection states that if God could be described by a temporal accident, He would never be free of it, making Him temporal. This entailment is supported by two proofs. The first proof argues that the subject cannot be free of a temporal and its opposite, and the opposite of temporal is also temporal. The second proof argues that the subject cannot be free of the temporal and its capacity, which is temporal, leading to the possibility of eternal temporal events. Taftazani states both proofs are weak. He refutes the first by distinguishing between conventional opposite and mere contradiction; non-existence is not necessarily temporal. The second proof is refuted by noting that capacity is an intellectual consideration of possibility; its eternity only implies the eternal possibility of the possible, not the possibility of its eternal actuality.

According to the first proof, why is the opposite of the temporal considered temporal?
A) Because it is the same as the temporal.

D) Because it is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection claims God would be temporal if He could have temporal accidents, using two proofs from opposites and capacity, but Taftazani declares both weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector argues that if God could change, He would be temporal. But you believe God is unchanging. When was the last time you acted as if God's character changes based on your circumstances? How will you remind yourself today that He is the same yesterday, today, and forever?
?
The first proof relies on the assumption that the opposite of a temporal is also temporal. Are there beliefs you hold about God that you've never examined critically? Will you take time this week to test one such belief against scripture and sound reasoning?
?
The second proof claims that an eternal capacity for temporals would allow an eternal temporal—a contradiction. Do you sometimes treat your own spiritual growth as if it were permanent, forgetting that it requires constant renewal? What is one temporal grace you need to seek from God today?

Refutation of the Two Proofs
Taftazani refutes the two proofs of the fourth objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes each of the two proofs supporting the fourth objection.

Core Idea
The first proof fails because the opposite may be non-existence; the second because capacity is mere possibility.

After presenting the fourth objection and its two proofs, Taftazani now refutes them. He challenges the first proof by distinguishing definitions of 'opposite' and argues that the opposite of a temporal is not necessarily temporal. For the second proof, he clarifies that capacity is an intellectual concept of possibility, so its eternity does not imply eternal actuality. Let's examine these refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the first proof
Read the passage starting with 'Amma al-awwal' until the refutation of the second proof. Notice the two interpretations of 'opposite'.
The first proof is refuted by analyzing the meaning of 'opposite'.
2
Understand Taftazani's two interpretations of 'opposite'
If 'opposite' means conventional opposite (like black and white), we deny every attribute has an opposite or that the subject is free from both. If 'opposite' means contradiction (including non-existence), then we deny the opposite of a temporal is temporal.
The key point: non-existence of the temporal is not temporal.
3
See the application to qidam and huduth
Taftazani discusses whether pre-eternity (qidam) and temporality (huduth) are attributes of existents or also applied to non-existents. If only for existents, non-existence before existence is neither eternal nor temporal. If applied to non-existents, it can be considered eternal, but its removal is not impossible.
This shows the opposite of temporal is not necessarily temporal.
4
Read the refutation of the second proof
The passage continues with 'Amma al-thani'. Here Taftazani argues that capacity (qabiliyya) is an intellectual consideration meaning possibility.
The eternity of capacity only implies the eternal possibility of the capable, not its eternal actuality.
5
Grasp the distinction between possibility and actuality
The second proof claimed that eternal capacity leads to eternal temporal accidents. Taftazani shows that it only leads to the possibility of eternal temporal, which is not impossible because possibility is not actuality.
This avoids the conclusion of an actual eternal temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه لو جاز اتصافه بالحادث، لزم عدم حلوله عن الحادث فيكون حادثاً لما سبق في حدوث العالم ولمساعدة الخصم على ذلك.
وأما الملازمة فلوجهين :
أحدهما: أن المتصف بالحادث لا يخلو عنه، وعن ضده. وضد الحادث حادث، لأنه منقطع إلى الحادث، ولا شيء من القديم كذلك، لما تقرر أن ما يثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه.
وثانيهما : أنه لا يخلو عنه وعن قابليته وهي حادثة لما مر من أن أزلية القابلية تستلزم جواز أزلية المقبول فيلزم جواز أزلية الحادث وهو محال ، وكلا الوجهين ضعيف .
أما الأول : فلأنه إن أريد بالضد ما هو المتعارف فلا نسلم أن لكل صفة ضد أو أن الموصوف لا يخلو عن الضدين، وإن أريد أن مجرد ما ينافيه وجودياً كان أو عدمياً حتى إن عدم كل شيء ضد له ويستحيل الخلو عنهما ، فلا نسلم أن ضد الحادث حادث فإن القدم والحدوث إن جعلا من صفات الموجود خاصة، فعدم الحادث قبل وجوده ليس بقديم ولا حادث وإن أطلقا على المعدوم أيضاً باعتبار كونه غير مسبوق بالوجود أو مسبوقاً به فهو قديم ، وامتناع زوال القديم إنما هو في الموجود لظهور زوال العدم الأزلي لكل حادث .
وأما الثاني : فلأن القابلية اعتبار عقلي معناه إمكان الاتصاف ولو سلم فأزليتهما إنما تقتضي أزلية جواز المقبول أي إمكانه لا جواز أزليته ليلزم المحال. وقد عرف الفرق .
The fourth: If it were permissible for Him to be described by the temporal, then He would not be free from the temporal, and thus He would be temporal, based on what preceded concerning the temporality of the world and the opponent's agreement to that.
As for the entailment, it has two aspects:

And the opposite of the temporal is temporal, because it pertains to the temporal, and nothing of the eternal (al-qadīm) is like that, because it is established that whatever is proven eternal, its non-existence is impossible.

And if they are also applied to the non-existent by virtue of its being not preceded by existence or preceded by it, then it is eternal, but the impossibility of the removal of the eternal is only in the existent, due to the evident removal of eternal non-existence for every temporal.
As for the second: For the capacity is an intellectual consideration, meaning the possibility of being described. Even if it is granted, their eternity only entails the eternity of the possibility of the capable, i.e., its possibility, not the possibility of its eternity, which would entail the impossible. The Seven Subsections of Chapter Three on Divine Attributes
The author enumerates the seven subsections of Chapter Three on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of Chapter Three on existential attributes and the seven topics it covers.

Core Idea
God's attributes are distinct from His essence and seven key ones are debated.

This passage serves as a roadmap for the entire chapter. The author, al-Sanusi, outlines seven investigations under the heading of 'existential attributes' (al-sifat al-wujudiyya). Understanding this outline will help you follow the detailed arguments in the subsections that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage

2
Identify the seven subsections
Look at each 'الثاني', 'الثالث', etc. and note the attribute discussed in each.
The first subsection sets a foundational principle: attributes are additional to the essence.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask why the author groups these particular attributes together. They are the existential attributes that are affirmed for God.
This chapter is a key part of the study of divine attributes (sifat Allah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث وفيه مباحث : في الصفات الوجودية الأول : الصفات زائدة على الذات الثاني : في أنه تعالى قادر الثالث : في أنه تعالى عالم الرابع : في أنه تعالى مريد الخامس : في أنه حي سميع بصير السادس : في أنه تعالى متكلم السابع : في صفات اختلف فيها
Chapter Three, and in it are investigations: On the existential attributes. First: The attributes are additional to the essence. Second: On that He, exalted, is powerful. Third: On that He, exalted, is knowing. Fourth: On that He, exalted, is willing. Fifth: On that He is living, hearing, seeing. Sixth: On that He, exalted, is speaking. Seventh: On attributes that are disputed concerning them.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a chapter outline. It begins by identifying the chapter ('الفصل الثالث') and states that it contains several investigations ('مباحث'). The overarching theme is 'الصفات الوجودية' (the existential attributes). The author then lists seven subsections in order: (1) The attributes are additional to the essence (meaning they are not identical to the divine essence but are distinct yet eternal). (2) That God is powerful (قادر). (3) That God is knowing (عالم). (4) That God is willing (مريد). (5) That God is living, hearing, seeing (حي سميع بصير). (6) That God is speaking (متكلم). (7) On attributes that are disputed (i.e., there is disagreement among theologians about their status or interpretation, such as whether certain attributes like being 'eternal' or 'self-sufficient' are additional or not). This outline sets the agenda for the detailed discussions in the rest of the chapter.

Divine Attributes

Attributes Are Additional to the Essence: The Ash'ari Position
Taftazani defines the issue of attributes and states the Ash'ari position, distinguishing agreed and disputed types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that God's positive attributes are real and additional to His essence, and how this differs from negative and relational attributes.

Core Idea
God's positive attributes are real distinct entities added to His essence.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a foundational debate in Islamic theology: are God's attributes identical with His essence or additional to it? The author, al-Taftazani, clarifies that all Muslim theologians agree on negative and relational attributes—they do not require additional entities. The real disagreement concerns the 'real positive attributes' like knowledge, power, and life. Here, the Ash'ari school (Ahl al-Haqq) affirms that these are eternal attributes distinct from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author begins with a clear thesis: 'Attributes are additional to the essence.'
The author uses 'صفاته زائدة على الذات' as his opening claim.
2
Distinguish the three types of attributes mentioned
The passage groups attributes into three categories: negative attributes (e.g., 'not in a direction'), relational and action attributes (e.g., 'the First', 'the Expander'), and real positive attributes (e.g., knowledge, power, life).
Only the third category is disputed.
3
Note where there is agreement and where disagreement lies
All parties agree that negative and relational attributes do not entail additional entities. The disagreement is specifically over the real positive attributes.
Philosophers and Mu'tazila deny addition; Ahl al-Haqq affirm it.
4
Understand the Ash'ari position: God is knowing by knowledge, etc.
The passage states that for Ahl al-Haqq, God has eternal attributes additional to His essence: He is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, living by life, and similarly for hearing and seeing.
This affirms that the attributes are distinct from the essence but not separate in existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول :
الصفات زائدة على الذات
صفاته زائدة على الذات، فهو عالم له علم، قادر له قدرة حي له حياة، إلى غير ذلك خلافاً للفلاسفة والمعتزلة .
في الوجودية لا خفاء ولا نزاع في أن اتصاف الواجب بالسلبيات مثل كونه واحداً ليس في جهة وحيز لا يقتضى ثبوت صفات له وكذا بالإضافات والأفعال مثل كونه تعالى العلي والعظيم، والأول، والآخر، والقابض والباسط، والخافض والرافع، ونحو ذلك. وإنما الخلاف في الصفات الثبوتية الحقيقية، مثل كونه العالم والقادر فعند أهل الحق له صفات أزلية زايدة على الذات، فهو عالم له علم قادر له قدرة حي له حياة، وكذا في السميع والبصير
The First Section: Attributes are additional to the Essence. His attributes are additional to the Essence: He is knowing with knowledge, powerful with power, living with life, and so on, contrary to the philosophers and the Mu'tazila. Regarding the existents, there is no concealment or dispute that the attribution of the Necessary Existent with negative attributes, such as His being one, not in a direction or place, does not entail the affirmation of attributes for Him; likewise with relational attributes and acts, such as His being the Most High, the Great, the First, the Last, the Constrictor, the Expander, the Abaser, the Exalter, and the like. Rather, the disagreement is concerning the real positive attributes, such as His being the Knowing and the Powerful. For upon Ahl al-Haqq, He has eternal attributes additional to the Essence: He is knowing with knowledge, powerful with power, living with life, and likewise regarding the Hearing and the Seeing.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage defines the issue of whether God's attributes are additional to His essence and states the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the Ash'aris and Maturidis). The author begins by declaring the title: 'Attributes are additional to the essence.' He then summarizes the Ash'ari position: God is knowing with knowledge, powerful with power, living with life, etc. This is contrasted with the views of philosophers and Mu'tazila, who deny that attributes are distinct and additional. Next, the author distinguishes between types of attributes. Negative attributes (like being one, not in a place) and relational/action attributes (like being the First, the Last, etc.) are agreed upon—they do not imply additional entities. The real dispute is over the 'real positive attributes' (al-sifat al-thubutiyya al-haqiqiyya) such as knowledge, power, and life. For Ahl al-Haqq, God has eternal attributes that are additional to His essence: He is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, etc. The same holds for hearing and seeing. The passage thus establishes the fundamental creedal position on the ontological status of God's attributes.

إضافات (relational attributes)
أفعال (actions)

عالم (knowing)

علم (knowledge)
قدرة (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All of God's attributes are either identical to His essence or merely negative/relational in nature.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while negative and relational attributes are agreed upon not to be additional, the real positive attributes (knowledge, power, life, etc.) are indeed additional entities according to Ahl al-Haqq.

Key Takeaway
The core Ash'ari creed affirms that God's positive attributes (like knowledge, power, life) are eternal entities additional to His essence, a position opposed by philosophers and Mu'tazila.
