The Attributes of God Are Additional to the Essence
Opening classification of divine attributes and statement of Ahl al-Haqq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of divine attributes into negative, relational, and real positive, and why the dispute between Ahl al-Haqq and their opponents concerns only the real positive attributes.

Core Idea
The divine attributes dispute is only about real positive attributes, not all attributes.

In Islamic theology, God's attributes are a central topic. This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces the classification of attributes and clarifies the position of Ahl al-Haqq. The author shows that while negative and relational attributes are agreed upon, the real positive attributes are what separate the orthodox from the philosophers and Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own structure come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author begins by stating the title and giving examples.
2
Identify the three categories of attributes
The passage classifies attributes into negative (e.g., being one, not in a direction), relational and act attributes (e.g., being the First, the Last), and real positive attributes (e.g., knowledge, power). Find and note each category.
3
Understand the scope of disagreement
The passage explicitly states that there is no dispute regarding negative and relational attributes. The disagreement is only about the real positive attributes. Why might that be?
This is the key point of the lesson.
4
Grasp the Ahl al-Haqq position
Ahl al-Haqq affirm that God has eternal attributes additional to the essence. They say: He is knowing with knowledge, powerful with power, etc. Compare this with the views of philosophers and Mu'tazila, who deny such additional attributes.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Think about why this classification matters. How does affirming real positive attributes affect one's understanding of God?
This connects to broader debates in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول : الصفات زائدة على الذات، فهو عالم له علم، قادر له قدرة حي له حياة، إلى غير ذلك خلافاً للفلاسفة والمعتزلة . في الوجودية لا خفاء ولا نزاع في أن اتصاف الواجب بالسلبيات مثل كونه واحداً ليس في جهة وحيز لا يقتضى ثبوت صفات له وكذا بالإضافات والأفعال مثل كونه تعالى العلي والعظيم، والأول، والآخر، والقابض والباسط، والخافض والرافع، ونحو ذلك. وإنما الخلاف في الصفات الثبوتية الحقيقية، مثل كونه العالم والقادر فعند أهل الحق له صفات أزلية زايدة على الذات، فهو عالم له علم قادر له قدرة حي له حياة، وكذا في السميع والبصير والمتكلم وغير ذلك.
The First Topic: The Attributes are Additional to the Essence. So He is knowing with knowledge, powerful with power, living with life, and so on, contrary to the philosophers and the Mu'tazila. Regarding existence, there is no obscurity or dispute that the Necessary Existent's being described with negative attributes, such as His being one, not in a direction or place, does not require the affirmation of attributes for Him. Similarly, with relational and act attributes, such as His being the Most High, the Great, the First, the Last, the Restrainer, the Expander, the Lowerer, the Lifter, and the like. The disagreement is only concerning the real positive attributes, such as His being the Knower and the Powerful. According to Ahl al-Haqq, He has eternal attributes additional to the essence, so He is knowing with knowledge, powerful with power, living with life, and likewise regarding the Hearing, the Seeing, the Speaker, and others.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of divine attributes. The author begins by stating the title: attributes are additional to the essence, giving examples (knowledge, power, life). He immediately notes that this is contrary to the philosophers and Mu'tazila. He then explains that negative attributes (e.g., oneness, not being in a direction) and relational/act attributes (e.g., being the Most High, the First) are undisputed—they do not require positing additional real attributes. The debate is confined to the real positive attributes (al-sifat al-thubutiyya al-haqiqiyya) like knowledge and power. For these, Ahl al-Haqq affirm that God has eternal attributes additional to the essence: He is knowing with knowledge, powerful with power, etc., including hearing, seeing, and speech. The author thus establishes the core position and delimits the area of disagreement.

Key Terms
الصفات الثبوتية
الصفات الثبوتية الحقيقية
أهل الحق
الفلاسفة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all categories of divine attributes are disputed between Ahl al-Haqq and their opponents.
Correction: The passage clarifies that negative and relational/act attributes are agreed upon; the dispute is only about real positive attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the only category of divine attributes that is disputed between Ahl al-Haqq and their opponents?
A) Negative attributes
B) Relational attributes
C) Real positive attributes
D) All attributes

Key Takeaway
The debate over divine attributes is limited to real positive attributes, which Ahl al-Haqq affirm as additional to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What step will you take this week to solidify your conviction, such as reading a response to their argument or discussing with a knowledgeable person?
?
The passage narrows the dispute to only real positive attributes. Does this clarity move you to gratitude or to deeper study? In what area of your life have you been treating God's attributes as negotiable rather than real? Write down one change you will make based on the reality of God's power.

The Salaf's Caution on Divine Attribute Terminology
Explanation of the cautious phrasing adopted by early scholars to avoid multiplicity of eternals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Salaf employed cautious phrasing regarding divine attributes to avoid implying multiplicity of eternal entities.

Core Idea
The Salaf guarded divine unity by carefully choosing words about attributes.

This passage reveals the meticulous care early Muslim scholars took when speaking about Allah's attributes. They recognized that even subtle phrasing could inadvertently suggest multiple eternal things. Their solution was to affirm eternal attributes without saying they are eternal independently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disagreement
Notice that the passage begins by noting disagreement about whether attributes are identical to the essence, after agreeing they are not identical. This sets the context for the caution.
2
Understand the caution against multiple eternals
The core concern is the phrase 'multiple eternals' (تعدد القدماء). The Salaf were so cautious that some forbade even saying 'His attributes are eternal' (صفاته قديمة), though they are eternal.
They preferred 'He is eternal with His attributes' (هو قديم بصفاته).
3
Learn the preferred positive phrasing
The Salaf advocated saying attributes 'subsist by His essence' (قائمة بذاته) or 'exist by His essence' (موجودة بذاته), avoiding any implication of separation.
4
Recognize terms to avoid
They prohibited saying attributes are 'in Him', 'with Him', 'adjacent to Him', or 'in a state in Him' because these imply distinction (تغاير).
5
Note the consensus on accidents
The passage concludes with their unanimous agreement that attributes are not described as accidents (أعراض), which would imply temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلاف في البعض ، وفي كونها غير الذات، بعد الاتفاق مع على أنها ليست عين الذات، وكذا في الصفات بعضها مع بعض ، وهذا لفرط تحرزهم عن القول بتعدد القدماء، حتى منع بعضهم أن يقال : صفاته قديمة، وإن كانت أزلية ، بل يقال هو قديم بصفاته وآثروا أن يقال قائمة بذاته أو موجودة بذاته. ولا يقال هي فيه أو معه أو مجاورة له أو حالة فيه، لإيهام التغاير، وأطبقوا على أنها لا توصف بكونها أعراضاً.
There is disagreement about some [attributes], and about them not being the essence, after agreement that they are not identical to the essence. Likewise, regarding the attributes relative to one another. This is due to their extreme caution against saying multiple eternals. Some even forbade saying 'His attributes are eternal,' even though they are eternal, but rather it is said 'He is eternal with His attributes.' They preferred to say they subsist by His essence or exist by His essence. It is not said that they are in Him, or with Him, or adjacent to Him, or in a state in Him, because that implies distinction. They unanimously agreed that they are not described as accidents.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage discusses the cautious terminology used by early scholars (the Salaf) regarding the divine attributes. It begins by noting disagreement about some attributes and about whether they are identical to the essence, though there is agreement that they are not identical. The Salaf were extremely cautious about implying multiple eternal entities (tata'ud al-qudama'). Out of this caution, some forbade saying that attributes are 'qadima' (eternal) because that might suggest separate eternal things. Instead, they said 'He is eternal with His attributes' (huwa qadim bi sifatihi). They preferred to say that attributes subsist by His essence (qa'ima bi dhatihi) or exist by His essence (mawjuda bi dhatihi). They avoided saying that attributes are 'in Him' or 'with Him' to prevent any notion of spatial or essential distinction. Finally, they unanimously agreed that attributes are not described as accidents (a'rad), which would imply temporality. Thus, the passage shows the Salaf's nuanced language to safeguard divine unity.

Key Terms
تعدد القدماء
قديم بصفاته
قائمة بذاته
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying 'attributes are eternal' is acceptable since they are eternal.
Correction: The Salaf saw that phrasing as implying multiple eternal entities, so they preferred 'He is eternal with His attributes' to avoid that implication.

Checkpoint
Why did some early scholars forbid saying 'His attributes are eternal' (صفاته قديمة)?
A) Because they believed attributes are not eternal
B) Because it might imply multiple eternal entities
C) Because attributes are accidents
D) Because attributes are identical to the essence

Key Takeaway
The Salaf's cautious terminology preserves the oneness of Allah by carefully distinguishing attributes from independent eternal entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak of Allah's knowledge or power, do you ever unconsciously imagine them as separate 'things' alongside Allah? How can you train your tongue to use phrases like 'He is eternal with His attributes' instead of 'His attributes are eternal'?
?
The Salaf's caution shows they feared even the slightest implication of multiplicity. How much do you guard your own words about Allah? Is there a phrase you commonly use that might be inaccurate according to the Salaf's standards? Will you stop using it today?
?
This lesson reveals that the Salaf prioritized protecting God's unity through precise language. Do you really believe words matter that much? If so, what will you change in your conversations about Allah starting now?

Al-Razi's Minimalist View on Knowledge Attributes
Al-Razi enumerates views on knowledge and states his own minimalist position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi reduces the number of entities in divine knowledge to just two: the essence and an additional relation called 'being-knowing'.

Core Idea
Al-Razi simplifies divine attributes to essence plus one relation.

This passage presents a methodological analysis by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi of the dispute about the nature of divine knowledge. He first surveys the positions of other theologians, who multiply entities (essence, attribute, relation, etc.), then states his own minimalist view. This lesson will help you see how al-Razi attempts to reduce complexity while still affirming that something additional exists in the knower.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the translation. Notice that al-Razi describes three distinct positions in order.
The passage is incomplete, but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the three positions
Count the different views: some say three things (essence, attribute, relation), some say four or five (depending on whether knowledge and being-knowing are distinguished), and al-Razi himself says only two.
The second position is split into two sub-options.
3
Focus on al-Razi's argument
Notice that al-Razi claims the relation called 'being-knowing' is additional to the essence, and he gives a reason: the concept of this relation is not the concept of the essence.
The argument is cut off, but the direction is clear.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does it matter how many entities we affirm? This is a key debate in Islamic theology about simplicity vs. multiplicity in God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في المطالب العالية : أهم المهمات في هذه المسألة، البحث عن محل الخلاف فمن المتكلمين من زعم أن العلم صفة قائمة بذات العالم، ولها تعلق بالمعلوم فهناك أمور ثلاثة الذات والصفة والتعلق ومنهم من زعم أن العلم صفة توجب العالمية، وأن هناك تعلقاً بالمعلوم من غير أن يعين أن المتعلق هو العلم أو العالمية ليكون هناك أمور أربعة أو كلاهما ليكون هناك أمور خمسة : ثم قال : وأما نحن فلا نثبت إلا أمرين الذات والنسبة المسماة بالعالمية، وندعي أنها أمر زائد على الذات موجود فيه للقطع ، بأن المفهوم من هذه النسبة ليس هو المفهوم من
He said in al-Matalib al-Aliyah: The most important matters in this issue is the investigation of the locus of disagreement. Among the theologians, some claimed that knowledge is an attribute subsisting by the essence of the knower, and it has a relation to the known, so there are three things: the essence, the attribute, and the relation. Some claimed that knowledge is an attribute that necessitates being-knowing, and that there is a relation to the known without specifying whether the related thing is knowledge or being-knowing, so that there would be four things, or both so that there would be five things. Then he said: As for us, we only affirm two things: the essence and the relation called 'being-knowing,' and we claim that it is something additional to the essence, existing in it, decisively, because the concept of this relation is not the concept of
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents a quoted argument from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's work al-Matalib al-Aliyah. It is a methodological analysis of the dispute about divine attributes. Al-Razi first surveys the views of other theologians: some hold that knowledge is an attribute subsisting in the knower with a relation to the known, yielding three entities (essence, attribute, relation). Others hold that knowledge necessitates the state of 'being knowing' (al-'alimiyya), with a relation, leading to four or five entities depending on whether one distinguishes between knowledge, being-knowing, and relation. Al-Razi then states his own position: he affirms only two things—the essence and the relation called 'being-knowing' (al-nisba al-musamma bi-l-'alimiyya)—claiming that this relation is something additional to the essence. The argument cuts off, but the intent is to show that al-Razi tries to reduce the number of distinct entities while still affirming an additional relation.

Key Terms
المطالب العالية
العلم
العالمية
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Razi is denying any additional attribute and reducing everything to the essence alone.
Correction: Al-Razi does affirm an additional entity: the relation called 'being-knowing' is something extra beyond the essence. He is not a mere reductionist but seeks to minimize the number of distinct entities.

Checkpoint

A) Three: essence, attribute, relation
B) Four: essence, knowledge, being-knowing, relation
C) Five: everything
D) Two: essence and the relation called being-knowing

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi reduces the number of entities in divine knowledge to two: the essence and an additional relation called 'being-knowing'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi reduces divine knowledge to a minimal relation to safeguard God's simplicity. In your own conception of Allah, do you tend to overcomplicate His attributes with human categories? What would it mean for you to trust that His simplicity is more profound than any mental dissection?
?
The passage ends with a rational argument: the concept of the relation is distinct from the concept of the essence. When you reflect on Allah's knowledge, do you rely more on logical deduction or on the Qur'anic descriptions? Could an overreliance on reason distance you from the sheer awe of His unknowable essence?
?
Al-Razi's position is a minority view that challenges mainstream theology. If you discovered that a cherished theological belief of yours was mistaken, would you have the courage to adopt a more humble, minimalist stance? What attachment to intellectual comfort might you need to abandon?

The Sifatiyya's Agreement and Caution on Attributes
The author explains the Sifatiyya's shared position on divine attributes and their cautious phraseology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sifatiyya's shared position on divine attributes and their careful choice of wording to avoid any implication of multiple eternal beings.

Core Idea
The Sifatiyya's cautious language protects tawhid by avoiding any hint of multiple eternal entities.

The Sifatiyya, or affirmers of attributes, represent a mainstream Sunni position that affirms that Allah has real attributes without identifying them with His essence or separating them into independent entities. This passage explains their shared ground and the precise language they adopted to avoid any suggestion of polytheism. Pay close attention to both what they say and what they forbid saying.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreement and disagreement
Read the passage to find what all Sifatiyya agree on (attributes are not identical to the essence) and where they differ (whether attributes are 'other than' the essence, and how attributes relate to each other).
Notice that the agreement is foundational, while disagreements are secondary.
2
Understand the reason for caution
The text states that their caution arises from a desire to avoid implying multiple eternal beings (taʿaddud al-qudamāʾ). Reflect on why this would be a concern.
This caution drives all the linguistic choices that follow.
3
Learn the preferred and prohibited phrases
Identify which phrases they prefer (e.g., 'He is pre-eternal with His attributes', 'subsisting by His essence') and which they forbid (e.g., 'His attributes are pre-eternal', 'in Him', 'with Him'). Understand the reasoning behind each prohibition.
The key is to avoid any wording that could suggest attributes are separate, co-eternal entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمتكلم وغير ذلك . اختلاف في البعض ، وفي كونها غير الذات، بعد الاتفاق مع على أنها ليست عين الذات، وكذا في الصفات بعضها مع بعض ، وهذا لفرط تحرزهم عن القول بتعدد القدماء، حتى منع بعضهم أن يقال : صفاته قديمة، وإن كانت أزلية ، بل يقال هو قديم بصفاته وآثروا أن يقال قائمة بذاته أو موجودة بذاته. ولا يقال هي فيه أو معه أو مجاورة له أو حالة فيه، لإيهام التغاير، وأطبقوا على أنها لا توصف بكونها أعراضاً.
And the speaker and other than that. Disagreement in some, and in the fact that they are other than the essence, after agreement [with ?] that they are not the essence itself, and likewise in the attributes with each other. And this is due to their excessive caution against saying there are multiple eternals, so much so that some of them forbade saying: His attributes are pre-eternal (qadīma), even though they are beginningless (azaliyya), but rather it is said: He is pre-eternal with His attributes. And they preferred that it be said: subsisting by His essence (qāʾima bi-dhātihi) or existing by His essence. And it is not said: they are in Him, or with Him, or adjacent to Him, or in a state in Him, because of the impression of distinction. And they agreed that they are not described as being accidents.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage describes the position of the Sifatiyya (the affirmers of attributes) regarding the divine attributes. It begins by stating that despite agreement that the attributes are not identical to the essence (ʿayn al-dhāt), there is disagreement on some points, such as whether they are 'other than' (ghayr) the essence, and whether attributes differ among themselves. The author explains that this caution stems from their extreme care to avoid implying the existence of multiple eternal beings (taʿaddud al-qudamāʾ). Some even forbade saying 'His attributes are pre-eternal (qadīma)' even though they are beginningless, preferring instead to say 'He is pre-eternal with His attributes.' They also insisted on phrasing such as 'subsisting by His essence' or 'existing by His essence' rather than 'in Him' or 'with Him' etc., to avoid any suggestion that attributes are separate entities. Finally, they unanimously reject describing attributes as accidents (aʿrāḍ).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the Sifatiyya affirm that attributes are separate, co-eternal entities distinct from Allah.
Correction: The passage shows that they go to great lengths to avoid any wording that could imply such separation. They choose phrases like 'subsisting by His essence' to emphasize that attributes are not independent.

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya's careful language about divine attributes reflects their commitment to avoiding any hint of multiple eternal beings, safeguarding the principle of tawhid.

Opponents of the Sifatiyya
Author lists the opponents of the Sifatiyya and their naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which groups opposed the Sifatiyya's claim of the increase of attributes and how they labeled the affirmers.

Core Idea
Most sects, especially philosophers and Mu'tazila, opposed attribute increase and named affirmers 'Ṣifātiyya'.

The author begins by identifying the opponents of the Sifatiyya. These are not a fringe group but include the majority of sects, such as the philosophers and the Mu'tazila, along with other innovators. This passage establishes the sectarian context for the debate over divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the list of opponents.
2
Identify the opponents
The text mentions 'most sects' and specifically names philosophers and Mu'tazila, plus those who follow them.
3
Understand the labeling
Note that they called the affirmers 'Ṣifātiyya' – a label that stuck.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخالف في القول بزيادة الصفات أكثر الفرق كالفلاسفة والمعتزلة ، ومن يجري مجراهم من أهل البدع والأهواء، وسموا القائلين بها بالصفاتية، ثم اختلفت عباراتهم فقيل : هو حي عالم قادر لنفسه، وقيل بنفسه. وقيل لكونه على حالة أخص صفاته، وقيل لا لنفسه، ولا لعلل . وكلام الإمام الرازي في تحقيق إثبات الصفات وتحرير محل النزاع، ربما يميل الى الاعتزال.
And the majority of sects—such as the philosophers and the Muʿtazila, and those who follow their path from the people of innovation and desires—opposed the statement affirming the increase (ziyāda) of the attributes. They called those who affirm them the 'Ṣifātiyya'. Then their expressions differed: it was said: 'He is living, knowing, able for His essence (li-nafsihi)'; and it was said: 'by His essence (bi-nafsihi)'; and it was said: 'because He is upon a state specific to His attributes'; and it was said: 'not for His essence, nor for causes.' And the words of Imam al-Rāzī in verifying the affirmation of attributes and determining the locus of dispute perhaps tend toward Muʿtazilism.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage identifies the opponents of the Sifatiyya: most sects except them, especially philosophers, the Muʿtazila, and other 'people of innovation' (ahl al-bidaʿ). These opponents reject the view that attributes are additional (ziyāda) to the essence and label the affirmers as 'Ṣifātiyya'. The passage then lists the different expressions the Sifatiyya themselves used to describe God: some said He is living, knowing, able 'for His essence' (li-nafsihi), others 'by His essence' (bi-nafsihi), others 'because He is upon a state specific to His attributes', and others 'not for His essence, nor for causes'. Finally, the author notes that Imam al-Rāzī's investigation into this matter may incline toward Muʿtazilism, i.e., denying the increase of attributes.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة
المعتزلة
الصفاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only the Mu'tazila opposed the Sifatiyya.
Correction: The text states that most sects, including philosophers and those who follow them, opposed the Sifatiyya.

Checkpoint

A) Only the Mu'tazila.

C) Only the philosophers.
D) All Muslims except the Sifatiyya.

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya's claim of attribute increase was opposed by most sects, especially philosophers and Mu'tazila, who called them by that name.

Misconception: The Sifatiyya had a single uniform expression for attributes.

Checkpoint
How many formulations of the Sifatiyya are listed in the passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya used various expressions to describe God's attributes, demonstrating internal diversity, and al-Rāzī's view may align with the Mu'tazila.

?

Al-Razi's Resolution of the Attribute Dispute
Al-Razi analyzes the dispute on attributes and offers his solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise point of disagreement among mutakallimūn on divine attributes and al-Razi's own position that only essence and the relation of being a knower are real.

Core Idea
Al-Razi reduces the attribute dispute to essence and a single additional relation.

Imam al-Razi, in his work al-Matalib al-Aliya, sharply delineates the exact locus of disagreement about divine attributes. He surveys the views of earlier schools—some affirming three, four, or even five distinct realities—before presenting his own minimalist position. Reading this passage will clarify what is actually at stake in the debate and how al-Razi attempts to cut through the complexity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views surveyed
Al-Razi presents the positions of other mutakallimūn. Read carefully what each group affirms and how they differ in counting the relata.
Notice that the dispute revolves around whether knowledge (ʿilm) itself is an attribute, or whether the relation (taʿalluq) is an additional entity.
2
Understand al-Razi's own view
Al-Razi states his position: only two things—the essence and the relation called being a knower (al-ʿālimiyya). He claims this relation is additional to the essence.
He argues that the concept of the relation is not the same as the concept of the essence.
3
Reflect on the argument's force
Ask yourself why al-Razi thinks his view is decisive. How does he justify that the relation is something extra, and how does that simplify the attribute debate?
He ties the argument to the necessity of qualifying the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في المطالب العالية : أهم المهمات في هذه المسألة، البحث عن محل الخلاف فمن المتكلمين من زعم أن العلم صفة قائمة بذات العالم، ولها تعلق بالمعلوم فهناك أمور ثلاثة الذات والصفة والتعلق ومنهم من زعم أن العلم صفة توجب العالمية، وأن هناك تعلقاً بالمعلوم من غير أن يعين أن المتعلق هو العلم أو العالمية ليكون هناك أمور أربعة أو كلاهما ليكون هناك أمور خمسة : ثم قال : وأما نحن فلا نثبت إلا أمرين الذات والنسبة المسماة بالعالمية، وندعي أنها أمر زائد على الذات موجود فيه للقطع ، بأن المفهوم من هذه النسبة ليس هو المفهوم من الذات، وإن من اعترف بكونه عالماً لم يمكنه نفي هذه النسبة إذ لا معين للعالم إلا الذات الموصوفة بهذه النسبة ، ولا للقادر إلا الذات الموصوفة بأنه يصح منه الفعل .
He said in al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliya: 'The most important of important matters in this issue is the investigation of the locus of disagreement. For among the mutakallimūn (speculative theologians) are those who claim that knowledge (al-ʿilm) is an attribute subsisting by the essence of the knower, and it has a relation (taʿalluq) to the known; thus there are three things: the essence, the attribute, and the relation. And among them are those who claim that knowledge is an attribute that necessitates being a knower (al-ʿālimiyya), and that there is a relation to the known without specifying whether the relatum is knowledge or being a knower, so that there are four things, or both, so that there are five things. Then he said: But as for us, we do not affirm except two things: the essence and the relation (al-nisba) called being a knower (al-ʿālimiyya). And we claim that it is something additional to the essence, existing in it, decisively, because the concept of this relation is not the concept of the essence. And whoever acknowledges that He is knowing cannot deny this relation, since the known thing is nothing but the essence described by this relation, and the powerful is nothing but the essence described by the fact that action proceeds from Him.'
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from Imam al-Rāzī's work al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliya, in which he clarifies the exact point of dispute regarding divine attributes. He first surveys the positions of other mutakallimūn: some say that knowledge is an attribute subsisting in the essence and has a relation to the known, resulting in three things (essence, attribute, relation). Others say that knowledge is an attribute that causes being a knower (al-ʿālimiyya), and there is a relation, but they differ on what exactly the relatum is—knowledge, being a knower, or both—leading to four or five things. Then al-Rāzī presents his own view: he affirms only two things—the essence and the relation called 'being a knower' (al-ʿālimiyya). He insists that this relation is additional to the essence and exists in it, because the concept of the relation is not the same as the concept of the essence. He argues that anyone who admits that God is knowing cannot deny this relation, because the 'knower' is nothing but the essence qualified by this relation. Similarly, the 'powerful' is the essence qualified by the relation of being such that action proceeds from Him.

Key Terms
العلم (al-ʿilm)
الصفة (al-ṣifa)
التعلق (al-taʿalluq)
العالمية (al-ʿālimiyya)
النسبة (al-nisba)
المطالب العالية (al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Rāzī reduces attributes to mere relations, denying any real distinction.
Correction: Al-Rāzī insists the relation (nisba) is something additional to the essence, existing in it decisively, not a mere conceptual distinction.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, how many things does he affirm regarding divine knowledge?
A) Three: essence, attribute, relation.
B) Four: essence, attribute, being a knower, relation.
C) Five: essence, attribute, being a knower, relation, and a second relation.
D) Two: essence and the relation called being a knower.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi reduces the attribute dispute to only essence and the single additional relation of being a knower (ʿālimiyya), arguing that this relation is conceptually distinct from the essence and cannot be denied by anyone who affirms God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's knowledge, do you reduce it to a vague concept or genuinely affirm that there is a real relation between His essence and everything He knows? How does that reality change the way you seek His guidance in your current struggles?
?
Al-Rāzī insists that the relation of being a knower (al-ʿālimiyya) is conceptually distinct from the essence. Examine your own understanding: Do you treat Allah's names and attributes as mere labels, or as real descriptions that shape your relationship with Him? What specific attribute will you meditate on today to deepen your awe?
?
If someone asked you why al-Rāzī affirms only two things (essence and relation), could you defend the necessity of that relation? How would your answer impact your certainty that Allah truly knows you—your innermost thoughts, fears, and hopes—right now?

Why Knowledge Is Not a Mere Relation
Al-Razi's argument that knowledge is not identical to a relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's logical argument that divine knowledge must be a real attribute, not a mere relation (idafa).

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot be relational because God knows what does not yet exist.

Al-Razi, a towering figure in Sunni theology, used a powerful logical argument to show that knowledge must be a real attribute, not a mere relation. This passage from his Nihayat al-Uqul cuts to the heart of a key issue in divine attributes. As we read, watch how he turns the relational view against itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a citation, and a conditional argument.
The passage begins with 'هذا : وقد عرفت أنه لا يجوز...'
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the terms الإضافة, المعلوم, المقدور. Understand their meanings: relation, known, object of power. These are central to the argument.
The argument hinges on the condition that relational things need both relata.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: if knowledge were a relation, it would depend on the known. But God knows impossibles (which never exist) and possibles (which exist only after creation). Creation depends on God's knowledge, so knowledge cannot be a relation.
This shows a circular dependency if knowledge were relational.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: Why does this matter? It establishes that God's attributes are real and subsist in His essence, not mere relationships to external things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا : وقد عرفت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون العلم نفس الإضافة . وقد صرح هو أيضاً بذلك . حيث قال في نهاية العقول، لو كان عالماً وقادراً مجرداً من إضافي لتوقف ثبوته على ثبوت المعلوم والمقدور لأن وجود الامور الإضافية مشروط بوجود المضافين، لكن المعلوم قد يكون محالاً ، وقد يكون ممكناً، لا يوجد إلا بإيجاد الله تعالى المتوقف على كونه عالماً قادراً.
This: And you have known that it is not permissible for knowledge (al-ʿilm) to be the very relation (al-iḍāfa). And he has explicitly stated that as well, where he said in Nihāyat al-ʿUqūl: 'If He were knowing and powerful stripped of the relational (al-iḍāfī), then His existence would depend on the existence of the known and the object of power, because the existence of relational things is conditioned by the existence of the two relata. But the known may be impossible, and may be possible, and does not exist except by the creation of God, which depends on His being knowing and powerful.'
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage provides a crucial argument against identifying knowledge with a mere relation (iḍāfa). The author first states that it is impossible for knowledge to be identical to a relation, and that al-Rāzī himself explicitly affirmed this. He then quotes al-Rāzī's argument from Nihāyat al-ʿUqūl: If God's being knowing and powerful were purely relational (i.e., consisted only in relations to known things and objects of power), then His attributes would depend on the existence of those objects. But relational things require the existence of both relata. However, God knows things that are impossible (which never exist) and possible things that do not exist yet except by His creation. God's act of creation depends on His being already knowing and powerful. Therefore, knowledge cannot be a mere relation; it must be a real attribute subsisting in the essence.

Key Terms
الإضافة (al-iḍāfa)
المعلوم (al-maʿlūm)
المقدور (al-maqdūr)
الإضافي (al-iḍāfī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine attributes are merely relationships to creation, not real attributes.
Correction: This argument shows that if attributes were mere relations, they would depend on the existence of their objects, but God knows things that do not yet exist. Hence, attributes must be real.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi argue that knowledge cannot be a mere relation?
A) Because relations are not real.
B) Because relations would depend on the existence of the known, but God knows things that do not yet exist.
C) Because God's knowledge is simple.
D) Because the Muʿtazila said so.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge cannot be a mere relation because relational attributes depend on the existence of their relata, yet God knows things that do not exist—a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your deeds does not depend on your existence, how does that affect the way you approach Him in repentance, knowing He has always known every sin you would commit?
?
Consider a time you felt spiritually insignificant. How does the truth that God's attribute of knowledge is utterly independent of creation—and therefore of your own deserving—reshape your confidence in His awareness of your struggles?
?
If God's knowledge is a real attribute and not merely a relation to your actions, then every moment of your life is eternally present to Him. How does that change what it means to 'live as if you see Him' (iḥsān)?

Why Knowledge Cannot Be a Mere Relation
Argument against knowledge as a mere relation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological argument that divine knowledge cannot be merely a relation to the objects of knowledge, because that would lead to a vicious circular dependence.

Core Idea
If knowledge were a mere relation, it would depend on its object—but the object depends on God's knowledge.

This passage from a classic theological work addresses a subtle but crucial point about the nature of divine attributes. Some theologians argued that attributes like knowledge are merely relations (idafa) between the essence and what is known. The author shows why this is impossible: it would trap God's knowledge in a vicious circle. Let's read how.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core assertion
Read the author's statement that knowledge cannot be identical to the relation. Notice how he connects 'knowing' to the essence described with the relation.
The author first establishes that 'knowing' implies a relation to the essence.
2
Examine the supporting quote
The author quotes from Nihayat al-Uqool to present the key argument: if God were knowing devoid of relation, His being knowing would depend on the existence of the known.
The quote highlights the conditional nature of relational matters.
3
Understand the circularity problem
The known may not exist yet (possible future) or may be impossible. If knowledge is a relation, it would require the known to exist first, but that existence depends on God's creative power, which itself is an attribute. This creates a circle.
4
Conclude the necessity of inherent attributes
Reflect on why this argument proves that knowledge (and attributes like power) must be inherent, not merely relational. They exist in the essence regardless of the existence of objects.
This safeguards divine independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذات، وإن من اعترف بكونه عالماً لم يمكنه نفي هذه النسبة إذ لا معين للعالم إلا الذات الموصوفة بهذه النسبة ، ولا للقادر إلا الذات الموصوفة بأنه يصح منه الفعل . هذا : وقد عرفت أنه لا يجوز أن يكون العلم نفس الإضافة . وقد صرح هو أيضاً بذلك . حيث قال في نهاية العقول، لو كان عالماً وقادراً مجرداً من إضافي لتوقف ثبوته (۱) على ثبوت المعلوم (۲) والمقدور لأن وجود الامور الإضافية مشروط بوجود المضافين، لكن المعلوم قد يكون محالاً ، وقد يكون ممكناً، لا يوجد إلا بإيجاد الله تعالى المتوقف على كونه عالماً قادراً.
The essence, and whoever acknowledges that He is knowing cannot deny this relation, for there is no determination of 'knowing' except the essence described with this relation, nor of 'powerful' except the essence described with that action is possible from Him. This: and you have come to know that it is not permissible for knowledge to be identical to the relation. And he himself explicitly stated that. For he said in Nihayat al-Uqool: if He were knowing and powerful devoid of relation, His being so qualified would depend on the existence of the known (or: effect) and the willed, because the existence of relational matters is conditioned by the existence of the two relata, but the known may be impossible, and may be possible, not existing except by the creation of God Almighty, which depends on His being knowing and powerful.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage argues against the view that divine attributes like knowledge are merely relations (idafa) between the essence and its objects. The author begins by stating that whoever affirms that God is knowing cannot deny the relation of knowledge to the essence, since 'knowing' has no meaning except an essence described with that relation; similarly for 'powerful'. Then he asserts that it is not permissible for knowledge to be identical to the relation itself. He supports this by quoting from 'Nihayat al-Uqool': if God were knowing and powerful without any relational attribute, then His being described as such would depend on the existence of the known and the willed objects, because relational matters require the existence of the two relata. However, the known may be impossible or merely possible, and a possible thing does not exist except by God's creation, which itself depends on His being knowing and powerful. This creates a circular dependency if knowledge is merely a relation, thus proving that knowledge must be an inherent attribute, not a relation.

Key Terms
الذات
العالم
القادر
العلم
الإضافة
المعلوم
المقدور
نهاية العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine attributes like knowledge are merely relations or modes of relation to creation.
Correction: This would make the attributes dependent on the existence of objects, which leads to circular dependence. Attributes must be inherent to preserve divine independence.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from Nihayat al-Uqool, why can't knowledge be a mere relation?
A) Because knowledge is an attribute of perfection.
B) Because the known may not exist yet, so knowledge would depend on it.
C) Because relation is a type of attribute.
D) Because God is knowing but not powerful.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge must be an inherent attribute, not a mere relation, to avoid the vicious circle of dependence on the objects of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you truly believe His knowledge of you is eternal and unchanging, or do you sometimes act as if He discovers what you do?
?
This argument shows that God's attributes are real and inherent, not just links to creation. When you think of God's mercy or justice, do you consider them as temporary reactions to your actions, or as eternal attributes that define who He is? Which view actually transforms how you approach Him in repentance?
?
The passage warns against circular dependence: if His knowledge depended on its objects, He would need the creature to be knowing. Is there any area of your life where you functionally expect God to depend on your choices or actions before He can respond? What would it look like to live completely under the reality that His attributes are self-sufficient and prior to all things?

First Proof: Definition of the Knowing and the Cause of Being Knowing
First proof: definitional argument for attribute addition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that the definition of a knowing being requires knowledge as an additional attribute, and that this definition applies equally to God and creatures.

Core Idea
The definition of 'knowing' implies knowledge as its cause, and this is universal.

Sa‘d al-Din al-Taftazani begins his refutation with a logical argument based on the very definition of a knowing being. Just as we call a human 'knowing' only because they possess knowledge, the same must hold for God. This proof establishes that the cause of being knowing is knowledge, and this does not differ between the witnessed and the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the Arabic passage that defines 'alim and states that the cause of being 'alim is knowledge.
Notice the phrase 'وهذا لا يختلف شاهداً وغائباً'.
2
Understand the definition
The definition of 'alim is 'one to whom knowledge has occurred'. This implies knowledge is a separate entity.
Contrast with accidental qualities like origination.
3
Apply to God
If God is 'alim, He must have knowledge. The argument is simple but foundational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على الفلاسفة والمعتزلة في عدم زيادة الصفات لنا وجوه الأول : أن حد العالم من قام به العلم وعلة العالمية أعني كونه عالماً هو العلم، وهذا لا يختلف شاهداً وغائباً بخلاف ما ليس من الوجوه (۱) التي توجب كون العالم عالماً كالعرضية والحدوث ونحو ذلك . الثاني : أنه لا يعقل من العالم إلا من له العلم، ومن المعلوم إلا ما تعلق به العلم فبالضرورة إذا كان عالماً وكان له معلوم كان له علم، فإن قيل : علمه ذاته قلنا فلا يفيد حمله على الذات ولا تتميز الصفات ولا يفتقر إلى الإثبات ، ويكون العلم مثلاً واجباً معبوداً صانعاً للعالم موصوفاً بالكمالات فإن قيل يكفي تغاير المفهوم كما في سائر المحمولات قلنا ليس الكلام في مثل العالم والقادر والحي بل في العلم والقدرة والحياة. فإن قيل ذاته من حيث التعلق بالمعلومات عالم بلا علم وبالمقدورات قادر بلا قدرة، كالواحد نصف الاثنين وثلث الثلاثة وهكذا مع أن الموجود واحد لا غير قلنا معلوم قطعاً أن الذات لا تكون علماً وقدرة بل عالماً وقادراً. ويبقى الكلام في المعنى الذي هو مأخذ الاشتقاق ولا يفيدك تسميته بالتعلق للقطع بأنه من الصفات الحقيقية لا الاعتبارات العقلية (٢) . الثالث : قوله تعالى أنزله بعلمه ) (۳)، (أنما أنزل بعلم الله ) (٤)، ذي القوة المتين (ه)، أن القوة الله (٦) ) .
The refutation of the philosophers and the Mu'tazilites regarding the non-addition of attributes. We have arguments: The first: that the definition of the knowing is one to whom knowledge has occurred, and the cause of being knowing, i.e., His being knowing, is knowledge, and this does not differ between the witnessed and the unseen, unlike what is not among the considerations that necessitate the knowing being knowing, such as accidentalness and origination and the like. The second: that nothing is rationally understood as the knowing except one who has knowledge, and nothing is known except that to which knowledge is attached; so necessarily, if He is knowing and has a known, He has knowledge. If it is said: His knowledge is His essence, we say: then applying it to the essence is not beneficial, nor are the attributes distinguished, nor does it need proof, and knowledge would be, for example, a necessary existent, worshipped, creator of the world, described with perfections. If it is said: the diversity of concept suffices as in other predicates, we say: the discussion is not about such as the knowing, the powerful, and the living, but about knowledge, power, and life. If it is said: His essence in relation to known things is knowing without knowledge, and in relation to willed things is powerful without power, like the one is half of two and a third of three and so on, while the existent is one not otherwise, we say: it is known with certainty that the essence is not knowledge and power but knowing and powerful. And the discussion remains about the meaning which is the basis of derivation, and calling it relation does not benefit you, because it is known to be from the real attributes, not from the mental considerations. The third: His saying, may He be exalted: 'He sent it down with His knowledge' (4:166), 'It was only sent down with the knowledge of God' (11:14), 'Possessor of Strong Power' (51:58), 'Indeed power belongs entirely to God' (2:165).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of philosophers and Mu'tazilites who deny that divine attributes are additional to the essence. The author presents three proofs (wujuh). The first proof uses the definition of 'alim (knowing): one who has knowledge, and the cause of being 'alim is knowledge. This definition applies equally to witnessed and unseen, unlike accidental or originated causes. The second proof is more detailed: it is rationally impossible to conceive of a 'alim without knowledge, so if God is 'alim and has a known object, He must have knowledge. Then objections are addressed: (i) if knowledge is said to be the essence, the response is that this renders attribution meaningless, fails to distinguish attributes, and would imply knowledge is a necessary existent and creator, which is absurd; (ii) if conceptual distinction suffices, the response is that the debate is about the real attributes (knowledge, power, life) not the derived names (knowing, powerful, living); (iii) if the essence is 'alim by relation without an inherent attribute, like 'one is half of two', the response is that the essence is not itself knowledge or power, and the real attribute (masdar of derivation) is not a mere relation but a real attribute. The third proof cites Quranic verses that explicitly attribute knowledge and power to God as things He possesses, indicating real attributes.

Key Terms
العالم
العلم
العالمية
الاشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the definition of a 'knowing' being?
A) One who can know
B) One to whom knowledge has occurred
C) One who knows by essence
D) One who is eternal

Key Takeaway
The definition of a knowing being universally requires knowledge as a cause, so God must possess knowledge as an additional attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attribute of knowledge is truly additional to His essence as this proof argues, does that make your concept of Him more personal or more abstract? The answer reveals something about how you relate to His attributes.
?
This proof says the definition of 'knowing' is the same for you and for God. When you claim to 'know' God, is that knowledge actually present in you as a real quality? Or is it just a label? Pause and examine your heart: has knowledge of God truly occurred to you?
?
The proof strips away accidental properties like origination to focus on the cause of being knowing. What accidental properties in your own life (habits, fears, comforts) do you cling to that prevent you from seeking knowledge as the cause of your being? What would change if you prioritized knowing God over all else?

Second Proof: Rational Necessity of Knowledge for a Knower
Second proof: rational argument with objections and responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that a knower necessarily has knowledge, and how the author refutes objections based on identity of essence and knowledge, conceptual distinction, and relational attribution.

Core Idea
A knower without knowledge is rationally inconceivable; objections fail because they confuse concepts with realities.

The second proof is more elaborate, arguing from the rational impossibility of a knower without knowledge. The author then addresses three objections: that knowledge is identical to the essence, that conceptual distinction suffices, and that the essence is 'knowing' by relation. Each is shown to be insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the statement: 'لا يعقل من العالم إلا من له العلم' and the conditional necessity.
2
First objection and response
If knowledge is the essence, the response shows absurd consequences.
Knowledge would be a necessary existent and creator.
3
Second objection and response
If conceptual distinction suffices, the response notes the debate is about real attributes not derived names.
The discussion is about knowledge, not 'knowing'.
4
Third objection and response
If the essence is knowing by relation like 'half of two', the response shows the essence is not itself knowledge, and the attribute is real.
The derivation basis is a real attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على الفلاسفة والمعتزلة في عدم زيادة الصفات لنا وجوه الأول : أن حد العالم من قام به العلم وعلة العالمية أعني كونه عالماً هو العلم، وهذا لا يختلف شاهداً وغائباً بخلاف ما ليس من الوجوه (۱) التي توجب كون العالم عالماً كالعرضية والحدوث ونحو ذلك . الثاني : أنه لا يعقل من العالم إلا من له العلم، ومن المعلوم إلا ما تعلق به العلم فبالضرورة إذا كان عالماً وكان له معلوم كان له علم، فإن قيل : علمه ذاته قلنا فلا يفيد حمله على الذات ولا تتميز الصفات ولا يفتقر إلى الإثبات ، ويكون العلم مثلاً واجباً معبوداً صانعاً للعالم موصوفاً بالكمالات فإن قيل يكفي تغاير المفهوم كما في سائر المحمولات قلنا ليس الكلام في مثل العالم والقادر والحي بل في العلم والقدرة والحياة. فإن قيل ذاته من حيث التعلق بالمعلومات عالم بلا علم وبالمقدورات قادر بلا قدرة، كالواحد نصف الاثنين وثلث الثلاثة وهكذا مع أن الموجود واحد لا غير قلنا معلوم قطعاً أن الذات لا تكون علماً وقدرة بل عالماً وقادراً. ويبقى الكلام في المعنى الذي هو مأخذ الاشتقاق ولا يفيدك تسميته بالتعلق للقطع بأنه من الصفات الحقيقية لا الاعتبارات العقلية (٢) . الثالث : قوله تعالى أنزله بعلمه ) (۳)، (أنما أنزل بعلم الله ) (٤)، ذي القوة المتين (ه)، أن القوة الله (٦) ) .
The refutation of the philosophers and the Mu'tazilites regarding the non-addition of attributes. We have arguments: The first: that the definition of the knowing is one to whom knowledge has occurred, and the cause of being knowing, i.e., His being knowing, is knowledge, and this does not differ between the witnessed and the unseen, unlike what is not among the considerations that necessitate the knowing being knowing, such as accidentalness and origination and the like. The second: that nothing is rationally understood as the knowing except one who has knowledge, and nothing is known except that to which knowledge is attached; so necessarily, if He is knowing and has a known, He has knowledge. If it is said: His knowledge is His essence, we say: then applying it to the essence is not beneficial, nor are the attributes distinguished, nor does it need proof, and knowledge would be, for example, a necessary existent, worshipped, creator of the world, described with perfections. If it is said: the diversity of concept suffices as in other predicates, we say: the discussion is not about such as the knowing, the powerful, and the living, but about knowledge, power, and life. If it is said: His essence in relation to known things is knowing without knowledge, and in relation to willed things is powerful without power, like the one is half of two and a third of three and so on, while the existent is one not otherwise, we say: it is known with certainty that the essence is not knowledge and power but knowing and powerful. And the discussion remains about the meaning which is the basis of derivation, and calling it relation does not benefit you, because it is known to be from the real attributes, not from the mental considerations. The third: His saying, may He be exalted: 'He sent it down with His knowledge' (4:166), 'It was only sent down with the knowledge of God' (11:14), 'Possessor of Strong Power' (51:58), 'Indeed power belongs entirely to God' (2:165).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of philosophers and Mu'tazilites who deny that divine attributes are additional to the essence. The author presents three proofs (wujuh). The first proof uses the definition of 'alim (knowing): one who has knowledge, and the cause of being 'alim is knowledge. This definition applies equally to witnessed and unseen, unlike accidental or originated causes. The second proof is more detailed: it is rationally impossible to conceive of a 'alim without knowledge, so if God is 'alim and has a known object, He must have knowledge. Then objections are addressed: (i) if knowledge is said to be the essence, the response is that this renders attribution meaningless, fails to distinguish attributes, and would imply knowledge is a necessary existent and creator, which is absurd; (ii) if conceptual distinction suffices, the response is that the debate is about the real attributes (knowledge, power, life) not the derived names (knowing, powerful, living); (iii) if the essence is 'alim by relation without an inherent attribute, like 'one is half of two', the response is that the essence is not itself knowledge or power, and the real attribute (masdar of derivation) is not a mere relation but a real attribute. The third proof cites Quranic verses that explicitly attribute knowledge and power to God as things He possesses, indicating real attributes.

Key Terms
العالم
العلم
القدرة
الاشتقاق
التعلق
الوجوه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That conceptual distinction between attributes and essence is sufficient to avoid multiplicity in God.
Correction: The author argues that the debate concerns the real attributes themselves, not just the concepts. Real attributes are additional to the essence.

Checkpoint
In the second proof, what is the author's response to the objection that conceptual diversity suffices?
A) Conceptual diversity is invalid in theology.
B) The discussion is about the names, not the meanings.
C) The discussion is about the real attributes, not the derived descriptions.
D) The objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
A knower necessarily has knowledge; objections based on identity, concept, or relation fail because real attributes are distinct from the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'Knowing', do you truly believe that His knowledge is a real attribute distinct from His essence, or do you unconsciously reduce it to a mere label? How would your worship change if you affirmed this truth wholeheartedly?
?
If you accepted that God's attributes are not additional to His essence, would your understanding of His perfection be diminished? What is at stake for you personally in affirming real attributes?
?
Consider a moment when you doubted God's knowledge of your specific situation. Was that doubt rooted in a misconception about the reality of His attribute of knowledge? How does this proof reinforce your trust in His complete awareness of you?

Third Proof: Quranic Verses for Additional Attributes
Third proof: scriptural evidence from Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses explicitly attribute knowledge and power to God as possessions, supporting the doctrine that attributes are additional to the essence.

Core Idea
Quranic phrases like 'with His knowledge' show that knowledge is something God possesses, not identical to Him.

The third proof is scriptural. Sa‘d al-Din al-Taftazani cites four Quranic verses that explicitly mention knowledge and power as something God has. These verses are clear evidence for the Ash‘ari position that attributes are additional to the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the cited verses
Read each verse in Arabic and English translation.
Notice the preposition 'bi-' meaning 'with' or 'by'.
2
Analyze the implication
The verses speak of knowledge and power as something God possesses or uses, implying they are not identical to His essence.
For example, 'He sent it down with His knowledge' implies knowledge is separate.
3
Understand the proof
The author uses these verses as evidence that the attributes are additional, as they are linguistically treated as distinct.

Source Text

Objection to the First Argument and Its Answer
Objection and answer on causality in divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection against reasoning about divine attributes through causality and how the answer defends the validity of analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
Necessity does not prevent causation; the cause is considered in its essence.

The strongest objection against using causal reasoning about God is that His attributes are necessary, not contingent like ours. If something is necessary, it cannot be caused, so how can we apply the same causal logic? Sanusi presents this objection and then gives a precise answer that saves the analogical method.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
Understand the claim that necessity prevents causation in the absent (God) and that analogy requires similarity, which is lacking due to differences like pre-eternity and origination.
The objection hinges on the condition of similarity for analogical reasoning.
2
Identify the conditions of analogy
Notice the condition that two things must be similar for analogy to work, but God's attributes differ in eternality and contingency, so the objection claims the transfer is invalid.
3
Examine the answer
See how the reply distinguishes between the cause being considered in its essential nature (e.g., knowledge as knowledge) versus its accidental properties (contingent or originated).
The answer states that necessity does not contradict causation; a necessary effect has a necessary cause.
4
Apply the principle
Consider how this defense preserves the validity of transferring judgments from witnessed to absent, focusing on the essence of the cause rather than its contingent or necessary status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم يتوجه ما قيل : إن هذه الأحكام إنما تعلل في الشاهد لجوازها، فلا تعلل في الغائب لوجوبها. وإن من شرط القياس أن يتماثل أمران فيثبت لأحدهما مثل ما يثبت للآخر، وهذه الأحكام مختلفة غائباً وشاهداً بالقدم والحدوث والشمول واللاشمول وغير ذلك .
وكذا الصفات التي أثبتوها عللاً لها
وأجيب : بأن الوجوب لا ينافي التعليل، غايته أنه لا يعلل إلا بالواجب والجائز يعلل بالجائز، وأنه لا اختلاف لهذه الأحكام، ولا للصفات فيما يتعلق بالمقصود. فإن العلم إنما يوجب كون العالم عالماً من حيث كونه علماً لا من حيث كونه عرضاً أو حادثاً أو نحو ذلك .
Indeed, what was said may be directed: that these judgments are only caused in the witnessed due to their contingency, so they are not caused in the absent due to their necessity. And from the conditions of analogy is that two things be similar, so that for one is established what is established for the other, and these judgments differ in the absent and the witnessed in terms of pre-eternity and origination, universality and non-universality, and other things. Likewise the attributes which they established as causes for them.
And it was answered: that necessity does not contradict causation; its extent is that it is not caused except by the necessary, and the contingent is caused by the contingent. And that there is no difference in these judgments, nor in the attributes, concerning what is intended. For knowledge only necessitates the knower being knowing from the aspect that it is knowledge, not from the aspect that it is an accident or originated or the like.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (i'tirāḍ) to the first argument and its answer. The objection claims that the judgments (like being knowing) are caused in the witnessed only because they are contingent, but in the absent (God) they are necessary, so causation does not apply. Moreover, analogy requires similarity, but the attributes in God are eternal and universal while in creatures they are temporal and limited. The answer (jīb) replies that necessity does not preclude causation; rather, a necessary judgment is caused by a necessary cause, and a contingent judgment by a contingent cause. The attributes themselves, like knowledge, are considered in their essence (from the aspect of being knowledge) not in their accidental properties (like being contingent or originated). Therefore, the transfer of the judgment is valid because the core causal relationship (knowledge causing being knowing) is the same.

Key Terms
تعليل (ta'līl)
وجوب (wujūb)
جواز (jawāz)
قياس (qiyās)
اختلاف (ikhtilāf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an attribute is necessary in God, it cannot be caused at all.
Correction: The answer clarifies that necessity does not negate causation; a necessary attribute has a necessary cause, and the cause is considered in its essential nature, not its contingent or necessary status.

Checkpoint
What is the crux of the answer to the objection that judgments in the absent cannot be caused because they are necessary?
A) Necessity contradicts causation entirely
B) A necessary judgment is caused by a necessary cause, and the cause (e.g., knowledge) is considered from its essential aspect
C) The judgments in the absent are not really caused
D) The objection is invalid because analogy does not require similarity

Key Takeaway
The answer shows that necessity does not negate causation; the cause is considered in its essential nature, allowing analogical reasoning to divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it mean for you to think of God's mercy as mercy in its essence, independent of human limitations? Does this lesson make you feel accountable for how you imagine God?
?

How does the answer—that the core causal relationship remains the same—force you to bridge that gap and take your own attributes as serious signs of God?

God's Knowledge as a Real Attribute: The Initial Argument and Objection
Author presents syllogisms and an objection against attribute reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational basis for affirming that God has knowledge as a real attribute and the objection that it might be identical to His essence.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of Himself and all things implies He possesses real knowledge, not just His essence.

This lesson introduces the second argument for the reality of divine attributes. It begins with a straightforward syllogism: God is knowing, and every knower has knowledge. But an immediate objection arises: why can't His knowledge be the same as His essence? This sets up the need for further proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the clear syllogistic structure.
2
Identify the two syllogisms
The passage offers two formulations: one from 'God is knowing' and one from 'God is known.' Find both and note their premises.
The second formulation uses the fact that God is known (ma'lūm) to infer knowledge ('ilm).
3
Understand the objection
The objection grants that God has knowledge, but questions why it must be additional to the essence. This is the identity thesis.
This objection is the standard position of those who deny real attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن الله تعالى عالم ، وكل عالم فله علم . إذ لا يعقل من العالم إلا ذلك، وكذا القادر وغيره.
وتقرير آخر أن الله تعالى معلوماً وكل من له معلوم فله علم، إذ لا معنى للمعلوم إلا ما تعلق به العلم . فإن قيل : سلمنا أن له علماً ، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون علمه نفس ذاته لا زايداً عليه. وكذا سائر الصفات؟
قلنا : لأنه يلزم منه محالات أحدها أن لا يكون حمل تلك الصفات على الذات مفيدة بمنزلة قولنا : الإنسان بشر والذات ذات والعالم عالم ، والعلم علم .
وثانيها : أن يكون العلم هو القدرة، والقدرة هي الحياة، وكذا البواقي من غير تمايز أصلاً، لأنها كلها نفس الذات، فينتظم قياس هكذا. العلم هو الذات، والذات هي القدرة لأن القدرة إذا كانت نفس الذات كانت الذات نفس القدرة ضرورة.
وثالثها : أن يجزم العقل بكون الواجب عالماً قادراً، حياً سميعاً بصيراً من غير افتقار إلى إثبات ذلك بالبرهان لأن كون الشيء نفسه ضروري. ورابعها : أن يكون العلم مثلاً واجب الوجود لذاته، قائماً بنفسه، صانعاً للعالم، معبوداً للعباد، حياً قادراً، سميعاً، بصيراً إلى غير ذلك من الكمالات،
The second point: that God is knowing, and every knower has knowledge. For nothing else is understood from 'knower' except that. Likewise the powerful and others. And another formulation: that God is known, and everyone who has something known has knowledge, for there is no meaning of 'known' except that to which knowledge is related. If it is said: we grant that He has knowledge, but why may it not be that His knowledge is the same as His essence, not additional to it? And likewise the rest of the attributes?
We say: because it entails impossibilities. One of them: that the attribution of those attributes to the essence would not be informative, analogous to our saying 'man is human,' 'essence is essence,' 'knower is knowing,' and 'knowledge is knowledge.'
Second: that knowledge would be power, and power would be life, and the rest without any distinction, because they are all the same essence, so a syllogism would be formed: knowledge is the essence, and the essence is power, because if power is the same as essence, then the essence is necessarily the same as power.
Third: that the intellect would conclusively judge that the Necessary is knowing, powerful, living, hearing, seeing without needing to prove that by demonstration, because a thing being itself is self-evident.
Fourth: that knowledge, for example, would be necessary of existence by itself, subsisting by itself, maker of the world, worshipped by servants, living, powerful, hearing, seeing, and so on of perfections.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thānī) for the reality of attributes distinct from the essence. It begins with a simple syllogism: God is knowing; every knower has knowledge; therefore God has knowledge. A similar argument uses the fact that God is known (ma'lūm), which entails knowledge. Then an objection is raised: why cannot His knowledge be identical to His essence (qāla: 'sallamnā anna lahu 'ilman, lakin limā lā yajūzu an yakūna 'ilmuhu nafsa dhātihi lā zā'idan 'alayhi?'). The answer lists four impossibilities (muḥālāt) that would follow: (1) Tautology – saying 'God is knowing' would be like 'man is human,' uninformative. (2) Collapse of distinctions – knowledge, power, life would all be identical, leading to contradictions. (3) Self-evident knowledge – no need for proof, contradicting the need for demonstration. (4) The attribute itself would be God – knowledge would be necessary, creator, worshipped, which is absurd. These four arguments refute the position that attributes are identical to the essence and support the Ash'ari view that attributes are real and distinct from the essence.

Key Terms
ذات (dhāt)
علم ('ilm)
عالم ('ālim)
معلوم (ma'lūm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second formulation's premise for proving God has knowledge?
A) God is knowing.
B) God is known.
C) Every knower has knowledge.
D) Attributes are identical to essence.

Key Takeaway
The syllogisms establish that God must have knowledge, but it remains to be shown whether that knowledge is identical to His essence or distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is truly real and distinct from His essence, does that make Him more personal or more distant? How does your understanding of God's knowledge affect the way you live each day?
?
You claim that God knows everything about you, including your secret thoughts. Is that a comforting truth or a terrifying one? What would change in your life if you acted as if God's knowledge of you were as real as your own self-awareness?
?
The objection suggests that maybe God's knowledge is exactly His essence. If that were so, would you feel less accountable for your actions? How does the reality of God's knowledge (whether identical or additional) demand a response from you today?

Four Impossibilities if Attributes Are Identical to the Essence
Author lists four absurdities to refute identity of attributes with essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical absurdities that follow from claiming God's attributes are identical to His essence.

Core Idea
If knowledge were the essence, it would lead to tautology, confusion of attributes, unnecessary proof, and attributing divinity to an attribute.

This lesson presents the refutation of the identity thesis. The author lists four impossibilities (muḥālāt) that would arise if attributes were identical to the essence. These arguments demonstrate why the Ash'ari position of real, distinct attributes is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic list of four impossibilities and the translation. Note the logical structure of each.
2
Analyze each impossibility
Go through each of the four points. Understand why each is absurd and how it refutes the identity claim.
The first point is about tautology; the second about collapse of distinctions; the third about self-evidence; the fourth about making an attribute into God.
3
Connect to the objection
Recall that the objection claimed knowledge could be identical to essence. These impossibilities show why that cannot be.
The fourth impossibility is particularly striking: an attribute would become the creator and worshipped.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن الله تعالى عالم ، وكل عالم فله علم . إذ لا يعقل من العالم إلا ذلك، وكذا القادر وغيره.
وتقرير آخر أن الله تعالى معلوماً وكل من له معلوم فله علم، إذ لا معنى للمعلوم إلا ما تعلق به العلم . فإن قيل : سلمنا أن له علماً ، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون علمه نفس ذاته لا زايداً عليه. وكذا سائر الصفات؟
قلنا : لأنه يلزم منه محالات أحدها أن لا يكون حمل تلك الصفات على الذات مفيدة بمنزلة قولنا : الإنسان بشر والذات ذات والعالم عالم ، والعلم علم .
وثانيها : أن يكون العلم هو القدرة، والقدرة هي الحياة، وكذا البواقي من غير تمايز أصلاً، لأنها كلها نفس الذات، فينتظم قياس هكذا. العلم هو الذات، والذات هي القدرة لأن القدرة إذا كانت نفس الذات كانت الذات نفس القدرة ضرورة.
وثالثها : أن يجزم العقل بكون الواجب عالماً قادراً، حياً سميعاً بصيراً من غير افتقار إلى إثبات ذلك بالبرهان لأن كون الشيء نفسه ضروري. ورابعها : أن يكون العلم مثلاً واجب الوجود لذاته، قائماً بنفسه، صانعاً للعالم، معبوداً للعباد، حياً قادراً، سميعاً، بصيراً إلى غير ذلك من الكمالات،
The second point: that God is knowing, and every knower has knowledge. For nothing else is understood from 'knower' except that. Likewise the powerful and others. And another formulation: that God is known, and everyone who has something known has knowledge, for there is no meaning of 'known' except that to which knowledge is related. If it is said: we grant that He has knowledge, but why may it not be that His knowledge is the same as His essence, not additional to it? And likewise the rest of the attributes?
We say: because it entails impossibilities. One of them: that the attribution of those attributes to the essence would not be informative, analogous to our saying 'man is human,' 'essence is essence,' 'knower is knowing,' and 'knowledge is knowledge.'
Second: that knowledge would be power, and power would be life, and the rest without any distinction, because they are all the same essence, so a syllogism would be formed: knowledge is the essence, and the essence is power, because if power is the same as essence, then the essence is necessarily the same as power.
Third: that the intellect would conclusively judge that the Necessary is knowing, powerful, living, hearing, seeing without needing to prove that by demonstration, because a thing being itself is self-evident.
Fourth: that knowledge, for example, would be necessary of existence by itself, subsisting by itself, maker of the world, worshipped by servants, living, powerful, hearing, seeing, and so on of perfections.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thānī) for the reality of attributes distinct from the essence. It begins with a simple syllogism: God is knowing; every knower has knowledge; therefore God has knowledge. A similar argument uses the fact that God is known (ma'lūm), which entails knowledge. Then an objection is raised: why cannot His knowledge be identical to His essence (qāla: 'sallamnā anna lahu 'ilman, lakin limā lā yajūzu an yakūna 'ilmuhu nafsa dhātihi lā zā'idan 'alayhi?'). The answer lists four impossibilities (muḥālāt) that would follow: (1) Tautology – saying 'God is knowing' would be like 'man is human,' uninformative. (2) Collapse of distinctions – knowledge, power, life would all be identical, leading to contradictions. (3) Self-evident knowledge – no need for proof, contradicting the need for demonstration. (4) The attribute itself would be God – knowledge would be necessary, creator, worshipped, which is absurd. These four arguments refute the position that attributes are identical to the essence and support the Ash'ari view that attributes are real and distinct from the essence.

صفات (ṣifāt)
ذات (dhāt)
علم ('ilm)
قدرة (qudra)
حياة (ḥayāh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if attributes are not identical to essence, they are separate entities that could exist independently.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that attributes are real and distinct in concept but are not separate existents; they subsist in the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the second impossibility, if attributes are identical to the essence, what would happen?
A) Only knowledge exists.
B) Attributes would be distinct.
C) All attributes would be identical to each other.
D) The intellect would need no proof.

Key Takeaway
The four impossibilities demonstrate that identity of attributes with essence leads to logical contradictions and absurdities, supporting the Ash'ari view of real, distinct attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah knows everything,' do you truly see His knowledge as a real attribute distinct from His essence, or do you subtly reduce it to a mere synonym for His being? How would this shift in understanding affect your reliance on His knowledge in your daily life?
?
If the attributes were just the essence, then naming them would be empty. What does that imply about the way you invoke God's names like Al-Rahman, Al-Qadir? Is your worship grounded in the reality of these distinct attributes, or in abstract concepts?
?
The fourth impossibility shows that identifying attributes with essence would make an attribute like knowledge the Creator. Search your heart: do you ever treat any of God's attributes—such as His mercy or justice—as if they were independent, almost like a separate force, instead of recognizing them as dependent on His essence? How would acknowledging their dependence change your awe of Him?
