The Second Proof: Why God's Attributes Must Be Additional to His Essence
Al-Taftazani presents a second proof and answers the objection that attributes are the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Taftazani's second proof that God's attributes are not identical to His essence, and the four impossibilities that would follow if they were.

Core Idea
If God's attributes were His essence, absurd consequences would follow—thus they must be distinct.

Al-Taftazani presents a second argument for the distinctness of divine attributes. After establishing that God is described as 'knower' and 'powerful', he argues that these descriptions imply distinct attributes. He then addresses the objection that attributes might be identical to the essence by showing four devastating consequences. This lesson walks through the proof and the four impossibilities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof from 'knower' and 'known'
Read the opening lines where Al-Taftazani argues that every knower has knowledge, and similarly for other attributes. Also note the alternative formulation using 'known'.
This sets up the need for distinct attributes.
2
Note the objection
Identify the objection: why can't knowledge be the very essence? This is a natural question after the proof.
3
Study the four impossibilities
Work through each of the four consequences Al-Taftazani lists as impossibilities. Understand why each is absurd.
They are: tautology, collapse of attributes, self-evidence, and the attribute becoming a necessary being.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this argument is important for tawhid. It shows that affirming distinct attributes does not compromise God's unity.
The four impossibilities guard against pantheistic or reductionist views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن الله تعالى عالم ، وكل عالم فله علم . إذ لا يعقل من العالم إلا ذلك، وكذا القادر وغيره. وتقرير آخر أن الله تعالى معلوماً وكل من له معلوم فله علم، إذ لا معنى للمعلوم إلا ما تعلق به العلم . فإن قيل : سلمنا أن له علماً ، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون علمه نفس ذاته لا زايداً عليه. وكذا سائر الصفات؟ قلنا : لأنه يلزم منه محالات أحدها أن لا يكون حمل تلك الصفات على الذات مفيدة بمنزلة قولنا : الإنسان بشر والذات ذات والعالم عالم ، والعلم علم . وثانيها : أن يكون العلم هو القدرة، والقدرة هي الحياة، وكذا البواقي من غير تمايز أصلاً، لأنها كلها نفس الذات، فينتظم قياس هكذا. العلم هو الذات، والذات هي القدرة لأن القدرة إذا كانت نفس الذات كانت الذات نفس القدرة ضرورة. وثالثها : أن يجزم العقل بكون الواجب عالماً قادراً، حياً سميعاً بصيراً من غير افتقار إلى إثبات ذلك بالبرهان لأن كون الشيء نفسه ضروري. ورابعها : أن يكون العلم مثلاً واجب الوجود لذاته، قائماً بنفسه، صانعاً للعالم، معبوداً للعباد، حياً قادراً، سميعاً، بصيراً إلى غير ذلك من الكمالات، وليس كذلك، وفاقاً، حين صرح الكعبي بأن من زعم أن علم الله يعبد فهو كافر.
The second aspect: that God Most High is knower (ʿālim), and every knower has knowledge (ʿilm). For nothing else is understood from 'knower' except that. Similarly for 'powerful' (qādir) and others. And another formulation: that God Most High is known (maʿlūm), and everyone who has a known thing has knowledge, for there is no meaning of 'known' except that to which knowledge is attached. If it is said: we grant that He has knowledge, but why is it not permissible that His knowledge be His very essence (dhāt) without being additional to it? And similarly for the other attributes? We say: because it entails impossibilities (muḥālāt). One of them is that the attribution of those attributes to the essence would not be informative (mufīda), like our saying 'man is human' and 'essence is essence' and 'knower is knower' and 'knowledge is knowledge'. And the second: that knowledge would be power (qudra), and power would be life (ḥayāh), and similarly the rest with no distinction at all, because they are all the very essence, so a syllogism would follow like this: knowledge is the essence, and the essence is power, because if power were the very essence, then the essence would necessarily be the very power. And the third: that the intellect would be certain that the Necessary One is knowing, powerful, living, hearing, seeing without needing to prove that by demonstration, because a thing being itself is necessary. And the fourth: that knowledge, for example, would be necessary of existence by itself, subsistent by itself, maker of the world, worshipped by servants, living, powerful, hearing, seeing, and so on of perfections—and it is not so, by agreement, when al-Kaʿbī declared that whoever claims that God's knowledge is worshipped is an unbeliever.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents a proof and then answers an objection about the divine attributes being identical to the essence. The proof is the 'second aspect' (al-wajh al-thānī): God is described as 'knower', and every knower must have knowledge (ʿilm) distinct from the knower. Similarly for other attributes like power (qudra) and life (ḥayāh). A parallel formulation uses the fact that God is 'known' (maʿlūm), which implies knowledge attached to Him. Then an objection is raised: granted that God has knowledge, why must it be additional? Could it be identical to the essence? The answer is that four impossibilities (muḥālāt) follow from identity. First: the attribution would be uninformative—saying 'God is knowing' would be like saying 'the essence is essence' or 'knowledge is knowledge' (i.e., a tautology). Second: all attributes would collapse into one another—knowledge would be power, power would be life, etc., because each would be the essence itself, causing a circle of identity. Third: it would make knowledge of God's attributes self-evident without need for proof, since the attribute would be the thing itself. Fourth: the attribute itself would become an independent necessary being (wājib al-wujūd), worthy of worship—a clear absurdity, as al-Kaʿbī and others agree. Thus the attributes must be additional to the essence.

Misconception: God's attributes are identical to His essence (no distinction).
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that this leads to absurd consequences; attributes must be additional.

A) Attributes would be uninformative.
B) Attributes would be indistinguishable from each other.
C) The attribute would itself be a necessary being worthy of worship.
D) The intellect would be certain of attributes without proof.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani's proof shows that divine attributes must be distinct from the essence, otherwise absurdities like the attribute becoming a separate deity would follow.

?
The fourth impossibility shows that if an attribute were identical to essence, it would become an object of worship. Have you ever, even unconsciously, shifted your devotion from God alone to a concept like 'His mercy' or 'His power'? What step will you take this week to purify your worship?

Refuting the Objection: Why Conceptual Distinction is Not Enough
Al-Taftazani refutes the objection that conceptual distinction avoids absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conceptual distinction between essence and attributes cannot avoid the absurdities that follow from identifying attributes with the essence, due to the linguistic derivation (ishtiqāq) of attribute terms.

Core Idea
Conceptual distinction fails because attribute terms are derived, not merely agreed upon.

This passage addresses a common objection: if we only make a conceptual distinction between attributes and essence, why can't we avoid the absurdities? Al-Taftazani answers by distinguishing between terms attributed by agreement (muwāṭaʾah) and those derived (ishtiqāq), showing that the latter require real distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'قلنا') which presents the opponent's view: that conceptual distinction suffices to avoid impossibilities, just as a human can be called 'writer' and 'laugher' without those being distinct real parts.
The opponent uses human examples to argue that conceptual difference does not entail real addition.
2
Understand the opponent's analogy
Notice that the opponent compares divine attributes to human adjectives like 'writer' (kātib) and 'laugher' (ḍāḥik). In humans, these terms refer to the same essence without requiring separate existent attributes. The opponent claims the same could hold for God.
The key is whether the human examples are truly analogous to the divine case.
3
Notice Al-Taftazani's key distinction
Al-Taftazani responds by distinguishing between two modes of predication: muwāṭaʾah (agreement) and ishtiqāq (derivation). He says the discussion is about terms like 'ilm (knowledge) and qudra (power), which are derived from the same root as the essence, not about adjectives like 'ālim (knower) which are agreed upon.
'Muwāṭaʾah' means the adjective is predicated on the subject in a straightforward, underived manner. 'Ishtiqāq' means the term is morphologically derived from a root that also gives the essence.
4
Grasp why derivation forces real distinction
If knowledge ('ilm) were identical with the essence, then saying 'God is knowing' would be a tautology, and there would be no distinction between knowledge and power. The derivation implies a real dependence that cannot be reduced to mere conceptual difference.
Al-Taftazani concludes that for derived terms, the impossibilities (like attributes being identical with each other or the essence) necessarily follow if they are not really distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : يكفي في عدم لزوم هذه المحالات، كون المفهوم من الذات، غير المفهوم من الصفات، وكون المفهوم من كل صفة مغايراً للمفهوم من الأخرى، وهذا لا نزاع فيه، ولا يستلزم الزيادة بحسب الوجود كما هو المطلوب. ألا نرى أن حمل مثل الكاتب والضاحك والعالم، والقادر على الإنسان يفيد، وربما يحتاج إلى البيان مع اتحاد الذات وعدم لزوم كون الكتابة هي الضحك، والضاحك هو الناطق . قلنا : ليس الكلام في العالم والقادر والحي ونحو ذلك، مما يحمل على الذات بالمواطأة بل في العلم والقدرة والحياة ونحوها مما لا يحمل إلا بالاشتقاق، فإنها إذا كانت نفس الذات كان لزوم المحلات المذكورة ظاهراً.
If it is said: It suffices to avoid these impossibilities that the concept (mafhūm) of the essence is different from the concept of the attributes, and that the concept of each attribute is different from the concept of the other—and there is no dispute about this—and it does not entail addition (ziyādah) in existence, as is desired. Do you not see that the attribution of such things as 'writer' (kātib), 'laugher' (ḍāḥik), 'knower' (ʿālim), and 'powerful' (qādir) to a human is informative, and perhaps needs explanation, despite the unity of the essence and the absence of necessitating that writing is laughing, and that the laugher is the speaker? We say: The discussion is not about 'knower' (ʿālim), 'powerful' (qādir), 'living' (ḥayy) and the like, which are attributed to the essence by agreement (muwāṭaʾah), but about 'knowledge' (ʿilm), 'power' (qudra), 'life' (ḥayāh) and similar terms, which are only attributed by derivation (ishtiqāq). For if these were the very essence, the aforementioned impossibilities would necessarily follow, obviously.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage contains a further objection and its answer. The objector says that the previous impossibilities (from Lesson 1) do not follow if we simply make a conceptual distinction: the concept of the essence is different from the concept of each attribute, and attributes are conceptually distinct from each other. This conceptual difference does not require any real addition in existence—just as a human can be called 'writer', 'laugher', 'knower', 'powerful' without those being distinct real parts. The objector argues that just as those human attributes are not separate existents, divine attributes could be conceptually distinct while identical in existence. The author (Al-Taftazani) answers by drawing a crucial distinction: he says the discussion is not about terms like 'knower' (ʿālim), 'powerful' (qādir), 'living' (ḥayy), which are attributed to the essence by 'muwāṭaʾah' (agreement, i.e., they are adjectives agreed upon with the subject). Rather, it is about the substantives 'knowledge' (ʿilm), 'power' (qudra), 'life' (ḥayāh), which are derived (ishtiqāq) from the same roots. The human examples (writer, laugher) are not derived in the same way; they are simple attributions. For the derived terms, if they were identical with the essence, the absurdities (like being the same as each other or the essence) would follow immediately. So the conceptual distinction does not solve the problem because the linguistic derivation shows a real dependence that requires distinction in existence.

مفهوم
مواطأة
اشتقاق
عالم
قادر
حي
علم
قدرة
حياة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that conceptual distinction between essence and attributes is sufficient to avoid the absurdities, just as a human can have multiple predicates that are conceptually distinct yet identical in existence.
Correction: The passage explains that for derived attribute terms like 'ilm (knowledge) and qudra (power), conceptual distinction is not enough because derivation implies real dependence. The human analogy fails because human adjectives like 'writer' are not derived from a root that names the essence in the same way.

Checkpoint
What is the key reason why conceptual distinction alone does not avoid the impossibilities?
A) Because the attributes are applied by muwāṭaʾah, not ishtiqāq.
B) Because the attributes are derived (ishtiqāq) from the same root, so identity would collapse them.
C) Because the conceptual distinction is not real.
D) Because the attributes are multiple in essence.

Key Takeaway
Conceptual distinction between attributes and essence is insufficient for derived attribute terms (ishtiqāq) like 'ilm and qudra, because their derivation implies a real dependence that would lead to absurdities if they were identical with the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing' (ʿAlīm), do you genuinely affirm that His knowledge is a real attribute distinct from His power, or do you treat it as a mere concept? How does that affect your awe and reliance on Him? This week, pause before reciting His Names and ask yourself: 'Is this Name a real attribute I am addressing, or just a label?'
?
The objection in this passage suggests conceptual distinction is enough. In your own faith, do you sometimes reduce Allah's attributes to pleasant ideas without letting them transform your actions? What would change if you took the reality of His knowledge and power more seriously—would you fear sin more, hope more, or trust more? Commit to one specific change this week based on the reality of one attribute.
?
The distinction between muwāṭaʾah and ishtiqāq seems technical, but it asks whether your worship engages with the real attributes of God. Imagine you discovered that one of Allah's attributes you recite is merely a linguistic convention—would your devotion weaken? How does affirming the real, derived nature of attributes like ʿilm strengthen your conviction that knowing Allah is possible?

Objection and Refutation: Muwata'ah vs Ishtiqaq
Taftazani presents an objection and refutes it by distinguishing muwata'ah from ishtiqaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between terms applied by common usage (muwata'ah) and those applied by derivation (ishtiqaq) in the context of divine attributes, and why this distinction refutes an objection against the real distinction of attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes like 'knowledge' are not the Essence itself; they are real meanings derived from it.

In this passage, Taftazani addresses an objection that conceptual distinction suffices, making a real distinction unnecessary. He refutes it by distinguishing between terms applied by common usage (like 'knowing') and those applied by derivation (like 'knowledge'). Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping why divine attributes are not identical to the Essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that claims conceptual difference is enough, so no real increase in existence is needed. Note the example of human attributes like 'writer' and 'laugher'.
The objector uses human analogy to argue against real distinction.
2
Identify the refutation's key move
The author replies by stating the discussion is about terms like 'knowledge' (ishtiqaq), not 'knowing' (muwata'ah). Understand why this distinction matters.
If 'knowledge' were the Essence itself, impossibilities would follow.
3
Connect to the broader doctrine
Reflect on how this refutation supports the Ash'ari position that attributes are real and distinct from the Essence without being separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس كذلك ،وفاقاً، حين صرح الكعبي بأن من زعم أن علم الله يعبد فهو كافر.

فإن قيل : يكفي في عدم لزوم هذه المحالات، كون المفهوم من الذات، غير المفهوم من الصفات، وكون المفهوم من كل صفة مغايراً للمفهوم من الأخرى، وهذا لانزاع فيه، ولا يستلزم الزيادة بحسب الوجود كما هو المطلوب.

ألا نرى أن حمل مثل الكاتب والضاحك والعالم، والقادر على الإنسان يفيد، وربما يحتاج إلى البيان مع اتحاد الذات وعدم لزوم كون الكتابة وهو الضحك ، والضاحك والناطق .

قلنا : ليس الكلام في العالم والقادر والحي ونحو ذلك، مما يحمل على الذات بالمواطأة بل في العلم والقدرة والحياة ونحوها مما لا يحمل إلا بالاشتقاق، فإنها إذا كانت نفس الذات كان لزوم المحلات المذكورة ظاهراً .
And it is not so, by agreement, when al-Ka'bi declared that whoever claims that God's knowledge is served is a disbeliever.

If it is said: It suffices for the non-necessitation of these impossibilities that the concept from the Essence is different from the concept from the attributes, and the concept from each attribute is different from the concept from the other, and there is no dispute about this, and it does not necessitate increase in existence as is required.

Do we not see that applying such as 'writer', 'laugher', 'knowing', and 'capable' to a human is informative, and perhaps needs explanation despite the unity of the essence and the non-necessity of writing being laughing, and the laugher being the speaker.

We say: The discussion is not about 'knowing', 'capable', 'living' and the like, which are applied to the Essence by common usage (al-muwata'ah), but rather about 'knowledge', 'power', 'life' and the like, which are only applied by derivation (al-ishtiqaq). For if they were the Essence itself, the necessitation of the mentioned impossibilities would be apparent.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. It begins with the author citing al-Ka'bi's statement that someone who considers God's knowledge an object of worship is a disbeliever, implying that knowledge is not God Himself. Then an objection is raised: it is enough to avoid the impossible consequences that the concepts of essence and attributes are distinct, and that this conceptual distinction does not imply real increase in existence. The objector gives the example of human attributes like 'writer' and 'laugher' which are informative despite the unity of the human essence. The author replies that the discussion is not about terms applied by common usage (muwata'ah) like 'knowing', but about the sources of derivation (ishtiqaq) like 'knowledge'. If those were the Essence itself, the impossibilities would arise.

Key Terms
الذات
الصفات
المواطأة
الاشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all attribute terms (like 'knowing' and 'knowledge') are applied in the same way, so a conceptual distinction is enough.
Correction: The author explains that 'knowing' is applied by common usage (muwata'ah) and does not imply a separate meaning, while 'knowledge' is applied by derivation (ishtiqaq) and indicates a real meaning distinct from the Essence.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the author's refutation in response to the first objection?
A) That the concepts of essence and attributes are identical.
B) That the discussion is about derived terms like 'knowledge' not about terms applied by common usage like 'knowing'.
C) That attributes are mere mental considerations.
D) That the Mu'tazilites are correct.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between terms applied by common usage and terms applied by derivation is critical to defending the real distinction of divine attributes.

?
The objector tried to reduce divine attributes to mere conceptual distinctions. Where in your own life have you reduced Allah's attributes to comfortable concepts instead of acknowledging their reality? Name one attribute you will strive to take more seriously starting today.
?

Second Objection: Consideration and Its Refutation
Taftazani refutes the claim that attributes are distinct only by consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine attributes are distinct only by mental consideration and Taftazani's refutation establishing that attributes are real meanings beyond the essence.

Core Idea
Divine attributes cannot be mere mental considerations; they are real.

In this passage, Taftazani addresses the second objection against the reality of divine attributes. The objector argues that attributes like knowledge and power are merely relational considerations, not real entities distinct from the essence. Taftazani refutes this by showing that knowledge and power are 'real meanings' (ma'ani haqiqiyya) and not mere mental constructs, thereby upholding the orthodox position. This lesson unpacks the objection and its powerful refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the Arabic passage (starting with 'فإن قيل') and its translation. Notice the objector's claim that attributes are distinct only by consideration, not in existence.
Pay attention to the analogy of 'one being half, third, etc.'
2
Identify the objector's key analogy
The objector uses the example of a single entity (one) being half of two and third of three to argue that distinctions by consideration do not imply multiplicity. Understand why this analogy is appealing.
This analogy is central to the objection.
3
Read Taftazani's refutation
Now read the refutation (starting with 'قلنا'). Taftazani rejects the analogy by distinguishing between mental considerations and real meanings.
Notice the concepts of 'al-i'tibarat al-'aqliyya' (mental considerations) and 'al-ma'ani al-haqiqiyya' (real meanings).
4
Understand the refutation's logic
Taftazani argues that like knowledge and power are real meanings, not mere mental constructs. Therefore, they cannot be the essence itself or mere relations. They must be distinct realities beyond the essence.
This establishes the required conclusion that attributes are real and distinct.
5
Connect to broader kalam principles
Reflect on why this objection matters: if attributes were mere considerations, it would undermine the distinction between attributes and essence, a key doctrine in Sunni kalam.
This passage is a classic example of refuting relational reductionism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إنما يلزم ذلك لو لم تكن الذات مع الصفات، وكذا الصفات بعضها مع البعض، متغايرة بحسب الاعتبار، وإن كانت متحدة بحسب الوجود، وذلك بأن تكون الذات من حيث التعلق بالمعلومات عالماً بل علما، ومن حيث التعلق بالمقدورات قادراً بل قدرة، ومن حيث كونه بحيث يصح أن يعلم ويقدر حياً بل حياة وعلى هذا القياس؛ ويكون معنى الحمل، أن الذات متعلق بالمعلومات وبالمقدورات مثلاً، ولا خفاء فى إفادته، وافتقاره إلى البيان، ولا في تمايز الاعتبارات بعضها عن البعض من غير تكثر في الذات أصلاً بحسب الوجود، وهذا كما أن الواحد نصف الاثنين، ثلث للثلاثة ربع للأربعة وهكذا إلى غير النهاية، مع أن الموجود واحد لا غير، والحمل مفيد والنصفية متميزية عن الثلثية .

قلنا : كون الذات نفس التعلق الذي هو العلم والقدرة مثلاً ضروري البطلان ككون الواحد نفس النصفية والثلثية، وإنما هو عالم وقادر فيبقى الكلام في مأخذ الاشتقاق، أعني العلم والقدرة فإنه لا بد أن يكون معنى وراء الذات لا نفسه، ولا يفيدك تسميته بالتعلق، لأن مثل العلم والقدرة ليس من الاعتبارات العقلية التي لا تحقق لها في الأعيان بمنزلة الحدوث والإمكان بل من المعاني الحقيقية، فلا بد من القول بكونها نفس الذات فيعود المحذور أو وراء الذات فيثبت المطلوب .
If it is said: That would only be necessary if the Essence and the attributes, and likewise the attributes with each other, were not distinct by consideration (al-i'tibar), even if united in existence. Such that the Essence in terms of relation (al-ta'allaq) to objects of knowledge is 'knowing' without knowledge, and in terms of relation to objects of power is 'capable' without power, and in terms of being such that it is valid to know and be powerful is 'living' without life, and so on. And the meaning of predication is that the Essence is related to objects of knowledge and objects of power, etc., and there is no obscurity in its informativeness and need for explanation, nor in the distinction of considerations from each other without any multiplicity in the Essence in existence. This is like how 'one' is half of two, a third of three, a fourth of four, etc., while the existent is one and nothing else, and the predication is informative and halfness is distinct from thirdness.

We say: That the Essence is the same as the relation which is knowledge and power, for example, is necessarily false, like the one being the same as halfness and thirdness. Rather, He is knowing and capable. So the discussion remains about the source of derivation, i.e., knowledge and power. For they must be a meaning beyond the Essence, not itself. And calling it a 'relation' does not help you, because like knowledge and power are not among mental considerations (al-i'tibarat al-'aqliyya) that have no reality in concrete existence like 'origination' (al-huduth) and 'possibility' (al-imkan), but rather they are real meanings (al-ma'ani al-haqiqiyya). So one must either say they are the Essence itself, then the objection returns, or they are beyond the Essence, then the required is established.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its refutation. The objector argues that the attributes are distinct only by consideration (i'tibar), not in reality. The Essence is 'knowing' by relation to objects of knowledge without a distinct attribute of knowledge, similar to how 'one' is 'half' of two by relation, yet the one is not really half in itself. The distinction between considerations does not imply multiplicity in the Essence. The author refutes this by stating that the Essence cannot be the same as the relation, because the relation is an accident that depends on the two relata. Moreover, knowledge and power are not mere mental considerations like 'origination' which have no real existence; they are real meanings. Therefore, they must be either the Essence itself (impossible) or distinct realities beyond the Essence.

العلم

الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine attributes like knowledge and power are merely relational concepts or mental considerations with no independent reality.
Correction: Taftazani argues that knowledge and power are 'real meanings' (ma'ani haqiqiyya) that exist objectively, not mere mental constructs. Therefore, they must be distinct attributes beyond the essence.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the objector use to argue that attributes can be distinct by consideration without multiplicity?
A) The example of a writer and a laugher.
B) The example of one being half, third, etc.
C) The example of origination and possibility.
D) The example of knowledge and power.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani refutes the objection by showing that divine attributes are real meanings, not mere mental considerations, thus establishing their distinct reality beyond the essence.

?

Concluding Arguments for Real Attributes
Taftazani concludes arguments for real attributes beyond the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's concluding arguments that divine attributes are real, distinct meanings beyond the essence, and why the Mu'tazilite position is untenable.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are real, additional meanings, not mere names.

In this passage, Taftazani wraps up his case for real attributes. He uses the logic of derived names and the impossibility of deficiency to show that attributes like knowledge and power are necessary and distinct from God's essence. He then critiques the Mu'tazilite position as absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice that the passage has three main points: the derived names argument, the impossibility of attributes being the essence, and the Mu'tazilite critique.
2
Analyze the derived names argument
Focus on the first part: 'And also the description of knowingness...' Taftazani argues that derived names like 'knowing' imply a real source of derivation (knowledge). Without that, the name is meaningless.
3
Understand why attributes cannot be the essence
The passage states that if attributes were the essence, they would subsist by themselves, which is impossible for attributes (they need a subject). Also, being devoid of them would be a deficiency.
This directly refutes the idea that attributes are identical to the essence.
4
Review the Mu'tazilite critique
Taftazani criticizes the Mu'tazilites for accepting a 'knower without knowledge' – an absurdity.
This highlights the logical inconsistency in their position.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Taftazani concludes that attributes must be real meanings distinct from the essence. Ask yourself: why is this necessary for a proper understanding of God?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً وصف العالمية أو القادرية، وكذا المعلومية أو المقدورية إنما تتحقق بعد تمام التعلق . فعلى ما ذكر يكون كل من العلم والقدرة عبارة عن تعلق الذات بأمر فلا بد في التمايز من خصوصية، بها يكون أحد التعلقين علماً والآخر قدرة، وهو المراد بالمعنى الزائد على الذات والحاصل أنه لا نزاع في أن الله تعالى عالم قادر حي ونحو ذلك، وهذه الألفاظ ليست أسماء للذات، من غير اعتبار معنى، بل هي أسماء مشتقة معناها إثبات ما هو مأخذ الاشتقاق، ولا معنى سوى إدراك المعاني والتمكن من الفعل والترك، ونحو ذلك، فلزم بالضرورة ثبوت هذه المعاني للواجب.

كيف والخلو عنها نقص، وذهاب إلى أنه لا يعلم ولا يقدر. ثم هذه المعاني يمتنع أن تكون نفس الذات لامتناع قيامها بأنفسها ولما سبق من المحالات فتعين كونها معاني وراء الذات .

والمعتزلة مع ارتكابهم شناعة العالم بلا علم، والقادر بلا قدرة لا يرضون رأساً
And also the description of 'knowingness' (al-'alimiyya) or 'powerfulness' (al-qadiriyya), and likewise 'being known' (al-ma'lumiyya) or 'being powered' (al-maqduriyya), are only realized after the completion of the relation. So according to what was mentioned, each of knowledge and power is an expression of a relation of the Essence to something. So there must be in the distinction a particularity by which one relation is knowledge and the other is power. And that is what is meant by the meaning additional to the Essence. The conclusion is that there is no dispute that God is knowing, capable, living, etc. These words are not names for the Essence without considering a meaning, but they are derived names whose meaning is the affirmation of what is the source of derivation. And there is no meaning besides the perception of meanings and the ability to act and refrain, etc. So necessarily these meanings are established for the Necessary Being.

How? For being devoid of them is a deficiency, and a going to the extent that He does not know nor is capable. Then these meanings are impossible to be the Essence itself because of the impossibility of them subsisting by themselves and due to the previously mentioned impossibilities. So it is determined that they are meanings beyond the Essence.

And the Mu'tazilites, despite their committing the disgrace of a knower without knowledge and a capable without power, are not satisfied at all.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage provides additional independent arguments for the reality of divine attributes. First, it argues that the descriptions like 'knowingness' and 'powerfulness' are realized only after the relation is complete, implying that the relation presupposes the attribute, not the other way around. Then it states that the derived names (like 'knowing') necessarily affirm the source of derivation (knowledge, power) as real meanings. Without these meanings, God would be deficient, which is impossible. Furthermore, these meanings cannot be the Essence itself because they are accidental qualities that cannot subsist by themselves; they are attributes, not essences. Therefore, they must be real meanings distinct from the Essence. The passage ends with a criticism of the Mu'tazilites who accept a knower without knowledge, which is an absurdity.

Key Terms

القادرية
المعلومية
المقدورية
المعاني الحقيقية
الاشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Derived names like 'knowing' can be applied to God without affirming a real attribute of knowledge.
Correction: Derived names necessarily imply the existence of the source of derivation (knowledge) as a real meaning, otherwise the name is empty.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for the attributes to be the Essence itself?
A) Because they are mental considerations.
B) Because they would then be deficient.
C) Because attributes cannot subsist by themselves, and they are not essences.
D) Because the Mu'tazilites deny them.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes like knowledge and power are real meanings distinct from God's essence, necessary for avoiding deficiency and absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If yes, how does that belief affect the way you trust His knowledge of your every thought and action? For instance, when you face a difficulty, do you turn to the All-Powerful with real confidence that His power is a reality beyond a mere name? What practical step can you take today to live in that reality?

Divine Attributes

Rational Proof for Extra-Essential Attributes
Taftazani argues that divine attributes are extra-essential meanings, not mere relations or identical with essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's rational proof that divine attributes are real meanings distinct from the essence, and how he refutes the view that they are mere relations or identical with essence.

Core Idea
God's attributes are real, distinct meanings, not just mental considerations or relations.

Taftazani presents a sophisticated argument to establish that attributes like knowledge and power are extra-essential meanings. He uses an analogy and logical reasoning to show that they cannot be mere relations or identical with essence. This lesson unpacks his proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's argument sink in before interpreting.
Notice the dense style and multiple sub-arguments.
2
Understand the analogy of the number one
Taftazani compares the Mu'tazilite position to saying the number one is itself half-ness, third-ness, etc. Grasp why he considers this absurd.
This illustrates that multiple relations can be predicated without multiplicity in the essence.
3
Distinguish mental considerations from real meanings
He argues that attributes like knowledge and power are real meanings, not mere mental considerations like temporality. See why this distinction is crucial.
If they were mere mental considerations, they would have no external reality.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
If attributes were the essence itself, absurdities follow; if they are beyond the essence, the point is proven. Trace this logical step.
The conclusion is that attributes are meanings additional to the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعضها عن البعض من غير تكثر في الذات أصلاً بحسب الوجود، وهذا كما أن الواحد نصف الاثنين، ثلث للثلاثة ربع للأربعة وهكذا إلى غير النهاية، مع أن الموجود واحد لا غير، والحمل مفيد والنصفية متميزية عن الثلثية . قلنا : كون الذات نفس التعلق الذي هو العلم والقدرة مثلاً ضروري البطلان ككون الواحد نفس النصفية والثلثية، وإنما هو عالم وقادر فيبقى الكلام في مأخذ الاشتقاق، أعني العلم والقدرة فإنه لا بد أن يكون معنى وراء الذات لا نفسه، ولا يفيدك تسميته بالتعلق، لأن مثل العلم والقدرة ليس من الاعتبارات العقلية التي لا تحقق لها في الأعيان بمنزلة الحدوث والإمكان بل من المعاني الحقيقية، فلا بد من القول بكونها نفس الذات فيعود المحذور أو وراء الذات فيثبت المطلوب . وأيضاً وصف العالمية أو القادرية، وكذا المعلومية أو المقدورية إنما تتحقق بعد تمام التعلق . فعلى ما ذكر يكون كل من العلم والقدرة عبارة عن تعلق الذات بأمر فلا بد في التمايز من خصوصية بها يكون أحد التعلقين علماً والآخر قدرة، وهو المراد بالمعنى الزائد على الذات والحاصل أنه لا نزاع في أن الله تعالى عالم قادر حي ونحو ذلك، وهذه الألفاظ ليست أسماء للذات، من غير اعتبار معنى، بل هي أسماء مشتقة معناها إثبات ما هو مأخذ الاشتقاق، ولا معنى سوى إدراك المعاني والتمكن من الفعل والترك، ونحو ذلك ، فلزم بالضرورة ثبوت هذه المعاني للواجب. له كيف والخلو عنها نقص، وذهاب إلى أنه لا يعلم ولا يقدر. ثم هذه المعاني يمتنع أن تكون نفس الذات لامتناع قيامها بأنفسها ولما سبق من المحالات فتعين كونها معاني وراء الذات . والمعتزلة مع ارتكابهم شناعة العالم بلا علم، والقادر بلا قدرة لا يرضون رأساً برأس، بل يباهون بنفي الصفات ويعدون إثباتها من الجهالات .
Some of them from others without any multiplicity in the essence at all in terms of existence, like the one being half of two, third of three, quarter of four, and so on ad infinitum, while the existent is one and nothing else, and the predication is beneficial, and half-ness is distinct from third-ness. We said: That the essence itself is the relation which is knowledge and power, for example, is necessarily false, like the one being half-ness and third-ness itself. Rather, He is knowing and powerful. So the discussion remains about the basis of derivation (makhadh al-ishtiqaq), namely knowledge and power, for it must be a meaning beyond the essence, not itself. And calling it relation does not benefit you, because knowledge and power are not among mental considerations that have no reality in external things, like temporality (huduth) and contingency (imkan), but are among real meanings. So one must either say they are the essence itself, and the absurdity returns, or they are beyond the essence, and what is sought is established. Also, the description of being knowing or powerful, and likewise being known or being an object of power, are only realized after the completion of relation. So based on what was mentioned, each of knowledge and power is an expression of the relation of the essence to something, so differentiation must be by a particularity by which one of the two relations is knowledge and the other power. And that is what is meant by the meaning additional to the essence. The conclusion is that there is no dispute that God is knowing, powerful, living, and so on, and these words are not names of the essence without consideration of meaning, but are derived names whose meaning is the affirmation of what is the basis of derivation. And there is no meaning other than the perception of meanings and the ability to act and refrain, and so on. So necessarily these meanings are established for the Necessary. How so? For being devoid of them is deficiency and a departure to the view that He does not know and is not powerful. Then these meanings cannot be the essence itself because of the impossibility of them subsisting by themselves and because of the absurdities already mentioned. So it is determined that they are meanings beyond the essence. And the Mu'tazilites, despite committing the ugly notion of a knower without knowledge and a powerful without power, do not agree at all, but rather boast of negating the attributes and consider affirming them to be among the ignorances.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) for the doctrine that God's attributes (such as knowledge and power) are real, distinct meanings (ma'ani) additional to His essence, against the Mu'tazilite view that they are identical with the essence. The argument proceeds in several steps. First, the author likens the Mu'tazilite position to the absurdity of saying the number one is itself half-ness, third-ness, etc., while being a single existent. This illustrates that multiple relations can be predicated without multiplicity in the essence, but the attributes are not mere relations. Second, he states that if knowledge and power were the essence's relation to objects, then the essence itself would be the relation, which is false. Rather, the basis of derivation (makhadh al-ishtiqaq) — the actual knowledge and power — must be a meaning beyond the essence. Calling them 'relations' does not help because they are real entities, not mere mental considerations like temporality. Third, he argues that if they were the essence, the absurdity returns; if they are beyond, the point is proven. Fourth, he notes that descriptions like 'knowing' are only realized after the relation, so there must be a distinguishing particularity. Fifth, he concludes that derived names like 'knowing' must refer to real meanings, and since being devoid of them would be a deficiency, they must be established. Finally, he asserts that these meanings cannot be the essence because they would then subsist by themselves, which is impossible. He ends with a remark on the Mu'tazilites' stubbornness in denying the attributes.

Key Terms
ذات (dhat) - essence
علم ('ilm) - knowledge
قدرة (qudra) - power
تعلق (ta'alluq) - relation
مأخذ الاشتقاق (makhadh al-ishtiqaq) - basis of derivation
معنى زائد (ma'na za'id) - additional meaning
اعتبارات عقلية (i'tibarat 'aqliyya) - mental considerations
معان حقيقية (ma'ani haqiqiyya) - real meanings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument merely shows that attributes are not mere relations, but it goes further to prove they are distinct entities.
Correction: Taftazani explicitly argues that attributes are real meanings (ma'ani haqiqiyya) added to the essence, not just that they are not relations.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's argument, what is the main problem with saying that God's knowledge and power are merely the essence's relation (ta'alluq) to objects?
A) Relations are impossible in God.
B) The essence itself would have to be the relation, which is absurd.
C) Relations cannot be predicated of God.
D) Knowledge and power are not real meanings.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani's argument establishes that divine attributes are real, distinct meanings added to the essence, contrary to the Mu'tazilite view that they are merely identical with the essence or mental relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's knowledge of your every action is a real, distinct attribute—not just a mental concept—how does this truth confront the way you live today? In what specific area of your life will you change your behavior because He truly knows and sees?
?
The passage warns that being devoid of attributes is deficiency. Have you ever, in your heart, reduced Allah's attributes to mere names without real meaning? What will you do today to repent from such reductionism and commit to studying His names with the seriousness they demand?
?
Taftazani uses the analogy of one being half and third to show that multiple relations do not cause multiplicity in the essence. When you contemplate that Allah's essence is simple yet His attributes are real and many, does this mystery increase your awe or your confusion? How can you train yourself to submit to this truth and let it deepen your tawhid rather than your doubts?

Scriptural Proof for Divine Attributes
Taftazani cites Quranic verses as undeniable proof for real divine knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani uses Quranic verses to prove that divine knowledge and power are real, distinct attributes that cannot be allegorized away.

Core Idea
The Quran itself commands us to affirm God's knowledge and power as real attributes.

Taftazani now turns to the third and final proof for divine attributes: scriptural evidence. He cites several Quranic verses that he argues are so clear in affirming knowledge and power that they cannot be reinterpreted allegorically. This lesson examines those verses and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the structure: Taftazani states his claim, then gives examples.
2
Identify each verse and its point
There are four verses cited. For each, identify which attribute (knowledge or power) it proves and note any special reasoning Taftazani provides.
Pay special attention to the second verse, where he clarifies the meaning of 'bi-'ilmillah'.
3
Understand why these verses are considered unambiguous
Taftazani insists these texts 'do not admit of interpretation' (la yahtamil al-ta'wil). Think about why he might say that. What would happen if someone tried to interpret them allegorically?
The commentary explains the potential problem with the second verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : النصوص الدالة على إثبات العلم والقدرة بحيث لا يحتمل التأويل كقوله تعالى : أنزله بعلمه ) وقوله ( فاعلموا أنما أنزل بعلم الله ) أي متلبساً بمعنى أنه تعلق به العلم، لا بمعنى مقارناً للعلم لئلا يلزم كون العلم منزلاً فيجب تأويله وكقوله تعالى : أن القوة الله وقوله تعالى : ( إن الله هو الرزاق ذو القوة المتين )
The third proof: The scriptural texts that indicate the affirmation of knowledge and power in a way that does not admit of interpretation, such as His statement: 'He sent it down with His knowledge' (4:166) and His statement: 'So know that what was sent down is with the knowledge of God' (11:14) meaning accompanied by the meaning that knowledge is connected to it, not in the sense of being concomitant with knowledge, lest knowledge be considered sent down, so it must be interpreted. And like His statement: 'Power belongs entirely to God' (2:165) and His statement: 'Indeed, God is the Provider, the Possessor of Strength, the Firm' (51:58).
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-wajh al-thalith) for the reality of God's attributes, namely scriptural evidence (nusus) from the Quran. Taftazani states that these texts indicate knowledge and power in a way that cannot be interpreted away (la yahtamil al-ta'wil). He cites four verses: (1) 'Anzalahu bi-'ilmihi' — He sent it down with His knowledge, showing that knowledge is an attribute distinct from the act of sending down. (2) 'Fa'lamu annama unzila bi-'ilmillah' — So know that what was sent down is with the knowledge of God, where 'bi-'ilmillah' means accompanied by knowledge as a quality, not merely coincidental, lest one be forced to say knowledge itself was sent down, which would require allegorical interpretation (ta'wil). (3) 'Inna al-quwwata lillah jami'an' — Power belongs entirely to God, affirming power as an attribute. (4) 'Inna Allaha huwa al-razzaqu dhu al-quwwati al-matin' — God is the Provider, the Possessor of Strength, the Firm, affirming power as a real attribute (dhu al-quwwa). These verses are presented as clear proof that knowledge and power are real attributes of God, not merely figurative or identical with essence.

Key Terms
نصوص (nusus) - scriptural texts
تأويل (ta'wil) - allegorical interpretation
علم ('ilm) - knowledge
قوة (quwwa) - power
على أن لا يحتمل التأويل (ala an la yahtamil al-ta'wil) - in a way that does not admit of interpretation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think these verses only affirm God's knowledge in general, but Taftazani uses them to prove that knowledge is a real, distinct attribute.
Correction: Taftazani specifically argues that the phrasing 'with His knowledge' and 'with the knowledge of God' shows knowledge is a distinct attribute accompanying the act, not identical to the essence.

Checkpoint
In the third proof, why does Taftazani insist that the phrase 'bi-'ilmih' in the verse means 'accompanied by' knowledge as a quality, not 'concomitant with' knowledge?
A) Because 'bi-' indicates the instrument.
B) To avoid the implication that knowledge itself was sent down.
C) Because the verse is about the Quran's revelation.
D) To emphasize God's power.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani's third proof establishes that scriptural texts affirm divine attributes in a manner that resists allegorical interpretation, thereby confirming that knowledge and power are real, distinct attributes of God.

{'question': "When you read the verse 'Anzalahu bi-ilmihi' (He sent it down with His knowledge), do you consciously reflect that God's knowledge is a real attribute, not just a metaphor? Does this awareness deepen your reverence for the Quran? Commit today to pausing at such verses and affirming Allah's attributes as taught by Ahl al-Sunnah."}
?
{'question': "Taftazani warns against interpreting these verses in a way that would make knowledge a created thing. Are there any areas in your own understanding of Allah's attributes where you might be unknowingly falling into ta'til or tashbih? Take a moment to examine your heart and seek correct knowledge."}
?
{'question': "The verse 'Inna al-quwwata lillahi jami'an' (Power belongs entirely to God) is absolute. Does your daily life reflect this belief, or do you sometimes act as if power lies in your own efforts, money, or status? What one change can you make today to depend fully on Allah's power?"}

Quranic Proofs for Divine Knowledge and Power
The author cites Quranic verses as definitive proof for attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'an provides definitive proof for Allah's attributes of knowledge and power in a way that cannot be allegorically interpreted away.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's own words settle the reality of divine attributes.

The author now turns to scriptural evidence for affirming divine attributes. He cites specific Quranic verses that explicitly attribute knowledge and power to Allah. These verses are chosen because they cannot be allegorically interpreted — their apparent meaning is the only valid one. This lesson explores those verses and the author's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the attributes
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify which two divine attributes are being proven and note the verses cited for each.
The passage focuses on knowledge (علم) and power (قوة).
2
Understand the verse for knowledge
Pay attention to the phrase 'with His knowledge' (بعلمه). The author explains it means the act is connected to knowledge, not that knowledge is a separate entity sent down. Why does this matter?
This clarification prevents a literal reading that could imply knowledge is created.
3
Reflect on the verses for power
Consider the verses about power. How do they conclusively affirm that power is an attribute of Allah without room for metaphorical interpretation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : النصوص الدالة على إثبات العلم والقدرة بحيث لا يحتمل التأويل كقوله تعالى : (أنزله بعلمه) وقوله ( فاعلموا أنما أنزل بعلم الله ) أي متلبساً بمعنى أنه تعلق به العلم، لا بمعنى مقارناً للعلم لئلا يلزم كون العلم منزلاً فيجب تأويله وكقوله تعالى : (أن القوة الله ) وقوله تعالى : ( إن الله هو الرزاق ذو القوة المتين )

Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument of the author for affirming divine attributes: textual evidence from the Qur'an that explicitly attributes knowledge and power to God in a manner that cannot be allegorically interpreted away. The author cites four verses: 'He sent it down with His knowledge' (4:166), 'Know that it was sent down with the knowledge of God' (11:14), 'That power belongs entirely to God' (2:165), and 'God is the Provider, the Possessor of Power, the Strong' (51:58). He explains that the phrase 'with His knowledge' means the knowledge is related to the act, not that knowledge is a concomitant thing sent down, because that would imply knowledge is a created entity, requiring metaphorical interpretation. Thus these verses necessitate real, eternal attributes.

Refuting the Claim that Attributes Make God Both Subject and Agent
First objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against additional attributes and how the author refutes it by denying the premise of impossibility.

Core Idea
The opponent's first argument is refuted simply by rejecting its claim of absurdity.

The opponents argue that if attributes are distinct, God would be both the receiver and producer of His own attributes—a combination they consider impossible. In this lesson, we examine this objection and the author's very concise refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the passage presenting the first objection: that if all things depend on God, especially His attributes, then He would be both receptive (qabil) and active (fa'il).
Notice how the objection derives a necessary consequence from the assumption of distinct attributes.
2
Identify the refutation
The refutation is simply 'by denying its falsity'—meaning the opponent's claim that the consequence is absurd is not conceded.
This is a dialectical move: the burden of proof remains on the opponent.
3
Reflect on the logic
Think about why this refutation is considered sufficient. In kalam, if an opponent asserts impossibility without proof, one can simply reject it.
The author does not explain why it is not impossible; he only denies the opponent's assertion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : تم قال : تمسك المخالف بوجوه : على الذات الأول : أن الكل مستند إليه سيما صفاته فيلزم كونه قابلاً وفاعلاً ورد بمنع بطلانه . الثاني : أنها صفات كمال فيستلزم استكماله بالغير ورد بأنها ليست غيره، ولو سلم فاستحالة الاستكمال بمعنى ثبوت صفة الكمال له نفس المتنازع . الثالث : أن عالميته مثلاً ،واجبة، والواجب لا يعلل ورد بعد تسليم كون العالمية غير العلم بأن الواجب بمعنى (۱) ما يمتنع خلو الذات عنه، لا نسلم استحالة تعليله بصفة ناشئة عن الذات (۲) الرابع : أن القول بتعدد القدماء كفر بإجماع . ورد بأنه لا تغاير ههنا فلا تعدد، ولو سلم فليس (٤) أزلي قديماً بل إذا كان قائماً بنفسه. ولو سلم فالكفر إجماعاً تعدد القديم بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالغير ولو سلم ففي الذات خاصة كما لزم النصارى)
He said: Then he said: The opponent clung to aspects regarding the Essence: First: That everything is dependent on Him, especially His attributes, so it necessitates that He is receptive and agent. It is refuted by denying its falsity. Second: That they are attributes of perfection, so it necessitates His being perfected by another. It is refuted by [saying] they are not other than Him, and even if granted, the impossibility of being perfected in the sense of the establishment of the attribute of perfection to Him is the very point of dispute. Third: That His being omniscient, for example, is necessary, and the necessary is not caused. It is refuted, after granting that omniscience is other than knowledge, that 'necessary' in the sense of what is impossible for the Essence to be devoid of—we do not concede the impossibility of its being caused by an attribute arising from the Essence. Fourth: That the statement of multiple eternals is disbelief by consensus. It is refuted by [saying] there is no distinction here so no multiplicity, and even if granted, it is not eternal as in pre-eternal, rather if it is self-subsistent. And even if granted, disbelief is [due to] multiplicity of the eternal in the sense of lack of priority by another, and even if granted, it is in the Essence specifically, as the Christians held).
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents four objections raised by opponents (likely Mu'tazila and others) against the doctrine of additional attributes, along with their refutations. 

First objection: If attributes are distinct, then God would be both the subject (qabil) and the agent (fa'il) of His own attributes, which is absurd. Refutation: The opponent's claim of absurdity is denied; it is not necessarily impossible. 

Second objection: Attributes are perfections, so affirming them implies God is perfected by another, which is impermissible. Refutation: The attributes are not 'other' than God in the sense of being separate entities; even if one grants that, the very point in dispute is whether God can be described with such perfections. 

Third objection: God's omniscience is necessary, and a necessary thing cannot have a cause. Refutation: Even if we grant that omniscience is other than knowledge (i.e., an attribute), the term 'necessary' here means that the Essence cannot be devoid of it, not that it is uncaused. We do not accept that an attribute arising from the Essence cannot be a cause. 

Fourth objection: Saying there are multiple eternal entities is unbelief by consensus. Refutation: There is no real distinction (taghayur) here between essence and attribute, so no multiplicity. Even if there were distinction, it does not mean each is eternal (qadim) in the sense of being pre-eternal (azali); rather, attributes are self-subsistent but not separate. Even if granted, the consensus is about multiplicity in the sense of independent entities, not about attributes inhering in the essence. The Christians erred by positing three hypostases in one essence, but that is not analogous.

The passage is structured as a series of objections and concise refutations, demonstrating dialectical skill.

Key Terms
المخالف
قابلاً وفاعلاً
بطلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that being both subject and agent is logically contradictory.
Correction: The author does not accept that; it is only contradictory if the opponent proves it, which he does not.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the first objection?
A) By explaining why the consequence is not impossible.
B) By denying the opponent's claim of falsity.
C) By proving that attributes are not distinct.
D) By citing consensus.

Key Takeaway
The first objection is refuted by simply denying the opponent's claim of impossibility, placing the burden of proof on them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone challenges a core belief of yours and you cannot fully explain the 'how,' are you willing to simply deny their claim of impossibility and rest in that humility—or do you feel pressured to produce an elaborate defense? How does this passage about a minimal refutation speak to your own trust in God's perfection even when you don't have all the answers?
?
This lesson shows the opponent's insistence on a logical absurdity, and the author's refusal to concede it. In your own life, have you ever dismissed a truth about God because it seemed contradictory from a human perspective? What would change in your worship if you accepted that some divine realities are beyond your categories of subject and agent, and you simply denied the charge of impossibility?

Refuting that Attributes Imply Perfection by Another
Second objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that attributes would mean God is perfected by another, and the two-part refutation.

Core Idea
Attributes are not other than God, so no external perfection; even if they were, the impossibility is the very point in dispute.

The second objection claims that if attributes are additional perfections, then God must be perfected by something external, which is incompatible with His self-sufficiency. The author offers a dual response: first, the attributes are not 'other' in a separate sense; second, even if they were, the question of impossibility is what is being debated.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the passage for the second objection: attributes are perfections, so they would imply God is perfected by another.
Note the conditional 'if they are perfections'.
2
Analyze the first refutation
The author says attributes are not 'other than Him' (ghayruhu). This means they are not separate entities that could serve as external perfectors.
This is a denial of otherness, not of distinction.
3
Analyze the second refutation
Even if one grants otherness, the impossibility of God being perfected by another is exactly what is disputed; the opponent cannot assume it.
This is a dialectical move: the opponent's premise is the very issue under debate.

intermediate

Refuting that a Necessary Attribute Cannot Have a Cause
Third objection and its refutation with definitional nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection concerning the uncaused nature of necessary attributes and the author's response redefining the meaning of 'necessary'.

Core Idea
'Necessary' means the essence cannot lack it, not that it is causeless.

The third objection argues that God's omniscience is necessary, and necessary things cannot have a cause. The author clarifies the meaning of 'necessary' (wājib) and argues that an attribute arising from the essence can be the cause without affecting necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the third objection: omniscience is necessary, and the necessary is not caused (lā yuʿallal).
The opponent has already granted that omniscience is other than knowledge (i.e., a distinct attribute).
2
Understand the redefinition of 'necessary'
The author defines 'necessary' as 'what the essence cannot be devoid of'. This is a specific meaning, not the general sense of 'uncaused'.
The footnote (1) in the text points to this definition.
3
See the conclusion
The author then says: we do not concede that such a necessary attribute cannot be caused by another attribute emanating from the essence.
The cause is internal, not external.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : تم قال : تمسك المخالف بوجوه : على الذات الأول : أن الكل مستند إليه سيما صفاته فيلزم كونه قابلاً وفاعلاً ورد بمنع بطلانه . الثاني : أنها صفات كمال فيستلزم استكماله بالغير ورد بأنها ليست غيره، ولو سلم فاستحالة الاستكمال بمعنى ثبوت صفة الكمال له نفس المتنازع . الثالث : أن عالميته مثلاً ،واجبة، والواجب لا يعلل ورد بعد تسليم كون العالمية غير العلم بأن الواجب بمعنى (۱) ما يمتنع خلو الذات عنه، لا نسلم استحالة تعليله بصفة ناشئة عن الذات (۲) الرابع : أن القول بتعدد القدماء كفر بإجماع . ورد بأنه لا تغاير ههنا فلا تعدد، ولو سلم فليس (٤) أزلي قديماً بل إذا كان قائماً بنفسه. ولو سلم فالكفر إجماعاً تعدد القديم بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالغير ولو سلم ففي الذات خاصة كما لزم النصارى)
He said: Then he said: The opponent clung to aspects regarding the Essence: First: That everything is dependent on Him, especially His attributes, so it necessitates that He is receptive and agent. It is refuted by denying its falsity. Second: That they are attributes of perfection, so it necessitates His being perfected by another. It is refuted by [saying] they are not other than Him, and even if granted, the impossibility of being perfected in the sense of the establishment of the attribute of perfection to Him is the very point of dispute. Third: That His being omniscient, for example, is necessary, and the necessary is not caused. It is refuted, after granting that omniscience is other than knowledge, that 'necessary' in the sense of what is impossible for the Essence to be devoid of—we do not concede the impossibility of its being caused by an attribute arising from the Essence. Fourth: That the statement of multiple eternals is disbelief by consensus. It is refuted by [saying] there is no distinction here so no multiplicity, and even if granted, it is not eternal as in pre-eternal, rather if it is self-subsistent. And even if granted, disbelief is [due to] multiplicity of the eternal in the sense of lack of priority by another, and even if granted, it is in the Essence specifically, as the Christians held).
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents four objections raised by opponents (likely Mu'tazila and others) against the doctrine of additional attributes, along with their refutations. 

First objection: If attributes are distinct, then God would be both the subject (qabil) and the agent (fa'il) of His own attributes, which is absurd. Refutation: The opponent's claim of absurdity is denied; it is not necessarily impossible. 

Second objection: Attributes are perfections, so affirming them implies God is perfected by another, which is impermissible. Refutation: The attributes are not 'other' than God in the sense of being separate entities; even if one grants that, the very point in dispute is whether God can be described with such perfections. 

Third objection: God's omniscience is necessary, and a necessary thing cannot have a cause. Refutation: Even if we grant that omniscience is other than knowledge (i.e., an attribute), the term 'necessary' here means that the Essence cannot be devoid of it, not that it is uncaused. We do not accept that an attribute arising from the Essence cannot be a cause. 

Fourth objection: Saying there are multiple eternal entities is unbelief by consensus. Refutation: There is no real distinction (taghayur) here between essence and attribute, so no multiplicity. Even if there were distinction, it does not mean each is eternal (qadim) in the sense of being pre-eternal (azali); rather, attributes are self-subsistent but not separate. Even if granted, the consensus is about multiplicity in the sense of independent entities, not about attributes inhering in the essence. The Christians erred by positing three hypostases in one essence, but that is not analogous.

The passage is structured as a series of objections and concise refutations, demonstrating dialectical skill.

Key Terms
واجب
تعليل
عالمية
ناشئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume 'necessary' always means 'uncaused'.
Correction: In this context, 'necessary' means 'essential', i.e., the essence cannot be without it, not that it is causeless.

Checkpoint
How does the author define 'necessary' (wājib) in his refutation?
A) What has no cause.
B) What is eternal.
C) What the essence cannot be devoid of.
D) What is self-subsistent.

Key Takeaway
The third objection is refuted by clarifying that 'necessary' means essential, not uncaused, and an internal cause is permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider God's attribute of omniscience, do you ever subconsciously assume it functions like human knowledge, subject to causes and limitations? How does this lesson's distinction between essential and causal necessity challenge that assumption, and what change in your perception of God does it demand?
?
This refutation shows that an attribute can be both necessary and caused from within the divine essence. Does your heart truly accept that God's attributes are not like created things—that they arise from His own being without external dependence? If not, what intellectual or spiritual resistance must you overcome to submit to this truth?
?
If you were to encounter someone who claims that a necessary attribute cannot have any cause at all, would you be able to explain the nuance from this passage? What would it cost you to learn and articulate this distinction with clarity and humility, rather than dismissing the objection superficially?

Refuting the Charge of Believing in Multiple Eternals
Fourth objection and multi-layered refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection that multiple attributes entail multiple eternal entities, and the layered refutation that distinguishes different senses of eternity and multiplicity.

Core Idea
Attributes are not separate eternals; even if they were, they do not meet the criteria for disbelief.

The fourth objection is the most serious: that affirming multiple attributes amounts to believing in multiple eternal beings, which is consensus disbelief. The author provides a four-step refutation, progressively granting assumptions to show that even then the charge does not hold.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first refutation
Read the fourth objection: saying multiple eternals is disbelief. The first refutation: there is no real distinction (taghāyur) between essence and attributes, so no multiplicity.
This directly denies the premise of multiplicity.
2
Examine the second and third refutations
Even if we grant distinction, the attributes are not eternal in the sense of pre-eternal (azali); they are self-subsistent but not independent. And disbelief concerns multiplicity in the sense of independent beings.
The author uses the phrase 'not eternal as in pre-eternal, rather if it is self-subsistent'.
3
Understand the final refutation
Even if all that is granted, the problem of multiplicity in the essence itself is specifically what the Christians held, but that is not our position.
This distinguishes the Sunni position from Christian trinitarianism.

Source Text

First Objection: Recipient and Agent
First objection and its response regarding the increase of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the increase of attributes and how the Ash'ari response refutes it without conceding the opponent's assumption.

Core Idea
The opponent confuses dependence with passivity; the response blocks the entailment.

The first objection argues that if attributes are added to the essence, then the essence must be both recipient of those attributes and their agent, which is absurd. The Ash'ari response denies that the supposed entailment is actually false. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic for the first objection and its translation. Notice the reasoning: everything depends on God, so attributes also depend, making God both recipient and agent.
The term 'قابل وفاعل' is key.
2
Understand the response
The response is short: it prevents (منع) the falsehood (بطلانه). That is, the objector assumes that being recipient is impossible, but the Ash'ari denies that assumption.
The response does not prove it possible; it shifts the burden.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider why the objector thinks being both recipient and agent is impossible. The Ash'ari shows that this is not self-evident and requires proof.
This is a typical kalam dialectical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أوجه المخالفين في زيادة الصفات قال : تم قال : تمسك المخالف بوجوه : على الذات الأول : أن الكل مستند إليه سيما صفاته فيلزم كونه قابلاً وفاعلاً ورد بمنع بطلانه . الثاني : أنها صفات كمال فيستلزم استكماله بالغير ورد بأنها ليست غيره، ولو سلم فاستحالة الاستكمال بمعنى ثبوت صفة الكمال له نفس المتنازع . الثالث : أن عالميته مثلاً ،واجبة، والواجب لا يعلل ورد بعد تسليم كون العالمية غير العلم بأن الواجب بمعنى ما يمتنع خلو الذات عنه، لا نسلم استحالة تعليله بصفة ناشئة عن الذات . الرابع : أن القول بتعدد القدماء كفر بإجماع . ورد بأنه لا تغاير ههنا فلا تعدد، ولو سلم فليس أزلي قديماً بل إذا كان قائماً بنفسه. ولو سلم فالكفر إجماعاً تعدد القديم بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالغير ولو سلم ففي الذات خاصة كما لزم النصارى) .
The faces of the opponents regarding the increase of attributes. He said: then he said: the opponent clung to aspects: upon the essence: First: that everything is dependent upon Him, especially His attributes, so it necessitates Him being both recipient and agent, and it was replied by preventing its falsehood. Second: that they are attributes of perfection, so it necessitates His being perfected by another, and it was replied that they are not other than Him, and if granted, then the impossibility of being perfected in the sense of the establishment of the attribute of perfection for Him is the very point of dispute. Third: that His being omniscient, for example, is necessary, and the necessary is not caused, and he replied after granting that omniscience is other than knowledge, that the necessary in the sense of what it is impossible for the essence to be devoid of, we do not grant the impossibility of its being caused by an attribute arising from the essence. Fourth: that the statement of the multiplicity of the eternals is unbelief by consensus. And it was replied that there is no otherness here so there is no multiplicity, and if granted, then it is not eternal in the sense of being uncreated but rather when it is self-subsisting. And if granted, then unbelief is due to multiplicity of the eternal in the sense of non-priority by another, and if granted, then specifically in the essence as befell the Christians.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage lists four objections raised by opponents (المخالف) against the Ash'ari view that God's attributes are additional to the essence, along with the responses (ورد) to each. The objections argue that if attributes are additional, then God would be both recipient and agent (first), would be perfected by another (second), that necessary attributes cannot be caused (third), and that multiple eternals would imply polytheism (fourth). The responses block each: for the first, it is denied that being recipient is impossible; for the second, the attributes are not other than Him and the supposed perfection is the very point at issue; for the third, the necessity of the attribute does not preclude its being caused by the essence; for the fourth, there is no real otherness, and even if considered eternal, it is not independent.

Key Terms
القابل
الفاعل
الصفات الزائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari response concedes that God would be both recipient and agent.
Correction: The response does not concede; it denies the falsity of that outcome.

Checkpoint
In the first objection, what does the opponent claim is necessitated if attributes are additional to the essence?
A) That God would be both recipient and agent
B) That God would be perfected by another
C) That there would be multiple eternals
D) That necessary attributes cannot be caused

Key Takeaway
The first objection against additional attributes is neutralized by denying that being both recipient and agent is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you assume that your human categories of 'agent' and 'recipient' apply to Him in the same way they apply to creatures? This lesson challenges that assumption. Are you willing to hold your theological beliefs with the humility that they might be limited by your own logic?
?
The Ash'ari response does not prove that God can be both agent and recipient; it only blocks the opponent's claim of impossibility. In your own faith, are you comfortable with beliefs that are not proven beyond doubt but are simply defended against objections? How does that affect your certainty?
?
This objection and response revolve around a subtle logical point. When you encounter theological arguments that seem convincing, do you examine whether they rely on unproven assumptions? What assumption in your own thinking about God might you need to reconsider today?

Second Objection: Perfection by Another
Second objection and its response regarding attributes of perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that additional attributes would entail God being perfected by another and the Ash'ari response that the attributes are not other than Him.

Core Idea
Attributes are not other than God, so no perfection by another.

The second objection claims that if attributes are added to the essence, then God is perfected by something else, which is impossible. The response reframes the issue: the attributes are not other than Him, and even if they were, the very dispute is about whether these attributes belong to Him or not.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic for the second objection: 'ثاني: أنها صفات كمال فيستلزم استكماله بالغير ورد بأنها ليست غيره'. Understand that the opponent sees attributes as separate perfections.
2
Analyze the response
The response has two parts: first, the attributes are not other than Him (ليست غيره). Second, even if they were, the impossibility of being perfected by another is the very point of dispute (نفس المتنازع).
The second part is a dialectical move: the opponent assumes what needs to be proved.
3
Consider the logic
Why does the opponent think being perfected by another is impossible? Because God is absolutely perfect. But the Ash'ari says that the attributes are His perfections, not separate beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
