Third Objection: Necessary Attribute and Causation
Third objection and its response concerning necessary attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that necessary attributes cannot have a cause and the Ash'ari response distinguishing between different meanings of 'necessary'.

Core Idea
Necessity of the attribute does not preclude causation from the essence.

The third objection argues that attributes like omniscience are necessary (واجبة), and what is necessary cannot be caused. The Ash'ari response distinguishes between necessity of existence (which would preclude causation) and necessity of attribution (which does not), showing that the attribute arises from the essence without an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic: 'ثالث : أن عالميته مثلاً ،واجبة، والواجب لا يعلل ورد بعد تسليم كون العالمية غير العلم بأن الواجب بمعنى ما يمتنع خلو الذات عنه، لا نسلم استحالة تعليله بصفة ناشئة عن الذات .'
2
Understand the key distinction
The response clarifies that 'necessary' here means 'the essence cannot be devoid of it' (ما يمتنع خلو الذات عنه), not 'necessary existence'. This kind of necessity does not prevent the attribute from being caused by the essence itself.
3
Reflect on the response's strategy
The response grants that omniscience is other than knowledge (غير العلم) for the sake of argument, but then shows that even so, the causation is internal, not external.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أوجه المخالفين في زيادة الصفات قال : تم قال : تمسك المخالف بوجوه : على الذات الأول : أن الكل مستند إليه سيما صفاته فيلزم كونه قابلاً وفاعلاً ورد بمنع بطلانه . الثاني : أنها صفات كمال فيستلزم استكماله بالغير ورد بأنها ليست غيره، ولو سلم فاستحالة الاستكمال بمعنى ثبوت صفة الكمال له نفس المتنازع . الثالث : أن عالميته مثلاً ،واجبة، والواجب لا يعلل ورد بعد تسليم كون العالمية غير العلم بأن الواجب بمعنى ما يمتنع خلو الذات عنه، لا نسلم استحالة تعليله بصفة ناشئة عن الذات . الرابع : أن القول بتعدد القدماء كفر بإجماع . ورد بأنه لا تغاير ههنا فلا تعدد، ولو سلم فليس أزلي قديماً بل إذا كان قائماً بنفسه. ولو سلم فالكفر إجماعاً تعدد القديم بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالغير ولو سلم ففي الذات خاصة كما لزم النصارى) .
The faces of the opponents regarding the increase of attributes. He said: then he said: the opponent clung to aspects: upon the essence: First: that everything is dependent upon Him, especially His attributes, so it necessitates Him being both recipient and agent, and it was replied by preventing its falsehood. Second: that they are attributes of perfection, so it necessitates His being perfected by another, and it was replied that they are not other than Him, and if granted, then the impossibility of being perfected in the sense of the establishment of the attribute of perfection for Him is the very point of dispute. Third: that His being omniscient, for example, is necessary, and the necessary is not caused, and he replied after granting that omniscience is other than knowledge, that the necessary in the sense of what it is impossible for the essence to be devoid of, we do not grant the impossibility of its being caused by an attribute arising from the essence. Fourth: that the statement of the multiplicity of the eternals is unbelief by consensus. And it was replied that there is no otherness here so there is no multiplicity, and if granted, then it is not eternal in the sense of being uncreated but rather when it is self-subsisting. And if granted, then unbelief is due to multiplicity of the eternal in the sense of non-priority by another, and if granted, then specifically in the essence as befell the Christians.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage lists four objections raised by opponents (المخالف) against the Ash'ari view that God's attributes are additional to the essence, along with the responses (ورد) to each. The objections argue that if attributes are additional, then God would be both recipient and agent (first), would be perfected by another (second), that necessary attributes cannot be caused (third), and that multiple eternals would imply polytheism (fourth). The responses block each: for the first, it is denied that being recipient is impossible; for the second, the attributes are not other than Him and the supposed perfection is the very point at issue; for the third, the necessity of the attribute does not preclude its being caused by the essence; for the fourth, there is no real otherness, and even if considered eternal, it is not independent.

Key Terms
الواجب
تعليل
صفة ناشئة عن الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari denies that the attribute is necessary.
Correction: They affirm its necessity but reinterpret what that necessity entails.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, what does 'necessary' mean in the context of divine attributes?
A) The attribute is necessary in existence
B) The essence cannot be devoid of the attribute
C) The attribute has no cause at all
D) The attribute is created

Key Takeaway
The third objection fails because the necessity of an attribute does not preclude it being caused by the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes like omniscience, do you ever stop at 'He knows everything' without reflecting on how that knowledge necessarily flows from His essence? How can you deepen your awe by meditating on the logical necessity of His attributes today?
?
The opponent assumed necessity precludes causation, but the response shows a deeper understanding. Where in your own life have you assumed that something's fixed nature (e.g., a habit, a circumstance) means it has no underlying cause or purpose? How will you challenge that assumption this week?
?
If God's attributes arise from His essence, then every attribute is a direct window into who He is. How will you spend five minutes tonight contemplating one attribute as not just a label but a necessary emanation from His Being? Write down one concrete action you will take to act on that attribute.

Fourth Objection: Multiplicity of Eternals
Fourth objection and its layered response regarding multiplicity of eternals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection that believing in multiple eternal beings is kufr and the layered Ash'ari response rejecting any genuine multiplicity in God's attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes are not other beings, so no multiplicity of eternals.

The fourth objection warns that if attributes are eternal along with the essence, that would imply multiple eternal beings, which is kufr. The Ash'ari response dismantles this by showing that attributes are not other than the essence, and even if they were, they are not independent agents. The response has multiple conditional steps to block the charge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic: 'رابع : أن القول بتعدد القدماء كفر بإجماع . ورد بأنه لا تغاير ههنا فلا تعدد، ولو سلم فليس أزلي قديماً بل إذا كان قائماً بنفسه. ولو سلم فالكفر إجماعاً تعدد القديم بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالغير ولو سلم ففي الذات خاصة كما لزم النصارى).'
2
Analyze the layered response
The response has four conditional steps (ولو سلم). First, no otherness, so no multiplicity. Second, even if granted otherness, the attribute is not eternal in the sense of being self-subsisting. Third, even if granted eternal, kufr applies to multiplicity in the sense of not being preceded by another. Fourth, even if granted, it is only in the essence, as the Christians claimed.
The response builds a cascade of defenses.
3
Understand the final reference
The response ends by drawing an analogy to the Christian Trinity: the attributes are not separate essences. The Ash'ari view avoids polytheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refutation of the Philosophers' First Argument Against Additional Attributes
The author presents and refutes the philosophers' argument that additional attributes would be contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that additional attributes would be contingent, and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between modes of dependence.

Core Idea
An attribute's contingency does not imply imperfection when it is necessitated by the divine essence.

This passage tackles a fundamental challenge from the philosophers against the Ash'ari doctrine of additional attributes. The argument claims that if God had attributes distinct from His essence, they would be contingent and thus imperfect. However, the author clarifies the nature of this contingency and counters with a nuanced account of divine agency. Let's follow the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the flow of the argument come through.
2
Identify the philosophers' premise
Notice the initial claim: an additional attribute would be possible (contingent) because it does not subsist by itself.
The philosophers equate 'additional' with 'not self-subsistent'.
3
Understand the author's clarification
The author explains that when some scholars say 'Allah and His attributes are necessary', they mean the attributes are necessary through the essence, not created by choice.
This distinguishes between necessary by essence and necessary through another.
4
Grasp the critique of the philosophers' principle
The author criticizes the philosophers' rule that need for a cause is due to origination, not possibility. He argues this should be restricted to non-divine attributes.
This shows inconsistency in the philosophers' own framework.
5
See the final refutation
Even if attributes are possible, they are effects of God, but this does not make God a recipient-agent. The response is a denial of the premise.
The author ends by noting that for philosophers, most contingents depend indirectly on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : وهو للفلاسفة . لو كانت له صفة زائدة لكانت ممكنة ، لأن الصفة لا تقوم بنفسها، فضلاً عن الوجود كيف وقد ثبت أن الواجب واحد، وما وقع في بعض كلام بعض العلماء من أن واجب الوجود لذاته الله تعالى وصفاته، فمعناها هو أنها واجبة لذات الواجب أي مستندة إلى الله تعالى بطريق الإيجاب لا بطريق الخلق بالقصد والاختيار ليلزم كونها حادثة وكون القدرة مثلاً مسبوقة بقدرة أخرى، وما ثبت من كون الواجب مختاراً لا موجباً، إنما هو في غير صفاته؛ وأما استناد الصفات عند من يثبتها فليس إلا بطريق الإيجاب. وكذا قولهم علة الاحتياج إلى المؤثر هو الحدوث دون الإمكان ينبغي أن يخص بغير صفاته، ولا يخفى أن مثل هذه التخصيصات في الأحكام العقلية بعيد جداً ثم صفاته على تقدير تحققها ولزوم إمكانها يجب أن يكون أثراً له لامتناع افتقار الواجب في صفاته وكمالاته إلى الغير فيلزم كونه القابل والفاعل وهو باطل لما مرّ. وأجيب بالمنع كما مرّ، وقد يقرر لزوم كونه الفاعل، بأن جميع الممكنات مستندة إليه وكأنه الزامي ، وإلا فأكثر الممكنات عند الفلاسفة أثر للغير، وإن كانت بالآخرة منتهية إلى الواجب، مستندة إليه بالواسطة ، وهذا لا يوجب كون الفاعل .
First: and it is for the philosophers. If He had an additional attribute, it would be possible, because the attribute does not subsist by itself, let alone existence, how while it has been established that the Necessary is one? And what occurs in some speech of some scholars that the Necessary of existence in itself is Allah and His attributes, its meaning is that they are necessary for the essence of the Necessary, i.e., dependent on Allah by way of necessity (ijab) not by way of creation with intention and choice so that they would be temporal and that power, for example, would be preceded by another power. And what has been established that the Necessary is free choice not necessary causation, is only concerning other than His attributes; as for the dependence of attributes for those who affirm them, it is only by way of necessity. And likewise their statement that the cause of need for an effect is temporal origination not possibility ought to be specified to other than His attributes, and it is not hidden that such specifications in rational judgments are very far-fetched. Then His attributes, assuming their realization and the necessity of their possibility, must be an effect of Him because of the impossibility of the Necessary being in need of another in His attributes and perfections, so it necessitates Him being both recipient and agent, and that is false as previously mentioned. And it was answered by prevention as previously, and the necessity of His being the agent can be established by saying that all contingent things are dependent on Him, as if it is an imposition, otherwise most contingent things according to the philosophers are effects of another, even if ultimately ending in the Necessary, dependent on Him indirectly, and this does not necessitate His being the agent.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument from the philosophers (الفلاسفة) against the Ash'ari position that God has attributes additional to His essence. The argument proceeds: if God had an additional attribute, that attribute would be contingent (ممكنة) because it would not subsist by itself and because the Necessary is one. The author then clarifies a potential misunderstanding: when some scholars say that God and His attributes are necessary of existence, it means the attributes are necessary by virtue of the essence of the Necessary, not that they are created by choice. Then the author criticizes the philosophers' principle that the need for an efficient cause is due to temporal origination, not possibility, arguing that this should be restricted to things other than God's attributes. Then the argument that if attributes are possible, they must be effects of God, leading to God being both recipient and agent, which is absurd. The response (وأجيب) is a denial (المنع) of the premises, and an alternative formulation is given: all contingent things are dependent on God, but for philosophers, most are indirectly dependent, so it does not entail God being the agent in a way that implies imperfection.

Key Terms
الصفة الزائدة
الممكن
الواجب
الإيجاب
الحدوث
الإمكان
القابل والفاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Additional attributes would be contingent in the same way as created things, implying imperfection.
Correction: The attributes are contingent by virtue of being dependent on God, but they are necessary through His essence, not temporally originated. Their contingency does not imply a lack of perfection because they are necessitated by the divine essence.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' argument, why would an additional attribute be contingent?
A) Because it would be created.
B) Because it does not subsist by itself.
C) Because God is not free.
D) Because it would be multiple.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument fails because attributes can be contingent yet necessitated by God's essence, not requiring a separate creator or implying imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says the philosophers' principle that need for a cause is due to temporal origination should be specified to other than God's attributes. Have you ever, consciously or unconsciously, applied a principle from human experience to God without realizing you were overstepping? What is one such principle you need to correct based on this lesson?
?
If you truly believed that God's attributes are not created but flow necessarily from His essence, how would that change the way you approach Him in prayer? Would you still bargain with Him or doubt His attributes when He does not act as you expect?
?
The philosophers' argument would make God dependent on attributes because they are contingent. This lesson refutes that by showing they are necessitated, not created. But deep down, do you sometimes feel that God is 'needy' of our worship? Does this lesson correct that? What specifically from the source challenges that feeling?

The Dilemma of Perfection and Its Refutation
The author refutes the argument that an additional attribute is either not perfection or entails perfection by another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against additional divine attributes and the Ash'ari response based on the 'neither He nor other than He' distinction.

Core Idea
Attributes are neither identical to Allah nor entirely other—they arise from His essence.

The second argument attempts to force a contradiction: either an attribute is not a perfection (so it must be negated) or it is a perfection (so Allah would be perfected by another). The Ash'ari response rejects both horns with key insights about the nature of divine attributes. This lesson unpacks the logic and the reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: a dilemma presented, then an answer beginning with 'وأجيب'.
The passage is compact but dense in meaning.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find the two possibilities: the attribute is not perfection (first horn) or it is perfection (second horn). Note the conclusion for each.
The first horn leads to negation, the second to deficiency in essence.
3
Analyze the response
The answer has two parts: (a) rejecting that non-perfection equals deficiency, and (b) rejecting that perfection by another is impossible when the attribute arises from the essence and is perpetual. Note the 'لا هو ولا غيره' principle.
This is a core Ash'ari position.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this refutation preserves both divine transcendence and the reality of attributes. Ask yourself: why does 'arising from the essence' solve the problem?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : الصفة الزائدة إن لم تكن كمالاً يجب نفيها عنه لتنزهه عن النقصان ، وإن كان يلزم استكماله بالغير، وهو يوجب النقصان بالذات فيكون محالاً. وأجيب : بأنا لا نسلم أن ما لا يكون كمالاً يكون نقصاناً. وأن ما لا يكون عين الشيء يكون غيره ، بل صفاته، لا هو ولا غيره ولو سلم فلا نسلم استحالة ذلك إذا كانت صفة الكمال ناشئة عن الذات دائمة بدوامه ، بل ذلك غاية الكمال.
Second: the additional attribute, if it is not a perfection, then it must be negated from Him for His transcendence above deficiency; and if it is a perfection, then it necessitates His being perfected by another, and that necessitates deficiency in the essence, so it is impossible. And it was answered: we do not grant that what is not a perfection is a deficiency. And that what is not identical to a thing is other than it, rather His attributes are neither He nor other than He; and even if granted, we do not grant the impossibility of that when the attribute of perfection arises from the essence, perpetual with His perpetuity; rather that is the utmost perfection.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument, which is a dilemma: either the additional attribute is not a perfection, in which case it must be negated from God to avoid deficiency; or it is a perfection, which would imply that God is perfected by another, which is also a deficiency. The response (وأجيب) rejects the first horn by saying that not being a perfection does not automatically mean it is a deficiency. As for the second horn, the response makes a key Ash'ari distinction: the attributes are neither identical to the essence nor entirely other; they are in a category of their own. Furthermore, even if we grant the dilemma, the perfection arising from the essence and being perpetual is not a deficiency but the ultimate perfection.

Key Terms
الصفة الزائدة
الكمال
النقصان
لا هو ولا غيره
ناشئة عن الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any attribute that is not identical to the essence must be either a deficiency or a perfection that requires an external source.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that an attribute can be neither identical nor entirely other, and a perfection that arises from the essence does not imply being perfected by another.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari response to the claim that an additional attribute being a perfection entails God being perfected by another?
A) The attribute is actually a deficiency.
B) The attribute is identical to the essence.
C) The attribute is neither He nor other than He and arises from the essence.
D) God does not have attributes.

Key Takeaway
The second argument fails because it wrongly assumes that any attribute not identical to the essence must be either a deficiency or externally sourced; the Ash'ari 'neither He nor other' principle and the concept of attributes arising from the essence resolve the dilemma.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes like mercy or power, do you ever imagine them as separate 'things' God uses? How does the 'neither He nor other' principle challenge that mental image, and what must change in how you relate to His attributes?
?
The passage says attributes arising from the essence are the 'utmost perfection.' If you truly believe God's perfection is from Himself, why do you sometimes feel your own worth depends on what others give you? How can you root your identity in Allah's self-sufficiency instead?
?
The opponent assumed that any perfection added to God implies deficiency in His essence. Have you ever acted as if God needs your worship or obedience to be complete? Commit today to recognizing that He is perfect without you, and worship Him out of gratitude, not need.

The Second Argument: Superadded Attributes and the Answer
Second argument against additional attributes and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against superadded attributes and the Ash'ari response that attributes are neither identical to the essence nor other than it.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are neither the essence nor other—they are a unique reality.

This passage presents a classic dilemma against the idea that God's attributes are additional to His essence. The argument claims that if an attribute is not a perfection, it implies deficiency, and if it is a perfection, it implies God is perfected by another. The answer offers a nuanced position that avoids both extremes by affirming attributes as neither identical to the essence nor other than it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its structure, then read the literal English translation. Notice how the argument is a conditional dilemma and the answer uses multiple denials.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Understand the dilemma
Identify the two horns of the dilemma: if the attribute is not a perfection it must be negated, and if it is a perfection it implies God is perfected by another. Ask yourself why each horn is considered problematic.
The dilemma is meant to force an impossible conclusion.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
The answer denies two premises: that 'not perfection' equals 'deficiency', and that 'not identical' equals 'other'. Then it states the positive position: attributes are neither He nor other. Consider how this resolves the dilemma.
This is a key Ash'ari principle in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : الصفة الزائدة إن لم تكن كمالاً يجب نفيها عنه لتنزهه عن النقصان، وإن كان يلزم استكماله بالغير، وهو يوجب النقصان بالذات فيكون محالاً. وأجيب : بأنا لا نسلم أن ما لا يكون كمالاً يكون نقصاناً. وأن ما لا يكون عين الشيء يكون غيره، بل صفاته، لا هو ولا غيره ولو سلم فلا نسلم استحالة ذلك إذا كانت صفة الكمال ناشئة عن الذات دائمة بدوامه، بل ذلك غاية الكمال.
The second [argument]: The superadded attribute, if it is not a perfection, must be negated of Him for His transcendence above deficiency, but if it is [a perfection], it entails His being perfected by another, which necessitates deficiency in essence, so it is impossible. And the answer: We do not grant that what is not a perfection is a deficiency, nor that what is not identical to a thing is other than it. Rather, His attributes are neither He nor other than Him. Even if granted, we do not grant the impossibility of that when the attribute of perfection originates from the essence, enduring with His endurance; rather, that is the utmost perfection.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the view that divine attributes are additional to the essence (al-ṣifa al-zā'ida). The argument is a dilemma: either the attribute is a perfection or it is not. If it is not a perfection, it must be negated of God to avoid deficiency. If it is a perfection, then it implies that God is perfected by another, which means He is deficient in Himself, making it impossible. The answer denies that what is not a perfection is necessarily a deficiency, and also denies that what is not identical to a thing must be other than it. Instead, attributes are neither identical to the essence nor other; they have a unique status. Even if one accepts the dilemma, the impossibility does not follow when the perfection attribute is derived from the essence and eternal with it; rather, that is the highest perfection.

Key Terms
الصفة الزائدة
النقصان
الكمال
لا هو ولا غيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'neither He nor other' is a contradiction or meaningless.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, this is a technical term that avoids both anthropomorphism (identity) and outright distinction (otherness), affirming a unique relation between essence and attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second argument, what is the status of God's attributes relative to His essence?
A) They are identical to the essence.
B) They are other than the essence.
C) They are neither identical nor other.
D) They are contingent creations.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position on attributes avoids both identity and otherness, preserving divine perfection without external dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God is perfect, but do you truly live as if He needs nothing from you? When you seek validation from others, are you not acting as if you are the one lacking? Let this lesson remind you that only God is self-sufficient—so where are you placing your ultimate reliance?
?
If God's attributes are neither His essence nor other, He is beyond your categories. Are you comfortable with a God you cannot fully grasp, or do you secretly wish to reduce Him to something you can control? Honesty: do you submit to His mystery, or do you try to fit Him into your own logic?
?
The answer in this lesson says that attributes originating from the essence are 'the utmost perfection.' Does your worship reflect that you see God's attributes as flowing from His very being—or do you treat His mercy, power, and knowledge as disconnected favors? How can you deepen your gratitude for who He is, not just what He gives?

The Mu'tazilite Objection on Divine Attributes and Its Answer
The Mu'tazilite argument that God's attributes are necessary and uncaused, and the answer distinguishing necessity from uncausedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that God's necessary attributes are uncaused, and how the Ash'ari response distinguishes between necessary existence and essential inseparability.

Core Idea
Necessity does not always mean uncaused; it can mean essential inseparability.

The Mu'tazila argue that since God's attributes are necessary, they cannot have a cause, as causality implies contingency. But the Ash'ari answer refines this: the necessity of an attribute means the essence cannot lack it, not that the attribute itself is necessary of existence. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite premise
Read the first part of the passage which states that God's being knowing is necessary and thus uncaused. Notice how they equate necessity with uncausedness.
The Mu'tazila use 'wājib' in a specific sense.
2
Identify the key principle
The principle 'al-wājib lā yu‘allal' (the Necessary Being is not caused) is central. Understand why: need for a cause arises from contingency (jawāz).
This is a core kalam principle.
3
Follow the answer's distinction
The answer grants that 'being knowing' is an attribute beyond the essence but clarifies that its necessity means the essence cannot be without it, not that the attribute itself is necessary of existence.
This distinguishes two meanings of necessity.
4
See how the answer allows causality
The answer states that the attribute can be caused by an attribute arising from the essence, possibly through an intermediary. This preserves the idea that attributes are not uncaused in every sense.
This aligns with the Ash'ari doctrine of states (aḥwāl).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : وهو للمعتزلة أن عالميته واجبة لاستحالة الجهل عليه، ولاستحالة افتقاره إلى فاعل يجعله عالماً، وكذا البواقي، والواجب لا يعلل، لأن سبب الاحتياج إلى العلة، هو الجواز لترجيح جانب الوجود فعالميته مثلاً لا تعلل بالعلم، بل يكون هو عالم بالذات بخلاف عالميتنا فإنها جائزة. والجواب : بعد تسليم كون العالمية أمراً وراء العالم معللاً به كما هو رأى مثبتي الأحوال أن وجوبها ليس بمعنى كونها واجبة الوجود لذاتها، ليمتنع تعليلها، بل بمعنى امتناع خلو الذات عنها، وهو لا ينافي كونها معللة بصفة ناشئة عن اللازم للذات قد يكون بوسط.
The third [argument]: It is for the Mu'tazila: His being knowing is necessary due to the impossibility of ignorance upon Him, and due to the impossibility of His needing an agent to make Him knowing; and likewise the rest. The Necessary [Being] is not caused, because the reason for need of a cause is contingency (al-jawāz) to preponderate the side of existence. So His being knowing, for example, is not caused by knowledge, but rather He is knowing by essence, unlike our being knowing, which is contingent. And the answer: After granting that 'being knowing' is something beyond the knower, caused as per the view of the affirmers of states (muthbitī al-aḥwāl), that its necessity is not in the sense of being necessary of existence in itself, such that its being caused would be impossible, but rather in the sense of the impossibility of the essence being devoid of it, and this does not contradict its being caused by an attribute arising from the necessary [attribute] of the essence, which may be through an intermediary.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument, attributed to the Mu'tazila. They argue that God's attributes like knowledge (al-‘ālimiyya) are necessary because ignorance is impossible for Him and He cannot need an external agent to make Him knowledgeable. The Necessary Being (al-wājib) cannot be caused, since the need for a cause arises only from contingency (al-jawāz) to tilt the balance towards existence. Therefore, God is knowing by essence, unlike created knowledge which is contingent. The answer first grants that 'being knowing' is something beyond the knower and is caused, following the view of the 'affirmers of states' (muthbitī al-aḥwāl). It then clarifies that the necessity of this attribute is not that it is necessary of existence in itself, which would prevent it from being caused, but rather it means the impossibility of the essence being without it. This does not contradict its being caused by an attribute that arises from the necessary [attribute] of the essence, possibly through an intermediary.

Key Terms
عالميته
الواجب لا يعلل
مثبتي الأحوال
الجواز
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is necessary (wājib), it must be uncaused.
Correction: Necessity can mean essential inseparability from the essence, not necessarily independent existence; such necessary attributes can still be caused by the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite argument, why is God's being knowing not caused?
A) Because knowledge is contingent.
B) Because the Necessary Being cannot be caused, as need for a cause arises from contingency.
C) Because God created His own knowledge.
D) Because knowledge is a separate eternal entity.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite objection that necessary attributes are uncaused is answered by distinguishing necessity of existence from essential inseparability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is necessary—like your own existence—it must be independent of God? How does this lesson's distinction between two kinds of necessity shake that assumption? Ask yourself: In what areas of my life do I still act as if I am 'self-sufficient' rather than dependent on the One who causes me at every moment?
?
When you think about God's attribute of knowledge, do you instinctively compare it to your own, as if His knowing is just a bigger version of yours? This lesson says His being knowing is necessary and uncaused in a different sense. How does that correct your mental image of God? Pause and ask: Am I willing to let go of my anthropomorphic ideas and submit to what the revelation and reason actually teach about His attributes?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that God's attributes cannot be caused because that would imply neediness. But the answer shows that being caused by the essence does not diminish perfection. What does this tell you about your own need for causes and reasons? In your worship, do you secretly resent needing to rely on means and causes (like prayer, scholars, or good deeds), thinking that true closeness to God should be 'uncaused'? Repent of that pride and embrace your creatureliness.

The Fourth Argument: The Strongest Proof for Negators of Attributes
The fourth argument against attributes using a dilemma with answer denying distinctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma used by negators of attributes and how the answer refutes it by redefining what it means for two things to be distinct.

Core Idea
The strongest argument against attributes is a false dilemma once you define 'distinct' correctly.

This passage presents the most powerful argument used by those who deny attributes in God. It poses a dilemma: either attributes are temporal or eternal, both leading to unacceptable conclusions. The answer, however, challenges the assumption that attributes are distinct from the essence in a way that implies multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
The argument presents two options: attributes are either temporal (ḥāditha) or eternal (qadīma). Both lead to problems.
This is a classic logical dilemma used to prove that neither option is acceptable.
2
Examine the first horn
If attributes are temporal, they inhere in God's essence, implying change and imperfection, and God would be devoid of perfections in pre-eternity. This is false by consensus.
This horn is rejected because it implies God is subject to temporal events.
3
Examine the second horn
If attributes are eternal, then there would be multiple eternal beings, which is considered disbelief (kufr). The argument cites that Christians were declared disbelievers for claiming two eternal beings.
This horn is rejected to avoid polytheism.
4
Analyze the answer
The answer denies the presupposition that the essence and attributes are distinct in a way that entails multiplicity. It redefines 'two distinct things' as those that can be separated in place, time, or existence.
Attributes cannot be separated from the essence, so they are not 'other' in the problematic sense.
5
Grasp the definition of al-ghayrān
The passage defines 'two distinct things' as those with possible separation. Attributes are not separable from the essence, so no multiplicity arises.
This subtle definition is crucial to the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : وهو العمدة الوثقى لنفات الصفات من المليين أنها إما أن تكون حادثة فيلزم قيام الحوادث بذاته، وخلوه في الأزل عن العلم والقدرة والحياة وغيرها من الكمالات وصدورها عنه بالقصد والاختيار أو بشرائط حادثة لا بداية لها، والكل باطل بالاتفاق، وإما أن تكون قديمة فيلزم تعدد القدماء وهو كفر بإجماع المسلمين، وقد كفرت النصارى بزيادة قديمين، فكيف بأكثر؟ وأجيب : بأنا لا نسلم تغاير الذات مع الصفات ولا الصفات بعضها مع البعض ليثبت التعدد فإن الغيرين هما اللذان يمكن لانفكاك أحدهما عن الآخر بمكان أو زمان، أو بوجود وعدم أو هما ذاتان ليست إحديهما الأخرى وتفسيرهما بالشيئين أو الموجودين أو الاثنين، فاسد لأن الغير من الأسماء الإضافية، ولا إشعار في هذا التفسير بذلك
The fourth [argument]: It is the strongest proof for the negators of attributes from among the adherents of religions: [Attributes] are either temporal (ḥāditha), which entails the occurrence of temporal things in His essence, and His being devoid in pre-eternity of knowledge, power, life, and other perfections, and their issuance from Him by will and choice or by temporal conditions without beginning – all of which is false by consensus. Or they are eternal (qadīma), which entails multiplicity of eternal beings, which is disbelief by consensus of Muslims; the Christians were deemed disbelievers for adding two eternal beings, so what about more? And the answer: We do not grant the distinctness (taghāyur) of the essence from the attributes nor of the attributes from each other such that multiplicity would be established. For two distinct things (al-ghayrān) are those that can be separated from each other in place or time, or in existence and non-existence, or they are two essences neither of which is the other. Interpreting them as two things, or two existents, or two is invalid, because 'other' is a relative term (ism iḍāfī), and there is no indication in this interpretation of that.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, considered the strongest proof for those who negate attributes (al-nufāt al-ṣifāt) from among the people of religions (al-milliyyīn). It is a dilemma: if attributes are temporal (ḥāditha), then they would inhere in God's essence, implying change and imperfection, and God would be devoid of perfections in pre-eternity – all false by consensus. If they are eternal (qadīma), then there would be multiple eternal beings, which is disbelief; Christians were declared disbelievers for claiming two eternal beings (Father, Son, Holy Spirit), so having more would be even worse. The answer denies the presupposition that the essence and attributes are distinct (mutaghāyir) in a way that entails multiplicity. It defines 'two distinct things' (al-ghayrān) as those that can be separated in place, time, existence, or are two independent essences. Since attributes cannot be separated from the essence, they are not 'other' in that sense. Interpreting 'other' as merely 'two things' or 'two existents' is invalid because 'other' is a relative term that implies separability.

Key Terms
العمدة الوثقى
نفات الصفات
قيام الحوادث
تعدد القدماء
الغيرين
تفسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer denies that attributes are distinct from the essence at all, meaning they are identical.
Correction: The answer denies only that they are distinct in the sense of being separable entities, which would entail multiplicity. Attributes are conceptually distinct but not separate entities.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of accepting that attributes are eternal according to the fourth argument?
A) It implies imperfection in God.
B) It entails multiplicity of eternal beings, which is disbelief.
C) It makes God dependent on His attributes.
D) It requires temporal conditions.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument fails because the attributes are not 'other' in the sense that would necessitate multiple eternal beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes, do you ever picture them as separate 'parts' of God? How does the definition of 'other' in this lesson challenge your mental image of Allah?
?
The dilemma warns that adding eternal beings leads to disbelief like that of the Christians. Have you ever considered that your own conceptualization of Allah's attributes might unintentionally lead to associating partners? What practical steps can you take to ensure your belief remains pure?
?
The answer says that attributes are not 'other' to the essence. This means every attribute of Allah is perfectly inherent in His being. How does this deepen your trust in His perfection, especially when you feel He is not aware of your struggles?

The Third Mu'tazili Proof on God's Knowledge and Its Answer
Taftazani presents the third Mu'tazili proof on God's knowledge and its Ash'ari answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili argument that God's knowledge is uncaused and the Ash'ari response that necessity can mean inseparability rather than uncaused existence.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is necessary but not necessarily uncaused.

The Mu'tazila argue that God's attribute of being knowing is necessary, and therefore cannot be caused. The Ash'ari response refines the meaning of necessity: it does not mean that the attribute is necessary of existence in itself, but rather that the essence cannot be devoid of it, which allows for being caused by an attribute arising from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the Mu'tazili proof is presented first, followed by the answer beginning with 'The answer'.
Pay attention to the key terms: العالمية, الواجب, العلة, الجواز.
2
Identify the Mu'tazili reasoning
Find the two reasons given for why God's being knowing is necessary: impossibility of ignorance and impossibility of needing an agent. Then note the conclusion that the necessary is not caused.
The Mu'tazila compare God's knowledge to human knowledge which is possible.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari answer
Read the answer: it concedes that being-knowing is an additional attribute but reinterprets its necessity. Distinguish between 'necessary existence per se' and 'impossibility of the essence being devoid of it'.
The answer mentions the view of the affirmers of modes (مثبتي الأحوال).
4
Reflect on the logical distinction
Consider how this distinction resolves the Mu'tazili objection: an attribute can be inseparable from the essence yet still be caused by an attribute that necessarily arises from the essence.
This is a key nuance in Ash'ari theology about the relationship between essence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : وهو للمعتزلة أن عالميته واجبة لاستحالة الجهل عليه، ولاستحالة افتقاره إلى فاعل يجعله عالماً، وكذا البواقي والواجب لا يعلل، لأن سبب الاحتياج إلى العلة، هو الجواز لترجيح جانب الوجود فعالميته مثلاً لا تعلل بالعلم، بل يكون هو عالم بالذات بخلاف عالميتنا فإنها جائزة الذات، فإن والجواب : بعد تسليم كون العالمية أمراً وراء العالم معللاً به كما هو رأى مثبتي الأحوال أن وجوبها ليس بمعنى كونها واجبة الوجود لذاتها، ليمتنع تعليلها، بل بمعنى امتناع خلو الذات عنها، وهو لا ينافي كونها معللة بصفة ناشئة عن اللازم للذات قد يكون بوسط.
The third: It is for the Mu'tazila that His being knowing is necessary due to the impossibility of ignorance upon Him, and due to the impossibility of His needing an agent to make Him knowing, and likewise the remaining [attributes]. And the necessary is not caused, because the reason for the need for a cause is possibility to prefer one side of existence. So His being knowing, for example, is not caused by knowledge, but rather He is knowing by essence, unlike our being knowing which is possible. The answer: After conceding that being-knowing is something beyond the knower and caused by it, as is the view of the affirmers of modes, that its necessity is not in the sense of being necessary of existence in itself, so as to preclude its being caused, but rather in the sense of the impossibility of the essence being devoid of it, and that does not contradict its being caused by an attribute arising from what is necessary for the essence, sometimes through an intermediary.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof of the Mu'tazila regarding God's attribute of knowledge, followed by its answer. The Mu'tazila argue that God's being knowing is necessary (wajib) because ignorance is impossible for Him and He cannot depend on an external agent to make Him know; similarly for other attributes. The necessary does not require a cause because the need for a cause arises only from possibility (jawaz) to tip the scale toward existence. Thus His knowledge is uncaused; He is knowing by essence, in contrast to human knowledge which is possible. The answer concedes that if we accept that 'being-knowing' is something over and above the knower and is caused, as the proponents of modes (ahwal) hold, then its necessity does not mean that it is necessary of existence in itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi), which would preclude it being caused, but rather means that the essence cannot be devoid of it. This does not contradict its being caused by an attribute that arises from what is necessary for the essence, sometimes through an intermediary.

Key Terms
العالمية
الواجب
العلة
الجواز
العلم بالذات
مثبتي الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an attribute is necessary for God, it cannot be caused at all.
Correction: Necessity here means the essence cannot be devoid of the attribute, not that the attribute is necessary of existence in itself. Thus it can still be caused by an attribute necessarily arising from the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila's third proof, why is God's being knowing not caused?
A) Because knowledge is an accident that cannot subsist in God.
B) Because the necessary does not require a cause, and need for a cause arises from possibility.
C) Because God's knowledge is identical with His essence.
D) Because the attributes are eternal and thus uncaused.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response clarifies that an attribute can be necessary in the sense of being inseparable from the essence, yet still be caused by an attribute arising from the essence, resolving the Mu'tazili objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that God's knowledge is both necessary (inseparable) and caused (by His essence). If God's attributes are perfectly grounded in His being, what does that say about the reliability of His knowledge of your deepest secrets? Will you live today with the confidence that He knows you fully and loves you still, or will you hide from His gaze?
?
The Mu'tazila assumed necessity precludes causation. In your own character, do you treat your flaws as unchangeable necessities? What if, like the Ash'ari understanding, your bad habits are necessary only in the sense of being deeply ingrained, but can be caused—and thus changed—by turning to the Root of your being? Will you take one concrete step today to re-examine a trait you thought was fixed?
?
The Ash'ari answer requires careful thinking about what we mean by 'necessary.' When you say 'God is all-knowing,' do you reduce it to a mere slogan, or do you ponder that His knowledge is both essential and active, always present and always fresh? Will you let this truth transform your prayer—talking to a God whose knowledge is not a static fact but a living connection?

The Fourth Proof: The Dilemma of Temporal vs Eternal Attributes
Taftazani presents the fourth proof as a dilemma against attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof against attributes, which presents a dilemma that either attributes are temporal, implying impossible consequences, or eternal, implying polytheism.

Core Idea
Attributes cannot be temporal or eternal without contradiction.

Taftazani presents what he calls the main support for those who deny attributes. The proof is a simple dilemma: if the attributes are temporal, then God would be subject to change and imperfection; if they are eternal, then there would be multiple eternal beings, which is polytheism. This lesson examines the proof and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the two conditional statements starting with 'إما أن تكون حادثة...' and 'وإما أن تكون قديمة...'.
Note that the author calls this the 'main support' (العمدة الوثقى) for attribute deniers.
2
Identify the two horns
Identify the two possibilities (temporal and eternal) and the conclusions drawn from each.
Each horn leads to an unacceptable conclusion that is considered false by consensus.
3
Understand why each conclusion is false
For the temporal horn: it implies temporal accidents in God, lack of perfections in pre-eternity, and actions with conditions. For the eternal horn: it implies multiple eternal beings, which is polytheism.
The example of the Christians shows that even two eternal beings are condemned.
4
Reflect on the dilemma
Consider why this passage is called the most important proof for attribute deniers. What does it accomplish?
It forces the opponent to either accept impossible consequences or reject the premise that attributes exist as separate entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : وهو العمدة الوثقى لنفات الصفات من المليين ، أنها إما أن تكون حادثة فيلزم قيام الحوادث بذاته، وخلوه في الأزل عن العلم والقدرة والحياة وغيرها من الكمالات وصدورها عنه بالقصد والاختيار أو بشرائط حادثة لا بداية لها، والكل باطل بالاتفاق، وإما أن تكون قديمة فيلزم تعدد القدماء وهو كفر بإجماع المسلمين ، وقد كفرت النصارى بزيادة قديمين، فكيف بأكثر ؟ وأجيب : بأنا لا نسلم تغاير الذات مع الصفات ولا الصفات بعضها مع البعض ليثبت التعدد فإن الغيرين هما اللذان يمكن لانفكاك أحدهما عن الآخر بمكان أو زمان، أو بوجود وعدم أو هما ذاتان ليست إحديهما الأخرى وتفسيرهما بالشيئين أو الموجودين أو الاثنين ،فاسد لأن الغير من الأسماء الإضافية، ولا إشعار في هذا التفسير بذلك قال صاحب التبصرة (١) ، وكذا تفسيرهما بالشيئين من حيث إن أحدهما ليس هو الآخر، لصدقه على الكل مع الجزء كالعشرة مع الواحد، وزيد مع رأسه مع أنه لم يقل أحد يكون الجزء غير الكل إلا جعفر بن حرب من المعتزلة، وعد هذا من جهالاته، لأن العشرة اسم للمجموع لا يتناول لكل فرد مع أغياره فلو كان الواحد غير العشرة لصار غير نفسه، لأنه من العشرة ؛ ولن تكون العشرة بدونه وقال أيضاً: كل الشيء ليس غيره، لأن الشيء لا يغاير نفسه. وأعجب من هذا من قال : لو كان الغيران هما الاثنان لكان الغيران والاثنان ليس بمستعمل، والغير مستعمل . عقلية . والقول ما قال إمام الحرمين : إن إيضاح معنى الغيرين مما لا يدل عليه قضية ولا دلالة قطعية سمعية، فلا يقطع ببطلان قول من قال : كل شيئين غيران . نعم يقطع بالمنع من إطلاق الغيرية في صفات الباري وذاته لاتفاق الأمة على ذلك. ثم قال : ولا يتحاشى من إطلاق القول بأن الصفات موجودات ، والعلم مع الذات موجودان وكذا جميع الصفات، فظهر أن القول بالتعدد لا يتوقف على القول بالتغاير. فقولنا : ولو سلم معناه لو سلم التغاير ،أو التعدد بدون التغاير.
The fourth: And it is the main support for those who deny attributes from among the religious people: that either they are temporal, which implies the occurrence of temporal accidents in His essence, and His being devoid in pre-eternity of knowledge, power, life and other perfections, and their issuing from Him by will and choice or by temporal conditions without beginning – all false by consensus; or they are eternal, which implies multiplicity of eternal beings, which is disbelief by consensus of the Muslims; the Christians were disbelieved for adding two eternal beings, so how about more? And the answer: We do not concede the otherness (taghayur) of the essence with the attributes, nor the attributes with each other, to establish multiplicity; for the two 'others' (al-ghiran) are those which can be separated from each other in place or time, or in existence and non-existence, or they are two essences neither of which is the other. And interpreting them as two things or two existents or two is invalid, because 'other' is a relative term, and there is no indication in this interpretation of that. The author of al-Tabsira said: Likewise interpreting them as two things in the sense that one is not the other, because that applies to the whole with the part, like ten with one, and Zayd with his head, even though no one said that the part is other than the whole except Ja'far ibn Harb from the Mu'tazila, and this was counted among his ignorances; because ten is a name for the whole, not including each individual with its others, so if one were other than ten, it would become other than itself, because it is part of ten; and ten would not exist without it. He also said: Everything is not other than itself, because a thing does not differ from itself. And more amazing than this is what he said: If the two 'others' were two, then 'the two others' and 'the two' are not used, while 'other' is used, intellectual. And the statement is what Imam al-Haramayn said: Indeed, clarifying the meaning of the two others is something that no speculative reasoning nor decisive scriptural indication demonstrates; so one cannot definitively rule that the statement 'every two things are two others' is false. Yes, one definitively rules the forbiddance of applying the term 'otherness' to the attributes of the Creator and His essence due to the consensus of the community. Then he said: And one should not shrink from saying that the attributes are existents, and that knowledge and the essence are two existents, and likewise all attributes. Thus it becomes apparent that the statement of multiplicity does not depend on the statement of otherness. So our saying: 'And if conceded' means if otherness is conceded, or multiplicity without otherness.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, which the author calls the main support (al-umda al-wuthqa) for those who deny attributes among the religious people (including some Muslims who deny eternal attributes). The proof is a dilemma: either the attributes are temporal (hawadith) or eternal (qadima). If temporal, that implies that temporal accidents subsist in God's essence, which is impossible, and also that God would be devoid of perfections in pre-eternity, and that actions issue from Him under temporal conditions without beginning – all false by consensus. If eternal, then there would be multiple eternal beings, which is disbelief by consensus; the Christians were disbelieved for affirming two eternal beings (the Father and the Son? or Persons), so how about more? The answer: We do not concede otherness (taghayur) between the essence and the attributes, nor between the attributes themselves, so multiplicity does not follow. The passage then examines the meaning of 'other' (ghayr). Two things are 'others' if they can be separated in place, time, or existence, or they are two essences neither of which is the other. Interpreting 'other' as simply 'two things' or 'two existents' is insufficient because 'other' is a relative term. The author of al-Tabsira (Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi) is quoted: interpreting them as two things in the sense that one is not the other is problematic because that would apply to the whole and its part (e.g., ten and one), but no one says the part is other than the whole except Ja'far ibn Harb, who was deemed ignorant; because ten is a name for the whole, and if one were other than ten, it would be other than itself since it is part of ten. The quote continues: 'Everything is not other than itself' because a thing does not differ from itself. Then a surprising statement: if two 'others' meant simply two, then the term 'two others' and 'two' would be synonymous, but 'others' is used while 'two' alone is not used in that sense (indicating a difference). The final word is from Imam al-Haramayn: the meaning of 'two others' cannot be definitively established by reason or decisive revealed proof, so one cannot definitively rule that every two things are two others. However, we definitively forbid applying the term 'otherness' to God's essence and attributes due to consensus. Moreover, one may still say that the attributes are existents, and knowledge and the essence are two existents, so multiplicity does not require otherness. The passage ends with the author's own statement: 'And if conceded' – meaning if otherness is conceded, or multiplicity without otherness.

Key Terms

الحادث
القديم
تعدد القدماء

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what is the consequence if attributes are eternal?
A) They would be temporal and thus impossible.
B) There would be multiple eternal beings, which is polytheism.
C) They would be identical to the essence.
D) They would be nonexistent.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof argues that attributes cannot be temporal or eternal without leading to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This dilemma forces you to choose between two unacceptable options about God's attributes. Have you ever felt your own reasoning about God reach a dead end? Are you willing to submit to the consensus of scholars when your logic fails, or do you insist on finding your own answer at all costs?
?
The passage states that God would be devoid of knowledge and power in pre-eternity if attributes were temporal. Does your daily worship reflect a deep conviction that God has always been and always will be All-Knowing and All-Powerful? Or do you sometimes act as if God's attributes are limited?
?
When you learn that the Christians were condemned for affirming two eternal beings, do you examine your own heart for any subtle association of partners with God? In what area of your life do you act as if something other than God is eternal or ultimate?

Divine Attributes

Denying Otherness: The Answer to the Fourth Proof
Taftazani provides the answer to the fourth proof by denying otherness and explaining the meaning of 'other'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the answer to the fourth proof, which denies otherness (taghayur) between essence and attributes, and the technical meaning of 'other' (ghayr) in kalam.

Core Idea
Affirming multiple attributes does not imply polytheism unless otherness is established.

The fourth proof seemed to corner those who affirm eternal attributes, but the answer is to deny that the attributes are 'other' than the essence in the sense that would make them separate beings. This lesson unpacks the concept of otherness and shows that multiplicity of existents does not entail multiplicity of gods.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it starts with 'We do not concede otherness'.
The answer directly challenges the assumption that attributes are 'other' than the essence.
2
Understand the definition of 'two others'
The passage defines al-ghiran as things that can be separated in place, time, or existence, or two essences neither of which is the other.
This definition is crucial for showing that attributes are not 'others' in this sense.
3
Examine the refutation of the simpler interpretation
The author refutes the interpretation that 'two others' simply means 'two things', using the example of ten and one.
This example shows that the part-whole relationship does not constitute otherness.
4
Consider Imam al-Haramayn's position
Imam al-Haramayn states that the meaning of 'other' cannot be definitively established, but it is forbidden to apply the term to God's attributes due to consensus.
However, one may affirm that attributes are existents without implying otherness.
5
Connect to the original proof
Reflect on how this answer dissolves the dilemma: even if we concede multiplicity of eternal existents, we do not concede otherness, so polytheism does not follow.
The author notes that 'and if conceded' refers to conceding otherness or multiplicity without otherness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : وهو العمدة الوثقى لنفات الصفات من المليين ، أنها إما أن تكون حادثة فيلزم قيام الحوادث بذاته، وخلوه في الأزل عن العلم والقدرة والحياة وغيرها من الكمالات وصدورها عنه بالقصد والاختيار أو بشرائط حادثة لا بداية لها، والكل باطل بالاتفاق، وإما أن تكون قديمة فيلزم تعدد القدماء وهو كفر بإجماع المسلمين ، وقد كفرت النصارى بزيادة قديمين، فكيف بأكثر ؟ وأجيب : بأنا لا نسلم تغاير الذات مع الصفات ولا الصفات بعضها مع البعض ليثبت التعدد فإن الغيرين هما اللذان يمكن لانفكاك أحدهما عن الآخر بمكان أو زمان، أو بوجود وعدم أو هما ذاتان ليست إحديهما الأخرى وتفسيرهما بالشيئين أو الموجودين أو الاثنين ،فاسد لأن الغير من الأسماء الإضافية، ولا إشعار في هذا التفسير بذلك قال صاحب التبصرة (١) ، وكذا تفسيرهما بالشيئين من حيث إن أحدهما ليس هو الآخر، لصدقه على الكل مع الجزء كالعشرة مع الواحد، وزيد مع رأسه مع أنه لم يقل أحد يكون الجزء غير الكل إلا جعفر بن حرب من المعتزلة، وعد هذا من جهالاته، لأن العشرة اسم للمجموع لا يتناول لكل فرد مع أغياره فلو كان الواحد غير العشرة لصار غير نفسه، لأنه من العشرة ؛ ولن تكون العشرة بدونه وقال أيضاً: كل الشيء ليس غيره، لأن الشيء لا يغاير نفسه. وأعجب من هذا من قال : لو كان الغيران هما الاثنان لكان الغيران والاثنان ليس بمستعمل، والغير مستعمل . عقلية . والقول ما قال إمام الحرمين : إن إيضاح معنى الغيرين مما لا يدل عليه قضية ولا دلالة قطعية سمعية، فلا يقطع ببطلان قول من قال : كل شيئين غيران . نعم يقطع بالمنع من إطلاق الغيرية في صفات الباري وذاته لاتفاق الأمة على ذلك. ثم قال : ولا يتحاشى من إطلاق القول بأن الصفات موجودات ، والعلم مع الذات موجودان وكذا جميع الصفات، فظهر أن القول بالتعدد لا يتوقف على القول بالتغاير. فقولنا : ولو سلم معناه لو سلم التغاير ،أو التعدد بدون التغاير.
The fourth: And it is the main support for those who deny attributes from among the religious people: that either they are temporal, which implies the occurrence of temporal accidents in His essence, and His being devoid in pre-eternity of knowledge, power, life and other perfections, and their issuing from Him by will and choice or by temporal conditions without beginning – all false by consensus; or they are eternal, which implies multiplicity of eternal beings, which is disbelief by consensus of the Muslims; the Christians were disbelieved for adding two eternal beings, so how about more? And interpreting them as two things or two existents or two is invalid, because 'other' is a relative term, and there is no indication in this interpretation of that. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If attributes are eternal existents, they must be separate gods.
Correction: The answer shows that multiple existents do not necessarily mean otherness; the attributes are not 'other' in a way that would make them independent beings, so polytheism does not follow.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the argument from multiplicity of eternal beings considered invalid by the answer?
A) Because attributes are not existent at all.
B) Because we do not concede otherness between essence and attributes, so multiplicity does not imply polytheism.
C) Because the attributes are temporal and not eternal.
D) Because the number of attributes is unlimited.

Key Takeaway
Affirming multiple attributes does not imply polytheism because otherness between essence and attributes is not conceded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes—His knowledge, power, mercy—do you imagine them as separate 'parts' or aspects that could exist independently of Him? This passage warns against such otherness. Ask yourself: in your heart, do you truly believe that God's attributes are not 'other' than His essence, or do you unconsciously treat them as semi-independent gods? What would change in your worship if you fully internalized that His attributes are nothing other than Him?
?
Imam al-Haramayn admits that the precise meaning of 'otherness' cannot be definitively established by reason or revelation. Are you comfortable with that uncertainty, or do you demand clear-cut definitions for every theological term? Reflect on an area of your faith where you have insisted on certainty—has that led you to make claims beyond what the pious predecessors would approve?
?
The passage states that applying 'otherness' to God's attributes is forbidden by consensus of the community. Have you ever spoken about God in a way that implied separation between His attributes and His essence—perhaps by saying 'God has knowledge' as if knowledge were a separate thing? Repentance begins with awareness: will you now watch your tongue and heart to ensure you never inadvertently diminish God's oneness?

Divine Attributes

The Two Senses of Qadim and Divine Attributes
Al-Taftazani distinguishes two meanings of qadim and defends attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the pre-eternity of divine attributes does not imply they are self-subsistent entities alongside God, and how al-Taftazani distinguishes essential from temporal qadim.

Core Idea
Attributes are pre-eternal in a temporal sense, not as self-subsistent entities.

Al-Taftazani faces a subtle objection: if attributes are eternal, does that not make them multiple eternal entities, leading to polytheism? In this passage, he carefully distinguishes two meanings of 'qadim' (ancient) and shows that divine attributes are only qadim in a temporal sense—they are not self-subsistent. This distinction protects Islamic monotheism while affirming the reality of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source passage carefully. Notice how al-Taftazani presents a series of concessions to build his argument.
2
Identify the two concessions
Find where al-Taftazani says 'even if it is conceded' (wa law sallama). There are two such concessive steps. Identify what he concedes and what he denies.
3
Understand the distinction between essential and temporal qadim
Essential qadim means not preceded by another being (self-subsistent). Temporal qadim means not preceded by non-existence. Attributes are qadim only in the temporal sense.
This is the key doctrinal point.
4
See why this refutes the charge of polytheism
Even if multiplicity of essential qadim were kufr, multiplicity of temporal qadim (attributes) is not. And even if all multiplicity of qadim were kufr, it would only apply to essences, not attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقول بأزلية الصفات لا تستلزم القول بقدمها لكونه أخص، فإن القديم الأزل القائم بنفسه، ولو سلم أن كل أزلي قديم. فلا نسلم أن القول بتعدد القديم مطلقاً كفر بالإجماع، بل في القدم الذاتي بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالغير، وقدم الصفات زماني، بمعنى كونها غير مسبوقة بالعدم. ولو سلم أن القول بتعدد القديم كفر ذاتياً كان أو زمانياً، فلا نسلم ذلك في الصفات، بل في الذوات خاصة، أعني ما تقوم بأنفسها والنصارى وإن لم يجعلوا الأقانيم القديمة ذوات، لكن لزمهم القول بذلك حيث جوزوا عليها الانتقال!، وقد سبق بيان ذلك، وقوله تعالى : ( وما من إله إلا إله واحد) بعد قوله : (لقد كفر الذين قالوا إن الله ثالث ثلاثة ) شاهد صدق على أنهم كانوا يقولون بآلهة ثلاثة فأين هذا من القول بإله واحد له صفات كمال كما نطق بها كتابه.
So the statement of the pre-eternity (azaliyya) of the attributes does not necessitate the statement of their being qadim (ancient) because it is more specific; for the qadim al-azal is that which is self-subsistent. And even if it is conceded that every azali is qadim, we do not concede that the statement of multiplicity of qadim absolutely is kufr by consensus, rather it is [kufr] in [the case of] essential qadim (al-qidam al-dhati), meaning the absence of being preceded by another, while the qadim of the attributes is temporal (zamani), meaning their being not preceded by non-existence. And even if it is conceded that the statement of multiplicity of qadim, whether essential or temporal, is kufr, we do not concede that in the attributes, rather in essences specifically, I mean what subsists by themselves. And the Christians, although they did not make the ancient hypostases (aqanim) essences, they are nevertheless forced to that statement because they allowed them to undergo transmission (intiqal); and this has been explained earlier. And His statement: 'And there is no god but one God' after His statement: 'They have certainly disbelieved who say that Allah is the third of three' is a true witness that they were saying three gods. So where is this from the statement of one God having attributes of perfection as His book has spoken of?
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage argues that asserting the pre-eternity (azaliyya) of the divine attributes does not entail that they are qadim in the same sense as the divine essence. The author explains that the term 'qadim al-azal' refers to something self-subsistent (qa'im bi-nafsihi). Even if we concede that everything azali is qadim, we do not accept that multiplicity of qadim is universally kufr by consensus. Instead, kufr applies only to essential qadim (al-qidam al-dhati), which means not being preceded by another being. The attributes, however, are qadim in a temporal sense (zamani), meaning they are not preceded by non-existence. The author then addresses a stronger concession: even if multiplicity of any kind of qadim were kufr, it would apply only to essences (dhawat), i.e., self-subsistent entities, not to attributes. The Christians are then refuted: although they do not explicitly call their hypostases (aqanim) ancient essences, their doctrine that the hypostases undergo transmission (intiqal) forces them to admit they are self-subsistent. The Qur'anic verse from Surat al-Ma'idah (5:73) is cited as evidence that Christians believed in three gods, not one God with attributes. The passage concludes by contrasting Christian tritheism with Islamic monotheism, which affirms one God with attributes of perfection.

Key Terms
الأزلية
القدم
الذاتي
الزماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pre-eternity of attributes means they are multiple self-subsistent eternal entities alongside God.
Correction: Attributes are pre-eternal only in the sense of not being preceded by non-existence (temporal qadim), not as self-subsistent entities (essential qadim).

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, in what sense are the divine attributes qadim?
A) Essential qadim (self-subsistent)
B) Temporal qadim (not preceded by non-existence)
C) Both essential and temporal
D) Neither essential nor temporal

Key Takeaway
Attributes are pre-eternal in a temporal sense, not as self-subsistent entities, thus avoiding multiplicity of eternal beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say that God's attributes are eternal, do you ever unconsciously imagine them as separate entities alongside God? How does the distinction between essential and temporal pre-eternity in this passage challenge that imagination? Refuting Christian Trinity: Hypostases and Quranic Evidence
Al-Taftazani refutes Christian trinitarian claims using logical and Quranic arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes Christian trinitarian doctrine by showing their hypostases imply self-subsistent entities, and how the Quran establishes strict monotheism.

Core Idea
Christian hypostases, if allowed transmission, become self-subsistent gods.

Al-Taftazani now turns to a specific historical opponent: Christian trinitarianism. He argues that even though Christians do not explicitly call their three hypostases (aqanim) 'ancient essences', their doctrine forces them to admit that these are self-subsistent because they allow them to undergo transmission (intiqal). He then cites the Quran to show that Christians believed in three gods, not one God with attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the remaining Arabic passage and translation
Focus on the part from 'والنصارى' to the end. Notice how al-Taftazani links the Christian position to the earlier discussion of essences.
2
Understand the Christian position as described
Christians do not explicitly say the hypostases are essences, but they claim the hypostases can undergo transmission (e.g., incarnation). Al-Taftazani argues that this implies they are self-subsistent.
Transmission (intiqal) is a key term.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Al-Taftazani quotes Quran 5:73 — 'They have certainly disbelieved who say that Allah is the third of three' — and the preceding verse about one God. He uses this to prove Christians believed in three gods.
4
Contrast tritheism with Islamic monotheism
The passage ends by contrasting Christian belief in three gods with Islamic belief in one God who has attributes of perfection, as revealed in the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So the statement of the pre-eternity (azaliyya) of the attributes does not necessitate the statement of their being qadim (ancient) because it is more specific; for the qadim al-azal is that which is self-subsistent. And even if it is conceded that every azali is qadim, we do not concede that the statement of multiplicity of qadim absolutely is kufr by consensus, rather it is [kufr] in [the case of] essential qadim (al-qidam al-dhati), meaning the absence of being preceded by another, while the qadim of the attributes is temporal (zamani), meaning their being not preceded by non-existence. And the Christians, although they did not make the ancient hypostases (aqanim) essences, they are nevertheless forced to that statement because they allowed them to undergo transmission (intiqal); and this has been explained earlier. And His statement: 'And there is no god but one God' after His statement: 'They have certainly disbelieved who say that Allah is the third of three' is a true witness that they were saying three gods. So where is this from the statement of one God having attributes of perfection as His book has spoken of?
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage argues that asserting the pre-eternity (azaliyya) of the divine attributes does not entail that they are qadim in the same sense as the divine essence. The author explains that the term 'qadim al-azal' refers to something self-subsistent (qa'im bi-nafsihi). Even if we concede that everything azali is qadim, we do not accept that multiplicity of qadim is universally kufr by consensus. Instead, kufr applies only to essential qadim (al-qidam al-dhati), which means not being preceded by another being. The attributes, however, are qadim in a temporal sense (zamani), meaning they are not preceded by non-existence. The author then addresses a stronger concession: even if multiplicity of any kind of qadim were kufr, it would apply only to essences (dhawat), i.e., self-subsistent entities, not to attributes. The Christians are then refuted: although they do not explicitly call their hypostases (aqanim) ancient essences, their doctrine that the hypostases undergo transmission (intiqal) forces them to admit they are self-subsistent. The Qur'anic verse from Surat al-Ma'idah (5:73) is cited as evidence that Christians believed in three gods, not one God with attributes. The passage concludes by contrasting Christian tritheism with Islamic monotheism, which affirms one God with attributes of perfection.
