Why Eternal Attributes Do Not Imply Multiple Pre-Eternal Entities
The author refutes the claim that eternal attributes imply multiplicity of pre-eternal entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinctions that prevent the attributes of Allah from being considered multiple pre-eternal entities.

Core Idea
Affirming eternal attributes does not imply multiple gods — the distinction between essence and attribute matters.

This passage tackles a common accusation: that Muslims who affirm eternal attributes for Allah are committing the same error as Christians who believe in three hypostases. The author, through careful conceptual distinctions, shows why this charge is invalid. You'll learn the difference between eternal (azali) and pre-eternal (qadim), essential vs. temporal pre-eternity, and why attributes are not essences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish azali from qadim
Read the opening lines where the author notes that 'azaliyya' (eternity) does not entail 'qidam' (pre-eternity) because the latter is more specific. Understand that 'qadim' properly refers to what subsists by itself.
This is a crucial semantic distinction.
2
Understand the two senses of qidam
Identify the two types of pre-eternity: essential (dhati) meaning not preceded by another, and temporal (zamani) meaning not preceded by non-existence. The author applies the charge of kufr only to essential pre-eternity, not temporal.
Attributes have temporal pre-eternity.
3
See the Christian example and Quranic proof
Notice how the author uses the Christian belief in hypostases (aqanim) to show that even they did not call them essences, yet their belief in transition forced them to treat them as such. Then reflect on the Quranic verse contrasting Christian tritheism with Islamic tawhid.
The verse proves Christians believed in three separate gods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو فالقول بأزلية الصفات لا تستلزم القول بقدمها لكونه أخص ، فإن القديم الأزل القائم بنفسه، ولو سلم أن كل أزلي .قديم. فلا نسلم أن القول بتعدد القديم مطلقاً كفر بالإجماع، بل في القدم الذاتي بمعنى عدم المسبوقية بالغير، وقدم الصفات زماني، بمعنى كونها غير مسبوقة بالعدم . ولو سلم أن القول بتعدد القديم كفر ذاتياً كان أو زمانياً، فلا نسلم ذلك في الصفات، بل في الذوات خاصة، أعني ما تقوم بأنفسها والنصارى وإن لم يجعلوا الأقانيم القديمة ذوات، لكن لزمهم القول بذلك حيث جوزوا عليها الانتقال ! وقد سبق بيان ذلك، وقوله تعالى : ( وما من إله إلا إله واحد) بعد قوله : (لقد كفر الذين قالوا إن الله ثالث ثلاثة ) شاهد صدق على أنهم كانوا يقولون بآلهة ثلاثة فأين هذا من القول بإله واحد له صفات كمال كما نطق بها كتابه
It is that the statement that the attributes are eternal (azaliyya) does not entail the statement that they are pre-eternal (qadim), because the latter is more specific. For pre-eternal (al-qadim) is the eternal (al-azal) subsisting by itself. And if it is granted that every eternal (azali) is pre-eternal (qadim), we do not grant that the statement of multiplicity of the pre-eternal is absolutely unbelief by consensus, rather it is in the essential pre-eternity (al-qidam al-dhati) meaning the absence of being preceded by another, and the pre-eternity of the attributes is temporal (zamani), meaning they are not preceded by non-existence. And if it is granted that the statement of multiplicity of the pre-eternal is unbelief whether essential or temporal, we do not grant that regarding the attributes, but rather regarding the essences (dhawat) specifically, meaning what subsists by themselves. And the Christians, even if they did not make the pre-eternal hypostases (aqanim) essences, it was necessary for them to say that where they permitted transition upon them, and this has been explained previously. And His statement, the Exalted: 'And there is no god but one God' after His statement: 'They have certainly disbelieved who say that Allah is the third of three' is a true witness that they were asserting three deities. So where is this compared to the statement of one God having attributes of perfection as His Book has pronounced?
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This is a doctrinal argument refuting the claim that affirming eternal attributes entails believing in multiple eternal entities (ta'addud al-qadim). The author begins by distinguishing between 'azaliyya' (eternity without beginning) and 'qidam' (pre-eternity), noting that the latter is more specific (akhas). He explains that 'qadim' in its proper sense refers to the eternal subsisting by itself, so not every azali is necessarily qadim in that sense. Even if one concedes that every azali is qadim, the charge of kufr for affirming multiple qadims is not absolute; it applies only to essential pre-eternity (qidam dhati) meaning the attribute of not being preceded by another, whereas the attributes' pre-eternity is temporal (qidam zamani)—they simply had no beginning. Even if one accepts that multiplicity of qadim is kufr in both senses, the author insists this applies only to essences (dhawat) that subsist by themselves, not to attributes. He then invokes the Christian example: they did not call their hypostases (aqanim) essences, but their belief in transition upon them necessitated that they treat them as such, and this has been refuted earlier. Finally, he cites the Quranic verse 'And there is no god but one God' after 'They have certainly disbelieved who say that Allah is the third of three' to show that the Christians believed in three separate gods (not a single God with attributes). Thus, the Muslim affirmation of one God with attributes is distinct from Christian tritheism.

D) There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
Affirming eternal attributes does not entail multiplicity of pre-eternal entities; the attributes are not essences and their pre-eternity is temporal, not essential.

Four Weak Doubts Against Divine Attributes
Sanusi enumerates four weak doubts against attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four weak doubts raised against divine attributes and why the author dismisses them as very weak.

Core Idea
Four objections against attributes are all fundamentally weak.

Sanusi presents a concise summary of four arguments used by opponents to deny divine attributes. These arguments touch on composition, exclusivity of pre-eternity, lack of proof, and the nature of indwelling. He dismisses each as 'very weak' (da'if jiddan), signaling that they do not withstand scrutiny. In this lesson, we will examine each doubt and understand why they fail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise, dismissive tone of the author.
The passage ends with a repetition indicating these are further weak doubts.
2
Identify the four doubts
List each of the four objections: composition, pre-eternity as exclusive characteristic, lack of proof, and indwelling implying location.
Each is introduced by 'و' (and) except the first which follows 'بأن'.
3
Understand why each is weak
Reflect on why Sanusi calls them very weak. The commentary suggests they rely on false assumptions or invalid reasoning.
For example, composition does not apply to simple essence; pre-eternity is not exclusive to God.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the quiz question to confirm you can count and identify the four doubts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما التمسك بأنه لو اتصف بالصفات لزم التركيب في الحقيقة الإلهية وبأن القدم أخص أوصاف الإله والكاشف عن حقيقته، فلو اشتركت الصفات فيه لكانت آلهة، وبأنه لا دليل على الصفات فيجب نفيها وبأنه لا يعقل من القيام إلا التبعية في التحيز فيلزم تحيز الباري فضعيف جداً) . قال : وأما التمسك إشارة إلى شبه أخرى ضعيفة جداً.
He said: As for the adherence to [the claim] that if He were qualified with attributes, composition would be necessary in the divine reality, and that pre-eternity is the most specific characteristic of the divine and the revealer of His reality, so if the attributes shared in it they would be deities, and that there is no proof for the attributes so they must be denied, and that no meaning of indwelling (qiyam) is understood except following/association in location (tahayyuz), so the localization of the Creator would be necessary—[this] is very weak. He said: As for the adherence, it refers to other very weak doubts.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage introduces a set of four objections that opponents raise against the affirmation of divine attributes. The author dismisses them all as 'very weak' (da'if jiddan). The four objections are: (1) composition (tarkib): if God had attributes, the divine reality would be composed, implying contingency; (2) pre-eternity (qidam) as the most specific characteristic of divinity: if attributes share in pre-eternity, they would be gods; (3) lack of proof: there is no rational or scriptural proof for attributes, so they must be denied; (4) indwelling (qiyam) of an attribute in a subject implies that the attribute occurs in a location following the subject, which would necessitate localization (tahayyuz) for God. The author then repeats that these are other weak doubts.

باري (Bari)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four objections are strong and sufficient to refute the belief in divine attributes.
Correction: The author classifies all four as 'very weak' (da'if jiddan), meaning they are not valid arguments.

Checkpoint

B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The four objections against divine attributes (composition, exclusivity of pre-eternity, lack of proof, and indwelling implying location) are all dismissed as very weak by Sanusi.

Does God Have Composition? Refuting the First Doubt
Sanusi presents and refutes the objection that attributes would make God composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response denies that the attributes imply composition in the divine essence, preserving God's oneness.

Core Idea
Attributes do not make God composite; they are necessitated by His essence.

The first doubt raised against the existence of attributes in God is that it would imply composition, making Him contingent. But this objection misunderstands the relationship between essence and attributes. In this passage, al-Sanusi presents the objection and gives the decisive answer: the divine reality is the essence that necessitates the attributes, not a composite of parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the logical steps: if God has attributes, then the divine reality would be composed of essence and attributes; every composite is contingent; therefore God would be contingent. This is impossible, so the existence of attributes is false.
Notice the term 'murakkab' (composite) and its link to contingency (mumkin).
2
Examine the answer: denial of implication
The answer (al-jawab) denies the implication (man' al-mulazama). It does not accept that attributes lead to composition in the usual sense.
This is a standard kalam move: breaking the logical chain.
3
Grasp the alternative: essence necessitates attributes
Sanusi states that the divine reality (haqiqat al-ilahiyya) is the essence that necessitates the attributes (al-dhat al-mujiba lil-sifat). This means attributes are not added parts but necessary concomitants of the essence.
This preserves true oneness while affirming real attributes.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutation defends against accusations of polytheism or multiplicity in God while maintaining the distinction of attributes in our understanding.
This is a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى : أنه لو كان موصوفاً بصفات قائمة بذاته؛ لكانت حقيقة الإلهية مركبة تلك الذات والصفات، وكل مركب ممكن لاحتياجه إلى الأجزاء. والجواب : منع من الملازمة بل حقيقة الإله تلك الذات الموجبة للصفات
The first: That if He were qualified with attributes subsisting in His essence, then the reality of divinity would be composed of that essence and the attributes, and every composite is contingent due to its need for parts. The answer: Denial of the implication; rather the divine reality is that essence which necessitates the attributes.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer unit. The objection states that if God is described with attributes subsisting in His essence, then the divine reality (haqiqat al-ilahiyya) would be composed of both the essence and the attributes. Since every composite (murakkab) is contingent (mumkin) because it needs its parts, God would be contingent, which is impossible. The answer (al-jawab) is a denial of the implication (man' al-mulazama). The author argues that the divine reality is not a composite; rather it is the essence that necessitates the attributes (al-dhat al-mujiba lil-sifat). In other words, the attributes are not separate parts added to the essence, but the essence itself is such that it necessarily entails the attributes, so no real composition occurs.

Key Terms
موصوف (mawsuf)
صفات (sifat)
قيام (qiyam)
حقيقة (haqiqa)
إلهية (ilahiyya)
مركب (murakkab)
ممكن (mumkin)
أجزاء (ajza')
ملازمة (mulazama)
موجبة (mujiba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari view of attributes as distinct from the essence entails composition in the sense of part-whole, making God composite and thus contingent.
Correction: Attributes are not parts; they are necessary concomitants of the essence. The essence necessitates them, so there is no real composition.

Checkpoint
What is the answer (jawab) to the objection that attributes would make God composite?
A) The objection is valid and must be accepted.
B) The divine reality is the essence that necessitates the attributes, so no composition occurs.
C) Attributes are not real but merely mental concepts.
D) God's essence is unknowable, so the objection cannot be answered.

Key Takeaway
God's attributes do not make Him composite because His essence necessarily entails them; the divine reality is one, not a compound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Second Doubt: Pre-Eternity as Exclusive Divine Characteristic - Refutation
Refutation of pre-eternity as exclusive divine characteristic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why pre-eternity (qidam) cannot be the exclusive distinguishing characteristic of God, and that necessity of existence (wujub al-wujudiyya) is the true distinguishing property.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity is not unique to God; necessary existence is what truly distinguishes the Divine.

This lesson examines a problematic claim: that pre-eternity (qidam) is the most specific characteristic that reveals God's reality. The objection argues that if attributes share pre-eternity with God, they would also share divinity. The answer clarifies that the true distinguishing characteristic is not pre-eternity but the necessity of existence. We will analyze this objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the claim that pre-eternity is the most specific characteristic and the revealer of God's reality.
Pay attention to the logical consequence drawn.
2
Identify the logical consequence
The objection argues that if the attributes share pre-eternity with God, they would also share in divinity. This leads to the charge of polytheism, similar to Christian doctrines.
Consider why this is problematic for the orthodox position.
3
Understand the refutation
The answer denies that pre-eternity is the most specific characteristic and the revealer. Instead, it is the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujudiyya) that distinguishes God. Thus, attributes can be pre-eternal without being divine.
This preserves the distinction between God and His attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : أن القدم أخص أوصاف الإله ، والكاشف عن حقيقته، إذ به يعرف تميزه عن غيره ، فلو شاركته الصفات في القدم، لشاركته في الإلهية ، فيلزم من القول بها القول بالإلهية كما لزم النصارى . والجواب : منع الأخص كون الكاشف هو القدم بل وجوب الوجودية .
The second: That pre-eternity is the most specific characteristic of the divine and the revealer of His reality, for by it His distinction from others is known. So if the attributes shared with Him in pre-eternity, they would share in divinity. Thus the statement of [the attributes] necessitates the statement of divinity, as was incumbent upon the Christians. The answer: Denial that the most specific [characteristic] and the revealer is pre-eternity; rather it is the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujudiyya).
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This is another objection-and-answer. The objection argues that pre-eternity (qidam) is the most specific characteristic (akhas sifa) of God and the revealer (kashif) of His reality, i.e., the distinguishing property by which God is known from others. Therefore, if the attributes share in pre-eternity, they would also share in divinity, leading to polytheism (as with Christians). The answer denies that the most specific characteristic and revealer is pre-eternity; rather it is the necessity of existence (wujub al-wujudiyya) — i.e., the fact that God's existence is necessary (wajib al-wujud). Pre-eternity is not exclusive to God, since the attributes are also pre-eternal, but they are not divine because they do not possess necessary existence in themselves.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
أخص (akhas)
أوصاف (awsaf)
إله (ilah)
كاشف (kashif)
تميز (tamyiz)
إلهية (ilahiyya)
وجوب الوجودية (wujub al-wujudiyya)
نصارى (Nasara)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That pre-eternity (qidam) alone is sufficient to define divinity and distinguish God from all else.
Correction: The correct distinguishing characteristic is necessity of existence (wujub al-wujudiyya); pre-eternity is shared by attributes but they are not divine.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this objection, what is the true revealer (kashif) of God's reality?
A) Pre-eternity (qidam)
B) Power (qudra)
C) Knowledge ('ilm)
D) Necessity of existence (wujub al-wujudiyya)

Key Takeaway
The necessity of existence (wujub al-wujudiyya), not pre-eternity, is the true characteristic that distinguishes God from all else.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The objector in this passage equated pre-eternity with divinity. ?
The refutation centers on necessary existence as God's exclusive attribute. In your daily life, what do you treat as 'necessary' in the sense that you cannot function without it? (e.g., your phone, a habit, a person) Does that attitude belong only to God? Repent from any misplacement of ultimate dependence and realign your heart.

Refuting the Third Doubt: Lack of Proof for Attributes
The author records and refutes the third doubt about proof for attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the third doubt regarding the lack of proof for the divine attributes and how the author refutes it by denying both premises.

Core Idea
The claim that attributes lack proof fails because both its premises are false.

This lesson presents a classic objection against the reality of divine attributes: that neither rational nor scriptural proofs can establish them. The author dismisses it by denying both premises, showing that rational proofs are complete and scriptural proofs do indicate the ontological reality of the attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and the translation. Note the objection's structure: two premises leading to a conclusion.
The objection is concise, with a clear if-then logic.
2
Identify the two premises
The first premise: rational proofs are incomplete. The second: scriptural proofs only show certain attributes (living, knowing, etc.) which are not disputed.
Both premises are denied in the answer.
3
Understand the refutation
The answer is 'man' al-muqaddimatayn'—denial of both premises. Rational proofs are complete in kalam, and scriptural proofs do establish the reality of the attributes.
This refutation hinges on rejecting the grounds for doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : أنه لا دليل على هذه الصفات لأن الأدلة العقلية لا تتم، والسمعية لا تدل إلا على أنه حي عالم قادر إلى غير ذلك. والنزاع لم يقع فيه، وما لا دليل عليه يجب نفيه كما سبق مراراً . والجواب : منع المقدمتين.
The third: That there is no proof for these attributes because the rational proofs are not complete, and the scriptural proofs only indicate that He is living, knowing, powerful, etc., and there is no dispute about that. And what has no proof must be denied, as has been repeated many times. The answer: Denial of both premises.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This objection claims that there is no proof (dalil) for the attributes. It gives two reasons: (1) rational proofs (al-adilla al-'aqliyya) are not complete (la tatimm); (2) scriptural proofs (al-sam'iyya) only indicate that God is living, knowing, powerful, etc., but there is no dispute about that (i.e., the attributes themselves are conceded, but the objection is about their ontological status). Then it invokes the principle that what has no proof must be denied. The answer is a simple denial of both premises (man' al-muqaddimatayn). In other words, rational proofs are indeed complete (as established in kalam), and scriptural proofs do indicate not just the existence but the mode of the attributes as real and eternal.

Key Terms
دليل (dalil)
أدلة عقلية (adilla 'aqliyya)
أدلة سمعية (adilla sam'iyya)
حي (hayy)
عالم ('alim)
قادر (qadir)
نفي (nafy)
مقدمة (muqaddima)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Scriptural evidence for attributes is merely semantic and does not establish their ontological reality as distinct from the essence.
Correction: The passage denies the premise that scriptural proofs are insufficient; they do indicate the reality of attributes beyond mere names.

Checkpoint
What is the answer (jawab) to the argument that there is no proof for the attributes?
A) That rational proofs are incomplete.
B) That scriptural proofs are the only ones needed.
C) Denial of both premises that rational proofs are incomplete and scriptural proofs are insufficient.
D) That the attributes are not real and therefore need no proof.

Key Takeaway
The third doubt is refuted by denying both premises, affirming that rational proofs are complete and scriptural proofs establish the reality of the attributes.

Fourth Doubt: Attribute Indwelling and Location
The author refutes the objection that indwelling in God entails location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that attributes in God imply location and the response that redefines 'qiyam' as a specific relation (ikhtisas) rather than spatial indwelling.

Core Idea
Attributes in God are not like bodies in space; they are real relations.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth doubt against the reality of divine attributes. The objection argues that the only known sense of 'indwelling' is spatial occupation, which is impossible for God. The answer clarifies the meaning of 'qiyam' (indwelling) as a special relation that grounds God's descriptions, not a physical location.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the logic: qiyam implies location, tahayyuz impossible for God, so qiyam impossible.
Notice the syllogistic form.
2
Study the answer
The answer redefines qiyam as ikhtisas (specific relation) that prompts description by states and judgments.
3
Contrast the two views
Compare the objection's spatial understanding with the answer's relational understanding.
This is key to understanding Ash'ari attribute theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة : أنه لا يعقل من قيام الصفة بالموصوف إلا حصولها في الحيز تبعاً لحصوله، والتحيز على الله تعالى محال، فكذا قيام الصفات به. والجواب : أن معنى القيام هو الاختصاص الباعث على ما هو مرادكم باتصافه بالأحوال والأحكام.
The fourth: That no meaning of indwelling (qiyam) of the attribute in the qualified is understood except its occurrence in location (hayyiz) following his occurrence, and localization (tahayyuz) is impossible for Allah most high, so likewise is the indwelling of the attributes in Him. The answer: That the meaning of indwelling is the specific relation (ikhtisas) that prompts, according to what you intend, His qualification with states and judgments.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This final objection concerns the concept of 'qiyam' (indwelling, inherence). The objection argues that the only intelligible sense of an attribute inhering in a subject is that the attribute occurs in a location (hayyiz) following the subject's location. Since God cannot be localized (tahayyuz), it is impossible for attributes to inhere in Him. The answer redefines the meaning of qiyam: it is not spatial occupation but rather 'ikhtisas' (specific relation) that causes the qualified entity to be described with states (ahwal) and judgments (ahkam). In other words, the attributes are not in God like a body in a place; they are real relations that ground the descriptions of God as living, knowing, etc.

Key Terms
قيام (qiyam)
صفة (sifa)
موصوف (mawsuf)
حيز (hayyiz)
تحيز (tahayyuz)
اختصاص (ikhtisas)
أحوال (ahwal)
أحكام (ahkam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributes inhere in God like qualities in a body, occupying a location.
Correction: The answer shows that qiyam (indwelling) is a specific relation (ikhtisas) that leads to God being described with states and judgments, not spatial occupation.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what does 'qiyam' (indwelling) of attributes in God mean?
A) The attribute occupies a location in God's essence.
B) It is a specific relation (ikhtisas) that prompts description of God with states and judgments.
C) It is a metaphorical expression with no real meaning.
D) The attribute is identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'qiyam' for divine attributes is not spatial inherence but a specific relation that grounds God's description with states and judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes like 'All-Knowing' or 'All-Merciful', do you subconsciously imagine them as qualities he 'contains' inside Him, much like a container holds water? How does the lesson's redefinition of qiyam as a relation rather than location challenge that image? Will you actively replace that mental picture with a relational one? If so, how will you remind yourself starting today?
?
Consider a moment in your life where you felt God was 'far away' or 'close'. Was that feeling based on a spatial analogy? What would it mean to instead think of your relationship with God as a spiritual relation that is never a matter of distance but of attentiveness and obedience? Name one specific thought pattern you will change to stop thinking of God in spatial terms.
?
The objection in the passage seems logical but is mistaken because it assumes only one meaning of 'indwelling'. Can you recall a doubt you have had about your faith that seemed logical but rested on a false assumption? What premise might you be assuming incorrectly? Commit to writing that doubt down today and looking for how Scripture or sound reasoning reframes it.

Four Weak Objections to Divine Attributes and Their Answers
Four weak objections to divine attributes with answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four weak objections raised against divine attributes and the method of answering them by denying premises or entailments.

Core Idea
Weak objections are answered by denying their premises or entailments, not by conceding the argument.

In this passage, the author presents four objections that opponents have raised against the doctrine of divine attributes. These objections are considered very weak, but they reveal important points about how kalam reasoning works. The author shows that each objection fails because it either assumes a false entailment or relies on an incomplete understanding of theological terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the introduction and the four objections
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how each objection is introduced with 'الأولى', 'الثانية', etc. The author first summarises the objections as weak, then presents each with its answer.
The objections are numbered, making it easy to follow the structure.
2
Identify the pattern in the answers
Each answer either denies the entailment (ملازمة) or denies one of the premises (المقدمتين). Trace how this pattern works in each case.
The third answer is the simplest: 'منع المقدمتين' (denial of both premises).
3
Understand the key terms used
Terms like 'تركيب' (composition), 'قدم' (pre-eternity), 'كاشف' (discloser), 'قيام' (inherence), and 'اختصاص' (particularization) are central. Make sure you grasp their meanings in context.
The answer to the fourth objection redefines 'قيام' as 'اختصاص' to avoid the implication of spatial location.
4
Ask why these objections are considered weak
Reflect on why the author dismisses these objections as 'ضعيف جداً' (very weak). The answers show that the objections either misunderstand the nature of divine attributes or rely on assumptions that are not granted.
5
Connect to the broader lesson on divine attributes
This passage illustrates how theologians defend the doctrine of attributes against common criticisms. It also reveals the importance of precise definitions in kalam.
This lesson prepares you for more detailed discussions on each attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة أخرى للمخالفين والرد عليها
قال : وأما التمسك بأنه لو اتصف بالصفات لزم التركيب في الحقيقة الإلهية وبأن القدم أخص أوصاف الإله والكاشف عن حقيقته، فلو اشتركت الصفات فيه لكانت آلهة، وبأنه لا دليل على الصفات فيجب نفيها وبأنه لا يعقل من القيام إلا التبعية في التحيز فيلزم تحيز الباري فضعيف جداً) .
قال : وأما التمسك إشارة إلى شبه أخرى ضعيفة جداً.
الأولى : أنه لو كان موصوفاً بصفات قائمة بذاته؛ لكانت حقيقة الإلهية مركبة تلك الذات والصفات، وكل مركب ممكن لاحتياجه إلى الأجزاء. والجواب : منع من الملازمة بل حقيقة الإله تلك الذات الموجبة للصفات
الثانية : أن القدم أخص أوصاف الإله ، والكاشف عن حقيقته، إذ به يعرف تميزه عن غيره ، فلو شاركته الصفات في القدم، لشاركته في الإلهية ، فيلزم من القول بها القول بالإلهية كما لزم النصارى .
والجواب : منع الأخص كون الكاشف هو القدم بل وجوب الوجودية .
الثالثة : أنه لا دليل على هذه الصفات لأن الأدلة العقلية لا تتم، والسمعية لا تدل إلا على أنه حي عالم قادر إلى غير ذلك. والنزاع لم يقع فيه، وما لا دليل عليه يجب نفيه كما سبق مراراً .
والجواب : منع المقدمتين.
الرابعة : أنه لا يعقل من قيام الصفة بالموصوف إلا حصولها في الحيز تبعاً لحصوله، والتحيز على الله تعالى محال، فكذا قيام الصفات به. والجواب : أن معنى القيام هو الاختصاص الباعث على ما هو مرادكم باتصافه بالأحوال والأحكام.
Another objection of the opponents and the response to it. He said: As for clinging to the fact that if He were described with attributes, composition would be necessary in the divine reality; and that pre-eternity is the most specific of the attributes of the divine and the discloser of His reality; so if the attributes shared in it, they would be deities; and that there is no proof for the attributes, so their negation is obligatory; and that inherence is not understood except as dependence in spatial location, so spatial location of the Creator would be necessary—[all that] is very weak. He said: As for clinging, it is an indication of other very weak objections. The first: that if He were described with attributes subsisting in His essence, then the reality of divinity would be composed of that essence and the attributes, and every composite is possible because it needs its parts. The answer: denial of the entailment; rather the reality of the divine is that essence which necessitates the attributes. The second: that pre-eternity is the most specific of the attributes of the divine and the discloser of His reality, for by it His distinction from others is known; so if the attributes shared in pre-eternity, they would share in divinity, and thus affirming them would entail affirming divinity, as happened to the Christians. The answer: denial that the discloser is pre-eternity specifically—rather it is necessary existence. The third: that there is no proof for these attributes because rational proofs are incomplete, and scriptural proofs only indicate that He is living, knowing, powerful, etc., and there is no dispute about that; and whatever there is no proof for must be negated, as has been repeated many times. The answer: denial of both premises. The fourth: that inherence of an attribute in the subject is not understood except as its occurrence in location following the subject's location, and spatial location is impossible for God, so likewise the inherence of attributes in Him. The answer: that the meaning of inherence is the particularization that prompts what you intend by His being described with states and judgments.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents four weak objections raised by opponents against divine attributes, along with concise answers. The author first mentions that clinging to these objections is very weak. The first objection argues that if God had attributes, His essence would be composite and thus contingent. The answer denies the entailment, affirming that the divine reality is the essence that necessitates the attributes, not a composite. The second objection claims that pre-eternity is unique to God, so attributes sharing in it would become deities. The answer denies that pre-eternity is the sole discloser, stating that necessary existence is. The third objection says there is no proof for attributes, so they must be negated. The answer denies both premises (that rational proofs fail and scriptural proofs only indicate attributes without dispute). The fourth objection argues that inherence implies spatial location, which is impossible for God. The answer redefines inherence as particularization, not location.

Key Terms
شبهة
تركيب
قدم
كاشف
قيام
اختصاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the objections are strong and require a detailed refutation.
Correction: The author dismisses them as very weak and answers them with brief denials, showing their flaws are obvious to a trained theologian.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the third objection that there is no proof for attributes?
A) Denial that attributes exist
B) Denial of both premises
C) Affirmation that rational proofs are sufficient
D) Claim that scriptural proofs alone suffice

Key Takeaway
Weak objections are refuted by denying their presuppositions or entailments, not by conceding the argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The answer to the first objection says the divine reality is the essence that necessitates the attributes. Do you truly believe that Allah's attributes are not additions to His essence but are His very reality? How might this reshape your worship and reliance on His attributes?

The Strong Objection Against Divine Attributes
The author presents the strong objection against divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that eternal attributes would lead to the inherence of meaning in meaning, a key challenge in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes risk an infinite regress of meanings inhering in meanings.

Philosopher-theologians debate the nature of divine attributes. One powerful objection argues that if God has eternal attributes, each attribute must itself be enduring, which introduces a problematic inherence. This lesson presents the objection and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the author's framing that this is a strong argument but with ad hominem premises.
The objection is not necessarily accepted by the author; it is presented as a challenge.
2
Identify the two premises
The objection uses two key premises: (1) The eternal is necessarily enduring (baqa'), and (2) Endurance is an additional attribute subsisting in the thing. Find these in the text.
The second premise is specific to the Ash'ari view.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that if God had eternal attributes, each attribute would require an enduring attribute (baqa'), leading to the inherence of a meaning (the attribute) in another meaning (baqa'), which is deemed impossible.
This is the 'inherence of meaning in meaning' problem.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this objection is considered strong. It forces theologians to explain how divine attributes can be eternal without implying an infinite regress of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من شبه المخالفين
قال : والقوي إلزاماً يعني أن من الشبه القوية في هذا الباب، وإن كانت مقدماتها إلزامية لا تحقيقية، أنها لو كانت له صفات قديمة، لزم قيام المعنى بالمعنى لأن القديم يكون باقياً بالضرورة. وعندكم أن بقاء الشيء صفة زائدة عليه قائمة به، وأن قيام المعنى بالمعنى باطل. فمن الأصحاب من لم يجعل البقاء صفة زائدة، بل استمراراً للوجود ، ومنهم من جوز في غير المتحيز قيام المعنى بالمعنى، وإنما الممتنع قيام العرض بالعرض، لأن معناه التبعية في التحيز والعرض لا يستقل بالتحيز، فلا يتبعه غيره، بل كلاهما يتبعان الجوهر. ومنهم من امتنع عن وصف الصفات بالبقاء، فلم يقل علمه باق وقدراته باقية ، بل :قال هو باق بصفاته، وهذا ضعيف جداً. لأن الدائم الموجد أزلاً وأبداً من غير طريان فناء عليه أصلاً، اتصافه بالبقاء ضروري، ولا يفيد التحرز عن التكلم به ومنهم من قال : هي باقية ببقاء هو بقاء الذات، فإنه بقاء للذات وللصفات، وللبقاء لأنها ليست غير الذات بخلاف بقاء الجوهر، فإنه لا يكون بقاء لأعراضه لكونها مغايرة له، والبقاء القائم بالشيء لا يكون بقاء لما هو غيره. بهذا صرح الشيخ الأشعري (۳) واعترض عليه :
From the objections of the opponents. He said: And the strong one as a charge means that among the strong objections in this chapter—even if their premises are ad hominem (ilmāmiyya) not investigative—is that if He had eternal attributes, the inherence of a meaning in a meaning would be necessary, because the eternal is necessarily enduring (bāqin). And according to you, endurance (baqā') of a thing is an additional attribute subsisting in it, and the inherence of a meaning in a meaning is invalid. Some of the followers did not make endurance an additional attribute but rather a continuation of existence. Some of them allowed inherence of meaning in meaning for what is not spatially located—only the inherence of accident in accident is impossible, because its meaning is dependence in spatial location, and an accident does not subsist by itself in location, so nothing else follows it; rather both follow the substance. Some of them refrained from describing the attributes with endurance, so they did not say 'His knowledge is enduring' and 'His power is enduring,' but said 'He is enduring with His attributes'—and this is very weak, because the eternal, brought into existence from pre-eternity to post-eternity without any passing of annihilation over it at all, its being characterized with endurance is necessary, and it does not help to avoid speaking of it. Some of them said: They are enduring by an endurance that is the endurance of the essence, for it is endurance of the essence and of the attributes and of endurance itself, because they are not other than the essence, unlike the endurance of a substance, for it is not endurance of its accidents because they are other than it, and endurance subsisting in a thing is not endurance of what is other than it. This is what al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari stated, and an objection was raised against it:
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents a strong objection against divine attributes and reviews several attempted responses. The objection is that if God has eternal attributes, each attribute must itself be enduring (bāqin), leading to the impossible scenario of a meaning (the attribute) inhering in another meaning (endurance)—since endurance is an additional attribute. The author then lists responses from different groups of followers. First, some deny that endurance is an additional attribute, regarding it as mere continuation. Second, some allow meaning-in-meaning inherence for non-spatial entities, limiting impossibility to accident-in-accident inherence because accidents depend on spatial location. Third, some refrain from calling attributes enduring, saying only God is enduring with His attributes—this is deemed very weak because the eternal necessarily is characterized by endurance. Fourth, some hold that attributes endure by the endurance of the essence itself, which encompasses essence, attributes, and endurance. The passage ends with a note that al-Ash'ari stated this view and an objection was raised against it.

Key Terms
بقاء
قيام المعنى بالمعنى
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this objection is a definitive refutation of divine attributes.
Correction: The author presents it as a strong objection, but the premises are based on the opponent's views (ilmamiyya), not necessarily accepted by all. The objection is meant to be answered, not to end the discussion.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The objection highlights the logical tension between eternal attributes and the concept of inherence, forcing theologians to refine their understanding of divine attributes and endurance.

Responses to the Objection on Divine Attributes
The author lists attempted responses to the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will examine four different responses to the objection that eternal attributes entail inherence of meaning in meaning, and understand their strengths and weaknesses.

Core Idea
Theologians offered varied defenses to avoid the inherence problem.

After presenting the objection, the author reviews several attempted solutions from different scholars. Each response tries to avoid the problematic inherence, but some are deemed weak. This lesson covers all four responses and their evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Read the responses passage
Read the Arabic passage listing the four responses. Notice the author's critical tone, especially for the third response.
The first response denies endurance is an additional attribute; the second allows meaning-in-meaning for non-spatial entities; the third avoids describing attributes as enduring; the fourth ties endurance to the essence.
2
Analyze the first response
Some followers deny that endurance is an additional attribute; it is merely continuation of existence. This undercuts the second premise of the objection.
This response avoids the inherence problem directly.
3
Analyze the second response
Some allow inherence of meaning in meaning for non-spatial entities (like God's attributes), limiting impossibility to accident-in-accident inherence. They argue that accidents depend on location, while meanings do not.
This response restricts the scope of the impossibility.
4
Analyze the third response
Some refrain from describing attributes as enduring, saying only God is enduring with His attributes. The author deems this very weak because the eternal necessarily has endurance.
This response is considered an unsuccessful evasion.
5
Analyze the fourth response
Al-Ash'ari and others hold that attributes are enduring by the endurance of the essence itself. This endurance applies to the essence, attributes, and even itself, because attributes are not other than the essence. This avoids inherence by making endurance a single attribute that covers all.
This is the most sophisticated response but faced an objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من شبه المخالفين
قال : والقوي إلزاماً يعني أن من الشبه القوية في هذا الباب، وإن كانت مقدماتها إلزامية لا تحقيقية، أنها لو كانت له صفات قديمة، لزم قيام المعنى بالمعنى لأن القديم يكون باقياً بالضرورة. وعندكم أن بقاء الشيء صفة زائدة عليه قائمة به، وأن قيام المعنى بالمعنى باطل. فمن الأصحاب من لم يجعل البقاء صفة زائدة، بل استمراراً للوجود ، ومنهم من جوز في غير المتحيز قيام المعنى بالمعنى، وإنما الممتنع قيام العرض بالعرض، لأن معناه التبعية في التحيز والعرض لا يستقل بالتحيز، فلا يتبعه غيره، بل كلاهما يتبعان الجوهر. ومنهم من امتنع عن وصف الصفات بالبقاء، فلم يقل علمه باق وقدراته باقية ، بل :قال هو باق بصفاته، وهذا ضعيف جداً. لأن الدائم الموجد أزلاً وأبداً من غير طريان فناء عليه أصلاً، اتصافه بالبقاء ضروري، ولا يفيد التحرز عن التكلم به ومنهم من قال : هي باقية ببقاء هو بقاء الذات، فإنه بقاء للذات وللصفات، وللبقاء لأنها ليست غير الذات بخلاف بقاء الجوهر، فإنه لا يكون بقاء لأعراضه لكونها مغايرة له، والبقاء القائم بالشيء لا يكون بقاء لما هو غيره. بهذا صرح الشيخ الأشعري (۳) واعترض عليه :
From the objections of the opponents. He said: And the strong one as a charge means that among the strong objections in this chapter—even if their premises are ad hominem (ilmāmiyya) not investigative—is that if He had eternal attributes, the inherence of a meaning in a meaning would be necessary, because the eternal is necessarily enduring (bāqin). And according to you, endurance (baqā') of a thing is an additional attribute subsisting in it, and the inherence of a meaning in a meaning is invalid. Some of the followers did not make endurance an additional attribute but rather a continuation of existence. Some of them allowed inherence of meaning in meaning for what is not spatially located—only the inherence of accident in accident is impossible, because its meaning is dependence in spatial location, and an accident does not subsist by itself in location, so nothing else follows it; rather both follow the substance. Some of them refrained from describing the attributes with endurance, so they did not say 'His knowledge is enduring' and 'His power is enduring,' but said 'He is enduring with His attributes'—and this is very weak, because the eternal, brought into existence from pre-eternity to post-eternity without any passing of annihilation over it at all, its being characterized with endurance is necessary, and it does not help to avoid speaking of it. Some of them said: They are enduring by an endurance that is the endurance of the essence, for it is endurance of the essence and of the attributes and of endurance itself, because they are not other than the essence, unlike the endurance of a substance, for it is not endurance of its accidents because they are other than it, and endurance subsisting in a thing is not endurance of what is other than it. This is what al-Shaykh al-Ash'ari stated, and an objection was raised against it:
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents a strong objection against divine attributes and reviews several attempted responses. The objection is that if God has eternal attributes, each attribute must itself be enduring (bāqin), leading to the impossible scenario of a meaning (the attribute) inhering in another meaning (endurance)—since endurance is an additional attribute. The author then lists responses from different groups of followers. First, some deny that endurance is an additional attribute, regarding it as mere continuation. Second, some allow meaning-in-meaning inherence for non-spatial entities, limiting impossibility to accident-in-accident inherence because accidents depend on spatial location. Third, some refrain from calling attributes enduring, saying only God is enduring with His attributes—this is deemed very weak because the eternal necessarily is characterized by endurance. Fourth, some hold that attributes endure by the endurance of the essence itself, which encompasses essence, attributes, and endurance. The passage ends with a note that al-Ash'ari stated this view and an objection was raised against it.

قيام المعنى بالمعنى
عرض
جوهر
ذات
إلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the third response is a valid solution.
Correction: The author explicitly calls it 'very weak' because the eternal necessarily has endurance, and merely avoiding the verbal description does not solve the logical problem.

Checkpoint
Which response does the author consider 'very weak'?
A) Denying endurance is an additional attribute
B) Allowing meaning-in-meaning for non-spatial entities
C) Refraining from describing attributes as enduring
D) Saying attributes endure by the endurance of the essence

Key Takeaway
The four responses show the range of theological strategies to defend divine attributes, but only the fourth (al-Ash'ari's) provides a sophisticated solution, though it too faced objections.

Al-Ash'ari's View on Attributes' Eternity and Its Critique
Al-Ash'ari's claim on attributes' eternity and its critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's claim that attributes are eternal by the essence's eternity, the objection that they are not identical to the essence, and why non-identity is the key issue.

Core Idea
Attributes are not other than the essence, but not identical to it—so their eternity cannot be simply borrowed.

This lesson presents Al-Ash'ari's nuanced position on the eternity of divine attributes. He argued that since attributes are not distinct from the essence, they share the essence's eternity. However, this claim faces a sharp objection: being not-other is not enough; they must be identical. This objection reveals the subtle distinction between 'otherness' (ghayriyya) and 'identity' (ayniyya) in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
2
Identify Al-Ash'ari's view
Find the claim that attributes are eternal by the same eternity as the essence, and the reason given: they are not other than the essence.
Contrast with the substance-accident example.
3
Understand the objection
The objection states that attributes are not identical (ayn) to the essence. Ask yourself why this matters for the attribution of eternity.
4
Analyse the illustration
Consider the example of knowledge not being living or powerful. This shows that being not-other does not mean sharing all attributes.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that the real impediment is non-identity, not distinctness. Reflect on how this refines the earlier argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : هي باقية ببقاء هو بقاء الذات، فإنه بقاء للذات وللصفات، وللبقاء لأنها ليست غير الذات بخلاف بقاء الجوهر، فإنه لا يكون بقاء لأعراضه لكونها مغايرة له، والبقاء القائم بالشيء لا يكون بقاء لما هو غيره. بهذا صرح الشيخ الأشعري واعترض عليه : بأن الصفات كما أنها ليست غير الذات ليست عينها، فكيف يجعل البقاء القائم بالذات بقاء لما ليس بالذات، ولما لم يقم به البقاء، ولهذا لا يتصف بعض صفات الذات، مع أنها ليست غير الذات بالبعض، فلا يكون العلم مثلاً حياً قادراً فظهر أن علة امتناع جعل بقاء الجوهر بقاء العرض ليست تغايرهما ، بل كون أحدهما ليس الآخر.
And among them is one who said: They (the attributes) are eternal by an eternity that is the eternity of the essence, for it is an eternity for the essence and for the attributes, and for the eternity, because they are not other than the essence, unlike the eternity of the substance (jawhar), for it is not an eternity for its accidents (a'rad) due to their being distinct from it, and the eternity subsisting in a thing cannot be an eternity for what is other than it. This is what Shaykh al-Ash'ari explicitly stated, and it was objected to: That the attributes, just as they are not other than the essence, are not identical to it; so how can one make the eternity subsisting in the essence an eternity for what is not identical to the essence, and for what the eternity does not subsist in? For this reason, some of the attributes of the essence are not characterized by some other attributes, even though they are not other than the essence; for example, knowledge is not living or powerful. So it became clear that the cause of the impossibility of making the eternity of the substance an eternity for the accident is not their distinctness (taghayur), but rather the fact that one is not the other.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth response from some Ash'ari scholars, particularly Al-Ash'ari himself: they claim that the attributes are eternal by the same eternity (baqa') as the divine essence, because the attributes are not other (ghayr) than the essence. Unlike the eternity of a substance (jawhar), which does not extend to its accidents due to their distinctness, the attributes are not distinct from the essence, so the essence's eternity includes them. The objection to this view is then given: while the attributes are not other than the essence, they are also not identical to the essence. Therefore, the eternity subsisting in the essence cannot be an eternity for what is not identical to it. This is illustrated by the fact that knowledge, though not other than the essence, is not living or powerful. Thus, the real impediment is not distinctness (taghayur) but the non-identity between the attribute and the essence. The passage concludes that the reason the eternity of a substance cannot extend to its accidents is not that they are distinct, but that the substance is not the accident.

Key Terms
بقاء الذات
قيام البقاء
غير الذات
عين الذات
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if attributes are not other than the essence, they automatically share the essence's eternity.
Correction: This passage clarifies that being not-other is insufficient—they must also be identical (ayn) to the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the objection to Al-Ash'ari's position, why can the eternity of the essence not be an eternity for the attributes?
A) Because the attributes are other than the essence.
B) Because the attributes are not identical to the essence, even though they are not other than it.
C) Because the essence is not eternal.
D) Because the attributes are accidents.

Key Takeaway
Attributes are not identical to the essence, so they cannot be eternal by the essence's eternity alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you tempted to simplify Him into something you can fully grasp? Will you accept the mystery and worship Him as He is, beyond your categories?

The theologians in this passage made a logical mistake by assuming 'not other' means 'share eternity'. Attributes: Neither Identical to Nor Other Than the Essence
Discussion on attributes and the problem of survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine attributes are neither identical to the essence nor entirely other, and why the survival of the essence cannot be applied to attributes straightforwardly.

Core Idea
Attributes are not the essence, but they are not other than it either.

This lesson introduces a foundational paradox in Islamic theology: if attributes are not identical to God's essence, how can they share in the attribute of survival? The author begins by establishing that attributes are neither the essence nor entirely distinct, then raises a specific problem regarding survival (baqa').

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the author states that attributes are neither identical to the essence nor other than it.
This paradoxical middle position is central to Ash'ari theology.
2
Notice the question about survival
The author asks: if survival subsists in the essence, how can it apply to attributes that are not the essence? Understand the force of this question.
3
Understand the example of knowledge
The example shows that knowledge is not living or powerful, illustrating that attributes do not qualify each other. Grasp why this supports the author's point.
4
Grasp the conclusion about the cause
The author concludes that the impossibility of transferring survival from substance to accident is due to their non-identity, not mere difference. This is a key distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأن الصفات كما أنها ليست غير الذات ليست عينها، فكيف يجعل البقاء القائم بالذات بقاء لما ليس بالذات، ولما لم يقم به البقاء، ولهذا لا يتصف بعض صفات الذات، الذات، مع أنها ليست غير الذات بالبعض، فلا يكون العلم مثلاً حياً قادراً فظهر أن علة امتناع جعل بقاء الجوهر بقاء العرض ليست تغايرهما ، بل كون أحدهما ليس الآخر ومنهم من قال : إن الصفة باقية ببقاء جوهر هو نفسها، والعلم مثلاً ، علم للذات، فيكون به عالماً وبقاء لنفسه، فيكون به باقياً، كما أن بقاء الله تعالى بقاء له، وبقاء للبقاء أيضاً، وهذا كالجسم يكون كائناً بالكون، والكون يكون كائناً بنفسه، وجاز حصول باقيين ببقاء واحد، لأن أحدهما كان قائماً بالآخر، فلم يرد إلى قيام صفة بذاتين بخلاف حصول متحركتين بحركة، وأسوديين بسواد . فإن قيل : معلوم أن الشيء إنما يكون عالماً بما هو علم، قادراً بما هو قدرة، باقياً بما هو بقاء إلى غير ذلك. وها هنا قد لزم كون الذات عالماً، وقادراً بما هو بقاء، والعلم باقياً بما هو علم، والقدرة باقية بما هي قدرة وهو محال قلنا : اختلاف الإضافة بدفع الاستحالة، فإن المستحيل هو أن يكون الشيء عالماً أو قادراً بما هو بقاء له، وباقياً بما هو علم أو قدرة له . واللازم هو أن الذات عالم أو قادر بما هو بقاء للعلم أو للقدرة، والعلم أو القدرة باق بما هو علم أو قدرة للذات . ولقائل أن يقول : فحينئذ حتى لا يبقى قولكم بقاء الباقي صفة زائدة عليه، قائمة به على إطلاقه . وأيضا إذا جاز كون بقاء العلم نفسه مع القطع بأن مفهوم البقاء ليس مفهوم العلم. فلم لا يجوز مثله في الصفات مع الذات بأن يكون عالماً بعلم نفسه، قادراً بقدرة هي نفسه، باقياً ببقاء هو نفسه إلى غير ذلك ، ولا يستلزم إلا كون الجميع واحداً بحسب الوجود لا بحسب المفهوم والاعتبار.
That the attributes, just as they are not other than the essence, are not identical to it, so how can the survival subsisting in the essence be a survival for that which is not the essence, and for which survival does not subsist? And for this reason, some attributes of the essence are not qualified by others, even though they are not other than the essence. So knowledge, for example, is not living, powerful. So it became apparent that the cause of the impossibility of making the survival of the substance a survival for the accident is not their difference, but rather that one is not the other. And among them is one who said: The attribute is enduring by the survival of a substance that is itself, and knowledge, for example, is knowledge for the essence, so through it it is knowing, and a survival for itself, so through it it is enduring. Just as the survival of God Almighty is a survival for Him and a survival for survival itself. And this is like the body: it comes into being by coming-into-being, and coming-into-being comes into being by itself. And it is permissible that two enduring things occur by one survival, because one of them was subsisting in the other, so it is not required that an attribute subsist in two essences, unlike the occurrence of two moving things by one motion, or two black things by one blackness. For if it is said: It is known that a thing is only knowing by what is knowledge, powerful by what is power, enduring by what is survival, and so on. But here it would be necessary that the essence be knowing and powerful by what is survival, and knowledge enduring by what is knowledge, and power enduring by what is power, and that is impossible. We say: Difference in relation removes the impossibility. For the impossible is that a thing be knowing or powerful by what is its survival, and enduring by what is its knowledge or power. The necessary is that the essence be knowing or powerful by what is a survival for knowledge or power, and knowledge or power endure by what is knowledge or power for the essence. And a proponent could say: Then your statement that the survival of the enduring is an additional attribute subsisting in it would not remain in its absolute form. Also, if it is permissible that the survival of knowledge itself be [something] while it is certain that the concept of survival is not the concept of knowledge, then why is the like not permissible regarding the attributes with the essence, so that He is knowing by a knowledge that is His self, powerful by a power that is His self, enduring by a survival that is His self, and so on? That would only require that all are one in terms of existence, not in terms of concept and consideration.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the relationship between God's attributes and His essence, specifically focusing on the attribute of survival (baqa'). The author begins by stating that attributes are neither identical to the essence nor completely other than it. He then raises a problem: if an attribute like knowledge is not the essence, how can the survival that subsists in the essence be a survival for that attribute? He presents a view that an attribute endures by its own survival, drawing an analogy with God's survival being for Himself and for survival itself. An objection follows: this would lead to absurdities, such as the essence being knowing by what is survival rather than by what is knowledge. The author responds by distinguishing relations: the essence is knowing by the survival of knowledge, while knowledge endures by being knowledge for the essence. Finally, an objector suggests that if survival can be distinct from knowledge, then attributes could be identical to the essence in existence while distinct in concept, which the author leaves as a possibility. The passage is dense, relying on distinctions between existence (al-wujud) and concept (al-mafhum).

Key Terms
صفات
ذات
بقاء
وجود
مفهوم
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes are either exactly the essence or entirely different entities.
Correction: The correct view is that attributes are neither identical to the essence nor other than it; they are distinct in concept but unified in existence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the cause of the impossibility of making the substance's survival a survival for the accident?
A) Their difference (taghayur)
B) That one is not the other (kawn ahadihima lays al-akhar)
C) The accident's lack of subsistence
D) The substance's lack of survival

Key Takeaway
Attributes are neither the same as God's essence nor completely distinct; the impossibility of applying survival across them stems from their non-identity, not mere difference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are neither separate from Him nor identical to His essence, how does that change the way you approach His attribute of mercy? Do you seek mercy as if it is something He gives, or as a real aspect of His being that defines your relationship with Him? This week, consciously meditate on one attribute (e.g., Al-Rahman) as being both distinct yet inseparable from God Himself, and let that reshape your prayer.
?
The passage argues that the impossibility of survival applying to attributes is due to non-identity, not difference. How does this affect your understanding of God's eternity? Is God's eternal nature merely a property of His essence, or is it also true of His attributes like His knowledge and power? If His knowledge is eternal but not the essence, does that make you more confident that His promises (based on His knowledge) are absolutely certain? Examine your heart: do you sometimes doubt God's promises because you treat His attributes as less than His essence? Repent and anchor your trust in the reality that His attributes share His eternal subsistence.

The Survival of Attributes and the Objection Resolved
Presentation of a view on attribute survival and its defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the view that attributes endure by their own survival and how the objection regarding absurd implications is resolved through the distinction of relations.

Core Idea
Attributes endure by their own survival, and different relations avoid absurdity.

In this passage, the author presents a solution to the problem raised earlier: that an attribute like knowledge endures by its own survival, just as God's survival applies to both Him and itself. An objection is then raised that this would make the essence knowing by survival rather than by knowledge. The author responds by clarifying the relations involved.

Guided Path
1
Read the view that attributes endure by their own survival
Read the Arabic and translation where the author says the attribute endures by a survival that is itself. Understand the claim.
The analogy with God's survival and the body's coming-into-being is used.
2
Consider the objection
An objector says this would lead to absurdities: essence knowing by survival, knowledge enduring by knowledge, etc. Identify the logical problem.
3
Grasp the response: difference in relations
The author replies that the impossible would be if the essence were knowing by survival itself, but here it is knowing by the survival of knowledge. Notice how this distinction resolves the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأن الصفات كما أنها ليست غير الذات ليست عينها، فكيف يجعل البقاء القائم بالذات بقاء لما ليس بالذات، ولما لم يقم به البقاء، ولهذا لا يتصف بعض صفات الذات، الذات، مع أنها ليست غير الذات بالبعض، فلا يكون العلم مثلاً حياً قادراً فظهر أن علة امتناع جعل بقاء الجوهر بقاء العرض ليست تغايرهما ، بل كون أحدهما ليس الآخر ومنهم من قال : إن الصفة باقية ببقاء جوهر هو نفسها، والعلم مثلاً ، علم للذات، فيكون به عالماً وبقاء لنفسه، فيكون به باقياً، كما أن بقاء الله تعالى بقاء له، وبقاء للبقاء أيضاً، وهذا كالجسم يكون كائناً بالكون، والكون يكون كائناً بنفسه، وجاز حصول باقيين ببقاء واحد، لأن أحدهما كان قائماً بالآخر، فلم يرد إلى قيام صفة بذاتين بخلاف حصول متحركتين بحركة، وأسوديين بسواد . فإن قيل : معلوم أن الشيء إنما يكون عالماً بما هو علم، قادراً بما هو قدرة، باقياً بما هو بقاء إلى غير ذلك. وها هنا قد لزم كون الذات عالماً، وقادراً بما هو بقاء، والعلم باقياً بما هو علم، والقدرة باقية بما هي قدرة وهو محال قلنا : اختلاف الإضافة بدفع الاستحالة، فإن المستحيل هو أن يكون الشيء عالماً أو قادراً بما هو بقاء له، وباقياً بما هو علم أو قدرة له . واللازم هو أن الذات عالم أو قادر بما هو بقاء للعلم أو للقدرة، والعلم أو القدرة باق بما هو علم أو قدرة للذات . ولقائل أن يقول : فحينئذ حتى لا يبقى قولكم بقاء الباقي صفة زائدة عليه، قائمة به على إطلاقه . وأيضا إذا جاز كون بقاء العلم نفسه مع القطع بأن مفهوم البقاء ليس مفهوم العلم. فلم لا يجوز مثله في الصفات مع الذات بأن يكون عالماً بعلم نفسه، قادراً بقدرة هي نفسه، باقياً ببقاء هو نفسه إلى غير ذلك ، ولا يستلزم إلا كون الجميع واحداً بحسب الوجود لا بحسب المفهوم والاعتبار.
That the attributes, just as they are not other than the essence, are not identical to it, so how can the survival subsisting in the essence be a survival for that which is not the essence, and for which survival does not subsist? And for this reason, some attributes of the essence are not qualified by others, even though they are not other than the essence. So knowledge, for example, is not living, powerful. So it became apparent that the cause of the impossibility of making the survival of the substance a survival for the accident is not their difference, but rather that one is not the other. And among them is one who said: The attribute is enduring by the survival of a substance that is itself, and knowledge, for example, is knowledge for the essence, so through it it is knowing, and a survival for itself, so through it it is enduring. Just as the survival of God Almighty is a survival for Him and a survival for survival itself. And this is like the body: it comes into being by coming-into-being, and coming-into-being comes into being by itself. And it is permissible that two enduring things occur by one survival, because one of them was subsisting in the other, so it is not required that an attribute subsist in two essences, unlike the occurrence of two moving things by one motion, or two black things by one blackness. For if it is said: It is known that a thing is only knowing by what is knowledge, powerful by what is power, enduring by what is survival, and so on. But here it would be necessary that the essence be knowing and powerful by what is survival, and knowledge enduring by what is knowledge, and power enduring by what is power, and that is impossible. We say: Difference in relation removes the impossibility. For the impossible is that a thing be knowing or powerful by what is its survival, and enduring by what is its knowledge or power. The necessary is that the essence be knowing or powerful by what is a survival for knowledge or power, and knowledge or power endure by what is knowledge or power for the essence. And a proponent could say: Then your statement that the survival of the enduring is an additional attribute subsisting in it would not remain in its absolute form. Also, if it is permissible that the survival of knowledge itself be [something] while it is certain that the concept of survival is not the concept of knowledge, then why is the like not permissible regarding the attributes with the essence, so that He is knowing by a knowledge that is His self, powerful by a power that is His self, enduring by a survival that is His self, and so on? That would only require that all are one in terms of existence, not in terms of concept and consideration.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the relationship between God's attributes and His essence, specifically focusing on the attribute of survival (baqa'). The author begins by stating that attributes are neither identical to the essence nor completely other than it. He then raises a problem: if an attribute like knowledge is not the essence, how can the survival that subsists in the essence be a survival for that attribute? He presents a view that an attribute endures by its own survival, drawing an analogy with God's survival being for Himself and for survival itself. An objection follows: this would lead to absurdities, such as the essence being knowing by what is survival rather than by what is knowledge. The author responds by distinguishing relations: the essence is knowing by the survival of knowledge, while knowledge endures by being knowledge for the essence. Finally, an objector suggests that if survival can be distinct from knowledge, then attributes could be identical to the essence in existence while distinct in concept, which the author leaves as a possibility. The passage is dense, relying on distinctions between existence (al-wujud) and concept (al-mafhum).

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, how does the essence become knowing?
A) By survival itself
B) By the survival of knowledge
C) By knowledge itself
D) By power

Key Takeaway
The distinction of relations resolves the apparent absurdity: the essence is knowing through the survival of knowledge, while knowledge endures as knowledge for the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are distinguished only by relation (إضافة) and not by separate entities, what does that mean for how you think about God's simplicity? Are you comfortable with a model that uses logical distinctions to avoid implying parts in God, or do you find it unsettling? How can you train your mind to accept that God's oneness is beyond our ordinary concepts of identity and difference?
?
The objection in this passage feels intuitive: if knowledge endures by itself, why wouldn't the essence then be enduring by knowledge? What does your own tendency to see such arguments as fatal reveal about your expectations of theological reasoning? Will you commit to trusting scholars even when their distinctions seem subtle, or will you dismiss them as sophistry?

Attributes and the Distinction Between Existence and Concept
Final objection and conclusion on attributes and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final objection that if survival is conceptually distinct from knowledge, attributes could be identical to the essence in existence while distinct in concept.

Core Idea
Conceptual multiplicity does not require existential separation.

The discussion culminates with a powerful objection: if survival can be distinct from knowledge in concept while coexisting in existence, why can't the same be said for attributes and the essence? The author implicitly accepts this, showing that attributes are one with the essence in existence but multiple in concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection to the previous reply
The objector says that the reply undermines the claim that survival is an additional attribute. Understand this challenge.
2
Grasp the analogical reasoning
If survival is conceptually distinct from knowledge yet exists with it, why not say attributes are identical to the essence in existence? Follow the logic.
The key is that distinctness in concept does not require separate existence.
3
Understand the conclusion
The objector concludes that all attributes are one with the essence in existence but distinct in concept and consideration. This is the resolution.

Mu'tazila Argument Against God's Knowledge and the Reply
Mu'tazila argument and author's reply on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's initial argument against God having knowledge as an attribute and the author's logical refutation of that argument.

Core Idea
Similarity in one aspect does not imply full equality—key to defending divine attributes.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God knew through a knowledge like humans, His knowledge would be similar to ours, leading to problems of temporality or hierarchy. In this passage, Sānūsī presents their argument and then refutes it by clarifying the limits of analogy. You will see how careful reasoning about necessary concomitants and relations defends the doctrine of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the Mu'tazila argument (three points), then the author's reply (four points).
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's three objections
Find the three points: similarity leading to shared eternity/temporality, infinite knowledges, and the verse implying a hierarchy above God.
3
Examine the author's four counter-arguments
Identify each reply: sharing a necessary concomitant does not entail similarity, similarity does not entail equality in attributes, one thing can have multiple relations infinitely, and general statements do not specify universality.
4
Reflect on the logical principles
Consider why the author's principles (e.g., 'lāzim' vs 'tamāthul') are important for defending divine attributes. Think about how analogical reasoning can be misapplied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعاء المعتزلة نفي العلم عن الله تعالى قال : وفي نفي العلم . أنه لو كان عالماً بعلم كما في الشاهد لكان العلمان متماثلين لتعلقهما بالمعلوم من وجه واحد فيلزم اشتراكهما في القدم أو الحدوث بخلاف العالمية فإنها فيه يتعلق الذات وفينا يتعلق العلم. ولكانت علومه غير متناهية لكونه عالماً بما لا نهاية له ولكان فوقه عليم لقوله تعالى : وفوق كل ذي علم عليم) قلنا لا يلزم من الاشتراك في اللازم التماثل ولا من التماثل الاستواء في الصفات ولا يمتنع كثرة تعلقات الواحد ولو إلى غير نهاية ولا تخصيص العمومات) .
The claim of the Mu'tazila negating knowledge from God Almighty. He said: Regarding the negation of knowledge: That if He were knowing by a knowledge as in the witnessed [world], then the two knowledges would be similar due to their relation to the known object from one aspect, so their sharing in eternity or temporality would follow, unlike the attribute of being knowing (al-‘ālamiyya), for in Him it pertains to the essence and in us it pertains to knowledge. And His knowledges would be infinite because He knows what has no end, and there would be above Him one who knows, due to His saying: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is one who knows' (12:76). We say: Sharing in a necessary concomitant does not necessitate similarity, nor does similarity necessitate equality in attributes, nor is it impossible for one [knowledge] to have multiple relations even to infinity, nor does it necessitate specification of generalities.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage begins with the heading 'The claim of the Mu'tazila negating knowledge from God Almighty'. It then presents an initial argument attributed to the Mu'tazila: if God knows through a knowledge (like humans do), then His knowledge and ours would be similar in their relation to the known object, leading to both being either eternal or temporal. The Mu'tazila argue that this is problematic because in God, knowingness pertains to His essence, while in us it pertains to a knowledge. They further argue that such a knowledge would be infinite, and that the verse 'above every possessor of knowledge is one who knows' implies a hierarchy. The author's reply (qulnā) states that sharing in a necessary concomitant (lāzim) does not necessitate similarity (tamāthul), nor does similarity necessitate equality in attributes; moreover, one thing can have multiple relations without limit, and general statements are not necessarily universal.

Key Terms

تماثل
