عالمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share any property, they are similar in all respects and must have the same essential attributes.
Correction: Sharing a necessary concomitant (like relation to an object) does not imply full similarity; similarity is limited to that aspect alone, not extending to other attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the author's reply, why does sharing a necessary concomitant not entail similarity?
A) Because different things can share a single property without being similar overall
B) Because necessary concomitants are always unique to each thing
C) Because similarity requires equality in all attributes
D) Because the Mu'tazila argument is invalid

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's analogical argument against God's knowledge fails because similarity in one respect does not imply complete equality; careful logical distinctions protect divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is true about humans, it must be true about God? This week, in which aspect of your belief are you most tempted to make God like yourself? What will you do to guard against that?
?
The Mu'tazila argued based on a verse from the Quran. When you read the Quran, do you trust your own understanding without checking deeper principles? Are you willing to accept that some verses do not apply universally? How will you humble your interpretation?
?
Recognizing that similarity does not imply equality—how does this apply to your relationships with others? Do you judge people based on one similarity and assume they are like you in all ways? How can you apply this lesson's logical distinction to your daily judgments, especially when you are quick to classify someone?

The First Mu'tazila Argument on Knowledge and Its Refutation
First proof of the Mu'tazila and the author's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first proof against God having a knowledge attribute, its logical structure, and the author's two-part refutation.

Core Idea
Similarity in one aspect does not force identity in all aspects.

The Mu'tazila advanced several arguments to prove that God cannot be 'knowing by a knowledge.' The first argument hinges on the entailment that if God's knowledge were similar to ours, then either His knowledge would be temporal or ours eternal—both impossible. In this passage, the author presents the argument and gives a nuanced response, showing the flaw in equating similarity with identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation once to get an overall sense of the argument and response.
Notice the logical flow: the Mu'tazila claim, then the reason, then the answer.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's premise and entailment
Find the conditional statement: if God had knowledge by an attribute, then either His knowledge is temporal or our knowledge is eternal. Understand why both options are considered false.
The phrase 'ظاهر البطلان' indicates they consider these outcomes impossible.
3
Understand the reasoning for similarity
Examine why the Mu'tazila claim that God's knowledge and our knowledge are similar: both relate to the same object in the same way (as knowledge of that object). This similarity is the basis for demanding equal temporal status.
The key phrase is 'كلاهما على وجه واحد' — both in one aspect.
4
Analyze the author's first response
The author denies that sharing one aspect (the relation) necessitates full similarity, because different things can share a necessary concomitant without being similar in essence.
This is a logical point: a single property can be common to diverse entities.
5
Analyze the author's second response
Even if similarity is granted, the author argues that similarity does not require equality in every attribute; things can be similar in one respect yet differ in others, like existences.
The example of 'existences' according to the theologians shows how similar things can differ in their natures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وفي نفي العلم تمسكوا في امتناع كونه عالماً بالعلم بوجوه : الأول : أنه لو كان كذلك لزم حدوث علمه، أو قدم علمنا، وكلاهما ظاهر البطلان ، وجه اللزوم أنه إذا تعلق علمنا بشيء مخصوص تعلق به علمه كان كلاهما على وجه واحد، وهو طريق تعلق العلم بالمعلوم. لا أن يكون علمه تعلق الذات، وعلمنا به بطريق تعلق العلم كما في عالميته، وعالميتنا. وإذا كان كلاهما على وجه واحد كانا متماثلين فيلزم استواؤهما في القدم أو الحدوث. والجواب : أن تعلقهما من وجه واحد لا يوجب تماثلهما لجواز اشتراك المختلفات في لازم واحد . ولو سلّم، فالتماثل لا يوجب تساويهما في القدم أو الحدوث لجواز اختلاف المتماثلات في الصفات، كالوجودات على رأي المتكلمين .
He said: Regarding the negation of knowledge, they adhered to proofs for the impossibility of His being knowing by a knowledge. The first: that if it were so, then either His knowledge would be temporal or our knowledge would be eternal, both of which are manifestly false. The aspect of the entailment is that if our knowledge is related to a particular thing, His knowledge is also related to it in one aspect, namely the way knowledge relates to the known. Not that His knowledge is a relation of the essence and our knowledge is by way of knowledge as in His attribute of knowingness and our knowingness. And if both are in one aspect, they are similar, so their equality in eternity or temporality follows. The answer: that their relation from one aspect does not necessitate their similarity because it is possible for different things to share a single necessary concomitant. And even if granted, similarity does not necessitate their equality in eternity or temporality because different similar things can differ in attributes, like existences according to the theologians.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the first formal proof from the Mu'tazila (after the initial summary). The proof argues that if God possessed a knowledge (like humans), then either God's knowledge would be temporal (ḥudūth) or our knowledge would be eternal (qidam), both impossible. The reasoning is that when our knowledge relates to a specific object, God's knowledge also relates to it in the same way—as knowledge of the object—not as a relation of the essence. Therefore, both knowledges are similar, and similarity demands they share the same temporal status. The author's answer first denies that sharing one aspect (relation) entails full similarity, because different things can share a necessary concomitant (lāzim). Second, even if similarity is granted, it does not force equality in every attribute; things can be similar in one way but differ in other attributes, just as existences (according to the mutakallimūn) are similar in existence but differ in quiddity.

Key Terms
علم
حدوث
قدم
تماثل
تعلق
لازم
متكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are similar in one respect (e.g., both being knowledge of X), they must be identical in all respects, including temporal nature.
Correction: Similarity in one aspect does not entail identity in all aspects; different things can share a property while differing in others.

Checkpoint
What is the core claim of the Mu'tazila in the first argument?
A) That God's knowledge must be temporal
B) That our knowledge must be eternal
C) That the similarity of relation forces both knowledges to share the same temporal status
D) That God cannot know particular things

Key Takeaway
Sharing one attribute (relation to the same object) does not make two things identical in all attributes, including temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Mu'tazila argument, did you find it initially convincing? Reflect on any pride you may feel in your own reasoning ability. How does the author's refutation show that human logic, without divine guidance, can lead to error about Allah? In what area of your faith are you trusting your own logic over revelation?
?
The Mu'tazila argued from the similarity of knowledge to conclude that God cannot have knowledge. Is there any attribute of Allah that you have unknowingly limited based on your own experience? What would it mean for you to accept that Allah's attributes are unlike any created thing, even if you cannot fully grasp them?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something shares a similarity with you, it must be like you in every way, including its origin? How does this lesson correct that assumption, and how might you apply this correction in your understanding of Allah's attributes?

One Knowledge Can Relate to Infinite Objects
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila infinite-knowledges argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's second proof against God having knowledge as an attribute, and al-Sanusi's refutation that one knowledge can relate to infinite known objects.

Core Idea
One knowledge can relate to many objects, even infinite ones.

This passage presents a key Mu'tazila argument: if God knows by a knowledge, He would need infinite knowledges to know infinite objects, which is impossible. Al-Sanusi refutes this by defending the possibility of a single knowledge encompassing many objects, and clarifying that known objects need not exist externally. Follow along to see how he dismantles each premise of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the structure: the Mu'tazila argument, an objection, and the author's refutation.
The passage is dense—take your time.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila argument
The argument says: if God knows with a knowledge, then since His known objects are infinite, He would need infinite knowledges (because one knowledge knows only one object). Find the premises and conclusion.
3
Understand the objection and its refutation
The interlocutor objects: 'How can known objects be infinite?' The author answers: known objects need not be existent. Then the author directly refutes the impossibility claim, arguing that heedlessness is itself the relation to the other object, and differences among knowledges are relational.
The author also addresses the risk of reducing all attributes to one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو كان عالماً بالعلم لكان له علوم غير متناهية لأنه عالم بما لا نهاية له والعلم الواحد لا يتعلق إلا بمعلوم واحد وإلا لما صح لنا أن نعلم كونه عالماً بأحد المعلومين مع الذهول عن علمه بالمعلوم الآخر، ولجاز أن يكون علمه الواحد، قائماً مقام العلوم المختلفة في الشاهد للقطع ، بأن علمنا بالبياض يخالف علمنا بالسواد، ولو جاز هذا الجاز أن يكون له صفة واحدة تقوم مقام الصفات كلها بأن يكون علماً وقدرة وحياة وغير ذلك، بل تقوم الذات مقام الكل . ويلزم نفي الصفات. وإذا لم يتعلق العلم الواحد إلا لمعلوم واحد لزم أن يكون له بحسب معلوماته الغير المتناهية علوم غير متناهية، وهو باطل وفاقاً واستدلالاً بما مر مراراً من أن كل عدد يوجد بالفعل فهو متناه . فإن قيل : فكيف جاز أن تكون المعلومات غير متناهية . . ؟ قلنا : لأن المعلوم لا يلزم أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج. والجواب : أنه لا يمتنع تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومات كثيرة، ولو إلى غير نهاية وما ذكر في بيان الامتناع ليس بشيء لأن الذهول إنما هو عين التعلق بالمعلوم الآخر، وعلمنا أيضاً بالسواد والبياض لا يختلف إلا بالإضافة ، ولو سلم فيقام علمه مقام علوم مختلفة لا يستلزم جواز قيام صفة واحدة له مقام صفات مختلفة الجنس .
The second: if He were knowing by a knowledge, He would have infinite knowledges because He knows what has no end, and a single knowledge is related only to a single known object; otherwise, it would not be correct for us to know that He is knowing one of two known objects while being heedless of His knowledge of the other, and it would be possible for His one knowledge to take the place of different knowledges in the witnessed world, since our knowledge of whiteness differs from our knowledge of blackness. If this were permissible, then it would be permissible for Him to have a single attribute taking the place of all attributes, becoming knowledge, power, life, etc., and even the essence would replace everything, leading to negation of attributes. And if a single knowledge relates only to one known object, then He must have, according to His infinite known objects, infinite knowledges, which is false by consensus and by the repeated proof that every number that exists actually is finite. If it is said: how then can the known objects be infinite? We say: because the known object does not have to exist externally. The answer: that it is not impossible for one knowledge to relate to many known objects, even to infinity, and what was mentioned in explaining the impossibility is nothing, because heedlessness is itself the relation to the other known object, and our knowledge of black and white does not differ except by relation. And even if granted that His knowledge takes the place of different knowledges, that does not entail the permissibility of a single attribute taking the place of attributes of different genera.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazila proof: if God knows through a knowledge, then since He knows infinitely many things, and one knowledge can only know one object, He would need infinitely many knowledges. The proof argues that if one knowledge could know multiple objects, then we could not be certain that God knows a specific object when we are unaware of His knowledge of another object. Also, in the witnessed world, one knowledge of whiteness is different from knowledge of blackness. If one knowledge sufficed, then one attribute could replace all, leading to the denial of attributes altogether. The author first responds to the objection that known objects could be infinite: the known object need not be existent externally. Then he gives the direct answer: it is not impossible for one knowledge to relate to multiple objects, even infinitely. He refutes the 'heedlessness' argument by noting that heedlessness is itself the relation to the other object. He also clarifies that difference between knowledge of white and black is only by relation, not by essence. Finally, even if one knowledge can replace multiple, that does not mean one attribute can replace attributes of different genera (like knowledge and power).

معلوم
غير متناه
تعلق
صفات
ذهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One knowledge can only relate to one object; if God knows infinite objects, He must have infinite knowledges.
Correction: Sanusi argues that one knowledge can relate to many objects, even infinitely, and the differences among knowledges are relational, not essential.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the author's answer to the second argument?
A) That infinite knowledges are possible for God
B) That one knowledge can relate to many objects, even infinitely
C) That the Mu'tazila argument proves the negation of attributes
D) That known objects must be finite

Key Takeaway
A single divine knowledge can encompass all known objects without requiring multiple knowledges.

?

Third Argument: The Verse 'Above Every Possessor of Knowledge'
Third proof from Quranic verse and author's reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third Mu'tazila argument against God's knowledge and the author's two-part reply based on Quranic interpretation.

Core Idea
The verse 'above every knower' does not apply to God; other verses affirm His knowledge.

The Mu'tazila cite a Quranic verse to argue that if God had knowledge, there would be someone more knowledgeable above Him — an impossibility. The author responds by denying the verse's universal scope and invoking other verses that confirm God's knowledge. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical structure: premise, conclusion, objection, and answer.
The verse is from Surah Yusuf (12:76).
2
Identify the Mu'tazila argument
The argument: If God possesses knowledge (ilm), then someone knows more than Him (alim) because of the verse. The consequent is impossible, so God cannot have knowledge.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the reply's first part
The author denies that the verse is general (amm) — it does not include God. The verse only applies to created beings.
This is a common tactic: restricting the scope of a proof text.
4
Examine the reply's second part
The author counters with other verses that affirm God's knowledge. These verses take precedence over the literal reading of the disputed verse.
The author refers to earlier discussion ('as mentioned before') for details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان الباري ذا علم لكان فوقه عليم لقوله تعالى: ﴿ وفوق كل ذي علم علیم ) واللازم باطل قطعاً. والجواب منع كونه على عمومه والمعارضة بالآيات الدالة على ثبوت العلم كما مرّ.
The third: if the Creator were possessor of knowledge, then there would be above Him one who knows, due to His saying: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is one who knows' (12:76). The consequent is absolutely false. The answer is to deny that it is general, and to counter with the verses indicating the affirmation of knowledge, as mentioned before.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
The third and final Mu'tazila proof is based on a literal reading of Quran 12:76: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is one who knows'. They argue that if God knows something, then there would be someone more knowledgeable above Him, which is impossible. The author's response is twofold: first, the verse is not general (it does not apply to God); second, there are other verses that affirm God's knowledge (e.g., 6:80, 4:166), which take precedence. The author does not elaborate here but refers to earlier discussion ('as mentioned before').

Key Terms

آيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'above every possessor of knowledge' proves that God cannot have knowledge, because if He did, there would be someone above Him.
Correction: The verse is not universal; it only applies to created beings. God is the ultimate knower, and other verses explicitly affirm His knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) He accepts that God is above all knowledge
B) He denies the generality of the verse and cites other verses affirming God's knowledge

Key Takeaway

Answer to the Objection that Divine Knowledge Requires Infinite Sciences
Al-Taftazani presents and answers the second objection against divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the objection that God's knowledge would require infinite sciences by showing that a single knowledge can relate to multiple known objects without multiplicity in the attribute itself.

Core Idea
A single divine knowledge can relate to infinite objects without becoming multiple.

The objection that God's knowledge must be infinite if He knows infinite things seems compelling at first. But al-Taftazani shows that the objection rests on a mistaken assumption about how knowledge relates to its objects. He defends the view that one knowledge can relate to many objects, even infinitely, and that differences in our knowledge of different objects are merely relational, not essential. This passage unpacks that argument and addresses a subsidiary question about infinite known objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premises
Carefully read the objection's claims: if God knows by a single knowledge, then because He knows infinite objects, He must have infinite sciences. Note the premise that a single knowledge relates only to a single known object.
Pay attention to the logical consequences drawn.
2
Identify key terms
Note the terms: علم (knowledge), معلومات (known objects), ذهول (heedlessness), إضافة (relation). These are central to the argument.
3
Follow the author's answer
Trace how al-Taftazani refutes each part: he denies that a single knowledge cannot relate to many objects, explains that heedlessness is just a relation, and argues that differences in knowledge are only relational.
Notice the subsidiary question and its answer about infinite known objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو كان عالماً بالعلم لكان له علوم غير متناهية لأنه عالم بما لا نهاية له والعلم الواحد لا يتعلق إلا بمعلوم واحد وإلا لما صح لنا أن نعلم كونه عالماً بأحد المعلومين مع الذهول عن علمه بالمعلوم الآخر، ولجاز أن يكون علمه الواحد، قائماً مقام العلوم المختلفة في الشاهد للقطع ، بأن علمنا بالبياض يخالف علمنا بالسواد، ولو جاز هذا الجاز أن يكون له صفة واحدة تقوم مقام الصفات كلها بأن يكون علماً وقدرة وحياة وغير ذلك، بل تقوم الذات مقام الكل . ويلزم نفي الصفات. وإذا لم يتعلق العلم الواحد إلا لمعلوم واحد لزم أن يكون له بحسب معلوماته الغير المتناهية علوم غير متناهية، وهو باطل وفاقـاً واستدلالاً بما مر مراراً من أن كل عدد يوجد بالفعل فهو متناه .
فإن قيل : فكيف جاز أن تكون المعلومات غير متناهية . . ؟ قلنا : لأن المعلوم لا يلزم أن يكون موجوداً في الخارج. والجواب : أنه لا يمتنع تعلق العلم الواحد بمعلومات كثيرة، ولو إلى غير نهاية وما ذكر في بيان الامتناع ليس بشيء لأن الذهول إنما هو عين التعلق بالمعلوم الآخر، وعلمنا أيضاً بالسواد والبياض لا يختلف إلا بالإضافة ، ولو سلم فيقام علمه مقام علوم مختلفة لا يستلزم جواز قيام صفة واحدة له مقام صفات مختلفة الجنس .
The second: If He were knowing by knowledge, then He would have infinite sciences, because He knows what is infinite, and a single knowledge does not relate except to a single known object; otherwise it would not be correct for us to know that He is knowing of one of the two known objects while being heedless of His knowledge of the other known object, and it would be possible that His single knowledge stands in place of different sciences in the witnessed realm, decisively, because our knowledge of whiteness differs from our knowledge of blackness. And if this were permissible, then it would be permissible for Him to have a single attribute that stands in place of all attributes—being knowledge, power, life, and so forth; rather the essence would stand in place of all. And this necessitates the negation of attributes. And if a single knowledge relates only to a single known object, then necessarily He would have, according to His infinite known objects, infinite sciences, and this is false unanimously and by inference from what has been repeated: that every number that exists in actuality is finite.
If it is said: How then are the known objects allowed to be infinite? We say: Because the known object does not have to exist externally. And the answer: It is not impossible for a single knowledge to relate to many known objects, even to infinity, and what was mentioned in showing impossibility is nothing, because heedlessness is precisely the relation to the other known object, and our knowledge of blackness and whiteness differs only by relation; and if it is conceded that His knowledge stands in place of different sciences, it does not necessitate the permissibility of a single attribute standing in place of attributes of different kinds.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair concerning divine knowledge. The objection argues that if God knows through a single knowledge, then because He knows infinite things, He must have infinite sciences, since a single knowledge can only relate to one known object. This leads to absurd consequences—either attributes collapse into one, or attributes are negated. The objection also appeals to our experience: our knowledge of whiteness differs from our knowledge of blackness. The author then presents a subsidiary question: how can known objects be infinite? His answer: known objects do not have to exist externally, so there is no problem. The full answer: a single knowledge can relate to many known objects, even infinitely. The claim that heedlessness prevents this is false because heedlessness is itself the relation to the other known object. The difference between our knowledge of white and black is only by relation, not by essence. Even if we concede that God's knowledge stands in place of different sciences, that does not imply that a single attribute stands in place of attributes of different kinds (like knowledge and power). Thus the objection is refuted.

علم
معلومات
ذهول
إضافة
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection successfully proves that God must have infinite sciences.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that a single knowledge can relate to multiple objects, so infinite sciences are not required.

Checkpoint

A) Because known objects do not exist externally
B) Because knowledge differs only by relation and not by essence
C) Because heedlessness prevents relation to multiple objects
D) Because knowledge is an attribute that can be divided

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani refutes the objection by arguing that a single knowledge can relate to multiple objects without requiring multiplicity in the attribute, as differences are merely relational.

Answering the Objection from Qur'an 12:76
Al-Taftazani refutes the third objection against divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the objection that divine knowledge would imply a higher knower, by denying the generality of the verse and citing counter-evidence.

Core Idea
The verse 'above every knower' does not apply to God; other verses confirm His unique knowledge.

This lesson addresses a clever objection against God's attribute of knowledge. The opponent quotes a Qur'anic verse to suggest that if God knows, there must be someone more knowing above Him. Al-Taftazani responds with two powerful arguments: first, the verse is not general, and second, many other verses explicitly affirm that God is the all-knowing. Let's see how he dismantles the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the verse quoted.
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the objection's premise and conclusion.
Notice the verse quotation: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is a knower.'
2
Understand the opponent's reasoning.
The opponent argues: if God knows, then He is a knower; the verse says above every knower is a knower, so there would be a knower above God, leading to an infinite regress or a higher being, which is impossible. Therefore, God cannot know.
This is a logical deduction based on a general reading of the verse.
3
Examine the first part of the answer: denial of generality.
Al-Taftazani says the verse is not general ('ala 'umumihi); it may not include God. The 'possessor of knowledge' refers to creatures, not the Creator. Thus, the implication does not apply to God.
This is a common interpretive move: limiting the scope of a general statement.
4
Examine the second part: countering with affirmative verses.
Al-Taftazani points to other Qur'anic verses that explicitly affirm God's knowledge, such as 'My Lord encompasses all things in knowledge' (6:80). These verses take precedence and show that God is the ultimate knower, so the objection fails.
This is a method of ta'arud (contradiction) and tarjih (preference).
5
Reflect on the overall refutation.
Together, the two moves defeat the objection. The verse cited by the opponent is not universally applicable, and the clear texts affirming God's knowledge override any apparent implication.
This shows the importance of considering all relevant evidence in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان الباري ذا علم لكان فوقه عليم لقوله تعالى: ﴿ وفوق كل ذي علم علیم ) واللازم باطل قطعاً. والجواب منع كونه على عمومه والمعارضة بالآيات الدالة على ثبوت العلم كما مرّ.
The third: If the Creator were possessed of knowledge, there would be above Him a knower (عليم) due to His statement: 'And above every possessor of knowledge is a knower.' The necessary conclusion is false decisively. The answer: denial that it is general (على عمومه) and countering with the verses indicating the affirmation of knowledge as has passed.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This is the third objection against divine knowledge. It cites the Qur'anic verse (12:76) 'And above every possessor of knowledge is a knower' to argue that if God knows, then there must be someone more knowing above Him, which is absurd. The answer consists of two parts: (1) Denying that the verse is general (على عمومه) in the sense that it applies to God; perhaps the 'possessor of knowledge' refers only to creatures. (2) Countering with other verses that affirm God's knowledge, such as 'My Lord encompasses all things in knowledge' (Q. 6:80) and 'God witnesses what He has revealed to you; He revealed it with His knowledge' (Q. 4:166). These verses establish that God is the knower par excellence, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
عليم
عموم
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the verse 'above every knower' applies absolutely to all, including God, thus denying God's attribute of knowledge.
Correction: Al-Taftazani explains that the verse is not general and is overridden by explicit affirmative verses, so it does not negate God's knowledge.

Checkpoint
How does al-Taftazani answer the third objection from the verse 'Above every possessor of knowledge is a knower'?

B) He says the verse is not general and cites other verses affirming God's knowledge
C) He argues that the verse refers only to angels
D) He claims the verse is abrogated

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani defends divine knowledge by showing that the objection's proof text is not universally applicable and is outweighed by explicit verses affirming God's omniscience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems to conflict with what you know about Allah's attributes, do you immediately question the verse or do you trust that there is a reconciliation? Ask yourself: have I ever dismissed a clear teaching because of one misunderstood verse?
?
Al-Taftazani uses mu'arada—countering with other clear texts. Think about your own life: when you face doubts about your faith, do you actively seek other evidences from the Qur'an and Sunnah, or do you let the doubt linger? What steps can you take today to arm yourself with the verses that affirm Allah's perfect attributes?
?
The objection's argument sounds logical but is based on a false application of the verse. In your own journey, where might you be applying a general rule to Allah in a way that limits His transcendence? Commit to one area where you will replace a limited understanding with the comprehensive knowledge of Allah from revelation.

Defining Divine Power: Ability to Act and Refrain
Al-Taftazani defines divine power and explains the role of motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of divine power as the ability to act or refrain according to motives, and why this does not contradict the necessity of action when the motive is complete.

Core Idea
Divine power is the ability to both act and refrain, not necessitation.

Al-Taftazani begins by defining what it means for God to be powerful (qādir). The well-known definition is that the powerful one is the one who, if He wills, He does, and if He wills, He refrains. This means that both action and inaction are possible for Him, depending on motives. This lesson clarifies this definition and distinguishes it from the concept of a necessitator (mūjib) that acts by essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of qādir
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the definition of القادر and its meaning.
Notice the phrase 'إن شاء فعل، وإن شاء ترك'.
2
Understand ability to act and refrain
The definition means both act and refrain are sound from God according to different motives. Reflect on why this does not make God a necessitator.
The example of fire helps clarify the distinction.
3
Examine views on the motive (dāʿī)
Al-Razi and others offer different explanations of what the motive is. Consider why this discussion matters for understanding divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
إثبات القدرة الله تعالى
قال : بمعنى تمكنه من الفعل والترك، وصحتهما عنه بحسب الدواعي، فأصل الباب، أن قدم الصانع مع حدوث المصنوع لا يتصور في القادر لامتناع التخلف، فإذا ثبت حدوث الكل أو صدور الكل عنه بواسطة فظاهر وإلا فلا بد من نفي تتعاقب حوادث لا بداية لها يكون شيء وطأ في صدور الحوادث عن الموجب القديم، وقد سبق وأن يوجب الموجب قديماً مختاراً يستند إليه الحوادث فهو وفاق) .
المشهور أن القادر (۱) هو الذي إن شاء فعل، وإن شاء ترك. ومعناه : أنه يتمكن من الفعل والترك. أي يصح كل منهما عنه بحسب الدواعي المختلفة. وهذا لا ينافي لزوم الفعل عنه عند خلوص الداعي بحيث لا يصح عدم وقوعه ولا استلزام عدم الفرق بينه وبين الموجب لأنه الذي يجب عنه الفعل نظراً إلى نفسه . بحيث لا يتمكن من الترك أصلاً، ولا يصدق أنه إن شاء ترك كالشمس في الإشراق. والنار في الإحراق .
وميل الإمام الرازي إلى أن الداعي من جنس الإدراكات وهو العلم أو الظن أو الاعتقاد أن في الفعل مصلحة ومنفعة مثلاً . وقيل : من جنس الإرادة، وقيل: نفس المصلحة ،والمنفعة ولاخفاء في أنها لا يلزم أن تكون كذلك في نفس الأمر، إذ ربما يظن المفسدة مصلحة فيقدم على الفعل، ثم الأصل المعول عليه في باب إثبات قادرية الباري أنه صانع قديم له صنع حادث، وصدور الحادث عن القديم إنما يتصور بطريق القدرة دون الإيجاب. وإلا فيلزم تخلف المعلول عن تمام علته، حيث وجدت في الأزل العلة دون المعلول، ولا يتم هذا إلا بعد إثبات أن شيئاً من الحوادث
(۱) هو قادر ومقتدر، ذو قدرة ومقدرة، والقدير : هو الفاعل لما يشاء على قدر ما تقتضي حكمته لا زائداً عليها ولا ناقصاً عنه ولذلك لا يصح أن يوصف به إلا الله تعالى ولا أحد يوصف بالقدرة من وجه إلا ويصح أن يوصف بالعجز من غير وجه الله تعالى فهو الذي ينتفي عنه العجر من كل وجه تعالى شانه .
The Second Topic: Establishing Power (القدرة) for God Almighty. He said: In the sense of His being able (تمكنه) to act and to refrain, and the soundness of each from Him according to motives (الدواعي). So the fundamental principle of the topic is that the eternity of the Maker with the temporality of the made is inconceivable for the powerful (القادر) due to the impossibility of [effect] lagging. Thus, if the temporality of all things or their issuance from Him through an intermediary is established, it is apparent; otherwise, one must deny [that] successive events without beginning have a foothold in the occurrence of events from the eternal necessitator (الموجب). And it has preceded that the necessitator necessitates an eternal, volitional [agent] to which events are attributed; that is agreed upon.
The well-known [definition] is that the powerful (القادر) is one who, if He wills, He does, and if He wills, He refrains. Its meaning: that He is able to do and to refrain, i.e., each is sound from Him according to different motives. This does not contradict the necessity of the act from Him when the motive is pure, such that its non-occurrence is not sound, nor does it necessitate no difference between Him and the necessitator (الموجب), because the latter is one from whom the act necessarily proceeds by virtue of His essence, such that He is not at all able to refrain, nor is it true that if He willed He would refrain—like the sun in shining and fire in burning.
Imam al-Razi inclined to the view that the motive (الداعي) is of the genus of perceptions—it is knowledge, conjecture, or belief that there is in the act a benefit or advantage, for example. And it is said: it is of the genus of will. And it is said: it is the benefit itself. And it is not hidden that these things are not necessarily such in actual fact, for perhaps one mistakes harm for benefit and proceeds to act. Then the principle relied upon in establishing the powerfulness (قادرية) of the Creator is that He is an eternal maker having a temporal making, and the issuance of the temporal from the eternal is only conceivable by way of power (قدرة), not by necessitation (إيجاب). Otherwise, the effect's lagging behind its complete cause would follow, where the cause existed in pre-eternity without the effect. And this is not complete except after establishing that something of events...
Footnote: He is powerful and omnipotent, possessor of power and capability. Al-Qadir is the one who does what He wills to the extent that His wisdom requires, no more and no less. Therefore it is not correct to describe anyone but God with it. No one is described with power in any respect unless he can be described with incapacity in some other respect. God is the one from whom incapacity is negated in every respect, exalted is His status.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of divine power. It begins with a definition: 'power' means the ability to act or refrain according to motives. The fundamental principle is that an eternal agent producing a temporal effect must be powerful (capable of action or inaction) because if the agent were a necessitator (موجب) that acts by essence, the effect would have to be co-eternal. The author then gives the well-known definition of القادر: 'one who if He wills, He does, and if He wills, He refrains,' meaning that both act and refrain are possible from Him relative to different motives. This does not contradict the necessity of the act when the motive is pure, because the agent is still capable of refraining in principle, unlike a necessitator (like fire). The author reports Imam al-Razi's view that the motive is a perception (knowledge, conjecture, or belief of benefit), and other views that it is will or benefit itself. The key point is that these motives need not correspond to actual reality; one might mistakenly think harm is benefit. Then the fundamental proof: to establish that the Creator is powerful, we argue that He is an eternal maker producing temporal works; this production must be through power, not necessitation, otherwise the effect would have to be eternal like the cause. The proof is left incomplete, but the principle is clear. The footnote expands on the term القدير, emphasizing that only God deserves it absolutely.

Key Terms
قدرة
قادر
فعل
ترك
داعي
موجب
قدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God always acts when He wills, He is a necessitator like fire.
Correction: The passage clarifies that true power includes the ability to refrain given different motives; necessity at a moment does not negate the capacity to do otherwise in principle.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the definition of 'qādir' entail?
A) The ability to act only
B) The ability to act or refrain according to motives
C) The inability to refrain
D) The necessity of acting always

Key Takeaway
Divine power is the ability to act or refrain according to will and motives, which distinguishes God from a necessitating cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your daily reliance on God, do you truly believe that His power to help you is never compelled but always a free act of His will? How does that change the way you ask Him for help?
?
If God's actions are tied to motives (like wisdom and mercy), does that make you more or less confident that He will answer your prayers? Examine whether you ever treat God as a 'vending machine' that must dispense based on your input.
?
The lesson teaches that only God has absolute power without any possibility of incapacity. In what areas of your life have you been acting as if your own power or someone else's power is absolute? How can you reorient that belief to rely solely on His unique capability?

Fundamental Proof of Divine Power: Eternal Maker and Temporal Effect
Al-Taftazani states the principle for proving divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental principle used to prove that God is powerful: an eternal maker producing a temporal effect must do so through power, not necessitation.

Core Idea
An eternal cause producing a temporal effect requires power, not necessitation.

Having defined divine power, al-Taftazani now presents the fundamental proof for establishing that God is powerful (qādir). The key insight is that if God were a mere necessitator (mūjib), His effect would have to be co-eternal with Him. Since we observe contingent, temporal events, their source must be a powerful agent who acts by choice. This lesson examines this principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle passage
Read the Arabic passage on the fundamental principle for proving divine power.
The passage is incomplete but the core idea is clear.
2
Understand the argument
If the cause is eternal and necessitates its effect, the effect would be eternal. Since events are temporal, the cause must act through power.
Contrast with the necessitator concept.
3
Reflect on the proof
Consider why the author says this proof is not complete without establishing that some events are temporal.
This points to the need for a prior proof of the world's temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
The temporality of the world proves God's voluntary power. When you see time passing and events unfolding, do you see them as signs of His will, or do you take them for granted? What is one way you can remind yourself today that every moment is a direct act of His power and choice?

The First Proof: God's Power and Free Will
Taftazani presents the first of six proofs for God's power and free will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof establishes that the Necessary Being must be powerful and free (qadir mukhtar) by eliminating the impossible alternatives of delay or infinite regress.

Core Idea
If God were not powerful and free, either an effect would be delayed or events would regress infinitely — both impossible.

Taftazani now begins a series of rational arguments to prove that God is not merely a cause but a powerful, freely acting agent. The first argument builds on earlier proofs of God's existence and the refutation of infinite regress. By examining the logical alternatives, it shows that only a being who is both powerful and free can account for temporal events without absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let Taftazani's own logical rhythm come through.
Notice the concise, argumentative style.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The argument presents two possibilities if God were not qadir mukhtar. Find and note each one.
3
Understand why each alternative fails
For each alternative, understand the reason it is impossible. The first leads to a delayed effect, the second to infinite regress.
Connect this to the earlier proof against infinite regress.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since both alternatives are impossible, the only remaining possibility is that God is powerful and free. State the conclusion in your own words.
This conclusion is necessary, not optional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولنعد من الأدلة عدة بعد التنبيه على أصل الباب يريد إيراد عدة تقريرات للأصحاب . الأول : لما ثبت بما سبق في إثبات الصانع وإبطال التسلسل انتهاء الحوادث إلى الواجب لزم كونه قادراً مختاراً، وإلا فإما أن يوجب حادثاً بلا واسطة فيلزم التخلف، حيث وجد في الأزل، ولم يوجد في الحادث أو لا فيلزم أن يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بآخر لا إلى نهاية وقد بين بطلانه .
He said: Let us return to several proofs after drawing attention to the principle of the chapter. He intends to present several detailed arguments of the associates. The first: Since it has been established by what preceded in proving the Maker and invalidating infinite regress that temporal events terminate in the Necessary Being, it follows that He is powerful and free; otherwise, either He necessitates an event without intermediary, leading to delay—since He existed in eternity and the event did not exist—or not, leading to every event being preceded by another without end, and its falsehood has been shown.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the introductory statement and the first of six arguments for God being powerful and free (qadir mukhtar). Taftazani first announces that he will present several proofs after clarifying the principle of the chapter. The first argument builds on earlier proofs of God's existence and the refutation of infinite regress. Since all temporal events (hawadith) terminate in the Necessary Being, He must be powerful and free. If He were not, two possibilities arise: either He would necessitate an event without an intermediary, but then the event would have to exist in eternity (since the cause is eternal), yet it does not—this is a delayed effect (takhalluf) which is impossible; or He would not necessitate any event directly, which would mean every event is preceded by another ad infinitum, which has already been shown false. Thus, the Necessary Being must be qadir mukhtar.

Key Terms
قادر مختار
تخلف
تسلسل
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power and will are proven only by scripture, not by reason.
Correction: This argument is purely rational, using logic and earlier philosophical proofs without relying on revelation.

Checkpoint
What is the immediate conclusion of the first argument if the Necessary Being were not powerful and free?
A) The world would be eternal.
B) Either a delayed effect or an infinite regress would follow.
C) God would be unjust.
D) The world would be self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
The first proof concludes that the Necessary Being must be powerful and free because any other scenario leads to an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Necessary Being were not powerful and free, your very existence would be impossible. How does this truth change the way you live this day, knowing that each moment is sustained by His free choice?
?
The proof eliminates both 'delay' and 'infinite regress' as explanations for why the world exists. In your own life, do you ever act as if God's will is delayed or distant? What would it mean to truly believe that He is directly and freely involved in every detail?
?
You have been given the gift of existence by a God who could have not created you. How will you respond to His free will with your own choices today? Commit to one concrete action that acknowledges His power and freedom over your life.

Second Proof: The Need for Power and Choice in Creation
Second argument that God's causal influence must be through power and choice, not necessary emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's causal influence on the world must be through power (qudra) and choice (ikhtiyar) rather than through necessary emanation (ijab).

Core Idea
God's creation is a deliberate act of power and choice, not a necessary overflow.

Taftazani now presents the second proof that God's influence on the world is not by way of necessitation. He argues that if it were by necessitation, we would face impossible consequences. This lesson walks through the three logical possibilities and why each fails, leaving power and choice as the only viable explanation. Understanding this argument is crucial for distinguishing the Islamic concept of creation from philosophical emanationism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice that Taftazani presents a concise argument with three branches.
The passage uses conditional reasoning: 'if it were by necessitation, then...'
2
Identify the three possibilities
Taftazani lists three possibilities if influence were by necessitation: 1) without an intermediary, 2) with an eternal intermediary, 3) with a temporal intermediary. Identify each in the text.
The first two lead to the eternity of the world; the third leads to an infinite regress.
3
Understand why each possibility fails
For each possibility, Taftazani gives a reason why it is impossible. Connect each reason to the earlier proof that the world is temporal (hadith) and that infinite regress of temporal events is false.
The proof relies on previously established conclusions about the world's temporality.
4
Grasp the conclusion
After eliminating all three possibilities, the only remaining option is that influence is through power and choice. Reflect on what this means for God's attributes.
Power and choice are essential attributes of the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : تأثير الواجب في وجود العالم يجب أن يكون بطريق القدرة والاختيار، إذ لو كان بطريق الإيجاب، فإما أن يكون بلا واسطة أو بوسط قديم فيلزم قدم العالم وقد بين حدوثه. وإما بوسط حادث، فينقل الكلام إلى كيفية صدوره، وتتسلسل الحوادث وقد بين بطلانه
The second: The influence of the Necessary Being upon the existence of the world must be by way of power and choice; for if it were by way of necessitation, then either it would be without an intermediary or with an eternal intermediary, leading to the eternity of the world, while its temporality has been shown; or with a temporal intermediary, then the discussion is transferred to its mode of emanation, and temporal events would regress infinitely, and its falsehood has been shown.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is the second argument, which is a proof by elimination. Taftazani argues that God's influence on the world must be through power (qudra) and choice (ikhtiyar). If it were through necessitation (ijab), there are three possibilities. First, no intermediary: the world would be eternal, but its temporality has been proven. Second, an eternal intermediary: again the world would be eternal. Third, a temporal intermediary: then we must explain how that intermediary emanates from God, leading to an infinite regress of temporal events, which is invalid. Therefore, the only remaining possibility is power and choice.

Key Terms
تأثير
إيجاب
قدرة
اختيار
قدم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'necessitation' (ijab) means any kind of determination, including divine decree, confusing it with power and choice.
Correction: Here, 'necessitation' specifically means emanation by necessity, where the effect follows from the cause automatically, like light from the sun. It does not involve will or choice.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility if God's influence were by necessitation without an intermediary?
A) The world would be temporal.
B) The world would be eternal.
C) An infinite regress would occur.
D) God would be powerless.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of the world is a free act of power and choice, not a necessary emanation; otherwise impossible consequences follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's creation of the world is a deliberate act of power and choice, not necessity, then every moment of your existence is a fresh act of His will. Does your daily life reflect this constant dependence on His choice, or do you drift along as if your existence were automatic?
?
The argument refutes the idea that God's action is like a blind, necessary force. Do you ever treat God's decrees as mechanical or impersonal, forgetting that each event in your life is chosen by a personal, all-powerful Lord? How would your gratitude change if you truly believed that every breath is a chosen gift?
?
God's creation requires no intermediary; He acts directly. How does this truth challenge your tendency to rely on secondary causes (people, circumstances) as if they were independent, rather than seeing them as mere tools in His immediate hand?

Third Proof: The Need for a Free Chooser from the Diversity of Bodies
Third argument from the diversity of bodily attributes to a powerful and free chooser.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for the existence of a powerful free chooser based on the diversity of bodily attributes and the principle of specification (takhsis).

Core Idea
Diversity in bodies requires a free chooser, not just a necessitator.

This lesson presents the third argument from the al-Maqasid, which uses the observed diversity of bodies in attributes like shape, color, and taste to prove the existence of a Creator who is both powerful and free in choice. The argument follows a logical chain: diversity cannot arise without a specifier, and that specifier cannot be the essence of body itself, nor can it be an infinite chain of specifiers. Hence, we must terminate in a free chooser.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Start by recognizing that bodies differ in attributes such as shape, color, taste, and smell. This is the observed fact that sets the argument in motion.
This premise is uncontroversial and grounded in everyday experience.
2
Identify the need for a specifier
From the diversity, the argument infers that there must be a specifier (mukhasis) because specification without a specifier is impossible. This is a fundamental principle in kalam.
The principle 'لا بد أن يكون المخصص' is the engine of the proof.
3
Eliminate false candidates
The specifier cannot be the essence of body (jismiyya) itself or any of its necessary concomitants, since these are common to all bodies and cannot account for differences.
This elimination forces the specifier to be external to the body.
4
Consider the regress problem
The discussion now shifts to the specifier itself: it must be specified to a particular body. This leads to a dilemma: either an infinite regress of specifiers (absurd) or a termination in a free chooser.
Infinite regress is rejected because it would never provide a final explanation.
5
Conclude with a free chooser
Since a mere necessitator (mujib) would relate equally to all bodies, it cannot produce specific differences. Therefore, the specifier must be a powerful free chooser (qadir mukhtar).
This is the desired conclusion (al-matlub).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: اختلاف الأجسام بالأوصاف، واختصاص كل بما له من الشكل واللون والطعم والرائحة، وغير ذلك، لا بد أن يكون المخصص لامتناع التخصيص بلا مخصص، فذلك المخصص لا يجوز أن يكون نفس الجسمية أو شيئاً من لوازمها لكونها مشتركة بين الكل بل أمر آخر، فينقل الكلام إلى اختصاصه بذلك الجسم، فإما أن تتسلسل المخصصات وهو محال، أو ينتهي إلى قادر مختار بناء على أن نسبة الموجب إلى الكل على السواء وهو المطلوب.
The third: The difference of bodies in attributes, and the specification of each with what it has of shape, color, taste, smell, and other things, necessarily requires a specifier (mukhasis), because specification without a specifier is impossible. That specifier cannot be the essence of body (jismiyya) itself or any of its necessary concomitants, since they are common to all; rather it is something else. Then the discussion is transferred to its specification with that body, so either the specifiers regress infinitely—which is absurd—or they terminate in a powerful free chooser, given that the relation of the necessitator to all is equal. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is the third argument, which uses the principle of specification (takhsis). Bodies differ in attributes like shape, color, taste, etc. Such specification cannot occur without a specifier (mukhasis). This specifier cannot be the essence of body (jismiyya) because that is common to all bodies, nor its necessary concomitants. So it must be something external. That external thing, when considered, itself needs specification regarding which body to specify, leading to either an infinite regress of specifiers or a termination in a powerful free chooser. The rationale: if the specifier were a mere necessitator (mujib), its relation to all bodies would be equal, so no specification would occur. Hence, the specifier must be a free chooser (mukhtar) with power.

Key Terms
تخصيص
مخصص
جسمية
قادر مختار
نسبة الموجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the specifier could be the body's own essence (jismiyya) or its necessary attributes.
Correction: The essence of body is common to all bodies, so it cannot explain their specific differences. The specifier must be external and free.

Checkpoint
Why cannot the specifier of bodies be the essence of body (jismiyya) itself?
A) Because it is common to all bodies.
B) Because it is temporal.
C) Because it is a necessary being.
D) Because it is abstract.

Key Takeaway
The diversity of bodies in attributes proves that their specifier must be a powerful free chooser, not a mere necessitator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the diversity in your own body and life, do you truly acknowledge that every detail is specified by a free Chooser? How does this affect your reliance on Him – do you trust His choices for you, including the difficult ones?
?
If the specifier were a mere necessitator, you would have no personal relationship with your Creator. Do you live as if God is a personal free Agent or as a distant force? What would change today if you truly believed He personally chose your particular form, fate, and circumstances?
?
The argument shows that infinite regress is absurd. In your own thinking, do you stop at God or do you let your mind wander into endless causes and excuses? Commit today to halt your search for ultimate explanations at the Creator and surrender to His sovereignty.

Fourth Proof: If God Were a Necessary Emanator
Sanusi presents a reductio argument against necessary emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument that if God were a necessary emanator, the world's removal would imply His removal, proving that God acts by power and choice.

Core Idea
If the world were a necessary effect, its removal would entail God's removal—impossible.

Sanusi presents a concise reductio ad absurdum against the notion that God is a necessary emanator. He argues that if the world were a necessary concomitant of God, its removal would force the removal of God Himself, which is impossible. Therefore, God must create through will and power, not by essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow sink in.
The Arabic uses technical terms like موجب بالذات and لازم.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break the argument into premise, implication, impossibility, and conclusion. Notice how the reductio works.
The argument assumes a premise and shows it leads to an absurdity.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why does the removal of the world imply God's removal? Because if the world is a necessary concomitant, its removal entails the removal of the primary. That is impossible for the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان موجد العالم وهو الله تعالى موجباً بالذات لزم من ارتفاع العالم ارتفاعه، بمعنى أن يدل ارتفاعه على ارتفاعه لأن العالم حينئذ يكون من لوازمه ومعلوم بالضرورة أن ارتفاع اللازم يدل على ارتفاع الملزوم، لأن ارتفاع الواجب محال . فتعين أن يكون تأثيره في العالم بطريق القدرة والاختيار دون اللزوم الذاتي والإيجاب.
The fourth: If the originator of the world—and He is God most high—were a necessitator by essence, then the removal of the world would necessitate His removal, meaning that the removal of the world would indicate His removal, because the world would then be among His necessary concomitants; and it is known by necessity that the removal of the concomitant indicates the removal of the primary, because removal of the Necessary Being is impossible. So it is determined that His influence on the world is by way of power and choice, not by essential concomitance and necessitation.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is the fourth argument, a reductio ad absurdum. If God were a necessary emanator (mujib bi al-dhat), the world would be a necessary concomitant (lazim) of His essence. Then, if the world were to cease to exist, that would imply the removal of its concomitant, which would entail the removal of the primary (the Necessary Being). But the removal of the Necessary Being is impossible. Therefore, the world cannot be a necessary concomitant. Hence, God's influence on the world must be through power and choice, not by essential necessity.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
لازم
ملزوم
ارتفاع
قدرة واختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God created the world necessarily, then God depends on the world.
Correction: This argument shows the opposite: if the world were a necessary concomitant, God would be dependent on it in the sense that its removal would entail His removal, which is impossible. Therefore, the world cannot be a necessary concomitant; God is independent.

Checkpoint
What would the removal of the world imply if God were a necessitator by essence?
A) The world would become eternal.
B) God would be removed.
C) Another world would appear.
D) Nothing would happen.

Key Takeaway
God's creation must be through power and choice, not by essential necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God's actions as mechanical necessities, forgetting that every moment of your existence is a fresh act of His free will? How would your daily gratitude change if you truly believed this?
?
The argument shows that God does not depend on the world. Yet do you live as if your happiness depends on worldly things? If the world were removed from your heart, would your awareness of God remain, or would it vanish too?
?
God's power and choice are proven by logic, but do you submit to His sovereign choice in the trials you face, or do you demand explanations as if He owes you a reason? How can you cultivate deeper trust in His wisdom today?

Fifth Proof: The Specification of Celestial Bodies Requires a Free Chooser
Fifth argument: specification of celestial bodies proves free chooser.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the allocation of celestial bodies to their specific places proves the existence of a free chooser (qadir mukhtar) rather than a mere necessitator.

Core Idea
Equal relation of a necessitator to all possibilities demands a free chooser to specify.

We now come to the fifth proof, which looks at the physical universe. The celestial bodies—stars and planets—occupy specific positions and dimensions. If they were produced by a mere necessary cause, that cause would relate equally to all possible positions, so any specific allocation would be arbitrary. This argument shows that such arbitrariness is impossible, thus the cause must be a free agent who chooses one possibility over another.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without translation. Notice the conditional structure: لو لم تكن...لزم. Then read the translation.
The phrase 'نسبة الموجب إلى جميع أجزاء البسيط على السواء' is key.
2
Identify the core premise
The argument begins with the fact of specification: celestial bodies and their dimensions are allocated to specific places. Understand that this is an observed reality.
3
Analyze the conditional reasoning
If the specifier were a necessitator (موجب), its relation to all parts would be equal, leading to 'preferment without a preferrer' which is impossible. Ask yourself: Why is equal relation problematic?
This is the logical heart of the argument.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the impossibility cannot hold, the specifier must be a powerful free chooser (قادر مختار) who selects one possibility over another. Reflect on how this argument supports divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : اختصاص الكواكب والأقطار المحالها لو لم تكن بقادر مختار بل بموجب لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، لأن نسبة الموجب إلى جميع أجزاء البسيط على السواء.
The fifth: The specification of the celestial bodies and the diameters of their places, if it were not by a powerful free chooser but by a necessitator, then preferment without a preferrer would follow, because the relation of the necessitator to all parts of the simple body is equal.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is the fifth argument, focusing on celestial bodies. The allocation of stars and planets to specific places and dimensions (diameters) requires a specifier. If this specifier were a mere necessitator (mujib), its relation to all parts of the simple continuum (basiṭ) would be equal, so there would be no reason for one particular allocation over another. This would be preferment without a preferrer (tarjih bi la murajjih), which is impossible. Therefore, the specifier must be a powerful free chooser (qadir mukhtar) who selects one possibility over another.

Key Terms
اختصاص
كواكب
أقطار
ترجيح بلا مرجح
نسبة الموجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A natural necessitator can explain the specific positions of celestial bodies.
Correction: A necessitator has equal relation to all possibilities, so it cannot specify one without a reason, thus requiring a free chooser.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the specification of celestial bodies were by a mere necessitator?
A) They would be in random positions.
B) There would be preferment without a preferrer.
C) They would all be identical.
D) They would not exist.

Key Takeaway
The allocation of celestial bodies to particular places is a case of specification that cannot be explained by a mere necessitator but requires a free chooser.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every specification in your life—your birthplace, your family, your circumstances—is by a free chooser, not by blind necessity or chance? What would change if you lived with this certainty?
?
When you see the vast differences in people's fates, do you attribute them to 'luck' or 'nature'? How does this proof challenge you to see the hand of a free agent behind every detail?
?
How does the concept of 'preferment without a preferrer' expose the absurdity of attributing any particular event to mere necessity? In what area of your own thinking have you been a 'necessitator' instead of acknowledging a free chooser?

Sixth Proof: Animal Forms Require a Free Chooser
Sanusi argues that animal forms necessitate a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the specific shapes of animals and their organs cannot be explained by mere nature but require a powerful free chooser.

Core Idea
Animal shapes prove that nature alone cannot produce them.

The sixth proof follows the pattern of indirect demonstration. It considers what would happen if the cause of animal formation were blind nature. The actual diversity of animal forms contradicts that, so the cause must be a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to grasp the logical flow.
Notice that Sanusi uses a conditional argument: if nature, then sphere; but animals are not spheres.
2
Identify the hypothesis
The passage presents a hypothetical: if the cause of animal formation were the nature of the sperm (simple or composite), then the animal would be spherical.
3
Compare with reality
Observe that actual animals have complex, non-spherical shapes. This contradicts the hypothesis.
This contradiction shows the hypothesis is false.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the cause cannot be blind nature, it must be a powerful free chooser (qadir mukhtar) that intentionally forms animals.
This is a classic kalam argument for divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : فاعل الحيوانات وأعضائها على صورها وأشكالها، يجب أن يكون قادراً مختاراً . إذ لو كان طبيعة النطفة أو أمراً خارجياً موجباً لزم أن يكون الحيوان على شكل الكرة، إن كانت النطفة بسيطة، لأن ذلك مقتضى الطبيعية، ونسبة الموجب إلى أجزاء البسيط على السوية، وعلى شكل كرات مضمومة بعضها إلى البعض إن كانت النطفة مركبة من البسائط بمثل ما ذكر.
The sixth: The agent of animals and their organs in their forms and shapes must be powerful and free. For if it were the nature of the sperm or an external necessitator, then the animal would necessarily be spherical in shape if the sperm were simple, because that is the requirement of nature, and the relation of the necessitator to the parts of the simple is equal; and if the sperm were composed of simple elements, then the animal would be in the shape of spheres stuck together, similar to what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is the sixth argument, which uses the example of animal development. The agent that produces animals with their specific organs and shapes must be powerful and free (qadir mukhtar). If the cause were merely the nature of the sperm or an external necessitator, then if the sperm were a simple body, the animal would be a perfect sphere, since nature acts uniformly; and if the sperm were composite, the animal would be a cluster of spheres. But actual animals have complex, non-spherical shapes. Therefore, the agent must be a free chooser who intentionally forms the animal.

Key Terms
فاعل
حيوانات
نطفة
طبيعة
كرة
قادر مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nature alone can produce the complex shapes of animals through simple physical processes.
Correction: The argument shows that blind nature, whether simple or composite, can only produce uniform spherical shapes. The observed diversity and complexity of animal forms requires a free agent.

Checkpoint
What would be the shape of an animal if its cause were a simple sperm acting by nature?
A) Cylindrical
B) Spherical
C) Cubic
D) Irregular

Key Takeaway
The specific forms of animals reveal that their maker is not blind nature but a powerful free chooser.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given that nature alone cannot produce the complexity of your own body, what does that imply about the One who formed you? How should this truth reshape your daily awareness of your existence?
?
Every time you look at your hand, you are witnessing a proof of a free Chooser. Do you truly live as if you are a direct creation of a powerful and free Agent, or do you take your form for granted? What one action can you take today to honor that reality?
?
The argument shows that the agent of animals must be powerful and free. How does your life reflect that the same Agent created you with purpose? If your actions today were the only evidence, would they point to a belief in a free Creator or to blind nature?

Evaluating Alternative Proofs for God's Power and Knowledge
Taftazani enumerates and critiques alternative proofs for God's power and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Taftazani considers scriptural, perfection, and order-based arguments for God's power and knowledge to be less robust than rational arguments.

Core Idea
Alternative proofs for God's attributes often rely on premises that themselves require rational proof.

After presenting rational arguments for God's power and knowledge, Taftazani turns to other commonly used proofs: consensus, scripture, perfection, and the order of creation. While these may seem persuasive, he points out that they each face logical difficulties—especially when used as primary evidence. This passage shows his critical method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to understand the overall flow.
2
Identify the three alternative proofs
Find the three arguments listed: consensus/definitive texts, perfection of attributes, and the order of creation.
These are introduced by 'وقد يتمسك'.
3
Notice the structure of critique
Observe how Taftazani groups the critiques: the first six (rational arguments), seventh (scriptural), eighth (perfection), ninth (order).
The text breaks off during the ninth critique.
4
Understand the critique of scriptural and perfection arguments
For scriptural: it presupposes belief in a powerful knowing God. For perfection: it depends on disputed premises about perfections.
This shows the need for prior rational grounding.
5
Reflect on why rational arguments are foundational
Consider why Taftazani implies that rational arguments (the first six) are more reliable than scriptural or perfection-based ones.
Rational arguments do not depend on prior theological commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يتمسك في إثبات كون الباري قادراً عالماً بالإجماع، والنصوص القطعية من الكتاب والسنة، وبأن القدرة والعلم والحياة ونحو ذلك صفات كمال، وأضدادها من العجز والجهل والممات سمات نقص يجب تنزيه الله عنها، وبأن صانع العالم على ما فيه من لطائف الصنع وكمال الانتظام والإحكام عالم قادر بحكم الضرورة. وهذه الوجوه لا تخلو عن محال مناقشة : أما الستة الأول : قلما لا يخفى على المتأمل فيها الواقف على قواعد الفلسفة وأما السابع : فلان مرجع الأدلة السمعية إلى الكتاب، ودلالة المعجزات، وهل يتم الإقرار بها، والإذعان لها قبل التصديق بكون الباري قادراً عالماً فيه تردد وتأمل، وأما الثامن : فلأنه فرع جواز اتصافه بها وكونها كمالات في حقه ووجوب اتصافه بكل كمال ونحو ذلك من المقدمات التي ربما يناقش فيها . وأما التاسع : فلابتنائه على
And one may also argue in proving that the Creator is powerful and knowing by consensus, and the definitive texts of the Book and the Sunna, and by the fact that power, knowledge, life, and the like are attributes of perfection, while their opposites—impotence, ignorance, death—are marks of deficiency from which God must be exalted, and by the fact that the Maker of the world, given what it contains of subtleties of craftsmanship and perfection of order and precision, is knowing and powerful by necessity. These points are not immune to critique: As for the first six, rarely are they hidden from the reflective person who is versed in the principles of philosophy. As for the seventh, because the reference of scriptural proofs is to the Book and the evidence of miracles, and whether one can acknowledge and submit to them before affirming that the Creator is powerful and knowing is a matter of hesitation and reflection. As for the eighth, because it depends on the permissibility of His being described with them, and that they are perfections in His regard, and the obligation of His being described with every perfection, and similar premises which may be disputed. As for the ninth, because it is based on...
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a critical evaluation of additional arguments often used to prove God's power and knowledge. Taftazani lists three other types: (i) consensus and definitive scriptural texts, (ii) the argument from perfection (that attributes like power and knowledge are perfections, and their opposites are deficiencies), and (iii) the argument from the order of creation (an a fortiori argument). He then notes that these arguments are not free from points of criticism. Regarding the first six (the rational arguments previously given), they are clear to those familiar with philosophy. The seventh (scriptural) is problematic because acceptance of scripture and miracles presupposes belief in a powerful, knowing God. The eighth (perfection) depends on the premise that it is permissible for God to have such attributes and that they are perfections, which may be contested. The ninth argument (which is incomplete in the text) is also based on contested premises. Thus, the rational arguments (the six) are more robust.

Key Terms
إجماع
نصوص قطعية
صفات كمال
أضداد
مناقشة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Scriptural arguments can stand alone as proofs for God's attributes.
Correction: They presuppose belief in a powerful, knowing God, thus are not independent proofs.

Checkpoint
Why does Taftazani consider the seventh argument (scriptural texts and miracles) weak?
A) Because scriptures are corrupted.
B) Because accepting them presupposes belief in a powerful, knowing God.
C) Because miracles are not reliable.
D) Because consensus is not proof.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani critiques alternative proofs for God's power and knowledge, showing that rational arguments are more foundational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a theological claim solely because it is in the Quran or hadith without first affirming the rational foundation? How does Taftazani's critique challenge your own approach to seeking knowledge? Will you now examine the rational basis of your beliefs more carefully?
?
Taftazani points out that the perfection argument depends on unproven premises. In your own life, are there beliefs about God that you hold without examining their rational basis? What would it cost you to examine them honestly?
?
The passage shows that even consensus and definitive texts can be critiqued. Are you willing to subject your most cherished religious beliefs to rational scrutiny, or do you fear that might weaken your faith? What does your reluctance reveal about your trust in God's guidance?
